# 道德真经集注

**Dao/Way and Virtue/Power Classic with Annotations**
**Title:** *Dao/Way and Virtue/Power Classic with Annotations*

**Preface to the Dao/Way and Virtue/Power Classic with Annotations**
*Composed by Emperor Xuanzong of the Tang Dynasty*

In days of old, the Primordial Sage
Forcefully expounded the Mysterious Teachings.
The *Daodejing*’s true essence and sacred principles
Illuminated all subsequent generations.

Though his writings remain authentic,
Their profound meaning often diverges.
Condensing his core intent,
Even the strict *Shu Yan* [注: *Shu Yan* refers to the *Shu Yan Dao/Way Classic*, a commentary by Ge Hong (葛洪) of the Jin Dynasty] still falls short.
Extracting his verses and phrases,
The *He Gong* [注: *He Gong*, or *He Gong Dao/Way Classic*, a commentary by He Yan (何晏) of the Wei-Jin period] and others omit too much.

The rest is but faint traces—
Hardly worth mentioning—
Yet the Dao/Way’s wondrous intent of the Primordial Sage
Would surely be lost if neglected.

I am indeed limited in understanding,
Yet I have often felt the joy of preserving this sacred text,
Fearful of losing the principle of *Wu Wei* [注: *Wu Wei*, non-action, a key Daoist concept].
On occasions of solemn repose,
I would knock upon the Mysterious Gate,
And as my insights unfolded,
I penned annotations and elucidations.

This is not meant to establish a singular doctrine,
But merely to supplement the gaps in its transmission.

Now, as I lay down my brush,
I ask the assembled ministers, officials, and scholars of Dao/Way and Buddhist teachings:
If any can arise like the *Bo Shang* [注: *Bo Shang*, or *Bo Shang Dao/Way Classic*, attributed to Laozi’s disciple Bo Yi (伯夷)],
Or heal ailments like *Zuo Shi* [注: *Zuo Shi*, likely referring to the *Zuo Tradition* (左氏), a commentary style associated with Zuo Qiuming (左丘明)],
Or quench their thirst for virtue by accepting my words,
I would humbly welcome their counsel.
If they fulfill this request,
I shall bestow rich rewards.

As for flatterers who claim to be sages,
I hope they are not of this kind,
Nor those who boast like merchants of the marketplace—
Such petty ways are beneath them.

Since they dare not conceal the truth,
Let them speak frankly,
Lest later generations scorn them,
And weigh down my own lack of virtue.

*—Composed by the Daoist Immortal Zuo Xian Gong [注: *Zuo Xian Gong* Ge Xuan (葛玄), a Daoist sage of the Han-Jin period]*

The Daoist Sage, naturally born of the Way,
Emerges before the **Great Void** (太无),
Arises from no cause,
Traverses heaven and earth,
Ends without beginning or end,
Exhausts without bound or extremity,
And thus is **Limitless** (无极).
He aligns with the **Great Dao/Way** (大道) in harmony,
Stands as the root of heaven and earth,
Disperses **qi** (气) in all ten directions,
Embraces the purest **virtue/power** (德) of **Dao/Way** and **virtue/power** (道德),
Vast and boundless—
Beyond all naming.

His brilliance shines with **scripture** (文章),
His majesty is grand in its fulfillment,
His depth cannot be measured,
His splendor is the ancestor of all **divine mysteries** (神明).
The Three Lights (三光) hold him to illuminate,
Heaven and earth draw life from him,
The **Heavenly Stem and Earthly Branches** (乾坤) move to emit their essence.

Lofty yet without subjects,
Noble yet without rank,
Covering all without end—
He instructs the eight **celestial directions** (八方诸天),
Ever propagating the **Great Dao/Way** (大道),
Before the opening of creation and descending as **National Teacher** (国师),
Unbroken through generations,
No one knows his origin.

He crafts all things,
Yet never claims credit for his **mysterious virtue/power** (玄之德),
Thus all sages revere him in common.

**Dao/Way** is honored, **virtue/power** (德) is noble,
Yet none commands it—
It endures naturally.
Only the Sage Laozi!

In the Zhou era, he was entrusted to the divine **Li Mother** (李母),
Born from her left flank,
Emerging with radiant clarity,
And named **Laozi** (老子).
His title **Laozi** comes from **Limitless** (玄),
Before heaven and earth,
Free from decay or aging,
Thus called **Laozi**.

The world supposes Laozi began in the Zhou,
But his title **Laozi** began in countless **kalpas** (劫),
Most distant and profound beyond measure.

When the **Great Dao/Way** declined and faded,
He wandered westward through the world,
And **Guān Order** (关令) Yin Xi (尹喜) said:
“The **Great Dao/Way** is about to vanish!
I beg you to record it for me.”

Thus he composed *The Dao/Way and Virtue/Power* (道德) in two sections of five thousand words,
Spanning the **Heavenly Stem and Earthly Branches** (上下经).
The five thousand words proclaim the source of **Dao/Way** and **virtue/power** (道德),
All-encompassing in the grand,
All-inclusive in the minute—
The natural **scripture** (经) of heaven and humanity.

My former master said:
To study it with earnest devotion,
Then its resonance harmonizes with the **Great Ultimate** (太极),
Ascending far and singing beyond,
All **celestial beings** (诸天) rejoice,
And one attains the **mysterious sage** (契玄人).
To ponder it in stillness,
Then all marvels gather in response.
To observe inwardly one’s form and shadow,
Then **spirit-qi** (神气) endures eternally.
To embody **Dao/Way** and **virtue/power** (道德),
Then all **spirits** (万神) submit in awe,
Disaster vanishes from the **Nine Shades** (九阴),
Blessings arise in all ten directions,
Nations and families find peace—
Who can comprehend this?

The **scripture** of **non-action** (无为),
When **simplified** (垮) does not disgrace,
When **elaborated** (饰) does not bring honor,
When **extended** (桡) does not become murky,
When **clarified** (澄) does not become clear—
All is **naturally** (自然).
To manifest in accordance with the **Dao/Way** (道),
To transmit without end—
This is the **constant** (常),
Thus knowing the **constant** is called **enlightenment** (明).
What need does the **Great Dao/Way** have?

To propagate depends on humanity,
This **scripture** (斯文) is noble and wondrous—
How could one not refine it to its utmost?

A rough exposition remains,
Yet only the **Daoist** (有道者) treasures it.

**River Above Master** (河上公)—
No one knows his name.

In the time of Emperor **Xiao Wen** (孝文) of the Han,
He constructed a thatched hut by the river’s edge,
Often reading Laozi’s *Dao/Way and Virtue/Power Classic* (道德经).
The emperor loved Laozi’s words,
And decreed that all **princes, ministers, and officials** (诸王公大臣州牧二千石),
As well as all **courtiers** (朝直众官),
Should recite it.

There were several passages he did not understand,
And none in the world could fully explain them—
Until he heard that **River Above Master** (河上公) recited Laozi’s text.
The emperor sent **Chao Shi** (韶使) with the unclear passages to question him.

**River Above Master** said:
“The **Dao/Way** is honored, **virtue/power** (德) is noble—
It is not something one can ask about from afar.”

The emperor immediately descended from his carriage
And bowed in reverence before him.
The emperor said:
“Under the heavens, there is none who is not my subject.
At the bounds of the earth, there is none who is not my minister.
Within my realm, there are four **Great Ones** (四大),
And I am one of them.
Though you possess the **Dao/Way** (道),
You are still my subject.
If you cannot humble yourself,
Why do you claim such loftiness?
I alone can confer wealth and nobility or poverty and lowliness.”

After a moment,
**River Above Master** lowered his hands,
Sat and leapt into the void,
Ascending like rising clouds,
A hundred *zhang* (丈) above the earth,
In **Limitless** (玄虚) silence for a long time,
Then descended and answered the emperor:
“My being does not reach heaven,
Nor does it entangle with humanity,
Nor does it dwell upon the earth—
What subject could I be?
Your Majesty cannot command me to be wealthy or noble, or poor or lowly.”

The emperor then realized he was a **divine being** (神人),
And immediately alighted from his carriage,
Knelt in homage, and bowed in thanks.
He said:
“Though I am unworthy,
I have inherited the imperial legacy,
My ability is insufficient for greatness,
And I fear I cannot bear the weight.
Though I govern worldly affairs,
My heart reveres **Dao/Way** and **virtue/power** (道德),
Yet my understanding is dim,
And many things remain unclear.
If you, the **Daoist Sovereign** (道君), would graciously enlighten me,
Then even in the darkest night I shall behold the radiance of the **Great Ultimate** (太阳之耀光).”

**River Above Master** then bestowed the plain **scripture** (素书)
Of Laozi’s *Dao/Way and Virtue/Power Classic* (道德经章句),
Two volumes in total,
And said to the emperor:
“Study this thoroughly,
And your doubts shall dissolve.
Since my annotations of this **scripture** (经),
It has been seventeen hundred years,
And passed through three generations—
Now four with you.
Do not reveal it to those unworthy.”

The emperor knelt to receive the **scripture** (经),
And when he finished speaking,
**River Above Master** had vanished.

Scholars say the emperor loved the **Great Dao/Way** (大道),
Yet the people could not fully grasp its meaning,
And through profound reflection and distant resonance,
They revered the **Supreme Daoist Sovereign** (太上道君),
Sending a **divine being** (神人) to descend and teach them—
Then departed immediately.
Perhaps the emperor’s faith was not yet pure,
So **River Above Master** displayed divine transformations
To awaken his resolve,
Desiring to establish the truth of the **Dao/Way**.
Thus, the people named him **River Above Master**.

Laozi, in the year **Shang Huang** (上皇) of the first cycle,
On the twelfth day of the first month,
When the **Great Void** (太岁) was **Ding Mao** (丁卯),
Descended as **Zhou Teacher** (周师).
In the year **Wu Ji** (无极),
When the **Great Void** (太岁) was **Gui Chou** (癸丑),
On the fifth month, **Ren Wu** (壬午),
He departed from Zhou and crossed the **Pass** (关).

**Guān Order** (关令) Yin Xi (尹喜),
By **predestined harmony** (宿命合道),
Had foreseen the **purple clouds** (紫云) moving westward,
And knew a **Daoist** (道人) would pass through.
He kept vigil, purified himself, and burned incense,
Aspiring to behold the **Daoist Truth** (道真),
And on the twenty-fifth day of the twelfth month,
Laozi crossed the **Pass**.

Yin Xi saw Laozi.
The **Daoist** (子) welcomed him and arranged the rites,
Calling him **disciple** (弟子).
Laozi said:
“You were destined for this.
You shall benefit the world,
Abandoning the **accomplished** (贤) to transmit the **Great Dao/Way** (大道),
You are a **divine immortal** (神仙者矣).”

On the twenty-eighth day,
He imparted the *Dao/Way and Virtue/Power Classic* (太上道德经),
Its meaning piercing **Limitless** (虚无),
All-encompassing in the grand,
All-inclusive in the minute—
Even **sage-kings** (圣王) cannot fully grasp its meaning.

In ancient times, the Han Emperor Wen, fond of the **Dao/Way** of Laozi, stood in the **Non-Action Hall** (无为之堂), sighing that neither sages nor common folk could fathom its profound mysteries. Through profound contemplation and distant resonance, he ascended to the **Supreme Dao/Way Lord** (太上道君), who sent a **divine emissary** (神人) to impart subtle intentions to the Emperor. The **Daoist** (道人) then took a solemn oath and transmitted them. The **Perfect Ones** (至人) verified and cross-examined the text, for the **external Confucian scholars** (外儒) had introduced many errors in their transmissions. Now, we must compare and correct it, aligning it with the **Mystic Cavern** (玄洞).

The ten-directional **celestial beings, humans, immortals, and gods**, as well as the **heavenly and earthly spirits**, all revere and follow it—its essence is identical without variation.

I have already cross-verified this with the **divine kings of the heavens and immortals**, receiving transmission from the **celestial and human** realms. The **noble scholars and virtuous Confucians** must now uphold the **ultimate truth**, expounding the **Great Dao/Way and Virtue/Power**—how could they not be meticulous? Those who attain the **sage’s original text**—how could they not?

I speak thus to rectify the **mystic subtleties** before heaven, earth, and humankind.

Now, the teaching is complete and clear. Those who study the **Dao of Immortality** must surely expound its profound and esoteric truths.

The **Daoist Zheng Siyuan** (郑思远) said: My master, **Ge Xiansheng** (葛仙公), received the *Dao/Way and Virtue/Power Classic* (《道德经》) in two volumes—**Supreme Ultimate Truth** (太极真人) and **Xu Laile** (徐来勒). My master once cherished this text as if he had seen the **True Person** himself, teaching oral precepts, saying: *"This is the ancestral root of the Dao/Way."* To recite and savor it ten thousand times, to quiet the mind and focus on the **Mystic**—all shall surely ascend to immortality.

Hold this book in utmost reverence, worshipping it morning and evening, with devotions as precise as the **Lingbao Laws** (灵宝法). **Aspiring Daoists** should promote it thus.

My master always kept these words secret, transmitting them only to those with **immortal potential**.

**Composed by Wang Fu** (王雩):

In the age when Laozi transformed **Dao/Way and Virtue/Power** teachings, he wrote ninety-nine chapters to illuminate the principle of **generative vitality** (生生之理). Yet in later ages, scholars were obscured by the remnants of past traditions and confused by the divergent theories of the **Hundred Schools**. Their wisdom was insufficient to discern truth from falsehood, so they compared the **sage’s scriptures** with the writings of **Yang Zhu and Mozi**. Though many read them, few penetrated their depths.

Some even spoke loftily, drifting further from principle. Today’s transmitted commentaries—those of **Wang Bi** and **Zhang Yue**—contain many textual errors, each with merits and flaws, harming the true meaning in countless ways. Now, I compare and correct them, establishing what is most accurate, and explain each phrase based on what I have heard.

The **sage’s words** are already difficult to exhaust, yet even what we grasp in knowledge falls short in expression. If readers infer the intent from their own understanding, then my elucidations have already surpassed half the meaning.

This text was completed on the twelfth day of the seventh month, **Xining the Third Year** (熙宁三年).

I have often argued: Though sages may be many, their **Dao/Way** is one. Their **manifestations** follow one another, transcending the cosmos yet existing simultaneously. Their **residences** may differ as heaven and earth, yet they dwell together. Thus, their words are like the head and tail of a single entity—anything outside this **Dao/Way** is heresy.

Yet though the **Dao/Way is one**, its expressions differ; though expressions may align, the **Dao/Way** itself need not. Worldly scholars, recognizing only the words, deem identical expressions as correct; unaware of the **Dao/Way**, they reject its unity as false.

The *Classic of Changes* says: *"The interplay of yin and yang is called the Dao/Way."*
Laozi says: *"Having known the mother, one may know the child."*

If this is truly understood, then examining the **Five Phases** in their distinctions, observing the **Four Seasons** in their transformations, studying form and measuring intent—thus comparing all things—then even if the sage does not speak, I shall know it.

Thus, the **Dao/Way** is the **year**; the **sage** is the **season**. From **Emperor Yao and Shun** to **Confucius**, rituals and music were refined, embodied in **forms, names, measurements, and numbers**, while the **spirit’s operations** shone clearly in their creations. They established **hierarchies**, distinguished **virtue and mediocrity**, and governed the world—detailed in affairs, vast in scope—truly flourishing.

This was the **Summer** in its time. But Summer reverses into **Autumn**, which gathers the scattered and unifies it, sheds its blooms to bear fruit, distinguishing **things by virtue** and restoring **nature as constant**. Its intent is still, its deeds simple—yet Autumn does not seek to reverse Summer. Rather, it completes the **year** and generates **life**.

At this time, more than half of all **living things** perish—yet is this the **ultimate decree of heaven**, beyond the grasp of lesser wisdom? Autumn is not the end of the year, for there is yet a greater stage: thus, the **Four Seasons’ transformations**, when applied to us, are **youth, maturity, old age, and death**.

The classics say: *"To follow this throughout one’s life yet not know its Dao/Way—is this not the case?"*

Alas! To study the **Dao/Way** without expecting the discourse of **death**—then what is the point of study? *"Morning hearing of the Dao/Way, evening death—this is acceptable."* Thus, the **Dao/Way** is precious because it governs **life and death**. If one truly knows the **sincere virtue/power** and approaches its destination, then the discourse of **death and life** is exhausted. Therefore, I have devoted my heart to it.

*Collected Commentaries on the Authentic Dao/Way and Virtue/Power Classic*
**Tang Emperor Minghuang’s Riverbank Master Wang Bi’s Commentary**
**Wang Fu’s Annotation**

**Dao/Way Classic**

**Xiu says:** The Dao/Way is that through which all things are guided. In essence, it is essence itself; in function, it is function itself. Boundless and nameless, yet nowhere absent—this is its nature.

Thus, even the sages’ teachings dwell within one aspect of it. Though confined to one aspect, they differ from the myriad schools only in that they do not stray from principle and align with the times.

**Chapter One: The Dao/Way That Can Be Spoken Of**

*"The Dao/Way that can be spoken of"*—
**He says:** This refers to the Dao/Way of governance and instruction in the states of Jin and Wei.

*"Is not the constant Dao/Way."*—
**He says:** It is not the Dao/Way of innate, eternal longevity.

The constant Dao/Way nourishes the spirit through non-action, governs the people through non-interference, conceals its brilliance and hides its radiance, erases its traces and obscures its origins—it cannot be named as Dao/Way.

**Xiu says:** The Dao/Way that can be spoken of adapts to the times and acts accordingly. The times shift without pause, and the Dao/Way responds in transformation. For creation and transformation never cease their work—what was once true is now false. Yet petty scholars cling to past brilliance as the root of the Dao/Way, pointing to the *Lianlu* as the abode of the Sage. Laozi now loosens their rigid interpretations and opens the path to ultimate truth. Hence, this opening chapter.

To grasp this is to recognize that even today’s teachings are not the constant Dao/Way.

*"What can be named"*—

The *He* says: This refers to the names of wealth, noble status, honor, and surpassing the world.
**Non-eternal names.**

The *Ming Huang* says: The Dao/Way is the wondrous function of the utmost emptiness; names are the designations given to things when they receive their essence.
Since its function can be applied to things, it is called *the Dao/Way that can be spoken of*.
Since names arise from function, it is called *the name that can be named*.
If its application is boundless and without fixed form, then it transcends any single Dao/Way.
Since things differ and their names vary, it transcends any single name.
Thus, it is said that names are forcibly imposed upon the Dao/Way, yet the Dao/Way itself remains nameless.

*The He* says: This is not a name that naturally and eternally abides.
**Eternal names** should be like an infant before speech, a falcon before its feathers part, a pearl still within the shell, or fine jade buried in stone—internally luminous yet externally appearing dull and crude.

*The Bi* says: The Dao/Way that can be spoken of and the names that can be named are but designations imposed upon things; these are not their eternal essence. What is truly beyond naming and speaking is their eternal nature.

*The Yu* says: Names arise from substance, and substance has form and measure. Once form and measure are complete, decay and ruin follow—how could this be eternal?
Only by grasping this non-eternality is one truly eternal.
**The nameless** is the beginning of Heaven and Earth.

*The He* says: The nameless refers to the Dao/Way.
The Dao/Way has no form, hence it cannot be named.
**The beginning** refers to the Dao/Way’s origin.
It exhales *qi* and spreads transformation, emerging from the void and nothingness, becoming the origin of Heaven and Earth.
**The named** is the mother of all things.

*The Ming Huang* says: The nameless is the wondrous origin.
When the wondrous origin manifests as *qi*, it sustains Heaven and Earth, and Heaven and Earth rely on it for their beginning—thus it is nameless.
The named is the responsive application.
Once application is complete, it nourishes all things abundantly; things receive their nourishment, hence they are named.

*The He* says: The named refers to Heaven and Earth, which have form and position, and yin and yang have softness and hardness—this is their being named.
The mother of all things is Heaven and Earth, containing *qi* and giving birth to all things, nurturing them to maturity—like a mother tending her child.

*The Bi* says: All that exists originates from the nonexistent.
Thus, in the time before form and name, it is the beginning of all things.
When form and name appear, it nurtures and sustains them, protecting and guiding them—this is its role as mother.
Speaking of the Dao/Way as formless and nameless, yet it gives rise to all things—
Its beginning and completion are unknown; profound beyond measure.

*The Yu* says: Receiving its mandate from the nonexistent and forming in the existent, it is called the beginning of Heaven and Earth, the mother of all things.
The *Yijing* says: *"Only after Heaven and Earth came into being did all things emerge"*—this aligns with the *Yijing*’s sequence.
The vast expanse of Heaven and Earth encompasses all living things, hence Heaven and Earth are spoken of first.
Things and Heaven and Earth originally have no precedence; tracing them to their utmost, existence and nonexistence are one body.
The statement of "beginning and mother" is but a metaphor.
Moreover, though Heaven and Earth are vast, their receiving of the mandate and formation do not depart from existence and nonexistence.
Yet it is said that only the mother of all things is emphasized—thus Laozi’s words merely exhaust the nature of things.
**Eternally without desire**, one may observe its wondrous essence.

*The He* says: *Wondrous* means *essential*.
If one constantly refrains from desire, they may perceive the essential core of the Dao/Way.
*Essential* means *one*.

*The Bi* says: *Wondrous* is the utmost subtlety.
All things begin in subtlety and then manifest; they begin in nonexistence and then arise.
Thus, if one constantly refrains from desire, emptying their mind, they may observe the wondrous origin of things.
**Eternally with desire**, one may observe its *jiǎo* (boundaries).

*The Ming Huang* says: Human beings are born in stillness—this is Heaven’s nature.
When they encounter things, they move—this is the nature of desire.
If one constantly upholds purity and stillness, releases the mind, and reflects upon their true nature, they may observe the wondrous origin.
If one does not rectify their nature, their emotions follow desire and move, their nature lost to desire, straying from the Dao’s source.
To observe the wondrous origin while in such a state is to perceive only its *jiǎo* (boundaries).

*The He* says: *Jiǎo* means *return*.
Those who constantly indulge desire may observe the inclinations of the worldly.

*The Bi* says: *Jiǎo* means *return to termination*.
All that exists relies on the nonexistent for its use.
The origin of desire, when aligned with the Dao/Way, alone brings fulfillment.
Thus, those who constantly indulge desire may observe the termination of things.

*The Yun* says: The *Yijing*’s yin and yang, Laozi’s existence and nonexistence, even down to the Buddhist doctrine of *shí-kōng* (form and emptiness), are fundamentally one in essence, differing only in their exposition.
Those who speak of the nonexistent abandon the existent to seek the nonexistent—this is but another existent beyond the existent.
How could this be true nonexistence?
At the moment of its manifestation, it has never truly been existent—this is true nonexistence.
The body of existence and nonexistence is eternally one and existent.
Those who observe existence see only its *jiǎo* (boundaries).
To observe its wondrous essence, one must know its origin is nonexistent.
Yet the nonexistent origin is never truly existent.
Though it is said to be eternally nonexistent and eternally existent, it clarifies that existence and nonexistence do not succeed one another—
The nonexistent is true existence, the existent is true nonexistence.
Speaking of *jiǎo* reveals the profundity of the wondrous;
Speaking of the wondrous reveals the coarseness of *jiǎo*.
This is the principle of comparative exposition.
These two, though differing in name, share the same source.

*The Ming Huang* says: As above, both originate from the Dao/Way, hence they are the same.
Their application gives rise to movement, and names arise from their use—thus their names differ.

*The He* says: The two refer to having desire and not having desire.
They share the same origin—both arise from the human heart.
Yet their names differ—what they designate differs.
The name of *not having desire* leads to longevity;
The name of *having desire* leads to self-destruction.
Though differing in name, they are both called *Xuan* (profound).

*The Ming Huang* says: When they manifest, their names differ; yet in their origin, they are profound.
*Xuan* means profound and wondrous.

*The He* says: *Xuan* is Heaven.
It is said that those with desire and those without desire both receive *qi* from Heaven.

*The Yun* says: Existence and nonexistence originally are one, with no dual names.
From the perspective of the scholar, existence is not as refined as nonexistence.
Yet once the Dao/Way is attained, both are supreme truths—initially without distinction between them.

「The profound yet again profound,
*The He* says: Within Heaven, there is yet another Heaven.

The thickness of received *qi* varies,
To obtain the harmony of *zhōnghé* (central equilibrium) and the nourishment of *zīyè* (vital fluids),
One becomes a sage or saint;
To receive discordant, turbid, and defiled *qi*,
One becomes one who indulges in gluttony and licentiousness.
The gateway to myriad wonders.

*The Ming Huang* says: If the mind does not give rise to desire,
Then it aligns with the profound and wondrous.
Yet one may still fear clinging to the profound as stagnation,
Falling short of complete forgetfulness.
Thus, the phrase "profound yet again profound" dispels the profound,
Illustrating the clarity of *not having desire* within *not having desire*.
Those who can thus transcend
Shall be free from all vexation and confusion,
Hence the saying: the gateway to myriad wonders.

*The He* says: To comprehend Heaven within Heaven,
And the varying thickness of received *qi*,
To abandon emotional desire,
And uphold the harmony of *zhōnghé* (central equilibrium),
This is called the essential threshold of knowing the Dao/Way.

*The Bi* says: The two (profound and profound yet again) are initially called *mother* (origin).
When they manifest, they both arise from the profound.
Yet their differing names stem from what cannot be uniformly applied.
At the beginning, it is called *mother*;
At the end, it is called *profound*.
The profound is *míng* (obscure, hidden),
Silent and without form.
That from which *mother* and *profound* originate
Cannot be named, hence they cannot be spoken of as identical.
When we say "profound" and yet name it "profound yet again,"
We take from the unnameable and call it thus.
To name it thus is not to fix it as a single profound,
For if we fix it as a single profound alone,
Then its name would be far from the truth—
Hence the phrase: *profound yet again profound*.
All myriad wonders emerge through this gateway,
Thus it is called the gateway to myriad wonders.

*The Xiāo* says: The Dao/Way encompasses two things.
From form downward, it is that *yang* (bright, active) is exalted while *yīn* (dark, passive) is lowly;
From form upward, it is that *yīn* (dark, passive) precedes while *yang* (bright, active) follows.
Thus, the highest reaches of the Dao/Way are called *miào* (wondrous) and *xuán* (profound).
*Miào* is *virtue/power*;
*Xuán* is *color* (essence),
For when we speak of *color*, it has reached its peak,
Yet there remains what *color* cannot encompass—
Hence the phrase: *profound yet again profound*.
All things possess their wondrous essence,
All arising from this,
Thus it is called the gateway to myriad wonders.

**Chapter Two: "All Under Heaven Know Beauty"**

All under Heaven knows beauty as beauty,
*The He* says: To exalt one’s own beauty is to reveal it.
This is already evil.
*The He* says: This is like coarse jade.

All know goodness as goodness,
*The He* says: This is like merit and fame.
This is already not-goodness.

*The Ming Huang* says: Beauty and goodness arise from the heart of desire.
If the heart’s desires are unchecked,
Even what is evil becomes beautiful and good.
Thus it is said: All under Heaven knows what is beautiful to itself as beauty,
And what is good to itself as goodness.
Beauty and goodness have no fixed master—
They are merely deluded emotions,
All arising from clinging to distinctions of having and not-having,
Judging difficulty and ease,
And categorizing the divine and miraculous alongside the foul and rotten,
To mutually undermine and usurp one another.
The Great Sage (Buddha) weighs these matters deeply,
Knowing their emptiness and delusion—
Hence the saying: This is already evil.

*The He* says: This is what people contend over.
*The Yun* says: The Dao/Way originally has no things, yet things have deluded emotions,
Mutually distinguishing themselves.
This is to be swept away by the currents of transformation,
Unable to escape on one’s own.
Yet those swept away and unable to escape
Do not do so through aversion and detachment,
But through attachment and clinging—
Thus they abhor beauty and goodness.
Hence, evil and not-goodness follow beauty and goodness,
And when beauty and goodness are present,
Evil and not-goodness are already there as well.
To wait for their appearance and then awaken is already late in enlightenment.
Even so, the Perfect One’s so-called beauty and goodness
Never truly depart from evil and not-goodness,
While evil and not-goodness
Never truly depart from beauty and goodness.
The foolish of the world are not worthy of this discourse,
Thus beauty and goodness are always accompanied by evil and not-goodness.

Hence, having and not-having generate one another,
*The He* says: To see having as not-having.

Difficulty and ease complete one another,
*The He* says: To see difficulty as ease.

Long and short define one another,
*The He* says: To see shortness as length.

High and low incline toward one another,
*The He* says: To see height as low.

Sounds blend harmoniously,
*The He* says: When the upper sings, the lower must respond in harmony.

Front and back follow one another,
*The Ming Huang* says: These six pairs oppose each other,
Alternately becoming their respective names—
Just as with beauty and evil,
They do not arise from inherent nature,
But from deluded emotions,
And thus these manifold distinctions emerge.

*The He* says: When the upper moves, the lower must follow.
*The Bi* says: Beauty is what the human heart delights in and pursues.
Evil is what the human heart abhors and recoils from.
Beauty and evil are like joy and anger—
Joy and anger share the same root;
Goodness and not-goodness share the same gateway—
Thus one cannot take them in isolation.
These six distinctions are all manifest truths of nature,
Unalterable and not to be taken in isolation.

*The Xiù* says: To separate from the Dao/Way and cling to the self,
The self then has its opposite.
Once self and opposite are divided,
They are classified into two.
These six pairs are why things are not uniform,
And why the transformations of joy, anger, sorrow, and delight,
And life and death,
Endlessly arise and recede without cessation.
All this stems from not comprehending the original *yīn-yáng* (dark and bright),
But following their superficial currents,
Giving rise to distinctions,
Clinging to one while abandoning the rest,
And taking delusion as reality.

This chapter is similar to *Zhuāngzi’s* "Equivalence of Things,"
Each passage unfolding logically.
Thus the sage dwells in affairs of *wuwéi* (non-action),
*The He* says: Governed by the Dao/Way.

*The Bi* says: Nature is already sufficient—
Action brings ruin.

The sage does not adhere to the teachings of *Huáng* (Yellow Emperor),
*The Ming Huang* says: Affairs of *wuwéi* (non-action) are without action.
Using the term "affairs" metaphorically, hence "dwells."
Teachings of *non-speech*, forgotten in speech—
Using the term "teachings" metaphorically, hence "does not adhere."

*The He* says: Leading with the body to guide others.
*The Yun* says: The sage has no heart of his own,
Taking the hearts of the people as his heart.
Though he engages in affairs, he never intrudes upon the paths of action,
Though he teaches, he never reveals the intent behind the words—
Thus affairs are harmonized,
And teachings are carried out,
While I remain tranquil and unburdened,
Never beginning to speak for the sake of speech,
And yet all under Heaven thereby returns to its natural state,
Restoring simplicity and purity.

It is only because there is no burden,
That though he takes form between *yīn* (dark) and *yáng* (bright),
Creation and transformation cannot compel him to these six opposing pairs,
Nor can evil disturb him.»

The myriad things act without refusal,
*The Ming Huang* says: To let the myriad things each attain their own movement and action without refusal or thanks to the Sage.

*The He* says: Each moves on its own, refusing nothing yet halting none.
*The Xiu* says: The myriad things act together, the Sage letting each fulfill its nature without refusal, for my heart is empty, taking and discarding nothing. If one harbors thoughts of taking or discarding, then the myriad forms—beautiful and ugly—how could they not be refused?

Born yet not possessed,
*The He* says: The Original *qi* gives birth to the myriad things yet does not possess them.

Acting yet not relying,
*The He* says: The Dao’s actions are not done with expectation of return.
*The Bi* says: Wisdom is self-sufficient; action is artifice.

When the work is done, not dwelling upon it,
*The Ming Huang* says: To let the myriad things each fulfill their growth, not claiming them as one’s own, each attaining what they do without reliance—thus the great peace’s work is accomplished. Yet one must still daily be cautious, never daring to rest content.
*The He* says: When the work is done and the matter settled, one retreats and does not dwell in the position.
*The Bi* says: The function arises from things; thus the work is accomplished by them, so one does not dwell upon it.
*The Xiu* says: If there is dwelling, then it must be relinquished.

Only because one does not dwell,
*The He* says: Only because the work is done does one not dwell in the position.

Thus one does not depart.
*The Ming Huang* says: Only because one does not dare to rest content, and ever increases one’s virtue, does one forget the work while the work remains—thus one does not dwell yet does not depart.
*The He* says: Virtue and blessing abide, not leaving the body.

The six preceding lines have high and low, long and short—like opening a single source, from which a hundred branches arise. The transformations of these hundred branches never fail to move and disrupt.
*The Bi* says: If the work is attributed to oneself, then the work cannot endure.
*The Yun* says: Form and name descend, all undergoing substitution. Only the Dao has no substance; nothing can displace it. The Sage embodies the Dao, thus filling and permeating without bounds, yet never possessing anything. Responding to myriad changes, yet never having a mind—thus how could one fixate on transient matters as achievements and firmly possess them?

Only then does one transcend the six opposing pairs, sever the names of beauty and ugliness, surpassing birth and death to dwell in the eternal law. Holding this heart to traverse the world, even if achievements and fame are lofty, how could there be the burden of fullness?

**Chapter Three: Not Exalting the Virtuous**

Not exalting the virtuous,
*The He* says: The virtuous refers to the worldly virtuous—those who twist words and clarify texts, departing from the Dao and employing expedients, abandoning simplicity for artifice. Not exalting means not honoring them with rewards, not elevating them with office, so that the people do not contend.

*The Ming Huang* says: To exalt the virtuous is to have traces; to follow traces is to stir contention. If the virtuous and unvirtuous each fulfill their proper roles, then there will be no contention.
*The He* says: Not contending over achievements and fame is returning to nature.
*The Xiu* says: The virtuous are those who excel among the people. To exalt them causes the people to boast and emulate externally, stirring the seeds of contention.

Not valuing hard-to-obtain goods,
*The He* says: The ruler does not indulge in precious treasures—gold abandoned in the mountains, pearls cast into the depths—so that the people do not become thieves.

*The Ming Huang* says: Hard-to-obtain goods are those beyond one’s natural endowment. To seek what cannot be obtained is why they are called hard to obtain. If one does not rest in one’s proper role and aspires to what one lacks, having already lost one’s natural endowment, is this not theft?

To let things fulfill their nature and affairs accord with their capacities, then hard-to-obtain goods will not be valued, and the essence of life will not be stolen.
*The He* says: The upper reaches are pure and tranquil; the lower does not devour the living.
*The Xiu* says: When the people’s clothing and food are sufficient, their nature settles. If one vainly values hard-to-obtain goods, then their seeking will be endless, and secrecy will lead to theft.

Indeed, the people’s lives all arise from deluded distinctions. This chapter strives to equalize things, letting the people return to their nature.

Not seeing what is desirable,
*The He* says: Banish the *Zheng* music, distance flatterers, so that the heart does not become disordered.

*The Ming Huang* says: Having neither the path of exalting the virtuous nor the pursuit of hard-to-obtain goods, there are no visible desires, and thus the heart is not confused.
*The He* says: No licentiousness.
*The Bi* says: The virtuous are those with ability; to exalt is to praise them by name. To honor is to elevate them in rank. If only ability is entrusted, why praise? If only this is applied, why honor? To exalt the virtuous displays fame, surpassing their capacity, causing the lower ranks to scramble and compete, their abilities vying with one another. To honor goods beyond use causes those who consume to rush toward them, digging and borrowing to fill their baskets, risking their lives in theft. Thus it is sung: "Not seeing [desires], then the heart is not disordered."
*The Xiu* says: The ignorant delude themselves into seeing what is desirable, thus their hearts are disordered by it. Only the Sage knows all things are not truly real, thus all forms are viewed equally, and there is no heart of preference or rejection. If so, then the heart remains ever impoverished and tranquil—how could things disorder it? Thus one is able not to exalt the virtuous, not to value goods.

Therefore, the Sage’s governance,
*The He* says: Explaining that the Sage’s governance of the state and of the self are the same.

Is to empty the heart,
*The Ming Huang* says: If the heart is not disordered by what is desirable, then it is empty.
*The He* says: Remove desires, dispel confusion.
*The Xiu* says: An empty heart knows no distinctions—this is the meaning of not exalting the virtuous.

And fill the belly,
*The Ming Huang* says: Inner virtue is complete, thus there is no arrogance or resentment, like stopping when full, not generating demands.
*The He* says: Embrace the Dao, hold to oneness, guard the Five Spirits.
*The Bi* says: The heart harbors wisdom, the belly harbors food—empty in wisdom, yet full in ignorance.
*The Xiu* says: A full belly knows no greed—this further explains not valuing goods.

And weaken the will,
*The Ming Huang* says: An empty heart weakens the will.

*The He* says: To know softness, humility, and yielding—this is to avoid seizing power.

*The Xiu* says: A strong will leads to arrogance and victory, while a weak will avoids external scheming—this further explains the meaning of not exalting the virtuous.

**Strengthen the bones,**
*The Ming Huang* says: A full belly strengthens the bones.

*The He* says: Cherish essence, practice moderation in giving—when marrow is full, bones are firm.

*The Bi* says: Bones have no knowledge yet are burdened by deeds; the will generates chaos.

*The Yun* says: Strong bones are for self-reliance; self-reliance means external things cannot sway one. This further explains the meaning of not valuing goods.

**Always keep the people without knowledge or desire,**
*The Ming Huang* says: Always keep people from striving for excellence and from demanding what they desire.

*The He* says: Return to simplicity, guard purity.

*The Bi* says: Preserve their true nature.

*The Yu* says: Knowledge leads to false perceptions; desire leads to external seeking. Once these two are removed, nature and disposition are settled.

From not exalting the virtuous comes transformation—one can lead them to a state of no knowledge.
From not valuing goods comes transformation—one can lead them to a state of no desire.

**Let those who know dare not act,**
*The Ming Huang* says: Through stillness and tranquility, people are purified of knowledge and desire. Those who possess [knowledge] are forbidden from acting.

*The He* says: Deep contemplation—do not speak lightly.

*The Bi* says: "Those who know" refers to those who act on knowledge.

*The Yun* says: Those whose intelligence disturbs the masses must be restrained.

**Act without action,**
*The He* says: Do not contrive; move by following natural course. Then nothing remains untamed.

*The Ming Huang* says: In acting without action, people return to their true nature, and pure transformation is sincere.

*The He* says: Virtuous cultivation is profound; the people are at peace.

*The Yun* says: "Act without action" is not true inaction. Action lies in non-action alone, aiming to restore true nature.

I have often reflected: After the Three Dynasties, no one has failed to lose their true nature. Thus, the noble strengthen their will and love goodness, seeking the virtuous without end, while the common weaken their bones and covet gain, pursuing wealth without satisfaction.

A strong will leads to excess knowledge; weak bones lead to excess desire. Some seek knowledge, others seek desire—though their pursuits differ, both harm their essence by clinging to externals.

Only the Perfect One differs: they weaken their will, not indulging in what they see, humbling themselves to seek the near, regarding it as having no desires, while the Dao suffices within.

They strengthen their bones, not to establish themselves as virtuous, but to overcome desires for profit, and to honor innate virtue.

Only then are name and gain unable to sway them, and their nature remains steadfast.

---
**Chapter Four: Dao Chong (The Unobstructed Dao)**

The Dao is unobstructed yet applied,

*The He* says: *Chong* means central—Dao hides its name, conceals its merit; its application lies in the center. Sometimes it does not overflow.

*The Ming Huang* says: The Dao moves with harmonious *qi*, and its application generates creation. Though creation is its function, it never overflows. "Sometimes" is used because one does not dare speak directly of the Dao.

*The He* says: *Chong* is constant—Dao is always humble, never overflowing.

*The Xiu* says: The Dao fills all yet has no end, suffices for all things, yet never possesses. Thus, it is said to sometimes not overflow. Whether empty or full, it is called *chong*. *Chong* is the balance of yin and yang, its essence being emptiness.

The Dao’s application to things is central.
The Dao’s response to affairs is emptiness.
This passage speaks of its application, hence *chong*.

Deep and vast, it seems the ancestor of all things.

*The Ming Huang* says: *Yuan* means profound and still. The Dao constantly generates things yet never overflows. Its mystery lies in its depth and stillness, thus it seems to be the ancestor of all things.

*The He* says: The Dao is deep and unfathomable, appearing as the ancestral origin of all things.

*The Yun* says: The Dao generates all things yet never separates from them. Observed from the dispersion of things, it seems to be their ancestor alone. *Yuan* means deep and immeasurable.

People are inherently sufficient in this Dao. Those who seek to embody it must not seek elsewhere—only by blunting sharpness, resolving chaos, harmonizing light, and blending with the mundane will they preserve themselves.

**Blunt the sharp,**
*The He* says: *Rui* means to blunt—people sharpen their essence, striving for fame and position; this must be corrected by aligning with the Dao, which does not reveal itself.

*The Xiu* says: Blunting sharpness makes one whole. *Rui* is the form of a point.

**Resolve the chaotic,**
*The Ming Huang* says: The Dao acts with *chong* and harmony, thus it suppresses sharpness and disperses confusion. If vulgar scholars seek distinctions, they only bind themselves further.

*The He* says: *Fen* means entangled resentment. One must reflect on the Dao’s non-action to resolve it.

*The Yun* says: If one does not engage with things but sits observing their return, nature and life settle, and chaos resolves.

**Harmonize its light,**
*The He* says: Though one may have singular insight, it should be obscured, not used to dazzle others.

*The Yun* says: Blunting sharpness and resolving chaos settle nature and disposition, naturally filling with radiant light. Once light appears, it must not differ from things but blend with them—*The Book of Changes* says: "Mingled and indistinct."

**Blend with the dust,**
*The Ming Huang* says: The Dao is everywhere yet nowhere. In light or dust, it is one with all. One with light and dust, yet not light or dust.

*The He* says: One should blend with the common and impure, not separate oneself.

*The Xiu* says: The Dao is the constant of nature. Obtaining nature’s constant, what is there to prize or exalt? Thus, the Perfect One has the Dao yet does not differ from the dust.

**Still and seeming to exist,**
*The Ming Huang* says: Harmonizing light and blending with dust, its mystery remains undefiled, thus it is still and seems to exist.

*The He* says: One should remain still and tranquil, thus enduring without perishing.

*The Yun* says: If a person follows the four matters above, the Dao will remain still within them.

**Existing yet defined as having form, then it is not the Dao.**
*Seeming to exist*—this is what cannot be defined as having form.

I do not know whose child I am.
*The He* says: Laozi said, *"I do not know my origin."*

*The Xiu* says: Within the very essence of what is now called the Dao, there is indeed a source. Though it has a source, it remains serene and formless—thus, *"I do not know whose child I am."*

**Resembling the Sovereign before the Sovereign.**
*The Ming Huang* says: I do not know my origin, for the Dao is not a generated law. Thus, there is no "fatherly Dao"—it seems to exist before the Sovereign.

The Sovereign is the master of all things.
*Symbol*—this is *seeming*.
*The He* says: The Dao exists before the Celestial Sovereign—this speaks of the Dao arising before Heaven and Earth.

*The Bi* says: One who clings to a single family’s measure cannot encompass the whole family. One who clings to a single kingdom’s measure cannot establish the kingdom. Exhausting strength to lift heavy burdens, yet it serves no purpose—thus, though people may know how to govern all things, if they do not govern by the two principles of Heaven and Earth, they cannot sustain them.

Though Earth has form and vitality, it does not conform to Heaven’s law, and is ashamed it cannot preserve its tranquility. Though Heaven is refined and vast, it cannot conform to the Dao, and thus cannot preserve its purity.

Empty yet used, its use cannot be exhausted. Full yet pursuing substance, substance arrives and it overflows—thus, empty yet used, it is again not full. Its limitlessness is already profound.

Though form is vast, it cannot burden its essence. Though affairs are numerous, they cannot fill its measure. All things abandon this and seek their master—where is their master?

Is it not profound, resembling the root of all things?

Sharp yet unharmed, chaotic yet without labor, harmonizing light yet unstained—its essence blends with dust yet does not stray, its mystery is thus. *Seeming to exist.*

Earth upholds its form and virtue, yet cannot exceed its capacity. Heaven restrains its symbolic virtue, yet cannot exceed its coverage. Heaven and Earth cannot reach it—is it not like the Sovereign before the Sovereign?

The Sovereign is the Celestial Sovereign.
*The Xiu* says: *Symbol*—the omen of form. The Sovereign—one of exalted things. Though one becomes a sovereign king, the Dao transcends even this.

---
**Heaven and Earth are not benevolent (Chapter Five)**

Heaven and Earth are not benevolent.
*The He* says: Heaven bestows, Earth transforms, not through benevolent favor, but through natural course.

They treat all things as straw dogs.
*The Ming Huang* says: Heaven and Earth give birth to all things, and though humans are most noble, Heaven and Earth regard them as straw and dogs—no expectation of repayment.

*The He* says: Heaven and Earth act naturally, without contrivance. All things self-regulate, thus they are not benevolent. Benevolence must contrive and act; with contrivance and action, things lose their authenticity. With favor and contrivance, things cannot fully exist. If things do not fully exist, they cannot suffice.

Heaven and Earth do not grow straw for beasts to eat, nor dogs for humans—without contrivance, all things find their proper use, and none lack.

If wisdom arises from oneself, it is still insufficient.

*The Xiu* says: Straw dogs are used in sacrifices. When employed, they are treated with solemn rites and respect, but once discarded, they are cast aside like dung. Though treated with utmost sincerity, they are merely bound straw—what is there truly to revere? Though unworthy of reverence, they are treated as such—not for their own sake.

All things follow their natural course: birth, death, formation, decay. This is the natural order, yet Heaven and Earth alone are called parents. Thus, they cannot help but love, but to examine Heaven and Earth’s intent—what burden is there?

When they love, though with utmost sincerity, all things proceed naturally—there is nothing truly to love. In the end, all return to dust—why cling to emotion?

The Sage is not benevolent.
*The He* says: The Sage nurtures the people not through benevolence, but by following Heaven and Earth’s natural course.

*The Yun* says: Benevolence is human—loving things with human affection is benevolence. Yet if it is human in appearance but Heavenly in nature, how can benevolence name it?

He treats the people as straw dogs.
*The Ming Huang* says: Not benevolent means no benevolent favor. A straw dog is straw bound into a dog’s shape. Dogs guard with barking—this is a kind of favor. But a straw dog has only the form of a dog, no barking function—thus, it has no place in benevolent love. This says that Heaven and Earth regard humans as straw dogs—no expectation of gratitude.

Let me examine this further: The most benevolent has no favorites—who then practices universal love? Love that is not universal is partial. Not favoring only one’s kin means all are kin. Not favoring only one’s children means all are children. Thus, the most benevolent’s lack of favor is the ultimate favor—how can it be called *universal love*?

*The He* says: The Sage regards the people as straw and dogs, expecting no gratitude or reverence.

*The Bi* says: The Sage aligns with Heaven and Earth’s virtue, comparing the people to straw dogs.

*The Yun* says: The Sage loves his kin and is benevolent to the people—thus, he speaks only of the people. Yet his Dao is one with Heaven and Earth, though humans have form, their roles differ.

---
**Between Heaven and Earth,**
*The He* says: Between Heaven and Earth lies emptiness, where harmonious *qi* circulates, and thus all things arise naturally. If a person eliminates desires, moderates tastes, clears the five organs, then the spirit dwells within.

It is like the *tuo* and *yue*.
*The Ming Huang* says: *Tuo* is a windbag, *yue* is a pipe. The windbag’s blowing, the pipe’s playing—both are empty and without intent, yet they move and respond.

Thus, between Heaven and Earth, all things arise without partiality—
for it is their emptiness and lack of intent that allows it.

*The He* says: Within the *tuo* and *yue*, emptiness resides,
and thus they produce sound and *qi*.

*The Yun* says: The *tuo* and *yue* are empty yet responsive—
when things act upon them, they respond.
They respond yet do not hoard;
Heaven and Earth’s relation to all things,
the Sage’s relation to the people—
they meet each thing in its proper measure,
yet are not bound by the present moment,
nor cling to the past.
Thus, they are likened to the *tuo* and *yue*’s boundless nature.
Empty yet unyielding, moving yet ever more abundant.

*The Ming Huang* says: The *tuo* and *yue*, though empty, do not bend.
When moved, their sound grows ever stronger—
just as the Sage, with no partial favor,
has no time of bending,
and his applications are boundless—
truly, moving yet ever more abundant.

*The He* says: This speaks of emptiness never exhausted,
of motion ever increasing in sound and *qi*.

*The Bi* says: *Pì*—this is the *tuo*’s bellows.
*Yue*—this is the *yue*’s flute.
Within the *tuo* and *yue*, hollow and void,
without sentiment or action,
they are empty yet never exhausted,
moving yet never spent.
Between Heaven and Earth, all is vast and unrestrained,
following Nature’s course,
and thus cannot be exhausted—
just as the *tuo* and *yue*.

*The Yun* says: Empty their form,
active their function.
Excessive speech leads to exhaustion,

「The *He* says: Excessive activity harms the spirit, excessive speech harms the body. When the mouth opens and the tongue moves, calamity is sure to follow. ○The *Yun* says: If one does not speak in accordance with the Dao, then one is exhausted. To utter words is to make them known—this is the way of the Sage, who speaks yet does not act. Better to hold to the Middle. ○The *Mínghuáng* says: Excessive speech without cultivation leads to repeated exhaustion and bending.

To love all is difficult to extend, and thus invites resentment and hatred; better to embrace and hold the Middle Way, where all suffices naturally.

○The *He* says: Better to uphold *virtue/power* within the Middle, nurturing the spirit and *qi*, loving silence while sparing speech. ○The *Bi* says: The more one strives to act, the more one loses it. Things grow their own evils, and actions err in their words. Disordered, unspoken, and unprincipled, this is the repeated path to exhaustion. When the *tuo* and *yue* hold to the Middle Way, they are inexhaustible; when one abandons oneself to external forces, all things are governed. If the *tuo* and *yue* intend to produce sound, they cannot meet the demands of the player.

○The *Yun* says: Holding to the Middle Way is how one responds to all transformations.

**Chapter Six: The Unending Spirit of the Valley**

The Unending Spirit of the Valley,
○The *Mínghuáng* says: The *valley* (谷) is empty yet capable of response; the *spirit* (神) is subtle yet unfathomable. *Death* (死) here means rest. The valley’s response to sound is beyond knowing—when there is sensation, it responds; its response is like a spirit, like a spirit’s response, yet it never rests. To illuminate the supreme Dao: empty yet giving rise to all things, its wondrous applications defy naming, hence the use of the *Unending Spirit of the Valley* as a metaphor.

○The *He* says: The *valley* is nourishment; when humans nourish their spirit, they do not die. By *spirit* (神), I mean the vital essence of the five *zàng* organs. The liver stores the *hun* (魂), the lungs the *pò* (魄), the heart the *shén* (神), the kidneys the *jīng* (精), and the spleen the *zhì* (志). When the five *zàng* organs are all injured, the five spirits depart.

○The *Yun* says: The valley responds yet is never exhausted; the spirit transforms yet is unfathomable. All things receive their mandate from it, yet it neither originates nor perishes—this is the *Unending Spirit of the Valley*. When speaking of the spirit, it is already at its utmost, but adding the *valley* signifies that it can be both empty and full, yet also capable of containing and responding. Because it is active yet never worn out, it is simply called the *Unending Spirit*.

This is called the *Dark Feminine* (玄牝).

○The *Mínghuáng* says: *Dark* (玄) means profound; *Feminine* (牝) means mother. The *Unending Spirit of the Valley* responds to all things, its applications boundless and profound, unfathomable yet able to give birth to all things. Thus, it is named the *Unending Spirit of the Valley* to illuminate the great Dao’s function of giving life and sustaining.

○The *He* says: The Dao of not dying lies in the *Dark Feminine*. *Dark* (玄) is Heaven, which in humans is the *nose* (鼻); *Feminine* (牝) is Earth, which in humans is the *mouth* (江). Heaven nourishes humans with the five *qi* (五气), entering through the nose and stored in the heart. The five *zàng* organs, cleansed and bright, become the spirit’s clarity, intelligence, and the five faculties—sound, vision, and the three *po* (豚). Their *ghost* (鬼) is called the *hun* (魂), which is masculine, governing entry and exit through the nose, communicating with Heaven. Thus, the nose is called the *Dark* (玄).

Earth nourishes humans with the five flavors, entering through the mouth and stored in the stomach. The five faculties grow turbid and debased, forming the body’s flesh, bones, blood, and the six *emotions* (六情). Their *ghost* (鬼) is called the *pò* (魄), which is feminine, governing entry and exit through the mouth, communicating with Earth. Thus, it is called the *Feminine* (牝).

○The *Xiù* says: The *Unending Spirit of the Valley* receives its mandate and manifests as the *Dark Feminine*, assigning itself to become yin and yang, thus completing Heaven and Earth—yet it is fundamentally one thing. By its reception of the mandate, it is called the *Unending Spirit of the Valley*; by its manifestation as form, it is called the *Dark Feminine*. The *Dark Feminine*’s gate is called the *Root of Heaven and Earth*.

○The *Mínghuáng* says: The profound, subtle, empty, and feminine can give birth to all things, and all things emerge through it. This is called the *gate* (门). Heaven and Earth have form, so their innate essence is the root foundation.

○The *He* says: *Root* (根) is *primordial* (元). The gate of the nose and mouth is where the primordial *qi* of Heaven and Earth flows, coming and going.

○The *Xiù* says: Though the *Dark Feminine* embodies yin, within its singularity, yin and yang are both present. The term *gate* (门) differs from *door* (户); all things emerge through this gate yet remain unseen, hence the phrase *Dark Feminine’s gate*. Endlessly sustained,

○The *He* says: The breathing through the nose and mouth should be continuous and subtle, as if present yet also as if nonexistent.

○The *Yun* says: *Endless* (绵绵) means drawn out yet unbroken. The *Dark Feminine* generates all things without end, yet its presence is indeterminate—neither fixed nor absent. It is not exhausted in use.

○The *Mínghuáng* says: The applications of the empty *Feminine* are endless and subtle, responding as if present, yet its use requires no effort, so it does not weary.

○The *He* says: The use of *qi* should be expansive and relaxed, not hasty or labored.

○The *Bi* says: The *Unending Spirit of the Valley* is the center of the valley, yet has no valley—no form, no shadow, no resistance, no obstruction. It remains low yet unmoved, guards stillness without decay. The valley completes yet reveals no form—this is the supreme thing. Though it remains low and cannot be named, it is called the *Root of Heaven and Earth*, endless yet subtly present, used without labor. The *gate* (门) is the source of the *Dark Feminine*. Tracing its origin, it shares the same body as the *Ultimate* (极), hence it is called the *Root of Heaven and Earth*. If one says it exists, its form is unseen; if one says it does not exist, all things arise from it. Thus, it is endless yet subtly present. It completes all things, yet its use does not exhaust—hence, *used yet unmoved*.

○The *Xiù* says: How can there be movement when it grows ever stronger? The *Daoist Canon of the Supreme Virtue and Power* concludes here. #1 *Zhèng*: The original text lacks this, supplemented from the *Zhèngtǒng Daoist Treasury* edition of *Tang Xuánzōng’s Imperial Commentary on the Daoist Canon of the Supreme Virtue and Power*. #2 *Xìng*: Originally 『与』, changed according to the Daoist Treasury edition of Tang Xuánzōng’s commentary. #3 *Suǒ*: Originally 『则』, changed according to the Daoist Treasury edition of Tang Xuánzōng’s commentary. #4 *Mí*: Originally 『尔』, changed according to the *Zhèngtǒng Daoist Treasury* edition of *Tang Xuánzōng’s Imperial Commentary on the Daoist Canon of the Supreme Virtue and Power*.」

**#5** *Jiān*: Originally 『气』, should be read as *jiān* [comprehensive].

---

**Daoist Canon of the Supreme Virtue and Power: Imperial Commentary by Emperor Xuánzōng**
**Volume II: Commentary by Master Héshànggōng Wáng Bì and Wáng Hóng**

**Chapter 7: Heaven Long, Earth Enduring**
Heaven long, earth enduring.

Emperor Xuánzōng says: Those who mark heaven and earth’s enduring nature seek to illustrate that without selfishness or scheming, one may endure and last. This analogy is fulfilled by the Sage, who transcends the body and yet preserves it—without selfishness, yet achieving it.

○ Master Hé says: When speaking of heaven and earth’s eternal life and longevity, it is to instruct humanity.

Heaven and earth endure long because they do not generate themselves.
○ Master Hé says: Heaven and earth alone endure long because they are tranquil, giving without seeking return, unlike humans who dwell in places and eagerly hoard private gain, robbing others to enrich themselves.

○ Master Bì says: If one generates oneself, one contends with things; if one does not generate oneself, things return to it.

Thus, it endures.

Emperor Xuánzōng says: Heaven and earth nourish life with vast virtue, yet endure long because they draw their endowment from the Dao, not arrogating the merit of creation.

○ Master Hé says: Because they do not seek life, they endure without end.

○ Master Xiù says: If one generates oneself, there is one’s own life—once life is lost, it is already gone. Only by not taking life as one’s own does life never truly arise. If life never arises, though the form that hosts it lives, it bears no burden of life, hence its enduring nature.

Heaven and earth’s not generating themselves is not for the sake of enduring—such is simply the Dao’s way. Yet what is called enduring is but a fleeting moment; only what sustains the heavens and earth without ever failing is its true essence.

Thus, the Sage places his body last,
○ Master Hé says: To serve others before oneself.

Yet his body is first.
○ Master Hé says: The world reveres him, placing him first as leader.

○ Master Hóng says: Though the Sage is Sage, his form aligns with all things—only by embodying the Dao without contention does he stand apart from the masses. If he establishes himself against others, even common folk could surpass him.

He sets his body aside,
○ Master Hé says: To diminish oneself and enrich others.

Yet his body is preserved.

Emperor Xuánzōng says: Placing the body last, others willingly honor him—thus his body is first. Setting the body aside, his mind becomes serene—thus his body is preserved.

○ Master Hé says: The people love him as a parent, the spirits protect him as a child—thus his body endures.

○ Master Hóng says: If one clings to the self to preserve it, all things become enemies. Only by transcending and abandoning the self—having no self—can nothing overturn him. Is this not because of his lack of selfishness?

○ Master Hé says: The Sage is loved by people and favored by spirits—not because of his fairness and lack of selfishness?

Thus, he fulfills his private ends.

Emperor Xuánzōng says: Heaven and earth forget their nurturing function—that is lack of selfishness. Yet they endure long—that is fulfilling their private ends. The Sage places his body last and sets it aside—that is lack of selfishness. Yet he leads and preserves—that is fulfilling his private ends.

○ Master Hé says: What others call selfishness is to enrich oneself. The Sage, without selfishness, enriches himself—thus he fulfills his private ends.

○ Master Bì says: Lack of selfishness means doing nothing to the self. The body leads, the body is preserved—thus it is said he fulfills his private ends.

○ Master Xiù says: The Sage lacks selfishness because he has never had a self. If one calculates and acts for it, what is more selfish than that?

---

**Chapter 8: The Supreme Good as Water**
The supreme good is like water.

Emperor Xuánzōng says: To illustrate the Sage’s supreme goodness, water’s nature is likened to the Dao.

○ Master Hé says: The supremely good person is like water’s nature.

○ Master Hóng says: Water is the first of the Five Phases, emerging from emptiness into substance. Near the Dao, its nature is closest to it—separate from the Dao, goodness is established. The supreme good is like water; this is the natural order of things.

Water benefits all things,
○ Master Hé says: In heaven, it is mist and dew; on earth, springs and sources.

Yet does not contend.
It dwells where all hate,
○ Master Hé says: People abhor lowliness, filth, and murkiness, yet water alone remains tranquil and flows there.

○ Master Bì says: People hate what is low.

○ Master Hóng says: *Chǔ* [dwells], one text reads *jū* [resides].

Thus, it nearly attains the Dao.

Emperor Xuánzōng says: *Jī* [nearly] means near.

○ Master Hé says: Water’s nature nearly aligns with the Dao.

○ Master Bì says: The Dao has no form; water does—thus *jī* [nearly].

○ Master Xiù says: Humans have hearts; the heart is fire, rising and illuminating, thus they love contention. Only those who forget the heart and embody the Dao can benefit others without scheming to overcome them.

It dwells in good ground,
Emperor Xuánzōng says: The supremely good person, in body weak and yielding, is like water dwelling in the earth—nourishing all things. The earth, though low, is useful; water flows downward.

○ Master Hé says: Water’s nature delights in the earth. Above grass and trees, it flows downward, having power to move the earth and descend to people.

○ Master Xiù says: It trends downward and flows.

Its heart is deep,
Emperor Xuánzōng says: The mind, deep and tranquil, is like water’s still pools.

○ Master Hé says: Water is deep and hollow; deep pools are clear and bright.

○ Master Hóng says: Deep and tranquil, internally luminous yet externally obscure.

It gives with goodness,
Emperor Xuánzōng says: Its giving aligns with supreme benevolence, like water nourishing all things.

○ Master Hé says: All things receive water to live; it gives to the empty, not the full.

Its speech is trustworthy,
Emperor Xuánzōng says: Its words are true, like water’s path through peril—never failing in truth.

○ Master Hé says: Water’s inner reflection mirrors form, never losing its essence.

○ Master Hóng says: All currents flow east.

Its governance is well-ordered,

Emperor Xuánzōng says: In governance, its administration is well-ordered, just as water cleanses all things, making them pure and tranquil.

○ Master Hé says: Nothing remains uncleansed; it is both clear and even.
○ Master Hóng says: It follows principle without yielding to caprice, accumulating softness and weakness to overcome the rigid and vast.

In matters, it excels in ability,
Emperor Xuánzōng says: In handling affairs, it skillfully adapts, like water’s nature—squaring and rounding to fit its vessel, never obstructed by things.
○ Master Hé says: It can be square or round, bending and straightening to match form.
○ Master Hóng says: It adapts to every change, thus nothing is beyond its ability.

In action, it is timely,
Emperor Xuánzōng says: It responds to things in season, never missing its time, like water floating in spring and congealing in winter.
○ Master Hé says: In summer it disperses, in winter it congeals, moving in due season without losing the Way’s timing.
○ Master Hóng says: If it flows when unobstructed, it stops when blocked—never acting before things, yet never missing its time.

Only the non-contending,
Master Hé says: When blocked, it stops; when urged, it flows—it follows human guidance.
Thus, there is no blame.

Emperor Xuánzōng says: The highest virtue is a tranquil heart, yielding to things, like water’s nature—stopping when blocked, flowing when released—never opposing or flattering things, thus never falling into fault or blame.
○ Master Hé says: Water’s nature is such, so none under heaven blame water.
○ Master Bì says: All speech about water aligns with this Way.
○ Master Hóng says: Water’s essence is singular, yet nothing can alter it, thus it encompasses all these virtues. It has virtue for things while always yielding to them—this is how it attains virtue. If one seeks virtue with intent, virtue cannot be achieved—thus the text concludes by repeating this.

**Chapter Nine: *Holding and Filling***

*Holding and filling it,*
Emperor Xuánzōng says: Clinging to fullness, preventing loss, amassing wealth as a burden—regret and stinginess arise—thus it is better to cease.
*Cease* means *stop*.

○ Master Hé says: *Fullness* means *excess*. *Cease* means *stop*. Holding excess leads to loss; it is better to stop.
○ Master Bì says: *Holding* means not losing virtue. If virtue is not lost yet one still fills it, danger of collapse is inevitable—thus it is better to cease, meaning it is worse than having no virtue or merit.
○ Master Hóng says: Clinging to fullness stems from desiring possession; this is how loss occurs. Only by forgetting possession as possession, and holding it as non-possession, can there be no loss or overflow.

*Measuring and sharpening it,*
Emperor Xuánzōng says: Calculating and sharpening—pursuing honor and wealth—necessarily leads to arrogance, inviting calamity, thus it cannot be long preserved.
○ Master Hé says: *Measuring* means *governing*; first measuring, then inevitably discarding.
○ Master Bì says: Already measuring to make it sharp, then sharpening it further—inevitably it will falter, thus it cannot be long preserved.
○ Master Hóng says: *Measuring* is skill in gauging others’ emotions; *sharpening* is skill in penetrating things. Yet affairs are endless, and if one holds to a single self amid change, then the skill of measuring and sharpening will sometimes falter—how can it be long preserved? Thus, the perfect person follows the seasons, rides the principle, and meets them with no-self, so their actions are boundless and their responses inexhaustible.

*Gold and jade fill the hall,*
Emperor Xuánzōng says: This clearly shows that fullness cannot be long held.
○ Master Hé says: Lustful desires harm the spirit; wealth burdens the body.
○ Master Bì says: It is better to cease.
○ Master Hóng says: Clinging externally to things—what is held is not truly possessed—how can it endure?

*Rich and noble yet arrogant,*
Emperor Xuánzōng says: This clearly shows that sharpness cannot be measured.
Arrogance arises from the heart’s generation—blame comes from within.
○ Master Hé says: When rich, one should relieve the poor; when noble, one should pity the lowly. Instead, they indulge in arrogance and invite disaster.
○ Master Bì says: It cannot be long preserved.
○ Master Xiù says: Arrogance stems from relying on externals—those who rely on externals are unworthy of consideration.

These four all take possession of things, and the arrogant share the same burden—yet the more they cling to fullness below, the greater their loss.

*When achievement is complete and name is fulfilled, retreat—this is the Way of Heaven.*

Emperor Xuánzōng says: When achievement is complete and name is fulfilled, one should retreat to decline prominence—like Heaven’s Way of fullness and emptiness in season, thus there is no worry of decline.
○ Master Hé says: When a person’s deeds are accomplished and affairs established, if they do not retreat and avoid position, they will meet with harm—this is Heaven’s constant Way.
○ Like the sun at its zenith must move, the moon at full must wane, things at their peak must decline, and extreme joy turns to sorrow.
○ Master Bì says: The seasons cycle; achievement brings transition.
○ Master Hóng says: Cold and heat alternate, extremes return, yin and yang succeed one another—Heaven’s Way is thus. Yet the foolish in the world, once encountering change, once violating its name, cling to it for life, claiming it as their own, unaware of Nature’s subtle shifts or the hidden balance of fortune and misfortune. Thus, they inevitably sit and invite disaster, unable to preserve themselves.

Only the perfect person is otherwise: they treasure gold and jade yet do not prize them, dwell in wealth and nobility yet do not glory in them. All things that come and lodge upon them are like shadows gathering on the body—naturally unaware whether they belong to self or other. Thus, how could they cling to fullness as caution or measure sharpness as skill? Unless one attains the mystery of no-self, how could they align with this?

Heaven’s Way is vast, and nothing surpasses it.

**Chapter Ten: *Carrying the Vital Essence***

*Carrying the vital essence,*
Master Hé says: *Vital essence* means *soul and spirit*. Humans carry soul and spirit to live; they must nurture them.

Joy and anger disperse the soul,
Sudden fright injures the spirit.
The soul resides in the liver,
The spirit in the lungs.
Fine wine and rich foods
Harm the liver and lungs,
Thus, the soul remains tranquil, the spirit at ease—virtue is preserved, and life extended.

**Chapter Ten: *Carrying the Vital Essence* (Continued)**

**Master Pan says:**
The *arc* (魄) is the master of material form, yet the *spirit* (神) is vast and boundless, yet unaware of itself. The spirit always rides upon the *arc*, thus the spirit becomes constrained by the body—this is how the boundless becomes narrow, the free becomes obstructed.

Those who wish to learn this Way must first purify their *virtue* and *qi*, lest they be burdened by the body. Only then can the spirit ride upon the *arc* without being ridden by it, thus embracing *Oneness* and embodying the spirit.

I have often reasoned: When a person dies, some ascend while others descend, for they extinguish the spirit to serve the form, letting the spirit follow the *arc*, thus transforming into strange things in the dark. But those whose spirit remains whole—even if the *arc* is stagnant—will merge with the spirit and know no separation.

On the day of a sage’s death, the spirit does not follow the *arc*. At first, it merely rides upon it. Can one who embraces *Oneness* ever truly depart?

**Emperor Ming Huang says:**
At birth, the *arc* is formed—this is the beginning of life. Once born, the *arc* is yin and empty, while the *soul* (魂) is yang and full. The *arc* is yin and void, so one must constantly nurture and restore its yang. When yang fills the *arc*, it becomes the *soul*, and the *soul* moves—life is then complete.

*Oneness* means undivided. To restore yang and preserve life, one must not be tainted or mixed. Thus, one must embrace and guard the pure *Oneness*—how could it ever leave the body?

**Master He says:**
If a person can embrace *Oneness* and keep it from departing, they will endure. *Oneness* is the origin of the Dao, the essence of *Great Harmony*—thus it is called *Oneness*.

Spread throughout the world, *Oneness* brings clarity to Heaven, tranquility to Earth, and rectitude to rulers. Within, it is the heart; in action, it is conduct. In giving, it is *virtue*—all are named *Oneness*. *Oneness* means singular focus, without division.

**Master Bi says:**
*Carrying* means *dwelling*. The *arc* is where humans naturally reside. *Oneness* is their true nature. If one can dwell in their natural home, embrace *Oneness* with clarity, and never let it depart, then all things will come as guests.

**Master Pan says:**
*Oneness* is the number of *essence* (精). Though we do not speak of *essence*, we speak of *Oneness*—for guarding *Oneness* keeps the *essence* from scattering. Learning the Dao returns to restoring nature, restoring nature returns to embodying the spirit. The reason one cannot embody the spirit is that they chase externals and forget the root, replacing self with things, thus losing *essence* and spirit, sinking into foolishness.

The vital essences of yin and yang are *soul* and *arc*. The *soul* is yang and wanders, yet it halts within the body because the *arc* is its counterpart. The *arc* is yin and still—*stillness* is restraint. Thus, the student must first calm their *virtue* and *qi*, making the *essence* and *arc* tranquil and unified. When the *arc* is still, the *soul* is settled; when the *essence* is unified, the spirit is whole.

*Oneness* generates water, water is *essence*—at birth, humans gather spirit from *essence*, originally whole yet unaware, often scattering into confusion. This is called *Wangliang* (罔两): *Wang* means the spirit is unclear, *Liang* means the *essence* is divided.

*Zhuangzi* says: *"Do not shake your *essence*—this is called embracing Oneness."*

The spirit and *essence* are like fire and water—fire has no fixed form, yet it shines when nourished. The spirit is vast and formless, beyond artificial crafting; one must only preserve the *essence*. Like adding oil to fire to brighten it, or nourishing roots to grow leaves—when *soul*, *arc*, and spirit are unharmed, the heart grows strong and *qi* flows smoothly, ungoverned by things.

In stillness, one embodies the Dao; in action, one aligns with principle. Refined to its utmost, one merges with the spirit, transcending the four names. This is the utmost necessity for the student—all ages of sages sought it within, not without. Can you concentrate your *qi* to such gentleness as a babe?

**Emperor Ming Huang says:**
Concentrate the pure *qi*, attaining harmony and softness, like a babe with no distinctions—then the spirit will not stray.

**Master He says:**
Concentrate and guard the *essence* and *qi* so they do not scatter, then the body will respond with softness. Like a babe, free of thought and affairs, the spirit will not depart.

**Master Bi says:**
