*Concentrate* means *commit*. *Attain* means *fulfill*. To commit to natural *qi* and fulfill supreme softness—like a babe with no desires—then all things are whole, and nature is realized.

**Master Xiu says:**
Humans have three aspects: *essence* (精), *spirit* (神), and *qi* (气). If *essence* is whole, the *spirit* is whole; if the *spirit* is whole, it can command the *qi*.

When the *spirit* weakens and cannot command the *qi*, the *qi* acts erratically, leading one astray. *Mencius* says: *"The will is the commander of *qi*."* Yang Xiong says: *"Qi is the horse that carries good and evil."* If the commander is weak and the troops rebel, the army will perish. If the horse rages and the driver falters, the chariot will fail.

If the *spirit* cannot concentrate the *qi*, then joy, anger, sorrow, and delight are all governed by *qi*, sinking one into confusion—this is inevitable. The ancient scholars all understood this. If one has not yet reached this understanding, they must clear the heart to prevent it.

The three warnings of Confucius are all about guarding *qi*. Among his disciples, only Yan Hui could concentrate *qi*, thus he *"did not transfer anger."* Anger is the most violent expression of yang, and if one can resist transferring it, what else is this but concentrating *qi*?

Humans are born with sacred nature, yet their *qi* scatters and cannot be concentrated, so from maturity onward, they drift further from the Way. If one embraces *Oneness*, the *spirit* is whole; if the *spirit* is whole, the *qi* is soft; if the *qi* is soft, the true self is whole—thus they align with the babe.

*Concentrate* means to possess and wield authority. Purify and observe the mysteries...

He said: *"The River"* replied: One must purify the heart, making it spotless and clear. The heart dwells in the profound and obscure, perceiving all things—thus it is called *playing with the profound* (玄览). Can it be without blemish?

The *Bright Sovereign* said: *Playing with the profound* means the heart’s illumination. Blemish refers to flaws and defects. To purify and remove this illumination, making it serene and clear—can it be without flaws?

*"The River"* said: It means not indulging in evil.

*"Bi"* said: *Profound* is the ultimate of things. To say one can purify and remove false adornments, reaching the utmost perception, yet not allowing things to obstruct one’s clarity—if blemishes arise in the spirit, then one ultimately aligns with the *Profound*.

*"Pan"* said: If one can concentrate *qi*, the nature settles; if the nature settles, wisdom becomes clear; if wisdom is clear, one can then purge and remove to attain the profound perception of ultimate truth. Once this truth is illuminated, all worldly phenomena become the most wondrous. If one were to perceive even a single blemish, it would mean failing to recognize the truth.

**Loving the people and governing the state:**

*"The River"* said: To govern oneself is to cherish *qi*, thus preserving the body. To govern the state is to love the people, thus securing the nation. Can one achieve *wuwei*?

The *Bright Sovereign* said: To nurture the ten thousand people and administer state affairs—can one achieve *wuwei*? One must transform oneself from above. If one cultivates the spirit, all will follow in self-cultivation. When the body is cultivated, virtue is complete—thus one can be a ruler.

*"The River"* said: To govern oneself is to regulate breathing and vital *qi*, ensuring it is not heard by the ears. To govern the state is to bestow benevolence and virtue, ensuring the people do not know of it.

*"Bi"* said: To rely on extension to seek completion, or to manipulate fate to seek concealment—this is wisdom. To *play with the profound* without blemish is like abandoning sageliness. To govern without wisdom is like discarding wisdom. If one can abandon wisdom, then the people will grow perverse while the state thrives.

*"Pan"* said: As the above speaks of the Dao for oneself, then one can extend it to others. Laozi’s words focus on restoring nature. If one acts, it is not the way to let the people settle into their true nature. Though one may love and govern, one does not exalt action—this is the ultimate of the Way.

**The opening and closing of the Celestial Gate:**

*"The River"* said: The Celestial Gate refers to the Purple Forbidden Enclosure of the North Pole (北极紫微宫). Opening and closing refer to the two extremes of beginning and end. In governing oneself, the Celestial Gate refers to the nostrils—opening means panting, closing means breathing. Can one act like the female?

The *Bright Sovereign* said: The Celestial Gate is where fate and destiny emerge. Opening and closing refer to order and chaos. It speaks of the sovereign receiving the Mandate of Heaven—if one can guard the receptive and tranquil, one can then endure and complete one’s celestial blessings. Another interpretation: The *Yijing* says, *"One closing and one opening is called change."* The sage governs by responding to transformations without fixed methods, not relying on force but maintaining receptivity—like the Celestial Gate’s opening and closing, yielding to excess and supplementing with humility.

*"The River"* said: In governing oneself, one should be like the female—tranquil, gentle, and weak. In governing the state, one should respond harmoniously without dominating.

*"Bi"* said: The Celestial Gate is the path through which all under Heaven flows. Opening and closing mark the transitions between order and chaos. Whether opening or closing, it governs all under Heaven—thus it is called the Celestial Gate’s opening and closing. The female responds without dominating, acts without forcing—if one can act like the female in the Celestial Gate’s opening and closing, then all things will submit willingly, and all will dwell in peace.

*"Pan"* said: The perfected one has no intention in action; the spirit enters and exits, responding without dominating. As *Zhuangzi* says, *"There is something that takes it."* In autumn, Laozi’s virtue aligns with metal—still, unified, and restoring nature—thus his emphasis is as such. As for the *Yijing*, both Heaven and Earth, before and after, are nothing but the Dao.

**Bright and all-pervading:**

*"The River"* said: The Dao is bright like the sun and moon, pervading all under Heaven, extending to the eight extremes—thus it is said that one cannot see it, cannot hear it, yet it shines forth in all ten directions, radiant and brilliant. Can one be without knowing?

The *Bright Sovereign* said: If the sovereign can be receptive and tranquil, the myriad people will joyfully extol their virtue. Though bright like the sun’s fourfold illumination, one must still forget one’s deeds and not lord over others—thus, can one be without knowing?

*"The River"* said: There is none who can know the Dao’s fulfillment across all under Heaven.

*"Bi"* said: The utmost clarity and pervasiveness mean no confusion, no doubt. If one can act without contrivance, then all things transform naturally. This is the Dao’s constant *wuwei*—if lords and kings can uphold it, all things will transform of their own accord.

*"Xiu"* said: Principle has no need of knowing. Knowing is for responding to things. Once one knows ultimate truth, one naturally transcends knowing. Only by being without knowing can one truly know nothing. If one were to appear clearly as knowing, then it is ignorance. The mystery of being without knowing—how can it be called knowing?

I have often reasoned: The wise and the foolish, the saintly and the base—originally, they differ not. It is only because of folly that wisdom is revealed, and thus the name "saint" arises. The sage’s original heart—how could it ever have been self-consciously saintly? Thus, though bright and all-pervading, it initially knows nothing—this is the true knowing of the sage.

**To nurture and raise:**

*"The River"* said: The Dao gives birth to all things and nurtures them.

*"Bi"* said: It does not block their source, nor does it restrain their nature.

*"Pan"* said: If the Dao is complete as above, then creation and transformation lie within oneself, and one can nurture all things.

**To give birth without possessing:**

*"The River"* said: The Dao gives birth to all things, taking nothing for itself.

*"Pan"* said: The Dao and all things are always as one—who can possess them?

**To act without relying:**

*"The River"* said: The Dao’s actions are not done with expectation of reward.

*"Pan"* said: If one acts out of necessity, even if there is the deed of acting, one transcends it naturally—how could one then boast or depend on it?

**To grow without dominating:**

*"The River"* said: The Dao nourishes all things without carving or controlling them for use.

*"Pan"* said: If the Dao is as above, one can then nurture all under Heaven through *wuwei*. Though above all things, like a branch to its standard, how could there be the function of division or control?

**The Mysterious Virtue:**

*"The River"* said: The Dao is the mysterious virtue—when it acts, it cannot be seen. The Emperor Ming Huang said: Let all things follow their natural course, and nurture them accordingly. Do not claim credit for their growth, nor depend on the deeds you perform. Though you stand above all things, do not rule or dominate them. If a person can live thus, he attains the profound and mysterious virtue.

*"The River"* said: The Dao’s manifestation and the practice of virtue are unfathomable—one cannot see them. Yet, its teachings guide one to live in accordance with the Dao.

*"Bi"* said: If one does not obstruct their source, things will grow of themselves—what need is there for deeds? If one does not restrain their nature, things will flourish on their own—what need is there for effort? Things grow and mature naturally; they do not need to be shaped or completed by me. Virtue exists without a master—how else could it be called mysterious?

All who speak of the mysterious virtue possess virtue, yet also understand that its origin lies in the unseen and unfathomable.

*"Pan"* said: The mysterious is the ultimate essence of virtue. Once, Shun dwelled in the humble abode of *Xiao Wei*, yet the records say he heard of the mysterious virtue. Indeed, the highest virtue is always found in the state of no thought, no action. The meaning of this chapter centers on *wuwei*—though it speaks of growth, the virtue still bears the name of the mysterious. This chapter fully outlines the progression from the scholar to the attainment of divine insight and sainthood through self-cultivation.

**The Thirty Spokes Chapter (Eleventh):**
The thirty spokes converge at one hub.
*"The River"* said: In ancient times, a chariot had thirty spokes, following the cycles of the moon. All spokes meet at the hub, which contains a hole—thus, the spokes unite in their function. For cultivating oneself, one must eliminate emotions and desires, leaving the five organs empty and void, so that the spirit may return to them. For governing a state, one must be few in ability yet gather the many, weak yet sustain the strong. When the hub is empty, the chariot’s use is realized.

The Emperor Ming Huang said: This illustrates the interplay of presence and absence, of utility and reliance. The thirty spokes show the making of a chariot. The hub unites them, demonstrating how the few can govern the many. The axle and spokes exist, yet it is their emptiness that allows the chariot to move. If the axle and spokes did not exist, there would be no chariot to use. If the chariot’s center were not empty, the axle and spokes would be useless.

*"The River"* said: The emptiness is what allows the chariot to turn and carry people. The hub’s emptiness enables the wheel to rotate; the axle’s emptiness allows people to ride upon it.

*"Bi"* said: The hub can govern the thirty spokes because it is empty. It is through its emptiness that it receives all things—thus, it can use its solidity to unite the many.

*"Pan"* said: *Gong* (共) was originally written as *yòng* (用, "use"). To knead clay and shape it into vessels.

*"The River"* said: *Yán* (涎) means "to blend." *Zhǐ* (埴) is clay—soil. Blending soil to make vessels for drinking and eating. When the vessel is empty, its use is realized.

The Emperor Ming Huang said: *Yán* (埏) means "to blend." *Zhǐ* (埴) is clay—soil. The potter blends the soil to form jars and pots.

*"The River"* said: The emptiness within the vessel allows it to hold and receive things. To hollow out doors and windows to make a dwelling.

When the dwelling is empty, its use is realized.

The Emperor Ming Huang said: In ancient times, people hollowed out caves to make dwellings, and they also opened doors and windows—thus, it is called "hollowed."

*"The River"* said: The doors and windows are empty, allowing people to enter and exit, to see and be seen. The dwelling’s emptiness allows people to reside within it—that is its use.

*"Pan"* said: In all these three things—whether for movement, storage, or dwelling—their utility comes from what is not present. Thus, the presence of things brings advantage.

*"The River"* said: Advantage belongs to things. Advantage comes from their form and function—when vessels contain things, or dwellings hold people, one fears they may break or collapse. When the belly contains the spirit, one fears the body’s decay and destruction.

The Emperor Ming Huang said: The form is advantageous when there is emptiness; emptiness is advantageous when there is form. The Dao, above form, is called *Dao*. Form, below the Dao, is called *instrument/vessel*. To clarify the Dao’s ultimate utility, we use coarse forms to illustrate it—thus, we borrow the advantage of emptiness in the presence of things to show the advantage of the mysterious emptiness in the presence of things.

*"The River"* said: The power of emptiness allows it to contain and receive all things—thus, it is said that emptiness can govern solid form. The Dao is emptiness.

*"Bi"* said: Wood, clay, and jade are shaped into these three things, yet all derive their use from emptiness. When speaking of emptiness, the advantage of things arises—all things rely on emptiness for their utility.

*"Pan"* said: Emptiness is merely the opposite of presence; presence and emptiness depend on each other. Within emptiness, there is an ineffable presence whose utility is inexhaustible. The function of this ineffable presence is not like that of solid things, which have fixed forms and cannot respond to the infinite. Thus, it is only called "advantageous." Advantage belongs to the realm of *yin*—though this is the discussion of presence and emptiness, when pushed to its extreme, emptiness does not depart from presence, and presence is truly emptiness. Neither presence nor emptiness is the true ineffable presence.

**The Five Colors Chapter (Twelfth):**
The five colors make the eyes blind,

---

**The Chapter on Favor and Humiliation (Thirteenth):**

Favor and humiliation are like a startling shock,
**The Dao/Way** says: To grasp them brings trembling, to lose them brings sorrow—so long as one does not forget favor and humiliation, all are startled.

**He** said: The body is startled by favor, and startled by humiliation.
**Pan** said: The heart rejoices in seeing favor, yet what humiliation is greater than that?

To value great affliction as one’s own body,
**The Dao/Way** says: The body is the foundation of life—thus, one treasures and guards it as if it were a great affliction. These two lines state that to prize one’s body as if it were a great affliction is the same as saying to prize great affliction as one’s own body. For when one values the body, it is indeed the greatest affliction.
**He** said: *Guì* (noble/valued) is *wèi* (fear/reverence). *Ruò* (if) means *zhì* (to reach). To fear great affliction reaching the body—thus, all are startled.
**Pan** said: To value the body and burden the heart with it—then its affliction is great indeed. Just as one has a body, and every movement weighs upon it—what is favor and humiliation?
**He** said: Asking what is favor, what is humiliation. Favor is honor and glory; humiliation is shame and disgrace. Those who ask about it in relation to the body are doing so to enlighten others.
**Pan** said: The root of these words is the pair *ruò* (if) and *shèng* (startling)—this is incorrect. For if favor is what the world honors, how could it be considered humiliation? The question is posed to introduce what follows.
Favor is the lesser of the two.
**The Dao/Way** says: Earlier, it was stated that favor and humiliation are like a startling shock, lest people not understand—thus, it asks: What is meant by favor and humiliation? To receive favor and become proud and full of self-satisfaction is to invite disaster without fail. Thus, one knows that favor is the root of humiliation—hence the reply: Favor is the lesser of the two.
**He** said: Humiliation is lowliness.
**Xìu** said: To receive favor from others is to follow the Dao/Way of lowliness. Zhao Meng was able to humble himself because he was favored by others. All living beings inherently possess a true sovereign, sufficient to surpass the world, and a true ruler, sufficient to govern all things—but the foolish lose their noble virtue, pursue external objects for glory, and yield to the power of gain and loss. This power of gain and loss lies in the hands of others. My joys and sorrows depend on what I gain and lose—how could I not be early and deeply confused?

Startling arises from having love yet fearing loss, so the burden of receiving favor is like a startling shock.

Those who serve three times yet three times renounce office, without showing signs of joy or displeasure—are they not startled?
Who receives favor yet does not take pride in it, yet remains unhumiliated in their favor?

To gain it is like a startling shock,
**He** said: To be startled by receiving honor and favor is to be in a high position yet feel as if in peril—valuing one’s station, not daring to be proud, not daring to indulge.
To lose it is like a startling shock,
**He** said: To lose favor and fall into disgrace is to fear that greater calamity will follow. This is what is meant by: Favor and humiliation are like a startling shock.

**The Dao/Way** says: Favor and humiliation cycle endlessly; favor is the root of humiliation. All emotional entanglements and stagnations—even in humiliation—do not make one startled by favor. Thus, the Sage warns: When you receive favor, regard it as if you had received humiliation and were startled. Then, when you lose favor and suffer humiliation, it will be as if you had heeded my warning—when you receive favor, yet are startled and fearful.

「Hence, **He** concludes: This is what is meant by *favor and humiliation are like a startling shock*.

**Pì** said: Favor must bring humiliation, glory must bring woe—when one is startled by humiliation, the same applies to glory.
If a ruler below receives favor, humiliation, glory, and woe as if startled, then he will not be sufficient to disrupt the world.

**Wén** said: Because at the moment of gain or loss, one has a startled heart—this is why it becomes humiliation.

What is meant by *"the greatest woe is like one’s own body"*?
**Míng Huáng** said: He fears that people do not understand that one’s own body is the root of all woes, hence he asks.

**Hé** said: Why return to your own door and yet fear others like your own body?

**Pì** said: The greatest woe belongs to the category of glory and favor.
If life is cherished so deeply, it must lead one to the realm of death—hence it is called the greatest woe.
People are bewildered by glory and favor, yet they return to their own bodies—hence it is said that the greatest woe is like one’s own body.

**Xiù** said: The noble are above others yet possess a state—if they cannot forget it, then their woe is great indeed.
Compare it to a person having a body: cherishing it and burdening it with cares, then cold and heat, sickness and pain, all arise—is this not the greatest woe?

**Shì Nán Zǐ** said: **Yáo** did not cherish his own person, nor did he see his own person in others.
The noble cherish their own person, those favored see their own person in others—only the Sage can avoid these two pitfalls.
The reason I have the greatest woe is because I have a body.

**Míng Huáng** said: The body is an illusion, fundamentally without true substance. The woe arises from clinging to the notion of having a body, feeling its cold and heat, pain and pleasure—hence it is the woe of the body.

**Hé** said: The reason I have the greatest woe is because I have a body. I worry about its toil, ponder its hunger and cold, succumb to emotions and desires—then I meet with calamity and woe.

**Pì** said: This arises from having a body.
When I have no body,
**Pì** said: I return to the natural order.
What woe will I have?

**Míng Huáng** said: If one knows the **Dao/Way** of heaven and earth in harmony, all are not mine to possess; abandoning form and discarding wisdom, realizing that the body is not the body, and aligning with the Great Unity—then what woe will there be?

**Hé** said: If I had no physical body, and attained the **Dao/Way** naturally, rising lightly on clouds, entering and exiting without hindrance, merging with the **Dao/Way** in spirit—then what woe would remain?

**Wén** said: If all things are one with me, then I am in harmony with the **Dao/Way**’s mystery, and all changes are forgotten—woes of fortune and misfortune cease.
But the foolish cannot free themselves; they rely on form as their own, so wherever the body goes, woes arise at every step.

**Lǎozǐ** first illuminates the burdens of glory and favor, yet these burdens all stem from clinging to the body—hence he uses the analogy of the body to explain the noble, and then proceeds to the subtlety of transcending the body.
**Zhuāngzǐ** says: Forget what one should not forget, and do not forget what one should forget—this is called true forgetting, also clarifying this meaning.

But **Kǒngzǐ**’s principle of *"no-self"* aligns with this. Learning culminates here.
Yet what is called *"no"*—does it mean to abandon and discard? Only that having it without ever truly possessing it, then it will not burden.

Moreover, nothing is higher or greater than wealth and nobility. If one truly attains *"no"* while still possessing them, then the Sage’s way of saving the world is also without woe.
This is how one regards favor—nothing more.

Hence, the noble use their bodies for the world, as if they could entrust the world to them.

**Hé** said: The ruler cherishes his own body while demeaning others, wishing to be the master of the world—this may allow him to establish his rule, but not to endure long.

**Pì** said: With nothing to exchange for his body, it is said he is noble—this is how one can entrust oneself to the world.
He who loves does not harm his body with favor, humiliation, glory, or woe—then he can truly be the master of the world.

**Wén** said: The Sage has no body, so the land can only be entrusted to **Yáo** and **Shùn**. If one has a body and seeks to do so, then it becomes a burden.
If one has not yet reached *"no body"*, then only by honoring life and valuing its roots, without exchanging oneself for external things, can one be as if entrusted to the world.
The noble do not humiliate their bodies; those who love do not endanger their bodies.
The disciples of **Zǐzhōu Zhīfù** and **Wángzǐ Sǒu** are examples of this.
If these two were like this, would they not have cherished their favor and burdened their nobility?

One text reads: *"This is how one can entrust the world"* and *"This is how one can entrust the world"*—this is **Wáng Bì**’s adoption of the *Nán Huá* text as definitive.
It is not so.

[From] *"Viewing it, one does not see"*—Chapter Fourteen of the *Zhuāngzǐ*.」

**"Viewing it, one does not see"**—its name is **Yí** (平易).
**Míng Huáng** said: This is the **Way of Illumination**.
Yí means **plain and easy**. The Way is not a color, so it cannot be seen.
Yet within the formless, it manifests as color—thus it is named **Yí**.

**Hé** said: **"Formless"** is called Yí—meaning it lacks hue and cannot be seen by the eye.

**Xiù** said: When the Way reaches **equanimity among all dharmas**, there is no high or low—it is beyond what the eye can perceive.

**"Listening to it, one does not hear"**—its name is **Xī** (希).
**Míng Huáng** said: Xī means **subtle**, the faintest of sounds.
The Way is not a sound, so it cannot be heard.
Yet within the silent, it alone harmonizes—thus it is called **Xī**.

**Hé** said: **"Soundless"** is called Xī—meaning it has no resonance and cannot be heard by the ear.

**Páng** said: When things interact, sound arises.
The Great Way does not dwell among phenomena—when sought with the mind, it is already distant, hence **Xī**, as the *Classic* calls it **"Great Sound."**

**"Grasping it, one does not attain"**—its name is **Wēi** (微).
**Míng Huáng** said: *Bó* means **to hold**; Wēi means **subtle**.
The Way has no form, so it cannot be grasped.
Yet within the formless, it acts upon things—thus it is named **Wēi**.

**Hé** said: **"Formless"** is called Wēi—meaning it has no substance and cannot be seized by the hand.

These three cannot be **exhaustively defined**,
**Hé** said: The three—**Yí, Xī, Wēi**—are without color, sound, or form.
Words cannot describe them; script cannot transmit them.
They must be **received in stillness**, sought with the **spirit**—they cannot be questioned and obtained.

Thus, they **merge into One**.
**Míng Huáng** said: The three are used to **explicate the Way**, but the Way is not sound, color, form, or law—
so explications cannot be fixed; only **Yí, Xī, Wēi** remain.
The Way is not **Yí, Xī, Wēi**, so it **merges back into One**.

**Hé** said: *Hùn* means **to unite**.
Thus, though united in three, it is named **One**.

**Bì** said: Without shape or image, without sound or echo,
it can **pervade all** and **go everywhere**—
yet it cannot be known.
Thus, naming it with my ears and eyes as "unknown," it cannot be exhaustively defined—
it **merges into One**.

**Páng** said: The three are originally **One**, yet people seek it
sometimes with sight, sometimes with hearing, sometimes with touch—
thus names are assigned according to circumstances.
Though the means of seeking and the object sought are never truly separate,
only when one **realizes Oneness** does it **naturally merge with the One**.

Its **upper** is not **bright**,
**Hé** said: The One in the heavens is not **dazzlingly luminous**.

Its **lower** is not **obscure**.
**Míng Huáng** said: Above, it must be clear; below, it must be dark—
yet the Way, though above, is not above, and though below, is not obscure.

**Hé** said: The One in the world is not **dimly hidden**.

**Xiù** said: All things have substance, thus they possess **yin and yang**—
above, bright; below, dark—this is their necessary nature.
Only the Way, without substance, **naturally merges**—this is the fixed nature of the Way.
The *Zhuāngzǐ* says:
*"Those who lose their Way see light above and become earth below."*

It is **endlessly dynamic**—**unnameable**,
**Hé** said: *Shéngshéng* means **endless motion**.
Unnameable—it is not a single color, so it cannot be distinguished by **blue, yellow, white, or black**.
It is not a single sound, so it cannot be heard as **gong, shang, jiao, zhǐ, or yǔ**.
It is not a single form, so it cannot be measured by **length, shortness, size, or smallness**.
It **returns to the formless**.

**Míng Huáng** said: *Shéngshéng* means **uninterrupted motion**.
Neither bright nor obscure, its motion is endless—
the function of giving birth to things is **unnamable**.
It is not that things create things; rather, it **constantly generates yet never begins with things**.
Its subtle origin is **profoundly still**—thus it is said to **return to the formless**.

**Hé** said: *Wù* means **substance**.
It must return to **without substance**.

**Páng** said: The Way has **constant measure**, its principle is reliable—thus it is called *shéngshéng*.
Though measured, it has no fixed form—thus it is **unnameable**.
At its utmost, it **never begins with things**—it is **perfect, beyond addition**.

This is called **"the state without state."**
**Hé** said: The One has **no form**, yet it **gives form to things**.

**Páng** said: **Being yet unseen**.

**"The image without image,"**
**Hé** said: The One has **no substance**, yet it **establishes forms for all things**.

**Bì** said: If one says it is **without**, yet things arise from it;
if one says it is **with**, yet its form is unseen—
thus it is called **"the state without state, the image without image."**

**Páng** said: **Being yet unattainable**.

This is called **hūhuǎng** (惚恍).
**Míng Huáng** said: This is the **state without state, the image without image**—
it cannot be named **being**, nor **non-being**.
Non-being is difficult to name—thus it is called **hūhuǎng**.

**Hé** said: The One is **flickering, elusive**—as if present, as if absent—**invisible**.

**Bì** said: It cannot be **fixed**.

**Páng** said: *Hū* is **being doubted as non-being**.
*Hǎng* is **non-being doubted as being**.
The Way, as a thing, is **neither being nor non-being**—it cannot be definitively named.

**Approaching it, one does not see its head,**
**Hé** said: The One has **no beginning or end**—it cannot be anticipated.
**Abandon passions, discard desires**—it **naturally returns to itself**.

**Following it, one does not see its tail.**
**Míng Huáng** said: It has **no beginning**, so approaching does not reveal its head.
It has **no end**, so following does not reveal its tail.

**Hé** said: The One has **no trace of advance**—it cannot be **fixed upon**.

**Páng** said: Things have **fixed forms**, thus they have **front and back**.
The Way, without form, has **no head or tail**.

This text speaks of the **Dao’s** operation—
thus it may be **welcomed and followed**.
Though it says **welcomed and followed**,
the **welcoming and following** lie within **things**,
yet the **Dao** itself has never differed.

To grasp the **Dao of antiquity**,
and use it to govern the **being** of the present.

**Míng Huáng** said:
To grasp the **Dao of antiquity’s** non-action,
and use it to govern the present’s **actions of being**,
then one **returns to simplicity and purity**.

**Hé** said:
The **Sage** grasps and upholds the **Dao of antiquity**,
holding to the **One** to govern **things**,
knowing that in the present there must be **being**.

**Bì** said:
There is **being**—there are **its affairs**.

**Páng** said:
The **Dao of antiquity** refers to the **Dao** that is **always One** through past and present,
and since it is **always One** through past and present,
it can govern the **ten thousand changes** of worldly affairs.

Can one know the **beginning of antiquity**?
**Páng** said:
**Extend it upward**—
until one reaches the **initial beginning of the beginningless**,
then one knows that **things** have **no origin**,
and thus the **nature of the Dao** is attained.

This is called **Dao’s principles**.
**Míng Huáng** said:
To know the **beginning of antiquity’s** practice,
this is called the **fundamental framework of Dao’s transformation**.

**Hé** said:
If a person can know the **origins of high antiquity**,
where **being** first emerged as **One**,
this is called knowing the **principles of the Dao**.

**Bì** said:
That which is **formless and nameless**,
is the **root of the ten thousand things**.

Though past and present differ,
and times change with customs,
still none fail to follow this,
to achieve their **governance**.

Thus one may grasp the **Dao of antiquity**,
and use it to govern the present’s **being**.

High antiquity, though distant,
its **Dao** remains—
so though in the present,
one can still know the **beginning of antiquity**.

**Páng** said:
The **essence of Dao’s principles**,
that which does not change between ancient and modern,
is what **Zhuāngzi** called the **beginningless framework**.

#1 Original text had "比" (*bǐ*), corrected to "此" (*cǐ*).

**Chapter Fifteen: The Virtuous Scholar of Antiquity**

The virtuous scholar of antiquity—
**He** said: *[This refers to] one who attains the Dao as a sovereign.*

**Subtle, mysterious, and penetrating,**
**He** said: *"Mysterious"*—this is Heaven. *[It describes] one whose resolve is profound and mysterious, whose essence communicates with Heaven.

**Exploring what cannot be known.**
**Emperor Minghuang** said: *"Scholar"* means *"to serve."* *[It speaks of] those in antiquity who took the Dao as their service—
toward those subtle and mysterious teachings of the Way,
none escape their profound insight and illumination,
yet their virtue and bearing are deep and unfathomable,
beyond recognition.

**He** said: The Dao’s virtue and power are profound and distant,
beyond recognition.
Within, it seems as though blind;
in reverse, it seems as though deaf—
none know its excellence.

**Need** said: *"Scholar"* is the name for serving the Dao—
subtle and mysterious,
profound and penetrating.
With this Dao, one retreats and hides in secrecy.
Secrecy is the innate nature within,
so nothing advances to be seen.
Those of their time who were scholars
differed from this,
thus they are called the virtuous scholars of antiquity.

Only because it cannot be known,
do they forcefully embody its form.
**Emperor Minghuang** said: Only because virtue and measure are difficult to recognize,
do they forcefully embody its form to make it clear—
referring to the following passage.

**Solemn, as though crossing a winter river.**
**Emperor Minghuang** said: *"Solemn"* means *"leisurely and composed."*
The virtuous scholar, in his composed state,
is like crossing a winter river—
others value [his caution],
so he fears and is wary.
Now, I am unstained,
so I am composed.

**He** said: When undertaking matters, he weighs them gravely,
hesitant, as though crossing a winter river—
his heart resists it.

**Bi** said: Crossing in winter,
composed as if desiring to cross,
yet as if not desiring to cross—
his true intent cannot be seen in his expression.

**Pang** said: *"Solemn"* means preparing in advance and warning oneself.
The Perfect One has no concern for things,
only when pressed does he act.
Crossing in winter—
approaching matters with caution,
as if unavoidable.

As *Zhuangzi* says: *"He does not engage in affairs."*

**Doubtful, as though fearing the four neighbors.**
**Emperor Minghuang** said: *"Doubtful"* means *"hesitant and uncertain."*
Earlier, it spoke of the virtuous scholar being unstained, thus composed,
yet when observing his actions,
he is deeply hesitant—
like a common man fearing neighbors and being cautious.

**He** said: His advance and retreat are hesitant,
as though bound by constraints,
fearing the four neighbors would know—
like one who has broken the law.

**Bi** said: The four neighbors unite to attack the central sovereign,
yet he remains uncertain of his direction.
The virtuous one’s beginnings and signs are unseen,
his virtue’s path is invisible—
just as this.

**Pang** said: Though without warning, he still warns himself—
day by day, the Perfect One is serene, secret, and deep,
never leaving his innate dwelling,
thus he always seems to fear neighbors,
restraining his conduct without excess.

**Solemn, as though a guest.**
**He** said: Like the bearing of **Yóuyú** the Perfect One,
solemn and without contrivance.

**Pang** said: He does not take affairs as his own responsibility,
so his bearing is tranquil.

**Dissolving, as though ice about to melt.**
**Emperor Minghuang** said: Though solemn and composed,
without contrivance,
yet he does not cling to things—
dissolving, like spring ice releasing.

**He** said: *"Dissolving"* means dispersing,
*"releasing"* means vanishing.
Abandoning emotion and desire,
this is called emptiness and void.

**Pang** said: At the beginning of life,
one is like the great void—
congealed into our form,
like water becoming ice.
Thus, for the Dao, there is ice that melts and thaws—
when matters reach this point,
his bearing no longer clings to one form,
dissolving as though about to release.

**Examining it further:**
Human nature is like water,
congealed by transformation,
so it becomes rigid and blocked.
The *"releasing"* returns to its origin.

**Thick, as though uncarved wood.**
**Emperor Minghuang** said: Though dissolving like melting ice,
he does not boast of himself,
yet remains sincere and thick—
like uncarved wood, without distinction.

**He** said: *"Thick"* means substantial and solid,
*"uncarved"* means form not yet divided—
within, guarding the spirit,
without, lacking ornament.

**Xiu** said: Before becoming an instrument, it is called *"uncarved"*—
a metaphor for the completeness of nature.
After ice melts,
only then can one preserve the fullness of Heaven’s nature,
without artificiality,
thus it is like uncarved wood.

**Vast, as though a valley.**
**Emperor Minghuang** said: His virtue and measure are vast and broad,
containing all without exception,
like a valley’s capacity.

**He** said: *"Vast"* means spacious.
*"Valley"* means empty and void—
without virtue, deeds, or fame,
yet encompassing all.

**Pang** said: Nature is complete and unharmed,
yet does not possess its completeness,
thus vast and empty—
like a valley’s reception and response.

**Mingled, as though turbid.**
**Emperor Minghuang** said: Harmonizing light, advancing naturally,
like turbidity yielding clarity.

**He** said: *"Mingled"* means preserving the original truth,
*"turbid"* means not brightly illuminated.
Blending with the multitude, not self-exalting.

**Bi** said: All these *"as though"* comparisons
speak of forms and symbols
that cannot be named or shaped.

**Pang** said: Water’s nature is originally clear,
yet turbidity arises from mingling with things.
If one does not purify oneself of things,
one becomes mingled, like turbid water.

**Who can turbid it and then still it,
gradually clarifying?**

**Emperor Minghuang** said: *"Who"*—who can, in that turbid state,
still it and settle it,
so that it gradually clarifies?

**He** said: *"Who"*—who can, like turbid water,
still it, gradually clarifying.

**Pang** said: Clarifying nature is like clarifying water—
if forced, it stirs and becomes more turbid.
Only through stillness can it gradually clarify.

The scholar of the Dao
is why nothing can turbid him—
because his stillness gradually clarifies.

**Who can settle it and then move it,
gradually bringing forth life?**

**Emperor Minghuang** said: Who can remain settled in this clarity for long,
then seek superior methods,
moving and cultivating,
so that the nature of clarity and stillness
does not stagnate in doctrine,
but gradually emerges in motion?
*"Life"* means emerging in motion.

**He** said: Who can remain settled for long,
gradually nurturing longevity?

**Bi** said: *"Obscure"* by applying Dao to things, one attains clarity;
*"Turbid"* by stillness, one attains purity;
*"Settled"* by movement, one attains life—
this is the Way of Nature itself.
*"Who can"*—this speaks of its difficulty.
*"Gradually"* means meticulous care.

**Pang** said: Returning to stillness and quiescence,
responding yet remaining unobstructed,
thus life emerges gradually,
and the end becomes a new beginning.
If life emerges gradually through movement,
then change is ever in motion—
nothing can halt it.
Whoever does this
truly follows the principles of yin and yang,
rides the flow of Nature,
and acts without contrivance—
thus all is spoken of as *"gradual."*
To speak of turbidity is to know how to settle into clarity;
to speak of settling is to know how to move into turbidity—
this aligns with the subtle language of the previous section.
Those who preserve this Dao
do not seek fullness.

**Emperor Minghuang** said: To preserve this gradual clarity and gradual life,
one must hold to nothing rigidly.
If one clings to clarity while seeking life,
this is called *"fullness"*—
and one will lose this Dao.
Hence, it is said: *"Do not seek fullness."*

**He** said: To preserve this gradual life,
one must not indulge in excess or overflow.

**Xiu** said: *"Not-full fullness"* is true fullness.
After attaining the Dao,
if one claims possession of it,
this is to have severed oneself from the Dao—
far from its path.
Only the not-full
can endure without decay,
without clinging to novelty.

**Emperor Minghuang** said: Only those who do not seek fullness
can truly embody it—
their actions are worn yet unobstructed,
not hindered by clinging to the new.

**He** said: Only those who do not seek fullness
can guard against decay without pursuing novelty.
Decay is the concealment of brilliance;
novelty is the pursuit of fame.

**Pang** said: The Dao has no fixed form,
cannot be contained by any vessel.
When applied, it overflows;
when sought, it eludes—
thus those who follow the Dao are never full,
yet their use is inexhaustible.
Those who attain the Dao are never full,
so those who perfect it are never novel.
The Dao’s function
permeates all things without decay,
for its lack of decay
means no novelty can be established or ruined.
Decay arises from novelty;
ruin arises from completion.
Though a scholar may perfect the Dao,
they must always appear decayed and ruined.
If, at the Dao’s outset, one prizes its novelty,
this contradicts the Dao’s intent—
not true perfection.
The Classic says:
*"Great perfection seems deficient—
its use is unexhausted."*
This passage, sentence by sentence, leads to completion,
yet completion seems like decay—
thus it is fulfilled.

---
**Chapter Sixteen: Attaining the Supreme Void**

**He** said: Those who attain the Dao
diminish emotions, abandon desires,
their five viscera pure and still—
reaching the supreme void.

**Guard stillness with sincerity.**

**Emperor Minghuang** said: The supreme void is the subtle origin.
It is said that when humans receive life,
they inherit this supreme void and subtle origin,
but once form takes in and expels,
the origin scatters.
Now, to restore the supreme void and subtle origin to the body,
one must utterly abandon worldly attachments,
guard this feminine stillness with sincerity—
then the Dao of the supreme void will naturally manifest in the body.

**He** said: Practice purity and stillness,
cultivate sincerity and depth.

**Bi** said: To attain the void is to reach the utmost sincerity;
to guard stillness is to grasp the true essence.

**Xiu** said: To attain the void, strive for the utmost;
to guard stillness, strive for depth.
For those who would observe the principles of things,
first attain unity.

The scholar’s affairs—
if excessive, they obstruct the Dao;
if active, they reveal true nature—
thus it is.

**All things arise together.**

**He** said: *"Arise"* means *"life."*
All things arise in life.

**Bi** said: Movement, action, growth, and flourishing.

**Pang** said: All things emerge from the void and stillness,
then return to the void and stillness—
the changes of spring birth and autumn death make this clear.
I observe their return.
**Emperor Minghuang** said: The Daoist Master says:
*"How do we know that guarding feminine stillness can attain the supreme void?"*
Observe how all things move and act—
when they return, they always rest in stillness—
thus it is known.

**He** said: I observe all things,
and see that none fail to return to their origin.
Humans should reflect deeply on their roots.

**Bi** said: By observing void and stillness,
one sees their return.
All that arises from the void,
all movement from stillness—
though all things arise and act,
they ultimately return to the void and stillness—
this is the utmost sincerity of things.

**Pang** said: Void and stillness bring clarity;
clarity reveals principle.
To perceive principle is not to cling to form—
rather, to observe the true nature of return.

**All things flourish abundantly,**
**He** said: *"Flourish abundantly"* means *"luxuriant growth."*
Each returns to its root.

**Emperor Minghuang** said: Again, it is said that all things return to stillness,
for luxuriant growth—
their life nature returns to the root.
Thus, those who speak of luxuriant growth as movement,
see that when they return, they rest in the root.
Speaking of things flourishing in movement,
when they return, they all rest in their origin.

**He** said: It is said that all things wither and fall,
each returning to its root to regenerate.

**Bi** said: Each returns to its beginning.

**Pang** said: *"Return"*—
returning to innate nature, returning to functional use,
returning to the root of nature.
Though plants and animals differ,
their principles converge in unity.

**Returning to the root is stillness.**
**He** said: *"Root"* refers to the foundation—
the root is settled, gentle, and weak,
humble and lowly—
thus it does not perish.

**Stillness is returning to one’s essential nature.**
**Emperor Minghuang** said: Luxuriant growth returns to its root,
then rests in stillness.
If humans return to the root in utmost stillness,
this is called restoring their inherent nature and vitality.

**He** said: Stillness is returning to one’s essential nature and vitality,
so that one does not die.

**Pang** said: There is a nature of life,
nature is bestowed by vitality,
and vitality exists before life begins—
the complete essence of the Dao.
The *Classic* says:
*"Exhaust principle, fulfill nature, and reach vitality."*
Observing return is exhausting principle;
returning to the root is fulfilling nature;
returning to vitality is reaching vitality—
reaching vitality is the utmost,
yet it does not depart from nature.

**Returning to vitality is the constant way.**

**He** said: Returning to vitality prevents death—this is the Dao’s eternal practice.

**Bi** said: Returning to the root brings stillness, hence *"stillness."* Stillness returns to vitality, hence *"returning to vitality."* Returning to vitality attains the constancy of nature and vitality, hence *"constant."*

**Pang** said: Birth brings entry into existence; existence binds one to fate. Thus, the subtle transformations of fate shift without pause, moment by moment. Only returning to vitality remains serene and eternally tranquil—nothing can alter it.

**Knowing constancy is clarity.**
**Emperor Minghuang** said: Guarding stillness and returning to vitality is to possess constancy. Knowing how to guard constancy brings yet greater clarity.

**He** said: To know the Dao’s eternal extremes is to be clear.

**Pang** said: What is known as not-constant is not true knowing. The sage values clarity above all—it is self-realization alone. Ignorance of constancy leads to reckless action and calamity.

**Emperor Minghuang** said: If one does not steadfastly uphold virtue, they may suffer the shame of ancestral sacrifices—departing from constancy and acting recklessly leads to utter ruin.

**He** said: Not knowing the Dao’s eternal practice, yet acting cunningly and deceitfully, loses divine insight—hence, calamity.

**Bi** said: Constancy, as a thing, is neither partial nor exaggerated—it lacks the states of brilliance or obscurity, warmth or cold. Hence, knowing constancy is clarity. Only this returning encompasses all things without exclusion. Losing this, evil invades one’s portion, and things stray from their place. Thus, not knowing constancy leads to reckless action and calamity.

**Pang** said: Not attaining the extremes of nature and vitality, one takes all forms and colors as real, giving rise to deluded emotions. Engaging with them only deepens confusion—through countless ages, one never awakens. The disasters of yin and yang are most dire.

**Knowing constancy is inclusivity.**
**Emperor Minghuang** said: Knowing to guard true constancy makes the mind and environment tranquil, like an empty valley—it contains all things without exclusion.

**He** said: Knowing the Dao’s eternal practice removes emotions and desires, encompassing all without exclusion.

**Bi** said: It encompasses all without exception.

**Pang** said: Knowing constancy reveals flawlessness—what cannot it contain? Inclusivity is impartiality.

**Emperor Minghuang** said: Containing all things without agenda, free of private bias, is true justice.

**He** said: Without exclusion in inclusivity, governance is impartial and without favor.

**Bi** said: Encompassing all without exception leads to boundless fairness.

**Pang** said: With no distinction between self and other, what private interest remains? Impartiality is sovereignty.

**Emperor Minghuang** said: Those who act with perfect justice and no favor are where all things turn.

**He** said: Impartial governance without favor is the way to rule the world.

**Bi** said: Boundless fairness reaches everywhere without omission.

**Pang** said: Inner impartiality leads to outer sovereignty. Sovereignty is heaven.

**Emperor Minghuang** said: When all things joyfully follow, like heaven’s embrace, one aligns with heaven’s virtue.

**He** said: To merge sovereign virtue with divine insight is to harmonize with heaven.

**Bi** said: Encompassing all without omission leads to unity with heaven.

**Pang** said: The sovereign is the ultimate of human Dao. Attaining human Dao merges with heaven. Heaven is the Dao.

**Emperor Minghuang** said: Sovereign virtue like heaven enables the Dao’s practice.

**He** said: Virtue merging with heaven aligns with the Dao.

**Bi** said: Merging virtue with heaven, embodying the Dao’s boundless flow, reaches the utmost emptiness.

**Pang** said: Only through the Dao does one accompany heaven. Accompanying heaven fulfills the Dao—who can distinguish whether it is heaven or humanity? The Dao is enduring.

**Emperor Minghuang** said: The Dao’s practice throughout the world brings lasting blessings.

**He** said: Aligning with the Dao brings enduring longevity.

**Bi** said: Attaining the emptiness of things, one grasps their constancy—reaching the utmost extremes.

**Pang** said: This is called *constancy.* The body perishes without peril.

**Emperor Minghuang** said: Aligning with heaven and practicing the Dao, one’s death is like the heavens’—eternal, without danger or harm.

**He** said: Able to be impartial and sovereign, harmonizing with heaven and the Dao, the four virtues are complete. Virtue and the Way are vast and distant—no calamity or blame. One dies with the heavens and earth, without peril.

**Bi** said: As a thing, emptiness cannot be harmed by fire or water, nor destroyed by metal or stone. Applied to the mind, rhinoceros and tigers cannot strike with their claws, nor weapons pierce with their blades—what danger could there be?

**Pang** said: Heaven and earth arise together; all things are one—who could harm them?

---
**Chapter Seventeen: The Supreme Sovereign**
The Supreme Sovereign, the lower knows there is one.

**Emperor Minghuang** said: The Supreme Sovereign is the pure ancient ruler. The lower knows there is a sovereign above—revered like heaven, yet without imposed teachings or artificial governance. Thus, people lack virtue yet still praise them.

**He** said: The Supreme Sovereign refers to the Great Ruler, the nameless sovereign. The lower knows there is a sovereign above but does not serve them—simple and unadorned.

**Bi** said: The Supreme Sovereign is the Great Person. The Great Person is above, hence *"Supreme."* The Great Person acts without doing, teaches without speaking—all things act, yet none originate from them. Thus, the lower only knows there is one.

**Pang** said: Following the Three Sovereigns’ emergence, they governed without action, letting people naturally fulfill their roles without awareness. How could they be personally praised?

The next level: they are loved and praised.

**Emperor Minghuang** said: When virtue declines, the ruler practices benevolent teachings. Kindness is seen, hence loved; merit is high, hence praised.

**He** said: Their virtue is visible, their kindness worthy of praise—thus, they are beloved and praised.

**Bi** said: Unable to govern through *wuwei* and teach without words, they instead establish good deeds and transform the people, so that the lower ranks come to love and praise them.

**Pang** said: When governance yields clear results, the people cherish the ruler’s benevolence and remember their virtue. Though sufficient for temporary order, the ruler has already strayed from complete virtue, and the people have lost their natural alignment—thus, disorder begins. This is the rule of *ren* and *yi*.

The next level: they are feared.

**He** said: Laws and punishments are imposed to govern them.

**Bi** said: Unable to rely on benevolence and virtue, they instead depend on coercion and authority.

**Pang** said: This is the rule of laws and institutions.

The next level: they are scorned.

**Emperor Minghuang** said: When virtue further declines, the ruler enforces flawed policies, driving the people with punishments—thus, they are feared. When deceit fills their hearts and the ruler cannot discern it, they are scorned.

**He** said: With excessive prohibitions and burdensome decrees, the people cannot sincerely submit, hence they deceive and mock.

**Bi** said: Unable to govern with proper laws, they instead rule with cunning schemes. The people know to avoid them, and their commands are not obeyed—thus, they are scorned.

**Pang** said: When virtue is lost and governance fails, the people scorn the ruler. [Original text: *"One version reads: ‘fear and scorn,’ omitting ‘the next level.’"*]

When trust is insufficient,

**He** said: If the ruler lacks trustworthiness below, the people will respond with deceit.

There is untrustworthiness.

**Emperor Minghuang** said: Those who are feared and scorned—all stem from the ruler’s lack of trust, hence untrustworthy subjects arise.

**He** said: When the ruler lacks trustworthiness below, the people answer with untrustworthiness, deceiving their ruler.

**Bi** said: [Referring to] the ruler’s words.

When the body loses its nature, sickness arises.
When assisting things loses authenticity, flaws and strife emerge.
When trust is insufficient, untrustworthiness arises—this is the Way of Nature.
One’s own shortcomings cannot be remedied by cleverness.

**Pang** said: Mencius said: *"To have it within oneself is called trust."* The *Classics* say: *"Within it is trust; when people fulfill their nature, they do not stray from the constant Way—thus, it is called trust."* When the ruler governs apart from the Dao, it is not the natural essence, and thus trust is insufficient. When the ruler lacks trust, the people descend into deceit and falsity—hence, untrustworthiness arises. When the people are untrustworthy, great disorder begins.

*Yóu*—how precious are words!

**Emperor Minghuang** said: Those who are loved and praised—because the ruler’s words of virtue and teaching are precious, hence they are loved and praised.

**He** said: Speaking of the Supreme Sovereign, their actions are as precious as the sacred words, lest they stray from the Dao and lose naturalness.

**Pang** said: *Yóu*—the hesitation of *yóu*, meaning "not hasty," precious meaning "not lightly given." *Zhuangzi* says: *"Words are like wind and waves; the Sage speaks as if always doubtful, daring not to speak lightly, much less to act lightly."* The people are difficult to govern because their rulers act. Thus, the Sage hesitates.

[Original text: *"One version lacks the ‘yóu’ character—incorrect."*]

When achievements are complete and matters settled,

**He** said: Meaning the land is at peace.

The people all say, *"It was natural."*

**Emperor Minghuang** said: When achievements are complete yet not grasped, when matters are settled yet without action, the people use them daily without knowing how—saying it was natural. Thus, the Supreme Sovereign is known below.

**He** said: The people do not recognize the ruler’s pure virtue, instead believing it was natural for them.

**Bi** said: The beginnings and signs of naturalness cannot be seen, nor can its intentions be glimpsed. Nothing can replace its words—when words are spoken, there is response. Hence, *"Yóu—how precious are words!"*

They govern through *wuwei*, teach without words, and do not impose forms on things. Thus, achievements are complete and matters settled, yet the people do not know how.

**Pang** said: This is like guiding the good and distancing wrongdoing, yet no one knows who does it.

---
**Chapter Eighteen: The Great Dao Declines**

When the Great Dao declines, *ren* and *yi* arise.

**Emperor Minghuang** said: When purity is diluted and simplicity dispersed, the Great Dao does not prevail. Thus, *ren* and *yi* emerge, achieving small success.

When insufficiency breeds corruption, and when excessive governance sparks decay, yet the Sage’s intent to save the world remains unchanged—though the paths differ, they do not diverge entirely.

**He** said: In the age of the Great Dao, every family had filial children, every household had loyal and trustworthy individuals—*ren* and *yi* were not needed. When the Great Dao declined and evil arose, only then did *ren* and *yi* become teachings to be transmitted.

**Bi** said: Losing *wuwei*, they instead apply clever schemes to promote goodness—the Dao advances things.

When cleverness emerges, great falsity arises.

**Emperor Minghuang** said: Those who rely on cleverness are about to establish laws. When laws emerge, deceit follows—thus, great falsity arises.

**He** said: The clever ruler devalues virtue and prizes words, devalues substance and indulges in ornamentation. Below, they respond with great falsity and deceit.

**Bi** said: Using vigilance and clarity to detect deceit, things reveal their forms, and the people know to avoid them. Thus, when cleverness emerges, great falsity arises.

When the six relationships are fractured, filial piety and kindness arise.

**Emperor Minghuang** said: Father and son, husband and wife, brothers—these are the six relationships. When bonds are distant and ranks disordered, they are fractured. Over time, even relatives become like children, hence filial piety and kindness arise. All stem from straying from the Dao, thus partial names emerge.

**He** said: When the six relationships are severed and kin are not harmonious, only then do filial piety and kindness arise to nurture and care.

**Pang** said: In the age of supreme virtue, the people fulfill their nature—filial piety and kindness are not named, for they are the natural constant. Now, we still honor filial piety and kindness, yet it is because the six relationships are fractured.

When the state is in chaos and darkness, loyal ministers arise.

**Emperor Minghuang** said: In times of great peace, the ruler and subjects mutually suffice—what need is there for such names? But when darkness and disorder prevail, and the perilous governance is exposed, then loyal ministers emerge.

**He** said: When decrees fail to take effect, and ruler and subjects harbor mutual resentment, while ministers vie for power, then loyal ministers arise to rectify and rescue their sovereign.

**Bi** said: The most exalted names arise from great vice—this is what is called the "inner gate of beauty and evil."

The six relationships are father and son, brothers, and husband and wife.
If the six relationships are naturally harmonious and the state governs itself, then filial piety, kindness, and loyal ministers would be unknown in their existence.

Fish forget the Dao of rivers and lakes, then the virtue of mutual moistening arises.

**Pang** said: When governance is enlightened, who is not loyal? Who then is the loyal minister?

**Chapter Nineteen: The Chapter on Abolishing the Sage and Discarding Wisdom**

**Abolishing the Sage**
**He** said: To abolish the sage’s artifices and return to the origin, guarding the primal—all five emperors were thus. When Cangjie created writing, it was inferior to the Three Sovereigns’ knotted cords without script.

**Discarding Wisdom**
**He** said: To discard wisdom and cleverness, returning to non-action.

**The Chapter on Abolishing the Sage and Discarding Wisdom (continued)**

**The People’s Gain is a Hundredfold.**
**Emperor Minghuang** said: To abolish the Sage’s words and teachings, then transformation returns to non-action.
To discard the common man’s cunning and deceit, then the people become pure and simple.
Pure and simple, then artifice and falsehood do not arise; non-action, then arrogance and rivalry cease.
Man draws upon Heaven’s harmony, yet things lack Heaven’s nature—this is the hundredfold gain.

○ **He** said: Farming has no partiality.

○ **Pang** said: The people’s innate nature is pure and flawless; because they have lost their true essence and sunk into falsehood, the Sage therefore firmly establishes virtue and wisdom to serve as their ruler and teacher.
Yet the people, following the Sage’s virtue and wisdom, further stray without returning, ever more distant from their roots. Thus there is but a fleeting small remedy, yet ultimately the people lose their priceless simplicity.
To abolish and discard them, then the people return to their natural state—its benefits are incalculable.

**Abolishing Benevolence and Discarding Righteousness**
○ **He** said: To abolish benevolence’s display of favor, to discard righteousness’ emphasis on trustworthiness.
Then the people return to filial piety and kindness.

○ **Emperor Minghuang** said: To abolish the benevolence of universal love, to discard the righteousness of discriminating laws, then man returns to great filial piety and kindness.
○ **He** said: Virtue’s transformation is pure.
○ **Pang** said: In an age of perfect virtue, father and son naturally cherish one another—this is enough. Now, if benevolence and righteousness are further introduced, then names and realities become entangled, gains and losses confused, and the people’s nature is disordered.
For what flourishes at the end must decay at its root—this is the Dao of Heaven.
Filial piety and kindness are the root of benevolence and righteousness. Some say Confucius and Mencius followed the enlightened Dao of Yao and Shun, focusing solely on benevolence and righteousness, while others take Laozi as the standard—how do they compare?

He said: Summer thrives by birth and growth as its virtue, while autumn excels by gathering and conserving as its power. One leads to prosperity yet departs from the root, the other to withering yet returns to the essence.
The Dao is the seasons; the Sage is the timing. To understand the Dao, then Confucius and Laozi complement each other as beginning and end.

**Abolishing Artifice and Discarding Profit**
○ **He** said: To abolish artifice means to dispel deceit and falsehood, to disrupt the true.
To discard profit means to block the path of food, to close the doors of power.
Then thieves and robbers have no place.

○ **Emperor Minghuang** said: When man boasts of his one-sided talents, he harbors a heart of contention for profit, hence to abolish artifice means no contention, to discard profit means self-sufficiency.
Self-sufficiency, then he will not become a thief or robber.

○ **Pang** said: When artifice and profit prevail, the people’s desires grow extravagant, their basic livelihoods decay—this must lead to thievery.
These three matters—all injure the root through their end.

「River said: This refers to the three matters above that are discarded and abolished.
He deemed it insufficient in terms of **Daoist cultivation** (文).
River said: He deemed it insufficient in terms of **Daoist cultivation** because **Daoist cultivation** (文) was insufficient to guide the people.

○ **Pang** said: **Daoist cultivation** (文) is secondary to innate simplicity (質), and if the secondary prevails, it will be insufficient. This is how the people lose their true nature.
A passage mistakenly reads "文而未足" (insufficient in terms of **Daoist cultivation**), which is incorrect.
Hence, he commanded that there be proper alignment.

○ Emperor Minghuang said: These three matters must all be discarded and abolished, yet no method of **cultivation** (修行) was demonstrated. Therefore, he deemed that **Daoist cultivation** alone was insufficient to guide the people, and further commanded that there be something to adhere to—referring to the following four matters.
○ River said: It should be as in the next sentences.
○ **Pang** said: Align their true nature with the four matters below.
**Seeing simplicity and embracing purity** (見素抱朴),
River said: "Seeing simplicity" means holding fast to simplicity and upholding truth, not favoring artificial adornment. "Embracing purity" means revealing its natural simplicity, so it can be emulated.
○ **Pang** said: Not seeing external things but seeing one’s own true nature.
Simplicity (素) is the essence of nature, and man is born in stillness, unstained by all things, hence unadorned and simple.
In autumn, when the season arrives, all things return to stillness, their hue becoming white, in accordance with the **Dao** (天理).
Purity (朴) is the completeness of nature, taking purity as the foundation and outward forms as secondary.

**Few selfish concerns, little desire** (少私寡欲).
○ Emperor Minghuang said: Reveal the true simplicity, embrace genuine purity, reduce selfish inclinations and evil, and curb excessive cravings and demands.
○ River said: "Few selfish concerns" means to rectify and eliminate selfishness. "Little desire" means to know contentment.
○ **Bi** said: **Sagely wisdom** (聖智) is the excellence of talent (才). **Virtue and righteousness** (仁義) are the excellence of humanity (人). **Skill and profit** (巧利) are the excellence of practical application (用).
Yet it simply states "abolish," making the **Daoist cultivation** (文) seem insufficient. Without proper alignment, its intent cannot be seen. Hence, he said that these three matters were deemed insufficient in terms of **Daoist cultivation**, and so commanded that there be something to adhere to, aligning them with **simplicity, purity, and few desires**.

○ **Pang** said: Selfish concerns and desires are all actions of the self. Having a self means having external attachments, and if there are attachments, the foundation is lost.

**Chapter Twenty: Abolishing Learning and Having No Worries** (絶學無憂)
**Abolishing Learning,**」

「**River** said: To **abolish learning** (絶學) is not true; it does not align with the **Daoist cultivation** (道文).
**No worries** (無憂).

**Emperor Minghuang** said: To **abolish virtue/ren** (絶仁) is to reject **worldly learning** (俗學), thus preserving **primordial simplicity and purity** (淳朴). Few desires and little selfishness (少私寡欲) are why there are **no worries**.

○ **River** said: To eliminate **superficiality** (浮華) is to remove **worries and afflictions** (憂患).

○ **Xiu** said: Learning is a means to seek **returning to the primordial state** (復命). When one reaches the **primordial state**, there is no perception of **phenomena** (物), and thus no need for learning. When one attains the **Dao** in this manner, one avoids both **auspicious and inauspicious events** (吉凶), so who could have **worries**?

**Confucius** said: **Take joy in Heaven and know the Mandate** (樂天知命).
The greatest **worry** is having **knowledge and joy**; if so, it is not in accordance with the **Dao**.

How far apart are **"Yes" and "No"** (唯之與阿)? **River** said: Though they are both **responses** (應對), how far apart are they? The **Dao** (文) is swift yet **base in quality** (疾時賤質), while **worldly learning** (文) is slow yet **noble in form** (貴文).
How different are **good and evil** (善之與惡)?

**Emperor Minghuang** said: **"Yes"** (唯) is **respectful response**; **"No"** (阿) is **compliant response**. Though they come from the same mouth, they are said to differ greatly. **Respectful response** is good, while **compliant response** is evil—this illustrates **worldly learning**. To **abolish** (絶) is to have **no worries**; not to **abolish** is to invite **afflictions**. It depends on the **mind’s self-illumination** (心識回照), so how could they be far apart?

○ **River** said: Those who do good seek **praise** (稱譽), while those who do evil engage in **contention** (練爭). How different can they be? The **Dao** despises **flattery** (忠直) and employs **crafty sycophants** (邪佞).

○ **Pan** said: The **essence of the Dao** (道體) is fundamentally one. Yet when it descends to **perfection** (全), it further divides into **yin and yang** (陰陽), giving rise to **turbulence and strife** (扰扰起矣). All these distinctions stem from **delusive mind** (妄心). Illuminated by **Daoist truth** (道真), they are like **dreams and illusions** (梦幻).

Moreover, **"Yes" and "No"** (唯之與阿) both emerge from the mouth—**originally without distinction** (元無異狀)—yet the world perceives **"Yes"** as **respectful** (恭) and **"No"** as **compliant** (慢). From this arise **prejudice and anger** (是非喜怒), and **delusion and attachment** (情). If one seeks how they differ, what meaning could there be? **Good and evil** are also thus divided.

What people **fear**, one must **fear**.
**Emperor Minghuang** said: What ordinary people **fear** are **complacency** (慢) and **evil** (惡). What **virtuous individuals** (善士) **fear** are **worldly learning** (俗學) and **action-driven existence** (有為). Both must be **abolished**, hence one must **fear** them.

○ **River** said: A **Daoist practitioner** (道人) fears a ruler who does not **abolish learning** (不絶學之君). One must **fear** such rulers, for they **pursue fame and glory** (令色), **slaying the virtuous and the wise** (殺仁賢).

○ **Bi** said: The lower section states that **learners increase** (為學者日益), while **Daoists decrease** (為道者日损). Thus, **learners seek increase**, sharpening their **talent** (智) through what they can acquire. If one already has **few desires and contentment** (無欲而足), why seek **increase**? If one **does not know yet attains** (不知而中), why seek **progress**?

The **swallow and sparrow** have their **matches** (匹), while the **dove and pigeon** have their **enemies** (仇). Even **cold-region dwellers** (寒乡之民) must know of **fine furs** (旃裘)—this is **natural sufficiency** (自然已足). To **add to it** (益之) is to invite **worries**. Thus, **lengthening a hare’s legs** (續兔之足) is no different from **severing a crane’s wings** (截鶴之經). To **fear praise and seek advancement** (畏誉而進) is akin to **applying unusual punishments** (異刑).

**"Yes" and "No"** (唯阿) are **good and evil** (美恶)—how different are they? Thus, what people **fear**, I also **fear**, yet I dare not **rely on them** (恃之以为用).

○ **Pan** said: The **perfected sage** (至人) sees **no single thing** (一物), and thus **good and evil** (善悪) have no place. Yet they do not **discard** (廢) the world’s laws of **good and evil**, but simply **cast them off without burden** (洒然不累).

From *"How far apart are they?"* (自相去何若) onward, it clarifies that the **mind is unburdened** (無累). The **unburdened** are **originally not different from the world** (本不自異於世), hence all distinctions are shared with the people—**auspicious and inauspicious events** (吉凶) **breathe with the people** (與民同息).

Could the **sage** (聖人) **abolish burdens** (絶累) and **forget form** (忘形), thus **resting** (息)?

Yet the *I Ching* (《易》) has **auspicious and inauspicious symbols** (吉凶之象) because it **follows the people’s inclinations** (因民情).

**Zhuangzi** said: **Do not neglect humanity** (不忽於人), yet the **Dao encompasses both forgetting and being forgotten** (道既兼忘). It seems one should **neglect worldly affairs** (忽人事), but in truth, there is **no neglect**.

How **vast and boundless** (荒兮其未央) is the **Dao**!

**Emperor Minghuang** said: If one does not **fear abolishing worldly learning** (不畏絶俗學), then **all beings’ true nature** (眾生正性) will be **wasted and neglected** (荒废), and there will be **no end to it** (未有央止之時).

○ **River** said: Some argue that **ordinary people** (世俗人) are **chaotic and disordered** (荒乱), and thus they **pursue learning and cultivation** (進學為文) to **halt this chaos** (未央止).

○ **Bi** said: **Lamenting** the **distance between the Dao and worldly ways** (叹与俗相返之遠).

○ **Pan** said: **All transformations** (万化) **propel each other** (相推), yet **end in beginning** (終則有始), **eternally generating** (生生無盡)—how could there be an **end** (央)? Yet **worldly people** (世人) **fixate on phenomena** (物物), **judging them as good or evil** (為之美惡). Is this not **delusion** (惑)?

Only the **sage** (聖人) can **know the unity of all ages** (万古一時), **without gain or loss** (無得失) in their midst.

The **multitude** (眾人) **hurry and scurry** (熙熙),
**River** said: **Hurrying and scurrying** (熙熙) is **licentiousness and indulgence** (淫放), **overflowing with desires** (多情欲).

Like **feasting on the grandest sacrificial offerings** (享太牢),
**River** said: Like the **hungry longing for the finest sacrificial meats** (如饥思太牢之具), their **intentions are never satisfied** (意無足時).

Like **springtime ascending a viewing platform** (如春登台).
**Emperor Minghuang** said: The **multitude** (眾人) with **worldly learning** (俗學) and **action-driven existence** (有為) **hurry after sensory realms** (熙熙逐境), like **approaching a grand feast** (如享太牢) or **springtime ascending a viewing platform** (春台登望), **every movement sparks greed** (動生貪欲).

○ **River** said: **Spring** (春) is when **yin and yang harmonize** (陰陽交通), and **all things respond** (万物感動). To **ascend a platform** (登台) and **observe it** is to let **intentions run wild** (意志淫淫然).

○ **Bi** said: The **multitude** (眾人) are **bewildered by beauty and advancement** (迷於美進), **confused by honor and profit** (惑於榮利), and their **desires compete** (欲進心競). Hence, they **hurry like feasting on grand sacrifices** (熙熙若享太牢) and **ascend platforms in springtime** (如春登台).

○ **Pan** said: **Creation** (造化) is **boundless** (无极), and **things continuously arise** (事物日生). Only the **sage** (聖人) can **unify past and present** (一古今) **without change** (無變). The **multitude** (眾人) **follow transformations** (随化而迁), and upon seeing **good or evil** (美惡), they **take delight in life** (樂得其生), **considering it beautiful** (自以为美).

**Spring** (春) is when **all things emit vibrant sounds** (万物音張), **departing from stillness** (離静) and **beginning to move** (而動), a time when they **lose their root** (去本) and **chase superficiality** (逐末).

A **platform** (台) is a place from which one can **view distant realms** (远览诸境). Those who **do not grasp ultimate truth** (不明乎至理) all **follow phenomena** (逐物), **generating emotions** (生情)—such is their state.

**I alone remain tranquil** (我独怕兮), **yet no sign of emotion** (其未兆).
**River** said: **I alone remain tranquil** (我独怕), **serene and undisturbed** (安静), **with no trace of desire** (未有情欲之形兆), like an **infant before it becomes playful** (如婴儿之未孩).

**Emperor Minghuang** said: **I alone remain tranquil** (我独怕然安静), **with no form or trace of desire** (於其情欲，略無形兆), like that **infant** (彼婴儿), **not yet able to act childishly** (未能孩孺).

○ **River** said: Like a **child unable to answer a doll** (未能答偶人時).

○ **Bi** said: **I am vast and empty** (廓然), **with no shape to name** (無形之可名) and **no sign to point at** (無兆之可舉), like an **infant before it becomes playful** (如婴儿未能孩).

○ **Pan** said: **My nature is tranquil and unperturbed** (性体怕然不萌智虑), **harboring no stirring of cleverness or calculation** (含和守一), **in harmony with that infant** (同於婴儿).
The ancient text reads *"廓兮"* (kuò xī), but this is incorrect. *"怕"* (pà) and *"泊"* (bó) are the same; *"怕"* means **still and unchanging** (不流之义), while *"廓"* (kuò) is merely the name for **vast emptiness** (虚大之名). Examining the text’s meaning, it should follow *"怕"* (pà). **I drift, drifting** (乘乘兮若无所归).

**Emperor Minghuang** said: **The Perfect One has no heart** (至人无心), **moving with things yet taking nothing** (运动随物无所取与), like a **traveler with no destination** (若行者之无所归).
*"乘乘"* (chéngchéng) is the **form of motion** (运动貌).

○ **River** said: **I drift, drifting like a remote and desolate place** (我乘乘如穷鄙), **with no destination to seek** (无所归就).

○ **Bi** said: **Like one with nowhere to dwell** (若无所宅).

○ **Pan** said: **Drifting** (乘乘) means **riding along with all transformations yet without selfishness** (乘万物之变而不自私), thus **like one with no destination** (故若无所归). **It simply adapts to change** (盖唯变所适), **with no fixed direction** (无所向着).
One text reads *"儡儡兮无所归"* (lěilěi xī wú suǒ guī), but this is incorrect.

**The common people all have excess** (众人皆有余),
**Emperor Minghuang** said: **They indulge in dusty affairs** (耽嗜尘务), **boast of cleverness and skill** (矜夸巧智), **and claim to have abundance** (自为有余), **to display their brilliance** (以示光大).

○ **River** said: **The common people use excess wealth for luxury** (众人余财以为奢), **and excess wisdom for vain debate** (余智以为许).

○ **Bi** said: **They survey all things with pride** (矜览庶物), **so they always have excess** (故常有余).

But **I alone seem abandoned** (而我独若遗).
**Emperor Minghuang** said: **I am always as if lacking** (常若不足), **with some forgotten intent** (有所遗志).

○ **River** said: **I alone seem like discarded goods** (我独如遗弃似不足).

○ **Bi** said: **The common people have no heart without desires or aspirations** (众人无不有怀有志), **overflowing their chests** (盈溢胸心), **so it is said they all have excess** (故日皆有余).
**I alone am vast and empty, without action or desire** (我独廓然无为无欲), **as if lost** (若遗失之也).

○ **Bi** said: **I transcend yet self-destroy** (超然自丧), **not claiming what I possess** (不有其有).
**I am indeed the heart of the foolish** (我愚人之心也哉).

○ **River** said: **I do not follow the common people** (不与俗人相随), **steadfastly guarding the One** (守一不移), **like the heart of the foolish** (如愚人之心).

○ **Bi** said: **I sever the heart of the foolish** (绝愚人之心), **making no distinctions** (无所别析), **my intentions free of likes or desires** (意无所好欲), **yet my true nature remains unseen** (犹然其情不可睹), **thus I appear so abandoned** (我颓然若此).

**Pure, pure** (纯纯兮).
**Emperor Minghuang** said: **Am I truly the heart of the foolish** (我岂愚人之心), **abandoned like this** (遗忘若此哉)?
**My heart is simply pure** (但我心纯纯), **so it seems abandoned** (故若遗尔).

○ **River** said: **Without distinctions** (无所分别).

○ **Bi** said: **No distinctions to name** (无所别析，不可为名).

○ **Bi** said: **Pure, pure like the foolish yet the utmost of true wisdom** (纯纯兮似愚而真智之极). One text reads *"沌沌"* (dùndùn) instead of *"纯纯"* (chéngchéng).
The common people are **bright and clear** (俗人昭昭),
**Emperor Minghuang** said: **They boast of cleverness** (矜巧智).

○ **River** said: **They are clear and discerning** (明且达).

○ **Bi** said: **They flaunt their brilliance** (耀其光).

But **I alone seem dim** (我独若昏).
**Emperor Minghuang** said: **I conceal myself in obscurity** (自韬晦).

○ **River** said: **Like darkness and confusion** (如暗昧).

○ **Pan** said: **I forget both things and self** (物我兼忘), **not clinging to distinctions** (不主分别), **so I seem dim** (故若昏).

The common people are **sharp and keen** (俗人察察),
**Emperor Minghuang** said: **They establish laws and institutions** (立法制).

○ **River** said: **Sharp and keen means hasty and urgent** (察察，急且疾).

○ **Bi** said: **Distinctions mean separate analysis** (分别，别析).

○ **Bi** said: **They apply the bright and clear intent to governance** (推昭昭之意加之政), **and thus become sharp and keen** (则察察矣).

But **I alone am dull and slow** (我独闷闷).
**Emperor Minghuang** said: **I am only broad and magnanimous** (唯宽大).

○ **River** said: **Dull and slow means no cutting or severing** (闷闷无所割截).

○ **Bi** said: **They apply the dim intent to governance** (推若昏之意加之政), **and thus become broad and magnanimous, dull and slow** (则宽大闷闷然矣).
**Dull and slow means not seeking petty pleasures of the day** (闷闷谓不取日前小快).

**Vague, like the sea** (澹兮其若海).
