○ **River** said: **I alone drift, drifting like the flowing of rivers and seas** (我独忽忽如江海之流), **no one knows their ultimate end** (莫知其所穷极).

○ **Bi** said: **Emotions are unseen** (情不可睹).

○ **Bi** said: **From tranquil yet unmanifest** (自怕兮未兆) to the arising of all things, **now it returns to the original nature** (而下有诸事物，及此又归於性本), **deep and vast like the sea’s accumulated waters** (湛然深广), **the nature of water complete** (水性全矣).

One text reads *"忽若晦"* (hū ruò huì), but this is incorrect.

**Gushing, like having no end** (飂兮似无所止).
**Emperor Minghuang** said: **My appearance is suddenly dim** (容貌忽然若昏晦), **yet my heart is serene** (而心寂兮), **cut off from worldly learning** (绝於俗学), **as if having no fixed end** (似无所止着).

○ **River** said: **I alone am light and airy** (我独漂漂), **like flying or rising** (若飞若扬), **with no end** (无所止), **my aspirations in the realm of the spirit** (志意在神域).

○ **Bi** said: **Without attachments** (无所系系).

○ **Pan** said: **Gushing like the sea** (飂兮若海), **speaking of the nature** (以言性体), **this then speaks of its function** (此则言其用), **rustling without obstruction** (飕然无所不通), **yet without fixation** (而无所系着), **what else could it be but its function** (非用而何).
One text reads *"寂"* (jì) instead of *"飂"* (liú), but this is incorrect.

**The common people all have purposes** (众人皆有以),

「Emperor Minghuang said: The common people, in the course of their lives, all have their own purposes, following the intentions of worldly learning and sensory experiences.

○ River said: *With purpose* (以), it means *to act with intention* (有为).

○ Bi said: *With purpose* (以), it means *to employ function* (用), all desiring to apply themselves to some use.

○ Pan said: When things are used by things, then they serve as *things* (物用). When things are used without being *used by things* (物於物), then they employ *things* (用物), yet nothing can truly *employ things* (物). But I alone am *unrefined* (顽).

○ River said: I alone am *without intention* (无为).

This seems *vulgar* (似鄙). Emperor Minghuang said: The *unrefined* (顽) means *without discrimination* (无分别), the *vulgar* (鄙) means *coarse and insufficient* (陋不足), yet my heart truly *enlightened and comprehends* (了悟). Though outwardly it may seem insufficient, hence the saying *like this* (似尔).

○ River said: The vulgar seems as if it does not reach (似若不逮).

○ Bi said: Without any desire to act, in a *stifled and dim state* (闷闷昏昏), as if recognizing nothing, hence the saying *I am unrefined and vulgar* (顽且鄙).

○ Pan said: Nothing can enter me, this may be called *unrefined* (顽). My perception seems as if I do not see, this may be called *vulgar* (鄙). One version reads *seems* (似) instead of *and* (且), which is also acceptable.

I alone differ from others.

○ Emperor Minghuang said: Others have *emotions and desires* (情欲), but I have no *attachment or defilement* (爱染). Others oppose the *Dao/Way* (道), but I align with it.

○ River said: I alone differ from others.

Moreover, I cherish *nourishment from the Mother* (食母).

○ Emperor Minghuang said: *Nourishment from the Mother* (求食於母) means to value oneself like an infant without need of external sustenance. Hence, the earlier passage says *like an infant before it can speak* (如婴儿之未孩), and the later passage says *thick with virtue* (含德之厚), comparing it to the *innocent child* (赤子). This is how I alone differ from others. The words *without seeking* (先无求) and *at both* (於两) were originally absent but are now added.

Furthermore, the *Sages* (圣人) in explaining the *scriptures* (经), originally did not avoid taboo words, but in teaching, there may be suspicions. The *principles* (理) are clear and the *original intent* (故义) cannot be altered, yet when facing the text, the phrasing must be stable and convenient. It is better to retain the ancient form while adapting to the present text.

Again, Sima Qian said: Laozi spoke over five thousand words, then *clarified principles and ceased speaking* (明理诣而息言), not establishing a fixed limit of five thousand words.

○ Bi said: *Nourishment from the Mother* (食母) is the *source of life* (生之本). All people abandon the *source of life* (生民之本), valuing only the *unadorned essence* (未饰之华), hence the saying *I alone desire to differ from others* (我独欲异於人).

○ Pan said: It is nothing but *pursuing things for nourishment* (逐物) and *taking sustenance from the Dao/Way* (取养於道), for the *Dao/Way* is the *Mother of all things* (万物之母). One version lacks the words *without seeking* (无求) and *at both* (於两), which is also acceptable.

Chapter Twenty-One: The Appearance of *Kongde* (孔德之容)

「The Yellow River said: *Kong* (孔) means vast. One with vast *virtue/power* (德) is boundless in all things, able to receive filth and turbidity, and dwell in humility. Only the Dao/Way is what one follows.

Emperor Ming Huang said: *Kong* (孔) means extreme. *From* (从) means to comply. If one asks how the *extreme virtue/power* (甚有德) person appears, it is said that their conduct follows only the Dao/Way of utmost emptiness.

○ The Yellow River said: *Unique* (唯) means alone. One with vast *virtue/power* (德) does not conform to the customs of the world but follows only the Dao/Way alone.

○ Bi said: *Kong* (孔) means void. Only by embracing voidness as *virtue/power* (德) can one then truly act and follow the Dao/Way.

○ Xiu said: *Virtue/power* (德) refers to the Dao/Way within oneself. When *virtue/power* (德) reaches its fullness, it merges with the Dao/Way, which cannot be contained. It manifests through *virtue/power*, which is selfless, and nothing but following the Dao/Way. The Dao/Way, as a phenomenon, is *vague and indistinct* (恍惚).

Emperor Ming Huang said: This describes the Dao/Way that the *Kongde* (孔德) person follows—neither having nor lacking, its essence is boundless and difficult to name, hence the saying *vague and indistinct* (恍惚).

○ The Yellow River said: The Dao/Way, in relation to all things, is *vague and indistinct* (恍惚), coming and going without fixed form.

○ Xiu said: The Dao/Way encompasses yin and yang. The subtlety of yin and yang—whether present or absent—is called *vague and indistinct* (恍惚). *Sudden and vague* (忽兮恍), within it there is form. The Yellow River said: The Dao/Way is *vague and indistinct* (恍惚) without shape, yet alone it serves as the model for all things.

○ Xiu said: One yin and one yang constitute form. Another version reads: *Sudden and indistinct* (忽兮恍兮). *Indistinct and sudden* (恍兮忽), within it there are things. Emperor Ming Huang said: *Things* (物) refers to the Dao/Way as a phenomenon mentioned above. From existence to return to nonexistence, it reverts to the Dao/Way, hence the saying *within it there are things* (其中有物).

○ The Yellow River said: The Dao/Way is *indistinct and sudden* (恍忽), within it there is one—nurturing transformation and creation, establishing *substance* (质) through *qi* (气).

○ Bi said: The Dao/Way begins things without form, yet does not cling to their formation. All things arise and are formed through it, yet they do not know their source, hence the saying: *indistinct and vague* (恍兮惚兮), *vague and indistinct* (惚兮恍兮), within it there are forms (象).

○ Pan said: One yang and one yin constitute things. *Vague and indistinct* (恍惚) refers to the subtlety of yin and yang, hence their ability to transform and create forms. Another version reads: *indistinct and sudden* (恍兮忽兮).

*Deep and dark* (窈兮冥兮), within it there is essence (精).

Emperor Ming Huang said: *Vague and indistinct* (恍惚) encompasses having and lacking, while *deep and dark* (窈冥) is unfathomable. Yet the essence of creation is exquisitely preserved.

○ The Yellow River said: The Dao/Way is *deep and dark* (窈冥), formless, yet within it there is essence (精)—the true brilliance of *qi* (气) and spirit (神) intermingling, where yin and yang converge.

○ Bi said: *Deep and dark* (窈冥) is the profound and distant deception. Profound and distant, yet unseen, yet all things arise from it. What is seen confirms its truth, hence the saying: *deep and dark* (窈兮冥兮), within it there is essence (精).

○ Pan said: Essence (精) is the beginning of things’ creation. Earlier, it was called *form* (象) of things—the Dao/Way’s concrete manifestation. Once the Dao/Way’s essence is complete, it generates myriad classes. The source of all things lies in the profound and unfathomable, hence the saying: *deep and dark* (窈兮冥兮).

Its essence is most true.

○ The Yellow River said: This speaks of the Dao/Way’s essence, *qi* (气), and spirit being profoundly true, without adornment.

○ Pan said: Essence (精) is the beginning of form. Essence is inherently true, yet it is further said to be *most true* (甚真) because things may lose their principles and essence, sinking into human artifice.

Within it there is trust (信).

Emperor Ming Huang said: The nature of *deep and dark* (窈冥) is fundamentally unadulterated. When things respond, they must do so, and their responses are never flawed, hence the saying *within it there is trust* (其中有信).

○ The Yellow River said: The Dao/Way conceals its function and hides its name; its trust lies within.

○ Bi said: Trust (信) means reliability. When things return to *deep and dark* (窈冥), the utmost essence is attained, and the nature of all things is fixed, hence the saying: *its essence is most true* (其精甚真), *within it there is trust* (其中有信).

○ Pan said: The essence of *deep and dark* (窈冥) generates myriad classes, yet each thing’s creation follows its own nature and measure, adhering to principles and forms. Once there is order and regulation, one can foresee without error, hence this is called trust (信).

*Zhuangzi* says: The formless has its portion. From ancient times to the present, its name does not vanish.

Emperor Ming Huang said: This refers to the Dao/Way, which from ancient times to the present generates all things. Things obtain their use from the Dao/Way, and their names are established through application. The use of creation, being the same from past to present, follows the Dao/Way’s application, hence its name does not vanish from ancient times to the present.

The Dao/Way is not thus.

○ Bi said: This is what was said above—how do I know that the beginning of all things is from nonexistence? It is known thus.

○ Pan said: The Dao/Way endures eternally, yet myriad beauties do not endure. Thus, it can observe all transformations. The sage knows that myriad beauties do not endure eternally because they embody the Dao/Way’s eternal constancy.

○ The Yellow River said: *Self* (自) means to follow (从). From ancient times to the present, the Dao/Way follows eternally.

○ Bi said: The utmost truth cannot be named. Without a name, that is its name. From ancient times to the present, all things arise from it, hence the saying: *from ancient times to the present, its name does not vanish* (自今及古，其名不去).

○ Pan said: The Dao/Way endures eternally, yet its manifestations change ceaselessly. It observes myriad beginnings.

Emperor Ming Huang said: *Observe* (阅) means to measure and discern. *Beginnings* (甫) means origin. This speaks of the utmost Dao/Way’s application—measuring and discerning the origins of myriad things, each fulfilling its own use of creation.

○ The Yellow River said: *Observe* (阅) means to receive. *Beginnings* (甫) means origin. This speaks of the Dao/Way bestowing and all things originating, receiving *qi* (气) from the Dao/Way.

○ Bi said: *Myriad beginnings* (众甫) are the origins of things. The Dao/Way is described as the origin of all things without a name.

○ Pan said: *Beginnings* (甫) means beauty. *Zhuangzi* says: *The divine and mysterious becomes foul and rotten, and the foul and rotten becomes divine and mysterious.* The myriad beauties of things shift and change without constancy, yet the Dao/Way endures eternally, hence its ability to observe all of them.

*Observe* (阅) is like *observing many people* (阅人多矣) in *Zhuangzi*.」

「How do I know that myriad beginnings are thus?
The Yellow River said: *How do I know?* It is because all things receive *qi* from the Dao/Way. *This* is the reason.

The Sage Emperor said: *This* is because today all things receive the Dao/Way, giving rise to wondrous transformations and creation, hence it is so.

○ The Yellow River said: *This* is *today*—because today all things obtain the refined *qi* of the Dao/Way and thus come to life, moving, acting, and dwelling. It is not that I know *how* the beginnings of all things arise from the formless; I simply *know* it.

○ Pan said: The Dao/Way endures eternally, yet myriad beauties are transient. Thus, it can observe all transformations. The Sage, therefore, knows that myriad beauties are transient because he embodies the Dao/Way’s eternal constancy.

---
*Qu then full, Chapter Twenty-Two*
*Qu then full*,

The Sage Emperor said: *Bend yourself to respond to duties*, then you are whole.

○ The Yellow River said: *Bend yourself to follow others*, do not assert your own views, then you are whole.

○ Bi said: *Do not reveal your own brilliance*, then you are whole.

○ [Another voice] said: The Perfect One is empty and void, his actions flow like water. He holds no intent toward things, yet follows their transformations. He does not contend with things—who could harm him? Thus, he remains ever whole.

The essence of this chapter aligns with *Zhuangzi’s* “Cultivating Life in the Way of Perfect Nourishment” (*Zhuangzi·Xiu Yangsheng Zhu*), *crookedness then straightness*.」

**Chapter Twenty-Two**
*Qu then full*,

The Sage Emperor said: *Bend yourself to fulfill others*, then you are upright.

○ The Yellow River said: *Bend and yield yourself* to fulfill others, and over time, you will naturally find uprightness.

○ Bi said: *Do not insist on your own correctness*, then your correctness will shine forth.

○ Pan said: *In form, you bend*; *in principle, you stand upright*. Those who cling to self will grasp their own uprightness when encountering things, yet fail to recognize the boundless transformations of things—*their uprightness becomes crooked*. Only by following the crookedness of things without holding to your own uprightness will *principle stand upright within you*.

One manuscript reads *直* (upright) as *正* (correct).

*Hollow then full*,
The Sage Emperor said: *Hold to humility and virtue*, then you will always be full.

○ The Yellow River said: The earth is hollow and low, so water flows to it. A person who is humble and low will have virtue bestowed upon them.

○ Bi said: *Do not self-praise*, then your achievements will naturally be recognized.

○ Pan said: By being humble and low like a marsh, all waters converge upon it. *Thus, it is ever full.*

*Worn then renewed*,
The Sage Emperor said: *Guard against defects and thinness*, then you will daily renew yourself.

○ The Yellow River said: *Receive defects and thinness upon yourself*, placing others before yourself—*the world will honor you*, and over time, you will naturally renew.

○ Bi said: *Do not boast of yourself*, then your virtue will endure.

○ Pan said: Without the withering of autumn and winter, there would be no splendor of spring and summer.

*Little then gain*,
The Sage Emperor said: *Hold to the One without separation*, then you will suffer no loss.

○ The Yellow River said: *Receive little upon yourself*, then you will gain much.

*The Dao/Way favors humility; the divine spirit entrusts itself to emptiness.*

○ Pan said: The Dao lies in subtlety; nature abides in simplicity. Those who attain it grasp its root, *thus they do not seek much*.

To embody the four matters above, only those who grasp the One can achieve it.

*Much then confusion*,
The Sage Emperor said: *If you have many actions*, then confusion and disorder arise.

○ The Yellow River said: Those with much wealth are confused by what they must guard; those with much learning are confused by what they must hear.

○ Bi said: The Way of nature is like a tree—*the more branches it turns out, the fewer its roots*. *The more it turns toward its roots*, the more it attains its essence.

*Much then distances from the true*, hence it is called *confusion*.
*Little then attains its essence*, hence it is called *gain*.

The Sage holds to the One as a model for all under heaven.

The Sage Emperor said: The Sage holds fast to the pure and undivided One, *thus he can serve as the law and model for all under heaven*.

○ The Yellow River said: He holds fast to the law and model.

The Sage knows all things by guarding the One, *thus he can serve as the law and model for all under heaven*.

○ Bi said: The One is the extreme of little. *Model* is akin to *standard*.

○ Xiu said: Those who hold to the One do not stray from its essence. *Only by holding to the One without division* can one embody the Dao and exhaust nature, achieving *mystical unity with all things*—*thus he can respond without end*.

This sentence follows the Dao’s principle from above: The Sage, by holding to the One, can perceive this principle, hence his actions align with what follows below.

*Do not reveal yourself, then your virtue is manifest*,
The Sage Emperor said: *If a person does not reveal his own virtue*, yet constantly bends himself to fulfill duties, then his virtue will be fully manifest.

○ The Yellow River said: The Sage does not claim to see beyond a thousand li, but instead uses the eyes of all under heaven to see—*thus he can manifest and reach far*.

○ Pan said: *If you reveal yourself*, then you have a self. Having a self, you will be worn down by it—*thus you cannot bend to respond to transformations*.

*Do not insist on your own correctness, then it will shine forth*,
The Sage Emperor said: *If a person does not insist on his own correctness*, yet bends himself to fulfill others, then his correctness will naturally shine forth.

○ The Yellow River said: The Sage does not insist on his own correctness to contradict others—*thus he can manifest and be displayed in the world*.

○ Pan said: Without insisting on your own correctness, you can bend to follow principle—*without seeking display, it will shine forth naturally*.

*Do not self-praise, then you have achievement*,
The Sage Emperor said: *If a person does not self-praise or take for himself*, then his achievements will naturally belong to him.

○ The Yellow River said: *Praise* is *taking*. The Sage’s virtue and transformation flow through all under heaven—*he does not take credit for his beauty*, thus he has achievement for all under heaven.

○ Pan said: *Praise* arises from arrogance and false pretension. Knowing that *hollowness is fullness*, how could there be the burden of arrogance and false pretension?

*Do not boast of yourself, then your virtue endures*,
The Sage Emperor said: *If a person can long guard against defects and thinness*, and not boast of himself, then others will esteem his enduring virtue.

○ The Yellow River said: *Boast* means *to magnify*. The Sage does not magnify himself—*thus he can endure without danger for long*.

○ Pan said: *Boasting* means *displaying his splendor*. Displaying splendor leads to weariness, *yet only by enduring weariness can one renew*.

*Only by not contending does one avoid contention under heaven.*

The Sage Emperor said: *If you do not contend with things*, who will contend with you?

○ The Yellow River said: This means that all under heaven—whether virtuous or unworthy—*cannot contend with those who do not contend*.

○ Pan said: *Only by being empty and void, not solid*, and having no intent toward things, will things desire to possess you yet fail—*how much more could they contend with you?*

The essence of this chapter lies in *forgetting the self*, hence it concludes with *not contending* and begins and ends with *qu then full*.

The ancients spoke of *qu then full*—was this not a true saying?

The Yellow River said: The ancients transmitted that *bending and following leads to wholeness*—*this is not false or baseless*.

*If truly whole, return to it.*

The Sage Emperor said: The ancients spoke of *qu then full*—was this not a true saying? *If one truly bends and follows*, he will grasp the principle and return to it.

○ The Yellow River said: *Sincerity* is *reality*. Those who can practice bending and following are *real in their body and substance*—*they return to their parents*, suffering no harm.

○ Pan said: *Bending* means *emptying oneself to follow principle*, letting things transform while not contending with them. All the sayings above converge on this: *If truly whole, return to it*—*this is the meaning of the butcher Ding’s skill with the knife yet hiding it*. I dare say this chapter’s essence lies in *nourishing life*.

The Sage’s approach to life does not rely on self-nourishment, *yet none nourish life better than he*.
The sayings *do not reveal yourself, do not insist on your own correctness, do not self-praise, do not boast*—*are not about suppressing the self to achieve wholeness*, but rather *an innate, effortless understanding*.

The **Daoist Classic of the Mysterious Pivot** (with collected annotations, Volume 3)
#1 Greed: [Original: "贫" (*pín*, "poverty"), amended from the Daoist Canon’s Tang Xuānzōng’s imperial annotation.]

**Volume 4** – *Annotations by Emperor Mínghuáng, Hé Shànggōng, Wáng Bì, and Wáng Piān*
**Chapter 23: The Dao of Effortless Speech** (*Xīyán zìrán*)

*"Effortless speech."* Emperor Mínghuáng said: *"Effortless"* means *"to transcend speech."* Yet it does not say *"forget speech"* but *"effortless"*—for the Dao is elucidated through speech, and speech cannot be entirely forgotten. When one awakens to the Dao, speech is forgotten, hence *"effortless."*

If one can grasp the Dao through speech without being bound by it, then one attains the natural.

○ Hé Shànggōng said: *"Effortless speech"* means *"to cherish speech."* Cherishing speech is the Dao of the natural.

○ Wáng Bì said: *"Speech that is heard yet not named"* is called *effortless*—the next chapter speaks of the Dao’s utterance: *"dim and flavorless"* (*dàn xī qí wú wèi*), *"seen yet indistinct"* (*shì zhī bù zú jiàn*), *"heard yet not fully audible"* (*tīng zhī bù zú wén*). Thus, speech that is *"dim and flavorless"* yet still heard is the *ultimate effortless speech* of the natural.

○ Wáng Piān said: *"Effortless"* shares the same meaning as *"heard yet not named"* in the previous chapter—*the natural* does not arise from things yet is not bound by them. *Effortless* does not align with things yet remains unaffected by them; it stands alone above all phenomena, hence it is natural.

Thus, gusting winds do not last a morning,
sudden rains do not last a day.

Emperor Mínghuáng said: *"Gusting winds and sudden rains"* bring ruin to the harvests and harm to things; swift speech and stubborn adherence bring loss of the Dao and delusion.

○ Hé Shànggōng said: *"Gusting winds"* are swift winds, *"sudden rains"* are torrential rains. Swift winds cannot endure, torrential rains cannot last.

○ Wáng Piān said: *"Gusting winds and sudden rains"* are the work of *yin-yang interaction*. Their swiftness and violence are the excess of interaction, hence they cannot endure. Who does this? *The heavens and the earth.*

「Hé said: *Existence* (执) is a matter of *who*—who causes the gusting winds and torrential rains? It is the heavens and the earth.
The heavens and the earth, even in their vastness, when they act violently and abruptly, harm things and cannot endure.
Hé said: They do not last until morning or evening. How much less can mortals endure?

Emperor Mínghuáng said: The heavens and the earth are boundless in their *virtue/power* (神), yet when they act violently and abruptly, they cannot last until morning or evening. How much more so for mortals who seek to do so?

*Stubborn adherence* (执) to words and *rigid fixation* on teachings lead to harm in the Dao.
Those who eagerly seek sudden enlightenment, can they truly attain it?

Hé said: When mortals act violently and abruptly, their deeds rise swiftly yet cannot endure.
Thus, those who engage in the Dao should not stubbornly adhere to words or rigidly fixate on teachings.

Emperor Mínghuáng said: Those who embody the Dao forget words, and thus align with the Dao.

Hé said: *Dao-practitioners* (道者) are those who love the Dao. To align with the Dao means their actions harmonize with it.

Bì said: *Engaging in the Dao* (从事) refers to movements and actions undertaken in the Dao.
The Dao accomplishes all things through *effortless non-action* (无形无为), so those who engage in the Dao should take *non-action* (无为) as their sovereign.
They should not speak as if teaching—if they do so even faintly, things will attain their true nature.
Thus, they align with the Dao in essence, and it is said: *to align with the Dao* (同於道).

Hóng said: The original text had no mention of *Dao-practitioners* (道者). *Virtue-practitioners* (德者) align with *virtue/power* (德).

Emperor Mínghuáng said: *Virtue/power* (德) is the name for the Dao’s manifestation. If mortals embody the Dao’s intent and exert their efforts, then their deeds align with the Dao’s manifestation.

Hé said: *Virtue-practitioners* (德者) are those who love *virtue/power* (德). To align with *virtue/power* (德) means their actions harmonize with it.

Bì said: *Attainment* (得) means *sparse/little* (少). Sparse is *virtue/power* (德), hence it is called *attainment* (得). To act in *attainment* (得) is to align with *sparse/little* (少), hence it is said: *to align with attainment* (同於得).

*Loss* (失) aligns with *loss* (失).

Emperor Mínghuáng said: Stubborn adherence to words and rigid fixation on teachings leave no path to enlightenment. Without enlightenment, one falls into delusion of the Dao, thus aligning with *loss* (失).

Hé said: *Loss* (失) means to indulge oneself while neglecting others. To align with *loss* (失) is to align with what is called *loss* (失).

Bì said: *Accumulation* (累) means *many*. Many accumulations lead to *loss* (失), hence it is called *loss* (失). To act in *loss* (失) is to align with *many/accumulation* (累), hence it is said: *to align with loss* (同於失).

Xiù said: In the original text, *virtue/power* (德) was written as *attainment* (得). To align with the Dao is for the Dao to also *attain* (得) it. Hé said: Those who align with the Dao also rejoice in its *attainment* (得). To align with *virtue/power* (德) is for *virtue/power* (德) to also *attain* (得) it. Hé said: Those who align with *virtue/power* (德) also rejoice in its *attainment* (得). To align with *loss* (失) is for *loss* (失) to also *attain* (得) it. Emperor Mínghuáng said: Like holding water to distinguish its kind, or using a concave mirror to attract fire—these are ways to classify things, and the outcome can be known.

Hé said: Those who align with *loss* (失) also rejoice in their *loss* (失).

Bì said: It means to follow one’s actions accordingly, hence aligning and responding.

Hóng said: All mortals are born without needing things to exist—this is called *spontaneous transformation* (独化).
Without needing things to exist, they inherently align with *sparse/little* (希) and are natural.
Yet those who lose their nature fabricate a false self, and in engaging with the Dao, they bend themselves to conform with it.
Thus, the Dao becomes their master, and what I call their sovereign is actually submitting themselves to the Dao.
Therefore, those who engage in the Dao have these three categories—*Dao* (道), *virtue/power* (德), *loss* (失)—yet all converge in *loss* (失).
This arises from not penetrating *sparse/little* (希), establishing oneself while awaiting things, and separating unity into duality, giving rise to *yin-yang interaction* (交感) and the generation of life and death.

*Faith* (信) is insufficient.
Hé said: If the sovereign’s faith is insufficient toward the people, the people will respond with their own insufficiency toward the sovereign.
There is *unfaithfulness* (不信).

Emperor Mínghuáng said: Stubborn adherence to words and rigid fixation on teachings leave no path to enlightenment—this is insufficient faith, aligning with *loss* (失). *Loss* (失) rejoices in *loss* (失), this is *unfaithfulness* (不信).

Hé said: This speaks of things of the same kind converging, and voices of the same tone resonating—clouds follow the dragon, winds follow the tiger, water flows to the moist, fire seeks the dry. These are natural correspondences.

Bì said: If loyalty and faith are insufficient toward the people, how can there be *unfaithfulness* (不信)?

Hóng said: Descending to *sparse/little* (希) means losing the Dao’s natural order, and in faith, it is insufficient.
If the sovereign’s faith is insufficient, the people will inevitably respond with *unfaithfulness* (不信). This further clarifies that refining oneself and governing others must all experience *sparse/little* (希).

*Those who advance do not stand firm* (跂者不立), Chapter Twenty-Four.
*Those who advance* (跂者) do not stand firm.
Hé said: *Advance* (跂) means *progress* (进). If one covets authority and craves fame, seeking功 and honor, one cannot long endure in the Dao.

Bì said: Things still progress and lose stability, hence it is said: *those who advance do not stand firm* (企者不立).

*Those who straddle do not walk* (跨者不行).
Emperor Mínghuáng said: *Advance* (跂) is to lift one’s heel and gaze ahead. *Straddle* (跨) is to grasp things with one’s stride. This likens seeking clarity in oneself—yet clarity is never truly obtained. How different is it from *advancing* (跂) to seek enduring stability or *straddling* (跨) to seek movement?

Hé said: If one assumes superiority and straddles others, the multitude will obstruct them, preventing them from walking.

Hóng said: Not conforming to one’s natural form means losing the nature’s function. *Self-illumination* (自见) is not true clarity.

Emperor Mínghuáng said: *Illustrious talent* (灵才) flaunts itself, and in action, it invites blame, hence it is not true clarity.

Hé said: If one admires their own appearance and actions, believing them to be good and fitting with the Dao, they do not truly know their own ugliness and the vulgarity of their conduct. *Self-affirmation* (自是) does not shine brightly.

Emperor Mínghuáng said: *Affirming oneself while denying others* (是己非人) is simply a source of resentment, hence it does not shine brightly.

Hé said: If one affirms themselves while denying others, the multitude will obstruct them, preventing them from shining brightly.

*Self-praise* (自伐) yields no merit.
Emperor Mínghuáng said: *Exclusive rigidity* (专固) and *self-praise* (伐取) are what things do not accord with, hence no merit is achieved.

Hé said: If one praises their own deeds and claims their merits as good, they immediately lose the merit of having truly benefited others.»

「Self-conceit does not endure.
Emperor Mínghuáng said: *Self-conceit* in one’s skills and abilities is despised by people, hence it does not endure. ○ Hé said: Those who delight in their own self-conceit cannot last long. In terms of the Dao, it is called *excessive accumulation* (余食) and *superfluous conduct* (赘行).
Hé said: *Superfluous* (赘) means *greed* (贪). If such self-conceited and boastful individuals were to govern a state, their *excessive taxation and plunder* (赋敛余禄) would be a greedy deed.

○ Bì said: If we speak solely of the Dao, it is like the *abundance of a neighbor’s harvest* (邻至之行) or the *surplus of their labor* (盛撰之余). Though the foundation may be virtuous, it can become even more corrupt. Though the foundation may have merit, if one boasts of it themselves, it becomes a *blemish or superfluous burden*—thus, it is no longer meritorious.

○ Pán said: All these things are *extraneous* (分外). People may detest them.
Hé said: This person in office, every action they take seeks to harm. Hence, there is nothing that does not fear and hate them. Therefore, those who follow the Dao do not engage with such matters.

Emperor Mínghuáng said: *Self-conceit* in one’s conduct, when discussed in terms of the Dao, is called *the food of the残余* (残余之食) and *superfluous deeds* (疣赘之行). Even ordinary things may hate them, so those who follow the Dao do not involve themselves in such affairs.

○ Hé said: It means that those who follow the Dao do not reside in such a state.

**Chapter Twenty-Five: The Formless Essence (有物混成)**

There is a formless essence that arises before Heaven and Earth.
Emperor Mínghuáng said: To clarify the origin of the Dao and its naming, it is said that there is a formless essence. This essence contains all things, and when tracing its transformation, it is seen to precede Heaven and Earth.

○ Hé said: It refers to the Dao being shapeless yet forming all things. Thus, all things exist before Heaven and Earth.
○ Bì said: Though the essence is formless and cannot be grasped, all things arise from it. Hence, it is called *formless essence* (混成).

We do not know whose offspring it is, so it is said to arise before Heaven and Earth.
○ Pán said: The essence within the Dao is formless yet complete. In its view of Heaven and Earth, it is but one thing. Laozi sought to gather the scattered, chaotic, and confused world and restore it to its original nature, hence he merely raises the formless essence. Indeed, from the perspective of myriad distinctions, this is the Dao’s entirety. Yet from the Dao’s original essence, even the formless is but a scattering of distinctions. When seen as a thing, the Dao’s essence reveals its own profound mysteries.

Silent and boundless,
○ Pán said: The formless essence has no manifestation.
Standing alone yet unchanging,
Hé said: *Silent* (寂) means without sound or voice; *boundless* (寥) means empty and shapeless. *Standing alone* (独立) implies no equal or pair. *Unchanging* (不改) refers to the constant nature of the virtuous.

○ Bì said: *Silent and boundless* (寂寥) means without form or substance. It has no equal in things, hence it is called *standing alone* (独立). It returns to transformation without end, never losing its constancy, hence it is called *unchanging* (不改).

○ Pán said: The essence of the formless is constant and unchanging.
Its circulation is endless and without peril,
Hé said: The Dao flows through the earth, entering everywhere without obstruction. In the yang, it does not linger; in the yin, it does not steal. It penetrates all things, never in danger or peril.

○ Pán said: This is the Dao’s function. All things arise from it in life and death; it eternally governs their fate. Who could threaten it? It can be the *mother of all under Heaven*.

Emperor Mínghuáng said: The essence of the Dao is silent, boundless, and empty—its profound origin is constantly serene and silent. It applies universally to all things, hence its circulation is endless and without peril. All things draw life from it and receive its nurturing virtue, so it can be called the *mother of all under Heaven*.

○ Hé said: The Dao nourishes all things like a mother nurtures her child.
○ Bì said: Though it circulates everywhere without omission, it remains free from peril, and it can generate the complete and vast form. Hence, it can be called the *mother of Heaven and Earth*.

I do not know its name,
Bì said: Names are used to define forms, but the formless essence cannot be defined. Hence, it is said that I do not know its name.

So I call it *Dao*,
Hé said: I do not see the Dao’s form, nor do I know how to name it. Yet I see all things arise from the Dao, so I call it *Dao*.

○ Bì said: Names are used to define forms, and words are used to express what is praiseworthy. The Dao takes shape from nothingness, and its greatest praiseworthy name is *Dao*. If forced to name it, I would say *Great* (大).

Emperor Mínghuáng said: I see this formless essence giving rise to all things, hidden without a name. Thus, I use *Dao* to manifest its virtue, calling it *Dao* to encompass its scope. Yet its name is simply *Great* (大).

○ Hé said: Though I do not know its name, I call it *Great* (大) because it is vast yet without limit, encompassing all without exclusion.
○ Bì said: I call it *Dao* because it is the greatest name that can be spoken of. When accounting for the origin of its name, it is rooted in *Great* (大). *Great* implies connection, and connection must entail distinctions. With distinctions, its ultimate essence is lost, hence it is said that if forced to name it, I would call it *Great*.

○ Xiù said: The Dao, when compared to all things, is without boundaries—thus it can be called *Great*. However, once the name *Great* is established, its full essence is diminished.

*Great* means *transient*,
Hé said: Its greatness is not like Heaven’s constant presence above or Earth’s constant presence below. Rather, it is transient, without a fixed abode.

○ Bì said: *Transient* (逝) means *circulation*. It does not cling to one thing; it is merely the great essence. Its circulation is boundless, hence it is called *transient*.

○ Pán said: Greatness implies function. *Transient* means it circulates through all things, and its function is manifest.

*Transient* means *distant*,
Hé said: *Distant* (远) means it extends to the limitless, spreading *qi* throughout Heaven and Earth without obstruction.

○ Pán said: The Dao’s function reaches all extremes—thus it is distant. Yet in its distance from the origin, it is also the formless essence. When the Dao is at its utmost, it is neither distant nor near.»

「*Distant* means *return*.

○ **Mínghuáng** said: Its wondrous function is boundless. If one forcibly names it, one cannot grasp it. Hence, when one seeks greatness, it *circulates and departs*; when one seeks it from its departure, one cannot reach it. If one can truly comprehend, then *return* is found within one’s own **body-mind** (*shēnxīn*) and realized.

○ **Hé** said: Though it is distant, it does not exceed the absolute—yet it is also present within the human form.

○ **Bì** said: *Distant* means *extreme*. It exhausts all limits without favoring any single place, hence it is called *distant*. It does not conform to any particular path; its intent stands alone, hence it is called *return*.

○ **Xiù** said: What is formed and named again returns to the formless and nameless, endlessly circulating, returning to its beginning. Hence, the **Dao/Way** is great, Heaven is great, Earth is great, and the **Sovereign** (*wáng*) is also great.

○ **Mínghuáng** said: Following what makes each great, one attains unity—Heaven, Earth, and the Sovereign. Heaven’s greatness lies in its ability to cover all; Earth’s greatness lies in its ability to bear all; the Sovereign’s greatness lies in following Earth while embodying Heaven and the **Dao/Way**, hence it is also called great.

○ **Hé** said: The greatness of the **Dao/Way** encompasses all **Heavens and Earths** (*zhūtian dì*), containing nothing it does not embrace. Heaven’s greatness covers all without exception. Earth’s greatness bears all without exclusion. The Sovereign’s greatness governs all without restraint.

○ **Bì** said: Though Heaven and Earth possess the nature of greatness, humanity is most noble, and the Sovereign is the master of humanity. Though not inherently great, the Sovereign nonetheless is great—equal to the three (*sān* [Heaven, Earth, and the Dao])—hence it is said the Sovereign is also great.

○ **Pán** said: Heaven and Earth can embody the **Dao/Way**, hence they are great. The Sovereign, though subordinate to the **Emperor** (*dìhuáng*) and the **Dao/Way**, is the culmination of human conduct. Even if the teachings of **Lǎozi** (*Old Master Lao*) do not exhaust the ultimate boundaries of the **Dao/Way**, they merely emphasize its inherent nature. The Sovereign, by naming *return and departure*, can exhaust the nature of humanity—then humanity will submit to it, just as all grains converge upon the rivers and seas. Exhausting nature means embodying the **Dao/Way**, hence it is shown as great.

Within the **Four Great** (*sìdà*), **Bì** said: The **Four Great** are the **Dao/Way**, Heaven, Earth, and the Sovereign. All things have names and designations, yet these are not their ultimate extremes. When one speaks of the **Dao/Way**, it implies a source and origin—only then is it called the **Dao/Way**. Thus, this is the greatness within names, not the greatness of the nameless. The nameless cannot be designated, hence it is called the **domain** (*yù*) of the **Four Great**. The **Dao/Way**, Heaven, Earth, and the Sovereign all exist within the nameless, hence it is said the **Four Great** are within the domain.

○ **Pán** said: The **Dao/Way**’s central embodiment relies upon things to be named, hence it does not depart from the domain of the **Four Great**. Yet the Sovereign occupies one position.

○ **Mínghuáng** said: The Sovereign is the guiding spirit of humanity; all things depend upon his rise and fall. To warn the Sovereign and establish his governance, it is said that the Sovereign occupies one position—this is to caution him to have laws, referring to the following text.

○ **Hé** said: Within the **Eight Extremes** (*bājí*), there are the **Four Great**, and the Sovereign occupies one.

○ **Bì** said: He occupies the greatness of the human master.

○ **Pán** said: Originally, it was said the Sovereign occupies one position. Humanity should follow Earth’s stillness and yielding softness. When planting, one reaps the **Five Grains** (*wǔgǔ*); when digging, one finds sweet springs—laboring without complaint, achieving merit without claiming it.

○ **Pán** said: It speaks of the Sovereign selecting those who exhaust humanity’s nature—now it speaks of humanity. The Sovereign’s **virtue/power** (*dé*) is confined to human affairs, yet humanity’s nature inherently possesses the Sovereign’s **virtue/power**. Earth follows Heaven. **Hé** said: Heaven is serene and unchanging, giving without demanding return, nurturing all things without taking anything. Heaven follows the **Dao/Way**. **Hé** said: The **Dao/Way** is pure and still, acting silently through refined **qi**, allowing all things to form naturally. The **Dao/Way** follows **Nature** (*zìrán*).

○ **Mínghuáng** said: When speaking of the Sovereign, one should first follow Earth’s stillness. Then, one should follow Heaven’s nurturing and generative function. Having achieved generativity, one should follow the **Dao/Way**’s purity and non-action, allowing things to transform themselves. If a ruler can do this, he embodies the **Dao/Way**’s natural, self-soothing essence.

○ **Hé** said: The **Dao/Way** endures and grows naturally, without need of governance.

○ **Bì** said: *Governance* (*fǎ*) refers to principles and laws. If humanity does not defy Earth, it attains full stillness—this is following Earth. If Earth does not defy Heaven, it attains full capacity to bear—this is following Heaven. If Heaven does not defy the **Dao/Way**, it can fully cover all—this is following the **Dao/Way**. If the **Dao/Way** does not defy **Nature**, then it attains its true essence—this is following **Nature**. In the square, one follows the square; in the round, one follows the round. In **Nature**, there is no defiance. **Nature** is the speech of the nameless and the words of the limitless. Using wisdom cannot reach the state of no-knowing, yet the **form and vitality** (*xíngpò*) cannot match the **refined essence and subtle appearance** (*jīngxiàng*), nor can the **refined essence and subtle appearance** match the formless. Having ritual propriety is not as good as having none—hence the **Dao/Way**’s relative governance.

The **Dao/Way** follows **Nature**, Heaven thus relies upon it. Heaven follows the **Dao/Way**, Earth thus conforms to it. Earth follows Heaven, humanity thus mirrors it. This is why it is said the Sovereign occupies one position as the master of the one.

○ **Pán** said: Though **Nature** precedes the **Dao/Way**, it is still not the ultimate of the **Dao/Way**. When relying on things to speak, these four—**Dao/Way**, Heaven, Earth, and the Sovereign—are like successive governance leading to discussion, yet each remains unaware of its origin, with no precedence. **Zhuāngzi** said: *The non-doing of Ji Zhen* (*jìzhēn zhī mòwéi*) meets *the doing of Zi You* (*zǐyǒu zhī huò*), yet both are within the realm of things, each bending in its own way. The **Buddhist School** (*fóshì*) says: It is neither caused nor spontaneous, nor is it **Nature**. **Nature** exists above all things yet emerges beyond the realm of things. This explanation lies beneath **Zhuāngzi** and the **Buddha**, yet **Lǎozi**’s non-doing is not the teaching of the **Unparalleled Sage** (*bùshèng shèng*). It is merely fitting for its time, not contradicting principle—thus scholars should not stop at **Nature** as a fixed conclusion, for this would lose the true meaning and be far from the truth. It must be carefully examined.

---

**Chapter Twenty-Six: "Heavy as the Root of Light"**
*Heavy as the root of light*,

○ **Mínghuáng** said: The heavy governs the light, hence the heavy is the root.

**Chapter Twenty-Six: "Heavy as the Root of Light"**
*Heavy as the root of light,*
**Mínghuáng** said: The heavy governs the light, hence the heavy is the root.
**Hé** said: A ruler who is not heavy will not be revered; cultivating the body without gravity will lose its essence. Flowers of grasses and trees are light, so they wither and fall, but roots are heavy, so they endure.

---

**Chapter Twenty-Seven: "Stillness as Sovereign of Turbulence"**
*Stillness as sovereign of turbulence,*
**Mínghuáng** said: Stillness governs movement, hence stillness is sovereign.
**Hé** said: A ruler who is not still will lose his essence; cultivating the body without stillness invites peril. The dragon remains still and thus transforms; the tiger is turbulent and thus meets early ruin.

**Bì** said: All things—light cannot bear what is heavy, small cannot govern what is great. What does not move makes movement possible, what does not stir controls turbulence. Thus, the heavy must be the root of the light, and stillness must be sovereign over turbulence.

Therefore, the virtuous person walks all day without abandoning his **zīzhòng** (supply wagons).
**Mínghuáng** said: *Zī* means "car." *Zhòng* refers to the carried goods.
The light and turbulent revere the heavy and still, for those who walk abandon their supply wagons and suffer cold and hunger, while those who love the light and turbulent invite disaster.

**Hé** said: *Zī* also means "stillness."
The Sage walks the Dao all day without departing from his stillness and his heaviness.

**Bì** said: Take heaviness as foundation and never depart from it.

**Páng** said: Movement relies on lightness for speed, yet must depend on supply wagons for sustenance. If one strays far from them, one will inevitably falter along the way.

Though there are palaces of splendor,
Though there are resorts of ease,
**Mínghuáng** said: A ruler must guard his heaviness and stillness, so even amid palaces of splendor, he must dwell in ease and detachment, unconcerned with superficial displays.

**Hé** said: *Hóngguān* refers to palaces and gates. *Yànchǔ* is where the consorts reside. *Chāorán* means to withdraw and not dwell there.

**Bì** said: This is because one does not fixate on them.

**Páng** said: Though palaces of splendor lie before him, he must first secure his place of ease to ensure his safety—this clarifies the meaning of turbulence and stillness.

How can the ruler of ten thousand chariots
**Hé** said: *Rúhé* is an exclamation of lament for the times. *Wànshèng zhǔ* refers to the king.

lighten himself against the world?
**Mínghuáng** said: *Rúhé* is an exclamatory sigh. The world is the seat of great treasure. It speaks of how a ruler might abandon his body to desire, lightly squandering himself and losing his throne.

**Hé** said: The king is most exalted—how can he act with lightness and turbulence? This laments the king’s extravagance and licentiousness.

**Xiù** said: The ruler takes the world as his root; he cannot treat his body lightly. If he does, he will lose his ministers.

**Hé** said: If the king indulges in lightness and turbulence, he will lose his ministers. If he is turbulent in self-cultivation, he will lose his essence.

**Bì** said: Lightness is not true heaviness; losing one’s foundation is to lose oneself. Losing one’s "sovereign" refers to losing one’s throne.

**Páng** said: *Chén* (ministers) is also *běn* (root).

---
**Chapter Twenty-Seven: "The Chapter of Virtuous Conduct"**

**Chapter Twenty-Seven: "The Chapter of Virtuous Conduct"**

**Virtuous Conduct has no fixed path,**
*Mínghuáng* said: Among all teachings, if one loses the true nature, conduct becomes formless—thus it is called *Virtuous Conduct*. When thus, the heart merges with the Dao, leaving no trace to follow.

○ *Hé* said: The practitioner of Virtuous Conduct seeks it within oneself—never descending the hall, never stepping beyond the door—thus leaving no tracks.

○ *Bì* said: Following nature spontaneously, neither contriving nor initiating, things arrive naturally without leaving traces.

○ *Páng* said: Embracing the spirit, one moves boundlessly without edge. Moreover, the Sage acts only when necessary, ensuring no traces remain in the world, lest the people abandon his virtue. This is what *Zhuangzi* calls "moving without leaving a trace."

**Virtuous Speech has no flaw or blame,**
*Mínghuáng* said: Mastering the teachings, one does not cling rigidly; abandoning images, one seeks intent. Logic confirms speech, yet speech is forgotten—thus, within teachings, there are no flaws or errors.

○ *Hé* said: Virtuous speech is choosing words carefully, so no flaw exists, nor does it err against the world.

○ *Bì* said: Following things’ nature without division or coercion, there is no need for selection—one may enter its gate.

○ *Páng* said: Words emerge like the sun’s daily rise, yet they do not originate from the heart. Though they fill the world, truly they have never spoken—how then could there be flaws or blame?

**Virtuous Planning requires no calculations,**
*Mínghuáng* said: Realizing all teachings stem from the single gate of non-duality, unified in one thread, no other perceptions arise—thus no need for artificial schemes. If not beyond all calculations, what could be more virtuous?

○ *Hé* said: The virtuous planner, devoted to the Dao, adheres to unity without deviation. Few calculations are needed, so no clever strategies are required.

○ *Bì* said: Following natural numbers without artificial means.

○ *Páng* said: One generates two, two generates three—thus onward, no clever reckoning can measure. Only merging with the singular aspect, avoiding all numbers, does one find nothing external. When things arise, they are understood—what need for calculations?

*(Alternative text: Virtuous Planning has no reckoning rods.)*

**Virtuous Closure has no door or bolt yet cannot be opened,**
*Mínghuáng* said: Forgetting speech and conduct, not entering other gates, the heart does not chase phenomena, bearing no burden of intention—though no doors or bolts exist, how could it be opened?

○ *Páng* said: Concealed in what cannot be pursued, closed by what has no outside, the closure admits nothing—who could open it? This resembles the *Yijing*’s "retreat and hide in secrecy."

Thus, even the spirit cannot spy its traces—how much less could one enter its gate?

**Virtuous Binding has no cords or knots yet cannot be undone,**
*Mínghuáng* said: Realizing true nature, one relies on emptiness and forgetting. If emptiness and forgetting are achieved, the heart unites with the Dao—though no cords or bindings exist, how could it be loosened or scattered?

○ *Hé* said: The virtuous one who binds matters through the Dao can bind the heart—nothing like cords can undo it.

○ *Bì* said: Following things’ nature without contrivance or application, no need for locks, cords, or bindings arises—thus it cannot be opened or undone.

These five virtues all speak of non-contrivance, non-application, following things’ nature without shaping them artificially.

○ *Páng* said: The Sage binds the world’s trust with personal integrity—who could undo it?

Thus, the Sage constantly rescues people.
○ *Hé* said: The Sage teaches loyalty and filiality to rescue people’s lives and nature. Hence, no one is abandoned.
○ *Hé* said: The noble and lowly each find their place.

○ *Bì* said: The Sage does not establish artificial names to govern things, nor does he contrive advancement or reject the unworthy. He aids all things’ natural unfolding without initiating—thus, no one is abandoned. If virtue is not exalted, the people do not compete; if rare goods are not prized, the people do not steal; if desires are unseen, the people’s hearts remain undisturbed. Constantly keeping the people’s hearts free of desire and confusion, no one’s nature is abandoned.

Constantly rescuing things,
○ *Hé* said: The Sage teaches the people to follow the four seasons, rescuing all things from harm. Thus, nothing is abandoned.
*Mínghuáng* said: Therefore, the Sage constantly uses these five virtues to teach, so none are

○ *Hé* said: When people act virtuously, the Sage takes them as teachers for others.

○ *Bì* said: By elevating the virtuous as teachers for the unvirtuous, they are called teachers.

The unvirtuous are the virtuous’ means of use.
*Mínghuáng* said: *Teacher* is *law/pattern*. *Means of use* is *to take*.
The virtuous may be emulated as a law, the unvirtuous taken to serve.

○ *Hé* said: *Means of use* is *to employ*.
Though people act unvirtuously, the Sage still instructs them toward virtue, that they may be employed.

○ *Bì* said: *Means of use* is *to take*.
The virtuous harmonize the unvirtuous with virtue, discard the unvirtuous with virtue—thus, the unvirtuous are what the virtuous take.

○ *Máng* said: Only when there are unvirtuous does the virtuous’ salvific function manifest—thus, *means of use*.

Not esteeming the teacher,
○ *Hé* said: It is because there is no reliance upon it.

Not cherishing the means of use,
*Mínghuáng* said: This passage holds deep meaning—teaching the simultaneous forgetting. If one retains the teacher and means of use, it is not yet the ultimate.
Now, the reason the Sage esteems the teacher is to preserve the *learning aspect*, but once the *learning aspect* is emptied, there is nothing to esteem. The reason one cherishes the means of use is to preserve the *teaching aspect*, yet in forgetting teaching, one does not cherish the means of use. Esteem and cherishing both forgotten, the Dao transforms of itself.

○ *Hé* said: There is none to employ.

○ *Máng* said: The Sage shares in the people’s joys and calamities, thus he firmly establishes the names of teacher and means of use, assuming the guise of salvific virtue. Yet when pushed to its limit, the Dao unifies as one—who then is teacher or means of use? Thus, it is not merely that I do not cherish the means of use, but I can also make the world forget me.
Thus, one arrives at making the world abandon the self—also through the five virtues alone.
Even the wise are deeply deluded,

「Hé said: Though one may deem oneself wise, yet this person is deeply deluded. ○ Bì said: Though there be wisdom, if one relies solely upon it and does not conform to the Dao through things, it must be lost—thus it is said, "Even the wise are deeply deluded." ○ Máng said: The wise possess knowledge, and with knowledge one can perceive the principles of things. Yet they fail to grasp the subtlety of the formless, thus when the Dao reaches such profundity, the wise are deeply deluded. This is called the *essential subtlety*.

○ Emperor Minghuáng said: When both teacher and means of use are forgotten, this is called *mysterious virtue*. The common multitude does not awaken to this, assuming it to be profound delusion—thus the Sage declares: Though knowing, the common multitude deems it profound delusion; yet when viewed through the Dao, this is called *essential subtlety*. ○ Hé said: To comprehend this meaning is to know the *Dao of subtle essentials*.

**Chapter XXVIII: Knowing the Masculine**

Knowing the masculine, guarding the feminine,
○ Máng said: The masculine is active, the feminine is passive; when passive, one does not stray from the True. Yet mere passivity is not the fullness of the Dao—thus the Perfect One regards the feminine as constant, yet always knows the masculine.

To become the valley of the world.
○ Hé said: The masculine symbolizes nobility, the feminine symbolizes humility; though one may know one’s own nobility and splendor, one must still guard it with humility, abandoning the masculine’s forcefulness and embracing the feminine’s gentleness—thus the world will turn to them, like water flowing into deep valleys.

○ Xiāo said: Valleys receive and accommodate all things, flowing and passing through them. By guarding the feminine, one can receive all dharmas with emptiness and tranquility, yet not be obstructed by things—thus it is said, "To become the valley of the world," this is the one who fully realizes their nature.

To become the valley of the world, constant virtue does not depart.
○ Hé said: If one can be humble to the world, like a deep valley, then virtue remains constant and does not depart from oneself.

○ Máng said: Constant virtue is fixed and does not shift—this is the Dao within oneself. "Does not depart" means unity within one’s nature, without externalization.

Returning to the state of infancy.
○ Emperor Minghuáng said: The masculine is loyal to the use of strength—thus knowing the masculine, one must guard the feminine. By humbling one’s virtue and accommodating things, one becomes the world’s valley; thus constant virtue abides, containing the Dao in harmony, returning to the conduct of infancy.

○ Hé said: One must return to the mind of infancy, devoid of knowledge yet complete.

○ Bì said: The masculine belongs to the realm of the prior, the feminine to the realm of the latter.

Knowing what is the world’s beginning, one must be its end.
Thus the Sage ends himself, yet himself is the beginning.

Valleys do not seek things, yet things return to them.
Infants do not employ wisdom, yet they embody natural intelligence.

○ Máng said: The infant contains harmony and guards unity—desires do not arise, the nature’s original truth is whole yet undivided, the utmost depth of virtue, thus returning to the beginning. If one attains this, they may be called *Great*.

Mencius said: The Great Person does not lose their childlike heart.

Knowing the bright, guarding the dark,
○ Máng said: The dark is the color of the Northern Pole—passivity cannot fully describe it. The Sage establishes all dharmas; without guarding the dark, there is no foundation for their true nature. This is verified by the emergence of spring and summer from the profound winter.

To become the model of the world.
○ Hé said: The bright symbolizes clarity, the dark symbolizes silence; though one may know their own brightness and clarity, one must still guard it with silence, like darkness where nothing is seen—thus one can become the world’s model, and virtue remains constant.

○ Bì said: *Shì* means the standard by which one models themselves.

To become the model of the world.
○ Máng said: The Perfect One takes the Sage as the world’s model—this is because the Sage embodies the central truth.

Constant virtue is not forceful,
○ Hé said: If one can become the world’s model, virtue remains constant within them and does not deviate toward forcefulness.

○ Bì said: *Wǔ* means deviation.

○ Máng said: In matters of great scope, if one doubts the use of forcefulness yet responds appropriately to things, like the natural cycles of cold and heat in Heaven, without the heart accumulating resentment, then one does not deviate toward forcefulness.

Returning to the boundless.
○ Emperor Minghuáng said: Though virtue may be noble and bright, one must always guard it with darkness and humility—when one responds to things without deviation, constant virtue is boundless, thus returning to the boundless.

*Wǔ* means deviation.
○ Hé said: When virtue does not deviate toward forcefulness, one attains longevity and eternal life, returning to the boundless.

○ Bì said: It cannot be exhausted.

○ Máng said: To transform and harmonize greatly is called *Sage*—harmonization is daily yet boundless, thus the boundless is the Sage’s essence.

From the essence, actions arise; returning to one’s origin.

Knowing the flourishing, guarding the abased,
○ Máng said: When the Sage reaches this stage, they equal all phenomena, yet have not returned to their origin. The utmost of virtue, not yet fulfilling the purpose of embodying the spirit, is like this. The ancient Daoists wore tattered garments and coarse robes, dwelling in filth and lowliness, teaching in obscurity—this is in accord with this principle.

To become the valley of the world.
○ Hé said: *Flourishing* symbolizes nobility and splendor, *abased* symbolizes filth and lowliness; though one may possess nobility and splendor, one must guard it with filth and lowliness—thus the world will turn to them, like water flowing into deep valleys.

○ Máng said: Valleys are both empty and full, yet can respond to all things—thus they mirror the spirit. The spirit is complete and boundless, without external limits; though it exists, one cannot grasp it, and though it acts, it is inexhaustible—this is guarding the abased with precision.

Precision thus can embody the spirit, only through precision can one enter the spirit.

To become the valley of the world, constant virtue is then sufficient.
○ Hé said: *Sufficient* means to stop or cease.

If one can become the world’s valley, virtue will then abide within them.

○ Máng said: Lowering the spirit by one degree, it becomes insufficient within one’s nature—thus when the Dao reaches the stage of embodying the spirit, it is the true fulfillment of nature.

Returning to the *Uncarved Block*.
○ Emperor Minghuáng said: Though virtue may be noble and flourishing, one must always guard it with abasement and darkness—when things are felt and responded to appropriately, like valleys echoing sounds, receiving endlessly, and constant virtue is complete, then one returns to the Dao itself.»

The Uncarved Block is the Dao.
○ Hé said: One should return to the natural simplicity of the body, unadorned by artifice.

○ Bì said: These three statements speak of constant return to the end—only then is virtue complete. What they dwell upon is the Dao’s movement: its function cannot be seized; one must always abide in its source.

○ Máng said: The Uncarved Block illustrates the purity of nature. Nature can give rise to myriad things yet does not cling to any single form—thus it is called the Uncarved Block. Though the spirit reaches its utmost, it never departs from the Uncarved Block.

When the Uncarved Block disperses, it becomes instruments.
○ Hé said: The Uncarved Block of all things disperses and becomes tools and uses.

If the Dao disperses, it becomes divine clarity, flowing as sun and moon, dividing into the Five Phases.
○ Máng said: Once returned to the Uncarved Block, it disperses again into instruments—thus, the end becomes the beginning, the constant way of the Dao.

The Sage employs it, and it becomes officials and leaders.
○ Emperor Minghuáng said: When virtue is contained and harmonized within, it returns to the Uncarved Block. When virtue is applied, it disperses into instruments. Once engaged with form and substance, there is also depletion of essence—thus, the Sage employs it to become the officials and leaders of the people.

○ Hé said: The Sage’s elevation in use becomes the supreme leader of all officials.

○ Bì said: The Uncarved Block is the True. When the True disperses, myriad virtues emerge distinct, like instruments taking form. The Sage follows its division and dispersion, thus establishing officials and leaders—using the good as teachers, the flawed as resources—to transform customs and return to unity.

○ Máng said: The Emperor, embodying the Uncarved Block, governs dispersion, yet knowing governance is merely to serve as officials.

Thus, great governance does not harm.
○ Emperor Minghuáng said: The Sage employs the Dao to govern all life, warm as spring, its blessings everlasting. All things flourish and grow, yet nothing is harmed or cut.

○ Hé said: The Sage uses the Dao to govern the world, causing no injury or harm. In governing oneself, the Sage uses the Dao to restrain desires, lest the spirit be damaged.

○ Bì said: Great governance is to take the heart of the world as one’s own—thus there is no harm.

○ Xiù said: To govern with the Dao is to follow its natural course, adapting without constraint—thus, though governance occurs, there is no path of coercion or mutilation.

This chapter’s words on honor and shame, black and white, are profound and far-reaching—speech cannot exhaust their meaning. Let the reader ponder deeply.

---
**Chapter Twenty-Nine: Those Who Would Take the World**

Those who would take the world,
○ Hé said: To become the master of the world.

And act to do so,
○ Hé said: To govern the people with deliberate action.

I have seen that they cannot help but fail.
○ Emperor Minghuáng said: The world is the supreme treasure—its possession must await the rightful mandate. Yet those who seize it by force, through violence or chaos, are warned: *I have seen that they cannot help but fail.*

The Old Master admonished: *I have seen that they cannot help but fail.*
○ Hé said: I have seen that they have already lost the Dao and the hearts of the people. The Dao abhors complexity and filth; the people abhor excess.

○ Máng said: The Perfect One embodies the spirit, harmonizing with transformation, becoming one with all things. Though they govern the world, they never possess—thus they act effortlessly, and all undertakings succeed. Like clay shaped into vessels, they mold emperors and kings.

But those who rigidly cling to self, using self to communicate with things—even within a six-foot frame, their movements are hindered, constrained as if barely contained. How much more so for the vastness of the world?

To take is to take from things—this is the presence of self.
To act is to contrive—this is the presence of doing.
Those who possess self and act will dwell in anxiety and exhaustion—how can they govern others?

The world is a divine instrument.
○ Bì said: Divine, without form or boundary; instrument, a coalescence. Without form yet coalescing—thus it is called the divine instrument.

○ Máng said: Only when one’s essence is fully exhausted, its responses boundless, can one employ it without action.

It cannot be acted upon.
○ Hé said: Instrument, thing. Man is the divine thing of the world. Divine things prefer tranquility and cannot be governed by deliberate action.

○ Máng said: To intend action is to possess self and things.

Action ruins it.
○ Emperor Minghuáng said: The supreme treasure is the divine instrument of Heaven and Earth—thus it cannot be seized by force. Hence, *action ruins it*—a warning to treacherous ministers.

○ Hé said: To govern with deliberate action seizes its natural essence, giving rise to deceit.

○ Bì said: All things take their nature from spontaneity—thus they can be guided but not forced, known but not seized. To contrive against their nature is to ruin them. To grasp their coming and going is to lose them.

Things either move or follow,
○ Hé said: When the superior moves, the inferior follows.

Or warm or chill,
○ Hé said: *Brith* (warmth), *blow* (cold).

To warm is to have cold.
○ Hé said: Where there is warmth, there must be cold.

To strengthen is to weaken,
○ Hé said: Where there is strength, there must be weakness.

To uplift is to bury.
○ Emperor Minghuáng said: To seek clarity through force is to fail; to seize is to lose. Thus, things either move ahead or follow behind, are warmed by embrace or chilled by wind, strengthened by support or weakened by breaking—when the Dao is present, affairs prosper; when virtue is absent, they decay.

○ Hé said: *Zài* (load/fulfill), tranquility. *Chē* (car), peril. Where there is tranquility, there must be peril. The enlightened ruler cannot act with intent, whether in governing the realm or nurturing the body.

○ Pán said: The Sage’s heart transcends presence and absence, is not bound by things. Thus, yin and yang alternate, yet my teachings remain unchanging. If one acts with fixed intent, then things oppose the act, and myriad transformations entangle in endless error, beyond reckoning. Hence, the Sage moves alone in the forefront, yet remains unaware of those who follow, like form and shadow inseparable. He warms with embrace, yet does not see the cold already brought by wind, as summer and winter follow in kind. Knowing only strength, he must await the coming of weakness. Knowing only fulfillment, he finds peril at hand. These are the great rhythms of creation, the daily shifts of nature—yet those who act with fixed intent do not know them, for they fail to grasp their own nature, and thus fall into this folly. When they vie against these laws, even a single trifling thing becomes a vast burden, filling heaven and earth. How could they wield the sacred tools without encumbrance?

Thus, the Sage avoids excess, extravagance, and overreach. Emperor Minghuáng said: The Sage perceives the rise and fall of things, knowing that grasping leads to loss, so he discards their extremes.

○ Hé said: Excess is gluttony in food and indulgence in music and beauty. Extravagance is in garments, adornments, and feasting. Overreach is in palaces and pavilions. To discard these three, to dwell in moderation, to act without contrivance—then the world transforms itself.

○ Bì said: All these sayings of *hé* (or) concern the reversal of affairs, their turning and returning without ceasing. This is the error of grasping and severing. The Sage attains the utmost of nature’s course, gives free rein to all things’ inclinations, so he follows without acting, conforms without forcing, removing what confuses and casting off what misleads. Thus, his heart remains undisturbed, and the true nature of things is revealed.

○ Pán said: The Sage’s heart aligns with the void, to respond to all change. In his actions, he does not cling to schemes, like a mirror reflecting form—sufficient and then complete. What lies beyond is not his concern. Those who act with fixed intent, however, see only appearances, pride themselves in their cleverness, and labor endlessly. Thus, their deeds always overreach, for they fail to grasp the interplay of movement and following, embrace and chill, strength and weakness, fulfillment and ruin.

「**Chapter Thirty: The Dao/Way as an Aid to the Sovereign**

To aid the sovereign with the Dao/Way means that **He** (the *Zhuanxu*[1]) says: the sovereign can use the Dao/Way to govern himself. He does not rely on arms to overpower the world.

**He** says: The sovereign who governs with the Dao/Way does not use military force to subdue the world; instead, he aligns with Heaven’s virtue/power (*Wu*[2]), and the enemy submits of its own accord.

○ **Wang Bi** says: When aiding the sovereign with the Dao/Way, one should not even consider using arms to overpower the world—how much less should the sovereign himself rely on them!

○ **Wang Fan** says: One who embodies the Dao/Way and possesses the world will have all things naturally submit as guests. Why speak of this at all? Thus, it is only said that the Sage aids the sovereign with virtue/power, using arms to supplement virtue/power. But his actions favor restraint.

**The Emperor Xuanzong** says: If a subject can aid the sovereign with the Dao/Way, he should yield to virtue/power and not rely on the threat of arms. How so? Arms are instruments of violence, and war is a perilous affair. If one confronts an enemy with arms, the enemy will retaliate in kind. Thus, the cycle of vengeance begins, and the outcome of victory or defeat cannot be measured.

○ **He** says: When his actions invite retaliation, it means he does not blame others but reflects on his own faults.

○ **Wang Bi** says: For governance, one strives to achieve results and establish affairs, but one who follows the Dao/Way strives to return to non-action (*Wuwei*). Thus, it is said that his actions favor restraint.

○ **Wang Fan** says: *Return* (*Huan*) means not to persist but to seek restoration, in harmony with the principle of aligning with Heaven and governing the people without force.

Where the military strategist dwells, thorns and brambles arise.
**He** says: Farming is neglected, fields left uncultivated.

After a great army, there will surely be a year of calamity.
**The Emperor Xuanzong** says: Where the military strategist dwells, war disrupts agriculture, fields are left uncultivated, and thus thorns and brambles arise. The *qi* of war brings harm, followed by droughts and floods; agriculture is ruined first, and disasters follow. There will be years of famine and ruin.

○ **He** says: Heaven responds with malevolent *qi*, harming the five grains, and when the five grains are exhausted, it harms the people.

○ **Wang Bi** says: Arms are instruments of violence and harm; they bring no benefit but only injury. The heart is wounded, and the people are plundered, fields ravaged—thus, it is said that thorns and brambles arise.

○ **Liu Xiu** says: The cruelty of slaughter disrupts the harmonious *qi* of Heaven and Earth.

A virtuous warrior achieves his purpose and then rests.
**He** says: A skilled warrior must be decisive and then cease.

He does not dare to rely on conquest.
**He** says: He does not use decisiveness to claim strength or dominance.

○ **Wang Bi** says: *Decisive* (*Guo*) is akin to *fulfillment* (*Jì*). It means that a skilled warrior pursues only the resolution of difficulties, not the conquest of strength for the sake of the world.

○ **Wang Fan** says: *Decisive* (*Guo*) is *completion* (*Cheng*). He seeks only to complete the task. Arms are like Heaven’s frost and snow: all that is needed to shape things is their power, for things cannot be formed without it. Thus, Heaven and the Sage both use force to supplement virtue/power—how dare one depend on a multitude to seek strength and claim profit for oneself?

Decisive yet not arrogant,
**He** says: He must be decisive yet humble, never boastful.

Decisive yet not self-praising,
**He** says: He must be decisive yet modest, never claiming glory for himself.

Decisive yet not proud,
**The Emperor Xuanzong** says: A virtuous advisor must be decisive in repelling the enemy. But his aim is to pacify the people and harmonize the multitude; he does not dare to seek victory or claim strength. Thus, though decisive in repelling the enemy, the enemy will not become a raider. He must guard against arrogance, self-praise, and pride, lest they lead to ruin—this is a solemn warning.

○ **He** says: *Pride* (*Jiao*) is *deception* (*Qi*). A decisive warrior must not deceive others with pride.

○ **Wang Bi** says: I do not hold military force as a virtue, and only use it when unavoidable—how can there be arrogance or pride?

○ **The Emperor Xuanzong** says: These three faults arise from attachment to victory. Only those without intent to conquer can truly be said to use arms. Decisive yet not forced,

**The Emperor Xuanzong** says: When the enemy advances to invade, there is no choice but to respond with arms. Thus, decisiveness lies in repelling the enemy, not in conquering for strength.

○ **He** says: A decisive warrior must not rely on strong arms and fortified defenses to oppress others.

○ **Wang Bi** says: Though the use of arms may be decisive in achieving results and resolving difficulties, when unavoidable, it should only be employed to suppress violence and disorder—not to rely on decisiveness for the sake of conquest.

○ **Wang Fan** says: In all four cases above, the principle is to avoid force.

When things reach their peak, they grow old.
**He** says: When grass and trees reach their full vigor, they wither and fall; when a person reaches his full strength, he grows weak and aged. It means that those who rely on strength cannot endure long.

○ **Wang Fan** says: When things reach their height, they decline—this is the natural order. In ancient times, there were those who met this fate, such as Qin. This is called *not conforming to the Dao/Way*.

**The Emperor Xuanzong** says: When things rely on their peak strength, it is due to the arrogance of arms. When things reach their height, they decline; when arms grow strong, they bring defeat. This is called *not conforming to the Dao/Way*—one must cease early and not persist.

○ **Liu Xiu** says: *Peak strength* (*Zhuang*) refers to the sudden rise of military force, illustrating those who rely on arms to overpower the world. A gusting wind does not last the morning, a sudden rain does not last the day—thus, a sudden rise in arms cannot be called the Dao/Way. *Cease* (*Zao*) means *to stop* (*Yi*).

---
**[1]** *Zhuanxu* (傳注) – "Commentary and Transmission" (a term used for ancient annotations or interpretations of texts).
**[2]** *Wu* (德) – "Virtue/power" (as in Heaven's benevolent or harmonious influence).**

The finest weapons are ominous instruments.
**He** says: *Ominous* (*Xiáng*), virtue/power.
Weapons unsettle the spirit and turbidify harmonious *qi*—they are not instruments of virtue/power, and should not be adorned.
Things often detest them.
**He** says: When weapons are wielded, harm is done, thus all things detest them.
Therefore, those who follow the Dao do not dwell upon them.
**Emperor Minghuang** says: *Finest* (*Jiā*), good. Weapons are stratagems. Common people refine speech and establish sincerity, yet cannot conceal their instruments with virtue/power, but instead take delight in martial stratagems. The use of stratagems lies only in conquest and slaughter, hence they are instruments of vice. Even things detest them, so those who follow the Dao do not place themselves within them.
**He** says: Those who follow the Dao do not dwell in their lands.
**Pang** says: *Do not dwell* (*Bù chǔ*) means to assist in emergencies alone, not to make it a constant practice.
Thus, the noble one at rest honors the left,
**He** says: Honoring the gentle and weak.
In war, honors the right.
**Emperor Minghuang** says: Left, *yang*. *Yang* harmony brings forth life, thus it is honored in peaceful times. Right, *yin*. *Yin* congealing brings solemnity and slaughter, thus it is honored in war.
**He** says: Honoring the strong and resolute—this speaks of martial ways contrasting with the noble’s path, differing in what is honored.
Weapons are ominous instruments,
**Emperor Minghuang** says: *Ominous*, virtue/power. Those who delight in weapons revel in slaughter, becoming instruments of vice.
**He** says: Martial implements are instruments of vice, not the noble’s tools.
**Emperor Minghuang** says: The noble relies on virtue/power as their instrument, not martial stratagems.
**He** says: Not the noble’s cherished instruments.
**Pang** says: The ruler governs with *wuwei*, the people with benevolence and kindness, thus martial prowess is not prized. Only when unavoidable is it used.
**He** says: When facing decline and chaos, disaster threatening the people, it is used for self-defense. Tranquility and simplicity are supreme.
**Emperor Minghuang** says: When barbarians invade, it is unavoidable. Victory without strife is the highest tranquility.
**He** says: Not coveting land or the wealth of others. Thus, it is not prized.
**He** says: Though victorious, it is not considered a gain or beauty. If it were prized, one would rejoice in it, and rejoicing is rejoicing in slaughter.
**He** says: Those who delight in victory are those who rejoice in killing.
