**Pang** says: Weapons are meant for slaughter, yet excessively adorned, making killing seem beautiful. Thus, those who delight in killing cannot achieve their aims in the world.
**Emperor Minghuang** says: To conquer an enemy, one must pity them, thus victory is not considered beautiful. Victory must entail much slaughter; if victory is prized, it is delighting in killing many. Delighting in killing many, people will not rally to you. To seek dominance is not easy.
**He** says: For a ruler to delight in killing is to never achieve dominance in the world, to arbitrarily control life and death, and recklessly execute punishments.
**Pang** says: Mencius said: Those who do not delight in killing can unite them.
Thus, auspicious matters honor the left,
**He** says: Left, the position of life.
Calamitous matters honor the right.
**He** says: *Yin* is the way of slaughter.
Thus, the *Piān Jiāngjūn* (副将军) is placed on the left,
**He** says: The *Piān Jiāngjūn*, though lowly, resides in the *yang* position, for he does not specialize in slaughter.
The *Shàng Jiāngjūn* (上将军) is placed on the right.
**Emperor Minghuang** says: The *Piān Jiāngjūn*, though lowly, is placed on the left, for he does not specialize in slaughter. The *Shàng Jiāngjūn*, though exalted, is placed on the right, for he oversees martial stratagems.
**He** says: The *Shàng Jiāngjūn*, exalted and on the right, speaks of his mastery over slaughter. When placed in a superior position, he is treated with funeral rites.
**Emperor Minghuang** says: Funeral rites honor the right; now the *Shàng Jiāngjūn* is on the right, thus he is treated with funeral rites.
**He** says: The *Shàng Jiāngjūn* on the right, funeral rites honoring the right—deceased honor *yin*. Many are slain, thus they are mourned with sorrow.
**Emperor Minghuang** says: Given the preciousness of life, yet exchanged in battle, there is a compassionate heart, thus they are mourned with sorrow.
**He** says: This sorrow comes from virtue/power being thin, unable to transform others through the Dao, thus harming the innocent.
Victory is treated with funeral rites.
**Emperor Minghuang** says: Brave warriors, victorious in battle, receive titles and sit in the right position—honoring the right is not auspicious, thus they are treated with funeral rites. Yet, as it is an ominous instrument, what need is there for mourning? Only white mourning garments are used.
**He** says: In antiquity, victorious generals sat in mourning positions, dressed in white, weeping. The noble values virtue/power over martial might, using force only when unavoidable, yet sorrowful, comparing it to mourning. Knowing later generations would not cease using force, they grieve.
**Bi** says: Doubt this is not the work of Laozi.

---
**Chapter Thirty-Two: The Dao Ever Nameless**

The Dao is ever nameless.
**Emperor Minghuang** says: The Dao’s constancy lies in its application, always able to respond to things, its responses not singular, thus it is nameless in constancy.
**He** says: The Dao can be *yin*, can be *yang*, can relax, can tighten, can preserve, can perish—thus it has no constant name.
**Pang** says: The Dao has no form, how then can it have a name?

Though crude and small, nothing under heaven can subdue it.
**Emperor Minghuang** says: *Crude* (*Pǔ*), the wondrous origin. The wondrous origin is refined and singular, thus it is called small. Yet in its applications, it achieves greatness, thus none dare to make the Dao their subject.
**He** says: Though the Dao is crude and small, subtle and formless, none dare to make the Dao their servant.
**Pang** says: In humans, *pǔ* is nature; in numbers, it is one. It does not dominate a single *qi*, yet can complete ten thousand forms, thus it is always before all things—who can subdue it?

「Yet taking from the singular thought of a single moment is sufficient to name it as small. The *Yijing* says: *"Returning to simplicity, yet distinguishing among things."* If lords and kings could hold fast to it, all things would willingly submit.

**Emperor Minghuang** says: If lords and kings could hold fast to the Dao in its refined singularity, act without contrivance yet transform through spontaneity, then all things would willingly submit and serve in accordance with virtue.

**He** says: If lords and kings could hold fast to the Dao in its non-action, then all things would willingly submit, obeying through the resonance of virtue.

**Pang** says: The Dao is formless and unattached, ever beyond naming, and thus it is said that the Dao is ever without name. The essence of *pǔ* lies in a mind of non-action, also without name, so those seeking the Dao should do no better than to preserve *pǔ*.

The wise can be made subjects through cunning,
The brave can be ruled through force,
The skilled can be employed through labor,
The strong can be entrusted with heavy tasks—
Yet *pǔ* is unyielding and uninclined,
Close to nothingness,
Hence it is said that none can subdue it.

Embrace *pǔ* and act without contrivance,
Do not burden your true nature with things,
Do not let desire harm your spirit—
Then all things will willingly submit,
And the Dao will be naturally attained.

**Pang** says: Submission is the act of yielding and returning to its source; if lords and kings embody this nameless *pǔ* as the standard for all under heaven, then without resorting to threats, coercion, or rewards, all things will submit naturally—just as children follow their mother. When heaven and earth harmonize, sweet dew descends.

**He** says: If the actions of lords and kings align with heaven’s harmony, then heaven will bestow auspicious blessings of sweet dew.

People submit without command,
And all is naturally balanced.

**Emperor Minghuang** says: If lords and kings could embrace and preserve this refined singularity, then earth would level and heaven would complete its course, mutual harmony would flourish, and peace would naturally arise—thus sweet dew would descend.

When sweet dew descends,
Grasses and orchids alike are nourished,
No need for instruction or command,
Yet all is balanced and equal.

Taking this as an analogy,
Lords and kings should govern by equitable application.

**He** says: When heaven sends auspicious blessings, no one needs to instruct or command—all things naturally adjust and equalize.

**Pang** says: The saying that when heaven and earth harmonize, sweet dew descends without being sought, and if I preserve my true nature in non-action, then the people will submit without being commanded.

If one governs by preserving *pǔ* in its namelessness,
Then the rising and falling of *yin* and *yang*
Will follow their natural order,
And harmonious *qi* will respond.

Sweet dew is born from the harmonious exchange of *yin* and *yang*,
Naturally distributed with equal balance,
No one compels its descent—
It is the Dao’s influence,
Unfailing in its reach.

Mencius said: *"To flow with heaven and earth above and below—is this not called the small supplement?"*
This is what he meant.

As in *Zhuangzi*, where the balance of cold and heat does not turn against and harm human form—
This is because the Dao is not lost.

The claim that *"the origin of humanity is the people"* is incorrect.
It was only when names were first established...」

The Yellow River said: *"The beginning is the Dao; names are the myriad things. The Dao, being nameless, can govern what has names; the formless can govern what has form."*

When names are established, the Sage Emperor said: *"The ruler of humanity must achieve balance through the Dao, only then can he govern the myriad things that have names. Thus, the myriad things that have names are all made to belong to the feudal lords and kings."* **"既" (jì)** means completion, exhaustion.

The Yellow River said: *"既" (jì) means completion, exhaustion. The myriad things that have names all possess desires and emotions; they rebel against the Dao and stray from virtue, hence their bodies are destroyed and dishonored.*

The Dao of cessation is also known—
The Yellow River said: *"If humanity can follow the Dao and cultivate virtue, the Dao of cessation will also naturally be understood."* Understanding cessation is how one avoids peril.

The Sage Emperor said: *"If the feudal lords and kings can govern the myriad named things, then those myriad named things will also know to submit and depend upon the feudal lords and kings. Knowing to submit to a ruler who follows the Dao is how one avoids dangerous peril."*

The Yellow River said: *"When the Dao is understood, divine numinous assistance will respond, and no peril will arise."* **Bì** said: *"The beginning of governance refers to when the undifferentiated *pǔ* first dispersed to form officials and leaders. When establishing officials and leaders, one cannot fail to set forth distinctions of rank and status to define high and low, hence the beginning of governance had names."*

Beyond this point, disputes over trifles will arise, hence it is said: *"When names are already established, humanity will also know to cease."* If one then follows names to designate things, the root of governance will be lost, hence understanding cessation is how one avoids peril.

**Pàn** said: *"Once distinctions of rank are set, the people will proceed without return, crafty schemes will proliferate, doctrines will diverge, and life and vitality will decay."* Hence, when names are first established, one must immediately know cessation, and only then will there be no peril of contention and strife.

Governance is the act of dividing the undifferentiated *pǔ* into instruments—
It is like the rivers and valleys in relation to the great seas.

The Sage Emperor said: *"Heaven sends sweet dew as a sign of blessing to those who follow the Dao, hence comparing a Dao-governed ruler to one who is at ease in the world, heaven responds to him, just as rivers and valleys flow into the great seas."*

The Yellow River said: *"This analogy speaks of the Dao’s presence in the world, harmonizing with humanity, like rivers and valleys flowing into the great seas."*

**Bì** said: *"Rivers and valleys do not seek out water, nor does the sea summon it; without summoning or seeking, it naturally returns. Those who follow the Dao in the world do not command yet achieve balance, do not seek yet obtain fulfillment, hence it is said: 'Like rivers and valleys flowing into the great seas.'"*

**Pàn** said: *"The great seas do not seek water, yet water flows to them, because the abundance of water makes their nature the same."* The Dao is the nature of the people; the Sage can gather its purity and completeness, and then all living beings will naturally come to pay homage to him, for it is their nature.

**Knowing humanity is wisdom—Chapter Thirty-Three of *Zhìzhāng***—
Knowing humanity is wisdom,

**Chapter Thirty-Four of *Zhìzhāng* — The Great Dao is Universal**

**Hé** said: *"The Dao is universal—*
Like water flowing without restraint,
As if present, as if absent,
As if seen, as if unseen—
It cannot be distinctly named.
Yet it can be applied to all sides.

**Mínghuáng** said: *"The great Dao is universal—
It has no fixed attachments yet responds to all things,
Moving freely without partiality to left or right.
○ **Hé** said: The Dao can be applied to all sides,
And is fitting in every circumstance.
○ **Bì** said: The Dao spreads far and wide,
Adapting to all things without limit,
Moving left and right, revolving and acting—
Then nothing is beyond its reach.
○ **Pàn** said: It is universal and undefined in any single thing,
Thus its application is without obstruction in all things.

**All living beings depend on it for life—*
*Depend* means they rely upon it,
For all things are sustained by the Dao
Yet it does not refuse them—

**Mínghuáng** said: *"The great Dao is universal—
It is the foundation of knowing oneself, then knowing what is precious,
Knowing what is precious, then being able to cultivate.
Cultivation lies in overcoming the selfishness of private desires.
Those who conquer their selfishness
Must first discern the distinction between inner and outer—
Then they can strengthen their resolve and advance along this Dao.
This is the order of fulfilling one’s nature and returning to the Commanding Way.

Indeed, those who know and conquer themselves
At first cannot fail to know humanity and conquer others,
But those who know and conquer others
Will ultimately seek to know and conquer themselves.
The Great Dao is Universal—Chapter Thirty-Four—

**Hé** said: *"The Dao is vast and boundless—
Like water, ever shifting,
As if present, as if not,
As if seen, as if unseen—
It cannot be distinctly named.
Yet it can be applied to all sides.

Hé said: *"The Dao does not decline to act, yet it does not forcefully obstruct.
When its work is complete, it does not claim ownership.
The Sage Emperor said: It means all things rely on the spontaneous functioning of the Dao to generate and transform, yet the Dao does not refuse to exert itself in labor. When its functions are fully realized, it does not name itself as the possessor.
Hé said: The Dao, when it acts, does not claim its merits.

It clothes and nourishes all things, yet does not rule over them.
Hé said: Though the Dao cherishes and sustains all things, it does not act like a sovereign who takes possession.
Thus, it is always without desire, and may be called *small*.
The Sage Emperor said: It cherishes all things, yet does not lord over them, having no desire for things—thus it may be called *small*. Yet this is not to say it is truly small.
Bì said: All things arise from the Dao, yet once born, they do not know their origin. Thus, in the world, there is always a time of no desire, and all things find their place. If the Dao had no relation to things, it would be called small.
Páng said: This is what is meant by *small* being truly *great*—for those who embody the Dao, desire and calculation do not arise; serene and unified within, how could this not be small? The *Yì* says: *Returning to the small, yet distinguishing among things*—one root clothes and nourishes, acting in love.

All things return to it, yet it does not know itself as sovereign.
Hé said: All things return to the Dao to receive *qi*, yet the Dao does not act like a sovereign who prohibits.
Thus, it may be called *great*.
The Sage Emperor said: It nourishes them, so all things return to it. Though there are differences among things, it does not rule over them—thus it may be called *great*. Yet this is not to say it is truly great, for it is neither small nor great—thus it is difficult to name.
Hé said: All things come and go freely, each abiding in its own place—thus it is not like the great.
Páng said: If one has intent as a sovereign, it opposes things. Only when one sovereigns over all things without ever intending to do so does one fill the boundless, and none can separate from it.

The nature of the Great Dao is such:
When it is small, it is never not great;
When it is great, it is never separate from the small.
It is only that observers perceive it differently.
In essence, it is neither small nor great—
beyond speech, yet graspable by intent.

Thus, the Sage can accomplish its greatness.
Hé said: The Sage follows the Dao, concealing virtue and hiding name, not seeking to be great.
Because it does not make itself great, it can accomplish true greatness.
The Sage Emperor said: Therefore, the Sage follows the Dao and forgets its merits, never seeking to magnify itself—thus it can accomplish its magnified work.
Hé said: The Sage leads by example, transforming without speech. All affairs are cultivated and ordered, thus it can accomplish its greatness.
Bì said: Greatness arises from the minute; difficulty from the easy.
Páng said: To claim greatness is to have greatness; having greatness is to be small. Only by not claiming greatness and yet naming it as such is true greatness achieved.

**Chapter Thirty-Five: Grasping the Great Symbol**

*Grasping the Great Symbol, the world turns toward it.*
The Sage Emperor said: The Great Symbol is the Dao. When the sovereign holds fast to the Great Dao to govern the world, all things return to it.
Hé said: *Grasping* means *guarding*. *Symbol* is the Dao. The Sage guards the Great Dao, and thus all under heaven move their hearts and turn toward it. Governing oneself, one clarifies the great principles, and they come and go within oneself.
Bì said: The Great Symbol is the mother of the heavenly patterns—neither cold nor warm, neither hot nor cool—thus it can encompass and统率 all things without harm or injury. If one grasps it, then the world turns toward it.
Páng said: The sovereign embodies the Dao, ruling with an unseen presence, allowing all under heaven to roam freely in self-realization. The Great Symbol is the complete, formless embodiment of the Dao.

If one grasps it to govern the world,
Praise and blame do not reach it—
It sends forth, yet does not call back.
How could it be compared to the stench of rotting meat?
It sends forth without harming.
Páng said: When the Sage governs the world, even if treasures are exchanged like discarded things, with no action from above, it allows heaven, earth, ghosts, beasts, grasses, and trees to flourish in their nature without mutual harm—this is true virtue.

Thus, it is peaceful, stable, and flourishing.
The Sage Emperor said: If things follow their nature without harm, they rest in peace and flourish.
Hé said: When all things turn toward it without harm, the state is secure, families are at ease, and great peace is achieved. Governing the body without harming the spirit, the body is secure and long-lived.
Bì said: Formless and unperceived, neither partial nor prominent—thus all things can turn toward it without harm or obstruction.
Páng said: First, there is peace; then, there is stability; then, there is flourishing.

Music and delicacies—
Passing guests will stop.
The Sage Emperor said: *Music* refers to music; *delicacies* to food and drink. It means that when a household has music and food, passing guests will linger.
Another explanation: Music gathers through sound; delicacies through flavor. Passing guests may pause briefly, but not for long.
Thus, even connected dwellings cannot be long inhabited—
Ren and righteousness bring many demands,
So the sovereign, embodying the Dao in tranquility and simplicity,
At first removes the scrutinizing governance,
And finally transforms the people into a pure and harmonious state.
Thus, the text concludes: *Its use cannot be exhausted.*

Hé said: *Delicacies* means *beauty*. *Passing guests* are *one*—when people delight in the beauty of the Dao, they pause as one. *One* means to depart from fullness and dwell in emptiness, drifting like passing guests.
Páng said: There is sound and flavor, so people take pleasure and linger.

The Dao’s spoken expression is
Dull and without taste.
The Sage Emperor said: The sovereign teaches with moral cultivation and tranquility. At first, when spoken, it is dull and without taste—unlike the common teachings of praise and blame.

The Dao enters and exits through speech,
Dull—dull, not like the five flavors with their sour, salty, bitter, sweet, and spicy tastes.
To gaze upon it is insufficient to see,
Hé said: *Sufficient* means *obtained*.
The Dao is formless, unlike the five colors—green, yellow, red, white, and black—which can be obtained by sight.

To listen to it is insufficient to hear,
Hé said: The Dao is not like the five tones—*gong*, *shang*, *jiao*, *zhi*, and *yu*—which can be obtained by hearing.

Its use cannot be exhausted.
The Sage Emperor said: With the Dao as steadfast tranquility, initially there is no spoken teaching. Thus, to gaze upon it is insufficient to see, to listen to it is insufficient to hear, yet pure customs flourish, all things thrive abundantly, and the year’s harvests overflow—thus its use cannot be exhausted. *Exhausted* means *fully spent*.

Hé said: When the Dao governs the state, the state is secure and the people prosper; when applied to the self, life is prolonged without end.

Pì said: This speaks of the Dao’s profound greatness. When people hear its words, they find them less appealing than music or delicacies, yet they spontaneously move and delight the heart in response to the times. Music and delicacies can make passing guests pause, but the Dao’s spoken expression is dull and without taste—insufficient to please the eye when gazed upon, insufficient to delight the ear when heard. If it seems to lack appeal, its use is yet inexhaustible.

**Chapter Thirty-Six: When One Would Gather, One Must First Spread**—
When one would gather, one must first spread it wide.

Hé said: *"He who first spreads it wide seeks to exhaust its excess and decadence.*
*When one would weaken it, one must first strengthen it.*
Hé said: *He who first strengthens it seeks to bring upon it misfortune and calamity.*
*When one would destroy it, one must first raise it up.*
Hé said: *He who first raises it seeks to make it proud and perilous.*
*When one would take from it, one must first give to it.*
Hé said: *He who first gives to it seeks to exhaust its greedy heart."*

Pī said: *"The interplay of yin and yang revolves like a turning wheel, reverses like a saw’s pull—when it reaches its extreme, it must return; when it is at its fullest, it must decline. Observe the moon’s waning after fullness, the sun’s reversal at its zenith, and one sees that all things conform to this principle, which is undeniably clear. Only the sage deeply grasps the masterful mechanism, understanding the formless Dao, thus:
In nurturing life, he adapts to contraction and expansion;
In conducting himself, he aligns with growth and decline;
In managing affairs, he knows the strategies of success and failure;
In confronting enemies, he masters the tactics of capture and release.
The ancients thus responded to myriad transformations with effortless ease—this is truly the Dao’s subtlety, which even ghosts and spirits cannot fathom, let alone the common folk. The *Yijing* says:
*The bending of the inchworm seeks its stretching;
The coiling of the dragon and serpent preserves its life.*
This is called *micro-enlightenment*.
The Sage Emperor said: *The *Yijing* states: ‘Upright speech seems contrary,’ and ‘The compliant can act with flexibility.’ Flexibility is what appears contrary to principle yet aligns with righteousness. Thus, the noble act with flexibility while adhering to righteousness, while the petty use it for deceit and trickery. Confucius said: ‘One may stand with him, but cannot act with flexibility.’ Truly so.
Thus, the Old Master in the previous chapter demonstrated *embracing the Great Image*—this is called *substance*. This chapter follows with *gathering and spreading*—this is called *flexibility*. To measure the empty root of nature, he covers substance with flexibility, revealing their interplay and leading back to the realm of life and death. Yet the confused say, ‘Those who forget morality and virtue are lost’—how deluded they are!
To gather and restrain the desires of all beings, one must first spread them wide, exhausting their extravagant hearts, so that they themselves become entangled in love and desire—then restraint follows. The principles of strength and weakness are similarly applied. This Dao is exceedingly subtle, yet its effects are clearly manifest, hence it is called *micro-enlightenment*.
Hé said: *These four matters are subtle in their Dao, yet their effects are clear.*
Pī said: *To remove the strong and violent, to eliminate chaos, one must use these four methods, exploiting the nature of things so that they destroy themselves. Hence it is called micro-enlightenment. When their spreading is sufficient, yet they still demand more, they become like the image of contraction. If their spreading is insufficient and they change, those who seek more will only grow bolder, while one’s own position becomes perilous.*

Pī said: *This principle is subtle yet clear; only those who discern the early signs can recognize it. The *Yijing* says: ‘To know the early signs is divine.’ The early signs are the subtle movements that precede good fortune. This is the marvelous application of the Dao, by which sages govern and command, aligning with the transformations of nature. Hence, this chapter concludes the *Dao Jing* with its meaning.
The compliant overcomes the rigid; the weak overcomes the strong.*
The Sage Emperor said: *The compliant and yielding can act with flexibility; flexibility in action allows one to control things, thus knowing that the weak and compliant must overcome the rigid and strong.*
Hé said: *The compliant endure long, while the rigid perish swiftly.*
Pī said: *Observing form reveals how the rigid dominate the compliant, but understanding principle reveals how the compliant overcome the rigid. The sage deeply grasps the meaning of micro-enlightenment, thus remaining modest without arrogance, empty without excess, not contending with others—yet none can contend with him either.
Nevertheless, this Dao is but a spoken principle. As for ever-changing transformations, who can define which is rigid or compliant, which is weak or strong? Even if rigid and strong, if the compliant and weak do not prevail, it is because their actions align with principle while their hearts never stray from compliance and weakness. From this, one again knows that the compliant and weak overcome the rigid and strong.
These two are called *sharp instruments*—one version reads: ‘The compliant and weak overcome the rigid and strong.’
A fish cannot escape from the depths.*
Hé said: *A fish escaping the depths is to leave rigidity for compliance—it can never return.*
*A state’s sharp instruments must not be shown to others.*
The Sage Emperor said: *Escape means loss. Sharp instruments refer to the Dao of flexibility. The Dao of flexibility must not be revealed to those unworthy, hence the analogy: A fish lost from the depths will surely be taken by others; if the Dao of flexibility is shown to the unworthy, it will be stolen for deceit and trickery.*
Hé said: *Sharp instruments are the Dao of flexibility. Those who wield state flexibility must not reveal it to their ministers; what should not be spoken must not be revealed to the unworthy.*
Pī said: *Sharp instruments are instruments beneficial to the state, using only the nature of things without relying on form to govern them. If the instrument is unseen yet all things find their place, then it is the state’s sharp instrument. To show it to others is to employ coercion; coercion for the state’s benefit will fail. A fish escaping the depths will surely be lost; if the state’s sharp instrument is displayed in form to others, it will also surely be lost.*
Pī said: *The fish yields to compliance and weakness, hiding itself in the deepest recesses to preserve its life. The sage retreats into obscurity yet wields supreme flexibility, alone directing countless transformations beyond measure. Thus, he turns the heavens and earth with effortless power, yet the world sees no trace of his influence; his might subdues the realm, yet none can name its source. How could he expose his divine essence and let the masses debate it?
I have privately reasoned: What distinguishes the sage from others is his discernment of early signs. If one meets things with rigidity, those things’ rigidity becomes an insurmountable foe. All under heaven believe rigidity overcomes things, yet I alone dwell in the realm of compliant non-contention. When I act, who can resist me?"*

「Behold the Dao of Heaven, and it is clear that autumn and winter are the counterparts to spring and summer—this too is a truth. The sage hides himself in the deepest recesses, yet appears as compliant and weak, thus guiding all things within the unseen realm of technique, so that none can fathom his origin. This is what is called *the subtle and solitary transformation* (密用独化).

*The I Ching* says: *"The infant moves with power"* (婴以行权), and *Zhuangzi* says: *"From the fish, one gains strategy"* (於鱼得计). Their meaning aligns here, though their words may be elaborate, none can truly name its essence.

Only the enlightened, with divine insight, comprehend it and grasp its intent—then it suffices.

**Chapter Thirty-Seven: The Dao of Non-Action (道常无为)**

The Dao ever abides in non-action.
*River* (河) says: *"The Dao regards things as evil when they are contrived."* (道以物为恶常也)
*Bì* (弼) says: *"It follows the natural order."* (顺自然也)
*Fán* (雱) says: *"Even when it acts, it never truly acts—yet nothing remains unacted upon."* (虽为之时，未尝有为，而无不为)

*Bì* says: *"All things are without fail governed and perfected through its action."* (万物无不由为以治以成之也)

*Fán* says: *"Though it appears as non-action, it never ceases to act."* (虽无为之时，未尝不为)

If lords and kings could uphold it,
*Fán* says: *"The ruler embodies the Dao to govern, thus relying on the seasons and their intrinsic principles without intent to act. Though he appears as non-action, he does not neglect the world’s affairs, yet truly, he never acts at all."* (君人体道以治，则因时乘理而无意於为，故虽无为而不废天下之为，而吾实未尝为也)

What does Heaven speak of? The four seasons pass, and all things flourish. (天何言哉，四时行焉，万物生焉)
The Dao of lords and kings is surely complete in Heaven. (侯王之道，天其尽之矣)
All things shall transform of their own accord. (万物将自化)

*Emperor Minghuáng* (明皇) says: *"The profound origin lies in stillness, hence the Dao of non-action."* (妙本谓静，故常无为)
Things shall be born, yet the Dao never ceases to act. (物将以生，而无不为也)
If lords and kings could uphold the Dao of non-action, then all things shall transform spontaneously. (侯王若能守道无为，则万物自化)
The ruler’s non-action is thus pure and unadorned. (君之无为，而纯朴矣)

*River* says: *"Speaking of lords and kings upholding the Dao, all things shall transform spontaneously—this is marvelous in itself."* (言侯王若能守道，万物将自化，妙於己也)

*Fán* says: *"They transform daily in the Dao yet remain unaware."* (日化於道而不知)

When transformation seeks expression,
*Fán* says: *"If transformation progresses daily like a child’s growth, it must reach a point where will and intent swell, and Heaven’s harmony wanes. Observe the mid-period of the Three Dynasties (三代中流): as things became refined and worn, this truth becomes evident."* (化而欲作，雱曰：化而日进则如婴儿之长，必至於志意充起，天和渐衰，观夫三代中流，物情雕敝，则可知也)

I shall restrain it with the nameless purity. (吾将镇以无名之朴)

*Emperor Minghuáng* says: *"When people follow the ruler’s transformation, they remain in non-action and stillness, yet if they then seek to move or contrive, I shall restrain them with the nameless purity."* (言人既从君上之化，已无为清静，而复欲动作有为者，吾将以无名之朴而镇静之)
The nameless purity is the Dao. (无名之朴，道也)

*River* says: *"I (the ruler) am the body (身), the nameless purity is the Dao."* (吾，身也，无名之朴，道也)
All things transform, yet their effect is mine. (万物以化，效於己也)
If they further seek to craft deceits, the lord and king must restrain them with virtue and power (道德)." (复欲作巧伪者，侯王当身镇抚以道德)

*Fán* says: *"Once the world has transformed under the Dao, governance advances daily. When governance reaches its peak, names and realities are fixed, and the people, drawn onward, never return. Thus, one must always embrace the nameless purity to steady their will and intent, guiding them back to their original nature. Though this may rescue what has already been formed, it is already too late."* (天下既化於道，则日进於治，治极则名实俱立，民逐而不返，故常抱无名之朴以镇定其志意，使一於性本，虽然救於已成，则亦晚矣)

Therefore, at the moment they seek to act,
*Bì* says: *"When transformation nears completion, I shall restrain it with the nameless purity—yet I shall not be its master."* (化而欲作，作欲成也，吾将镇之以无名之朴，不为主也)
I shall also not desire. (亦将不欲)
*Bì* says: *"Not desiring means no competition."* (无欲竞也)

*Xiù* (秀) says: *"The sage’s embrace of purity and non-action is his natural state. Thus, though he responds to the world’s changes and entangles himself in affairs, he never departs from it. How could he desire to warn the world of disorder and then act? If one acts because they desire to act, they already possess the reality of contrived desire—how could this be the true nameless purity? Only the one who remains undivided and unaware yet naturally so, does not seek to be pure but remains ever pure."* (圣人之抱朴无为，乃其自然，故虽应世之变，纠纷於事物之时，未尝离也，岂欲警天下之乱而后为之哉。 夫欲为而为之，则据此已有为欲之实，岂真无名之朴欤。 唯浑然常一，不知其然而自然者，不期为朴而朴常全也)

Not desiring through stillness. (不欲以静)
*River* says: *"The lord and king restrains with virtue and power (道德), yet the people shall not desire to change. One must lead them with stillness and purity."* (言侯王镇抚以道德，民亦将不欲改，当以清静导化之也)

The world shall rectify itself. (天下将自正)

*Emperor Minghuáng* says: *"When the ruler restrains the people with the nameless purity, he must not cling to it lest they find a path (逵) to deviate. If he fears they will advance and lose their root, or fall back into contrivance, he must also forget this purity. Not desiring in non-desire, non-desire is forgotten. Thus, tranquil and still, the world rectifies itself."* (言人君既以无名之朴镇静苍生，不可执此无名之朴而令有逵，将恐寻进丧本，复入有为，故於此无名之朴，亦将兼忘，不欲於无欲，无欲亦忘，泊然清静，而天下自正矣)

*Fán* says: *"If one desires and acts, they stray from their nature. Straying from nature is not rectification—how could one who is not yet rectified rectify the world?"* (欲而动则离性，离於性则非正也，己且未正，安能正天下哉)
Thus, only the still and non-desiring can rectify themselves and thereby rectify all things. (故唯不欲而静者，能正己而物正也)

This is the Dao’s efficacy, yet the Dao is also lost here. (此为道之效，而道亦丧於此)
Thus, the Daoist Classic concludes. (故道经终焉)

*#1何 (原作『句』, 据正统道藏本唐玄宗御注本改)*

**Volume Six of the *Zhuangzi* with *Dao* Commentaries (道德真经集注卷之六)**

*Emperor Minghuáng* (明皇), *River* (河), *Bì* (王弼), and *Fán* (王雱) commentaries.»

**Chapter Thirty-Eight of the *Dao De Jing* with *Dao* Commentaries (上德不德章第三十八)**

**上德不德** (The Supreme Virtue/Power Does Not Virtue/Power)
*River* (河) says: "Supreme Virtue/Power refers to the sovereign of the primordial age, before names were established. Their Virtue/Power was boundless and without equal, hence 'Supreme Virtue/Power.' To say they did not Virtue/Power means they did not use Virtue/Power to instruct the people. Instead, they followed nature spontaneously, nurturing the lives and essences of all things. Their Virtue/Power was not overtly displayed, hence 'did not Virtue/Power.' Thus, they possessed Virtue/Power.
*River* (河) says: "Their Virtue/Power harmonized with Heaven and Earth, blending *qi* in seamless flow, allowing the people to thrive fully. Thus, they truly had Virtue/Power.
*Fán* (王雱) says: "The person of complete Virtue/Power embodies the Dao fully and exhausts the constants of nature. Beyond Virtue/Power, there is nothing else to be seen—thus, no name of Virtue/Power remains. The Supreme Virtue/Power does not display Virtue/Power, for it is already complete.

**下德不失德** (Lower Virtue/Power Does Not Lose Virtue/Power)
*River* (河) says: "Lower Virtue/Power refers to the sovereign of the named age, whose Virtue/Power does not reach the level of the Supreme Virtue/Power, hence 'Lower Virtue/Power.' To say they do not lose Virtue/Power means their Virtue/Power is visible, and their achievements can be praised. Thus, they lack Supreme Virtue/Power.
*Emperor Minghuáng* (明皇) says: "Virtue/Power is the application of the Dao. *Zhuangzi* says: 'When things are allowed to flourish, it is called Virtue/Power.' In the pure and ancient times, Virtue/Power was not yet manifest, and there was no name for it—thus, it was called 'did not Virtue/Power.' Yet their pure Virtue/Power did not disperse; their non-action refined the transformation, hence 'thus, they possessed Virtue/Power.' When Virtue/Power declined, its functions became more pronounced, though the heart still embodied the Dao. The traces, however, involved some action—thus, they could be named as Virtue/Power, hence 'did not lose.' The traces involved action; thus, this is inferior to the Supreme, hence 'thus, they lacked Supreme Virtue/Power.'
*River* (河) says: "They established names and titles for themselves.
*Fán* (王雱) says: "When Virtue/Power reaches its fullness, it transcends the name of Virtue/Power. The people of Lower Virtue/Power merely attain the Virtue/Power of their age—this is not the Virtue/Power spoken of by the Supreme Sage.

**上仁为之** (The Supreme Benevolence Acts)
*River* (河) says: "Supreme Benevolence refers to the sovereign who practices benevolence without limit, hence 'Supreme Benevolence.' To say they act means they act with benevolent grace. Yet they do not cling to benevolence.
*Emperor Minghuáng* (明皇) says: "Benevolence is the name for universal love. When Lower Virtue/Power declines, Supreme Benevolence appears, acting as universal love. Thus, they act with benevolence. Yet in practicing benevolence, they forget benevolence, also desiring to attain non-action. Thus, they do not cling to benevolence—this is when the heart acts, yet the traces do not.
Yet, the Supreme Benevolence is called non-action, for it seeks to align with the Supreme Way by appearing without action, though it cannot yet speak to the action of Lower Virtue/Power.
*River* (河) says: "When achievements are complete and affairs established, there is nothing to cling to as Virtue/Power.
*Fán* (王雱) says: "Benevolence is the longest form of goodness, another name for Virtue/Power. Since it is distinct from Virtue/Power, it is called 'acting.' Yet the Sage’s benevolence is complete in nature and requires no further cultivation—thus, they do not cling to it.

**上义为之** (The Supreme Righteousness Acts)
*River* (河) says: "Righteousness acts by distinguishing and severing.
Thus, they do cling to righteousness.
*Emperor Minghuáng* (明皇) says: "Righteousness means to judge what is wrong. It is called 'acting' because they judge what is wrong. Yet they cling to righteousness to distinguish and sever, allowing things to find their proper place—this is when both the heart and traces involve action.
*River* (河) says: "They move and act for themselves, killing others to establish their authority, and plundering the lower to sustain themselves.
*Fán* (王雱) says: "When benevolence is insufficient, they resort to righteousness.

**上礼为之** (The Supreme Ritual Acts)
*River* (河) says: "Supreme Ritual refers to the sovereign whose ritual is without limit, hence 'Supreme Ritual.' To say they act means they establish ritual systems, measures, and ceremonial decorum. Yet these are not responded to.
*River* (河) says: "Their rituals are extravagant in form yet lacking in substance, filled with falsehood and complexity, and any movement departs from the Dao—thus, they are not responded to. Then they roll up their sleeves and enforce them.
*Emperor Minghuáng* (明皇) says: "When the six relationships are not harmonized, they act with rituals to remedy them. Rituals emphasize reciprocity—if there is no reciprocity, it is not ritual. When they perform rituals upon others, yet others do not respond, they roll up their sleeves in anger and enforce them, drawing others along by force."

「○ *River* says: When rituals become excessive and unanswered, the sovereign and subjects clash in anger, thus they roll up their sleeves and drag one another into contention.

○ *Páng* says: Rituals serve to establish the hierarchy between sovereign and subjects, distinguish near and distant relationships, and determine what is exalted or diminished. Through myriad distinctions, gains and losses become apparent. Yet when sincerity is lost, external forms are fabricated. Once these forms are rigidly enforced, expectations deepen. Though rituals may be named as tools to curb vice, in truth they become the seeds of strife.

I have observed that rituals, like fire, are essential to material existence, yet their proper season is summer—when all things depart from their roots and flourish at their extremities. Examining the four seasons, one sees that rituals are unavoidable even for the Sage. If the aim is to return to the root and abandon the extremities, then condemning rituals is also unavoidable. The Sage’s teaching adapts to the times—what constancy can there be? Yet the call to return to the root is crucial for the learner.

Thus, when the Dao is lost, virtue arises.
*River* says: When the Dao declines, virtuous transformation emerges.

When virtue is lost, benevolence arises.
*River* says: When virtue fades, loving-kindness becomes manifest.

When benevolence is lost, righteousness arises.
*River* says: When benevolence wanes, distinctions of righteousness clarify.

When righteousness is lost, rituals emerge.
*Emperor Minghuáng* says: To lose the Dao is to lose the highest virtue—since the highest virtue aligns with the Dao, it is called losing the Dao.

Virtue, morality, benevolence, and righteousness are merely the names for the times’ simplicity or complexity. Thus, when the Dao declines, virtue appears; when virtue is lost, benevolence remains; when benevolence vanishes, righteousness is established; when righteousness is abandoned, rituals intervene—these are but adaptations to the times.

Therefore, discussing rituals in an age of purity and simplicity is either madness or heresy, while forgetting rituals in an age of corruption and decadence is either folly or deceit. If one can dissolve and transform them, they should retreat from rituals and practice righteousness, retreat from righteousness and practice benevolence, retreat from benevolence and practice virtue, and forget virtue to merge with the Dao. When people return to simplicity, the highest virtue manifests in non-action.

Rituals are the thinning of sincerity and trust—
*River* says: Rituals abandon the root to govern the extremities, causing sincerity and trust to dwindle daily—
and the origin of chaos.
*Emperor Minghuáng* says: Those who establish rituals do so because sincerity and trust have weakened, making rituals the first cause of disorder.

The use of rituals lies in pacifying the sovereign and ordering the people—what need is there for jade and silk?

○ *River* says: Rituals value form over substance, so uprightness diminishes while deceit and disorder flourish.

○ *Páng* says: The Dao of antiquity descended into imperial virtue, and imperial virtue further descended to become the rule of kings. Only then did kings begin to rely on rituals for governance. Since the age of kings, the customs of the world have become apparent. Thus, the claim that rituals are the first cause of chaos is no mere fabrication.

Foreknowledge is the ornament of the Dao—
*River* says: To speak of knowledge before attaining it is foreknowledge—this is the true loss of the Dao and the superficial attainment of its ornament. It is the beginning of folly.
*Emperor Minghuáng* says: Foreknowledge refers to human perception—it means imposing these external checks on ritual before the natural unfolding of human perception. Though it may seem to dull the times, it actually erodes simplicity, hence it is the ornament of the Dao.

Rituals, meant to curb chaos, seek harmony, yet those who violate ritual intent will prize jade and silk while their people become deceitful. Such is the nature of human folly—its beginning lies in ritualistic pretense.

○ *River* says: Those with foreknowledge are the instigators of ignorance.

○ *Páng* says: Wisdom resides in the virtue of assisting others, just as the essence of plants manifests in their blossoms. Though blossoms may beautify a moment, their abundance signals the decline of the root. Wisdom is also the essence of virtue, naturally meant to be deeply rooted, yet it is exposed externally as foreknowledge.

All things follow their natural course. When their time arrives, they respond—why anticipate their signs in advance? Through myriad distinctions, one displays cleverness, and the world sees this as prescience. Yet the Sage regards it as the beginning of true folly.

Confucius said: *"Do not anticipate deceit, nor presume upon dishonesty—how I detest mere superficiality!"*

Thus, the virtuous person abides in substance—
*River* says: The virtuous person, meaning the sovereign who has attained the Dao, abides in substance.
To abide in substance is to abide in sincerity and trust—
*River* says: Not to place oneself in contradiction to the Dao, lest the world become embroiled in turmoil.
And not in ornament.
*Emperor Minghuáng* says: Action is the thinning of the Dao. Rituals and righteousness are the ornaments of virtue. Therefore, the Sage engages in non-action, abiding in substance rather than ornament. Retreating from the practice of rituals and righteousness, one naturally returns to sincerity and trust.

○ *River* says: Do not exalt speech.

Thus, discard the superficial and take the substantial.
*Emperor Minghuáng* agrees with *River*’s commentary.

○ *River* says: Abandon the superficial and ornamental, embrace the substantial and real.

○ *Bì* says: Virtue is that which is attained—constantly possessed without loss, beneficial without harm, hence it is called *virtue*.

How does one attain virtue? Through the Dao.
How does one fully realize virtue? Through non-action.

To employ non-action means all things are carried within it. Thus, without it, nothing escapes its influence; with it, nothing suffices to preserve life. Therefore, though heaven and earth are vast, they govern with non-action at their core, and though the Sage-King is great, they rule with emptiness as their foundation.

Thus, it is said: *"To restore and observe is to perceive the heart of heaven and earth; to reflect upon it at the zenith of the sun is to behold the essence of the ancient kings."*

Therefore, to extinguish selfishness and lose the self is to have all under heaven gaze in reverence, near and far flocking without end. To distinguish oneself and harbor intentions is to fail to preserve even one’s own body—flesh and bone cannot coexist in harmony. Hence, those of the highest virtue use only the Dao, without clinging to virtue, without grasping or employing it. Thus, they possess virtue yet perform no deeds, attain without seeking, accomplish without acting—though they have virtue, they bear no name of virtue.*»

The lesser virtue seeks and obtains, acts and accomplishes—thus establishing goodness to govern things, and so the name of virtue arises.

To seek and obtain is to necessarily lose something; to act and accomplish is to necessarily fail. When the name of goodness emerges, evil inevitably follows. Thus, the lesser virtue acts and yet still clings to its deeds.

Those who do not cling to deeds act without partiality.

All who cannot act without acting are of lesser virtue—such are *rén* (仁), *yì* (義), *lǐ* (禮), and *jié* (節).

To distinguish between higher and lower virtue, one must contrast lesser virtue with greater virtue. When one reaches the point of not clinging to deeds, they have exhausted the measure of lesser virtue—this is the highest *rén* (仁).

Yet even when one reaches the point of not clinging to deeds, they still act. To act without clinging is to exhaust all deeds.

The root lies in *wuwei* (無為), the mother in *wu* (無名). To abandon the root and grasp the child, though the deed be great, it will inevitably fail. Though the name be beautiful, deceit will inevitably arise. If one cannot accomplish without acting, cannot govern without exertion, then one must act. Thus, those who practice vast, universal, and generous benevolence, yet love without partiality, are those who act without clinging.

If love cannot be all-encompassing, then there arise those who uphold justice, righteousness, and principle—those who resent injustice, aid the wronged, and assist others in their affairs, yet still act with intent. Thus, the highest *yì* (義) acts while clinging to deeds.

If righteousness cannot prevail, then there arise those who praise, adorn, and revere—those who value propriety and respect, who scrutinize and criticize, and from whom anger arises when not met. Thus, the highest *lǐ* (禮) acts, yet none respond, so they force compliance.

The ultimate extreme is the Dao alone. From this point onward, what is there to revere?

Thus, even if virtue is abundant and deeds are great, possessing all things, each still has its own virtue and cannot encompass all. Therefore, heaven cannot be the vehicle, earth cannot be the cover, and humanity cannot be the reverence. Though all things are precious, if they are used with *wuwei*, they cannot grasp the essence of not clinging.

To fail to grasp the essence of not clinging is to lose what is great. This is called "losing the Dao and then having virtue."

To use *wuwei* is to honor the mother of virtue, thus able to act without labor while things are governed. Below this, one loses the mother of use—unable to act with *wuwei* while valuing universal benevolence, unable to value universal benevolence while valuing righteousness, unable to value righteousness while valuing adornment and respect. This is called "losing virtue and then having *rén*, losing *rén* and then having *yì*, losing *yì* and then having *lǐ*."

For *lǐ* is where it begins: when sincerity and trust are not deep, when simplicity and openness are not thorough, when criticism is superficial, and when subtle conflicts arise. Benevolence and righteousness arise from within, yet their actions are still false—how much more so when they merely adorn externally and expect endurance? Thus, those who violate *lǐ* are deficient in sincerity and trust, and this is the origin of chaos.

The wise of old were those who foresaw and recognized—those of lesser virtue. They exhausted their intelligence for foresight, labored their wisdom for affairs, though they grasped the situation, their deceit grew ever more refined. Though they amassed praise, they lost sincerity. Though they labored, their governance became corrupt. Though they exhausted their wisdom, the people suffered more. To abandon oneself and entrust to things is to act with *wuwei* and attain tranquility; to cling to simplicity and naturalness is to disregard established norms. To heed what is gained while abandoning what is held—this is the flower of Dao, yet the root of folly.

If one obtains the mother of deeds, then all things act without refusal, all affairs are preserved without labor. To use without form, to govern without name—thus *rén* and *yì* can be manifest, *lǐ* and respect can be clear.

To bear upon the great Dao, to settle with *wu* (無名), then things have nothing to revere, the will has nothing to scheme. Each fulfills its integrity, affairs rely on sincerity—thus virtue is thick, righteousness is upright, and respect is pure.

To abandon what bears them, to forsake what generates them, to use what is formed, to labor with intelligence—then benevolence loses sincerity, righteousness becomes competitive, and respect becomes contentious. Thus, the thickness of virtue is not achieved by using *rén*, the uprightness of righteousness is not accomplished by using *yì*, and the purity of respect is not attained by using *lǐ*.

To bear upon the Dao, to unify with the mother—thus to manifest without reverence, to clarify without competition. To use the nameless, so names are sincere; to use the formless, so forms are complete. To guard the mother to preserve the child, to exalt the root to raise the branch—then form and name are both present, yet evil does not arise. Great beauty aligns with heaven, yet no artificiality emerges. Thus, the mother cannot be distant, the root cannot be lost. *Rén* and *yì* are what the mother generates, yet they cannot be the mother. Form and instruments are what the artisan creates, yet they cannot be the artisan.

To grasp the mother while using the child, to abandon the root while pursuing the branch—then names will have divisions, forms will have limits. Though extreme in greatness, there will inevitably be gaps. Though abundant in beauty, there will inevitably be suffering. Deeds lie in action—how can they suffice?

○ *Yún* said: To extricate oneself from the common flow and strive to emulate the virtue of the Sage-King—who among true great men can do this?

From the chapter *"The Ancient Who Obtained the One"* (第三十九) *"The Ancient Who Obtained the One"*...

「Emperor Minghuang said: *One* is the harmony of the Dao/Way, referring to the *chōng qì* [pure, unstructured qi]. Since its wondrous application lies in all things as *One*, it is thus called *One*.

○ **Hé** said: *Xī* [ancient] means *past*, and *One* is the *unmanifest* [wuwei], the child of the Dao/Way.

○ **Bì** said: *Xī* [ancient] means *beginning*, and *One* is the origin of numbers while also the ultimate of things. Each attains *One* as the principle of its generation, thus becoming the master. Once things attain this *One*, they are formed—then they abandon *One* to dwell in their completed state. Dwelling in completion means losing their mother, and thus all things split, wither, and falter.

The heavens obtain *One* to become clear,
○ **Hé** said: The heavens obtain *One*, thus able to manifest the *symbols* [of the cosmos] in purity and clarity.

the earth obtains *One* to become tranquil,
○ **Hé** said: The earth obtains *One*, thus able to remain stable and unmoving in quietude.

the spirits obtain *One* to become numinous,
○ **Hé** said: The spirits obtain *One*, thus able to transform and act without form.
○ **Yún** said: *Spirits* refers to the *ghost-spirits* [guishen]. *Numinous* is the scattering of the spirits.

the valleys obtain *One* to become full,
○ **Hé** said: The valleys obtain *One*, thus able to remain overflowing without exhaustion.
○ **Yún** said: *One* as *One* is nowhere absent—thus, the valleys, though empty, can respond, for *One* abides within.

all things obtain *One* to generate,
○ **Hé** said: All things must rely on the Dao/Way to arise and take form.

and the feudal lords obtain *One* to uphold the *perennial* [zhēn] virtue of the world.
○ **Hé** said: The feudal lords obtain *One*, thus able to bring stability and rectitude to the world.

Their attainment of *One* is this.

Emperor Minghuang said: Things obtain the Dao/Way through its application, and names are established based on this application. When the Dao/Way is present, names stand; when the application is lost, reality is forfeited. Thus, the heavens’ clarity, the earth’s tranquility, the spirits’ numinousness, the valleys’ fullness, and all things’ generation—all arise from the wondrous application of *One*, hence the saying, *Their attainment of One is this*.

○ **Hé** said: *Attainment* is also *admonition* [zhì]. It refers to the five matters below.

○ **Bì** said: Each attains this clarity, tranquility, numinousness, perenniality, fullness, and generation through its own *One*.

○ **Yún** said: *One* is undivided—present in all and everywhere. Its principle is ever *One*, without separate *Ones*—thus, only *One* can attain *One*; no other means can attain *One*.

The meaning of *One* is the utmost *precision* [of the cosmos], and only through *precision* can it become numinous. When numinous, it is fully realized, and yet the numinous as *virtue/power* [dé] remains ever in *One*.

If the heavens were to cling to clarity alone, they would risk splitting;
○ **Hé** said: The heavens must have the interplay of *yin-yang* [opposing forces], the expansion and contraction of day and night—if they were to seek only clarity without end, they would risk dividing and failing to be the heavens.

○ **Bì** said: The Dao/Way is used to attain clarity, not clarity to attain clarity. To preserve *One* is to attain clarity; to lose the application of clarity risks splitting. Thus, it is the foundation of deeds, and cannot be abandoned.

Hence, all things fear losing their foundation through neglect of their application.

If the earth were to cling to tranquility alone, it would risk erupting;
○ **Hé** said: The earth must have *high and low, firm and yielding*, the rhythms of the *Five Phases* [wu xing]—if it were to seek only tranquility without end, it would risk upheaval and failing to be the earth.

If the spirits were to cling to numinousness alone, they would risk ceasing;
○ **Hé** said: The spirits must have *kings and ministers, imprisonment and death, rest and decay*—if they were to seek only numinousness without end, they would risk stagnation.

If the valleys were to cling to fullness alone, they would risk drying up;
○ **Hé** said: The valleys must have *overflow and contraction, emptiness and substance*—if they were to seek only fullness without end, they would risk exhaustion and failing to be the valleys.

If all things were to cling to generation alone, they would risk annihilation;
○ **Hé** said: All things must follow the *seasons* [shí] in their birth and death—if they were to seek only generation without end, they would risk perishing and failing to be things.

If the feudal lords were to cling to their nobility alone, while seeking loftiness, they would risk faltering.

Emperor Minghuang said: Those who obtain *One* must not pride themselves in its application, lest they falter. The heavens must not cling to their clarity, lest they split; the earth must not cling to its tranquility, lest it erupt; the spirits must not cling to their numinousness, lest they cease; the valleys must not cling to their fullness, lest they dry up; all things must not cling to their generation, lest they perish. If the feudal lords pride themselves in their nobility, they will risk toppling.

The sacred teachings descend through the ages, fundamentally for the sake of *sentient beings* [shénlíng]. Though they elevate the heavens and earth in their clarity and tranquility, their ultimate convergence rests with the feudal lords preserving the *feminine* [yīn] and applying the Dao/Way.
○ **Hé** said: The feudal lords must humble themselves to serve the people, earnestly seeking the worthy—if they were to seek only nobility above others without end, they would risk toppling and losing their position.

○ **Yún** said: *One* as *One* is nowhere absent. To speak of its principle, words cannot exhaust it—yet even in human form, when people are first born, *precision* [jīng] is their foundation. From *precision*, the spirits gather; then the body and its symbols take form. Once *precision* is lost, the form dies and the spirits depart—this too reflects the principle of *One* being undivided.

*One* is the perennial foundation of the unmanifest.

Thus, nobility must take *humility* [jiàn] as its foundation,
○ **Hé** said: If one must seek eminence, it is with *lowliness* [bòjiàn] as the foundation—like *Yǔ* [Great Sage] and *Jì* [Sage of Agriculture] toiling in the fields, *Shùn* [Emperor Shun] fashioning vessels by the riverbank, and the Duke of *Zhōu* [Zhougong] dwelling in lowly halls.

and loftiness must take *humility* as its base.
○ **Hé** said: If the feudal lords seek nobility and height, the people are not lowly—those who govern the realm must take the people as their foundation. They must labor in humility to gather them, and let their *joyous and kind* [kǎidí] transformations prevail, lest there be separation and dispersal.

○ **Yún** said: If one must seek eminence, it is with *humility* as the foundation—through building walls and performing deeds, through *lowliness* to achieve *height*, for if the base is not firm, the structure will later collapse and become perilous.

○ **Yún** said: Water, among the *Five Phases*, is the first in number, yet it flows downward without contention—this is the nature of *yin-yang*. Those who know this Dao/Way, though they occupy noble heights, do not forget their humble foundations. Thus, they are secure in their positions, just as preserving the spirits while maintaining *precision* ensures the spirits remain ever present.

Therefore, the feudal lords refer to themselves as *lonely and solitary* [gūguà], not *hub* [wú].
*One* variant reads *hub*.
○ **Hé** said: *Lonely and solitary* symbolizes *isolation*; *not hub* means they cannot function like a *wheel-hub* [wú] for all spokes to revolve around. This is how they take *humility* as their foundation.

○ **Hé** said: The feudal lords, though most noble, can refer to themselves as *lonely and solitary*—this is not taking *humility* as their foundation? To enlighten others.»

「Is it not so? *Mínghuáng* said: *Lonely and solitary* and *not hub* are what the *few* [jǐ] detest, yet the feudal lords adopt these terms as their foundation. If not so, then it is clearly taking *humility* as *lowliness* [jiàn] instead. ○ **Hé** said: These are words of sighing and lamentation.

○ **Páng** said: *One* is the least in number yet the origin of all things; hence, knowing that the foundation lies in *lowliness*, one understands that *lowliness* is true *nobility*. Therefore, they bring forth *many praises* yet are without *praise*. (The original text reads *many chariots* [shùyú wúyú], but *Bì* corrects it to *many praises* [shùyù wúyù].) ○ *Mínghuáng* said: *Number* gives, yet is without giving; the *wheel-hub* is the origin of the *spokes* [fú]. *Number* bestows nobility, yet is without nobility; *lowliness* is the foundation of *nobility*. The *original* [yuán] is the origin of the *spokes*, and one must preserve the *original* to establish the *spokes*. *Lowliness* is the foundation of *nobility*, and one must uphold *lowliness* to secure *nobility*. Thus, I urge the feudal lords to take *lowliness* as their foundation, leading to this discourse of *number* and *spokes*.

○ **Hé** said: *Politics* [zhèng], *approaching* [jiù]—when people gather around the chariot, the *spokes* become the *wheel*, the *hub*, the *axle*, and the *yoke*, yet there is no name for the chariot itself. Hence, it is called the *chariot*, illustrating how the feudal lords do not name themselves with titles of *nobility*, thus achieving their true *nobility*.

○ **Páng** said: Those who know *One* take *lowliness* as their foundation, yet internally conceal their utmost *nobility*. Thus, the world cannot truly *noble* them nor *lowly* them; if they seek *praise* [yù] for themselves and constantly bring forth *praises*, how can this be true *praise*? (The variant reads *praise* as *chariot* [yú], which is incorrect.) They do not wish to be radiant like *jade* [yù], nor rugged like *stone* [shí]. ○ *Mínghuáng* said: *Radiant* [yángyáng] refers to the appearance of *jade*; *rugged* [luòluò] refers to the appearance of *stone*.

○ **Páng** said: *Lowliness* is the greatest *virtue*. ○ **Hé** said: *Radiant* [yángyáng] symbolizes *scarcity*; *rugged* [luòluò] symbolizes *abundance*. *Jade* is scarce, hence it appears *noble*; *stone* is abundant, hence it appears *lowly*. This means they do not wish to be *noble* like *jade* nor *lowly* like *stone*—they must dwell in moderation.

○ **Bì** said: *Purity* [qīng] cannot be *purity* itself, nor *fullness* [yíng] be *fullness* itself; both have their *mothers* [mǔ] to preserve their forms. Thus, *purity* is not sufficient for *nobility*, nor *fullness* for *abundance*—*nobility* lies in its *mother*, yet the *mother* has no *noble* form. *Nobility* is rooted in *lowliness*, and *height* in *humility* as its base. Therefore, bringing forth *many praises* yet being without *praise* is the ultimate *virtue*. *Jade* and *stone*, radiant or rugged, are exhausted in their forms; hence, they are not desired.

○ **Páng** said: *Jade* and *stone* are firm in substance yet rigid in form, unable to bend or change—this is not what is meant by *One*. If all transformations arise yet remain *One*, then this is true *One*. Thus, *jade* is radiantly *noble* yet cannot be *lowly*, and *stone* is ruggedly *lowly* yet cannot be *noble*. The *Old Master* [Lǎo Shì] having clarified the meaning of *One*, fears those who do not comprehend it, so he takes *One* as unchanging, firm like *jade* and *stone*, thus losing the true principle of *One*.

Those who truly embody *One* are both *noble* and *lowly*; their *virtue/power* is like *water*, adapting to square or round, crooked or straight, responding to all things endlessly yet never departing from *One*. Thus, they cannot be truly *nobled* nor truly *lowly*—because *One* transcends *nobility* and *lowliness*. This chapter’s meaning is the most profound and difficult to express; I now roughly clarify its main points.

The *Dao/Way* generates *One*; *One* is the complete *virtue/power*, and when embodied in things, it approaches the *Dao/Way*. The previous chapter introduced the grand essence of the *Scripture on Virtue* [Déjīng], and this chapter discusses the *virtue/power* of the *Dao/Way*, just as the *Dao/Way* concludes with *small states and few people* [xiǎoguó guǎmín] and further emphasizes *trustworthy speech without adornment* [xìn yán bù měi].

**Chapter 40: The Dao/Way’s Movement Through Contrariness**
Contrariness is the movement of the *Dao/Way*. ○ *Mínghuáng* said: This clarifies *adaptation* [quán]. *Contrariness* is taken to mean *contrary to the scripture yet harmonizing with its essence*. ○ *Mínghuáng* said: *Contrariness* means *adaptation* [quán], and *adaptation* is the *Dao/Way*’s movement. When the *Dao/Way* adapts, it is the *conduct of the Sage*, and *conduct of the Sage* is the *Dao/Way*’s movement in action, hence the saying: *Contrariness is the Dao/Way’s movement*.

○ **Hé** said: *Contrariness* is the *foundation*; the *foundation* is what the *Dao/Way* uses to move. Movement generates all things; to turn away from it is to perish. ○ **Bì** said: *Height* is rooted in *humility* as its base, and *nobility* in *lowliness* as its foundation. To have *nothingness* as *use* is this *contrariness*. Movement arises from what has no form, thus all things are harmonized; hence, it is said: *Contrariness is the Dao/Way’s movement*.

○ **Páng** said: To return to the *foundation* is to attain *stillness* [jìng], and *stillness* is the capacity to move. For instance, autumn and winter can give rise to spring and summer.

**Chapter 41: The Earthly Sage Hears the Dao/Way**
*Weakness* is the *Dao/Way*’s use. ○ *Mínghuáng* said: This clarifies *reality* [shí]. *Weakness* is taken to mean *softness*, *gentleness*, and *feminine stillness*—*softness*, *gentleness*, and *feminine stillness* are how the *Sage* embodies *reality*. To embody *reality* is the *Dao/Way*’s constant use, hence the saying: *Weakness is the Dao/Way’s use*.

○ **Hé** said: *Softness* and *gentleness* are what the *Dao/Way* always employs, hence their enduring power. ○ **Bì** said: *Softness* and *gentleness* are universally effective; their limits cannot be exhausted. ○ **Páng** said: The *Dao/Way*’s use conquers all things—truly *healthy* [jiàn]. Yet *health* alone cannot sustain itself; it must take *weakness* as its foundation. This is the principle of *mutual generation*, hence the following passage returns to its origin to clarify it.

All things under the heavens are born from *having* [yǒu]. (The variant reads *all things* [wànwù].) ○ **Hé** said: All things arise from *Heaven* and *Earth*; *Heaven* and *Earth* have form and position, hence it is said they are born from *having*.

Yet *having* is born from *nothingness* [wú]. ○ *Mínghuáng* said: When *reality* [shí] is applied to *adaptation* [quán], it comes from *nothingness* generating *having*, hence those who practice *adaptation* must reverse to *reality*, and those who use *having* must rely on *nothingness*. Yet the supreme *Dao/Way* is empty and tranquil, detached from names; all *dharma* is inherently *void* [kōng], not depending on each other. If one can forget both *adaptation* and *reality*, and efface both *having* and *nothingness*, then *many chariots without chariots* [shùyú wúyú] can be said to transcend.

○ **Hé** said: *Heaven* and *Earth*’s spirits are divine and luminous; insects fly and worms wriggle—all arise from the *Dao/Way*. The *Dao/Way* has no form, hence it is said they are born from *nothingness*. This means the *origin* surpasses the *ornate*, the *weak* surpasses the *strong*, and *humility* surpasses *fullness*.

○ **Bì** said: All things under the heavens are born from *having*, yet the beginning of *having* is *nothingness*. To preserve *having* completely, one must return to *nothingness*.

○ **Páng** said: *Nothingness* generates *having*, and *having* returns to *nothingness*—they generate each other cyclically, and all things thus align in *One*.

The *Earthly Sage* [tǔshì] hears the *Dao/Way*.」

The **Superior Sage** hears the *Dao/Way* and diligently practices it.

○ **Emperor Minghuang** said: *Enlightenment* moves one to action.

○ **Hé** said: The **Superior Sage**, upon hearing the *Dao/Way*, acts of his own accord, striving with all his might.

○ **Bì** said: This is *resolve*.

○ **Páng** said: A **Sage** is a *learner*, so he values *resolve*. Without *resolve*, one can never speak of the *Dao/Way*.

The **Middle Sage** hears the *Dao/Way* as if it exists, as if it does not.

○ **Emperor Minghuang** said: The **Middle Sage** may ascend or descend, hence his doubt. Doubt makes him waver between existence and nonexistence.

○ **Hé** said: The **Middle Sage** must hear the *Dao/Way*, cultivating his person to prolong life, governing his state though it is not yet pacified, cherishing it with joy—yet when confronted with wealth, desire, honor, or passion, he loses it again.

○ **Páng** said: This is the man who hears the Master’s *Dao/Way* and takes delight in it.

The **Lesser Sage** hears the *Dao/Way* and laughs at it.

○ **Emperor Minghuang** said: The **Lesser Sage** is confused and disbelieving, hence his laughter.

○ **Hé** said: The **Lesser Sage** is greedy, cruel, and filled with desires. Seeing the *Dao/Way* as weak, he fears it; seeing it as plain, he deems it vulgar—thus he laughs at it.

○ **Páng** said: The *Dao/Way* is vast yet seems unworthy; shallow men do not recognize it, hence they laugh. Truly, as the following text says, can the common folk truly perceive it?

Not to be laughed at is not enough to be the *Dao/Way*.

○ **Emperor Minghuang** said: If the *Dao/Way* is not laughed at by the **Lesser Sage**, it is not profound enough to be the supreme *Dao/Way*.

○ **Hé** said: If the *Dao/Way* is not laughed at by the **Lesser Sage**, it cannot be called the *Dao/Way*.

○ **Páng** said: A solitary form, presented to the world’s eyes—is that truly the *Dao/Way*? Hence the saying was established.

○ **Emperor Minghuang** said: *Jiàn* means *to establish*.

○ **Hé** said: *Jiàn* means *to set forth*. The following sentences establish how one with the *Dao/Way* should be.

○ **Bì** said: *Jiàn* comes from *establishing*.

○ **Hé** said: Those who illuminate the *Dao/Way* seem obscured.

○ **Bì** said: Bright yet not dazzling.

○ **Páng** said: Great clarity seems dim; to discern good and evil with sharpness is not to understand the *Dao/Way*.

The *Dao/Way* in simplicity seems ordinary.

○ **Emperor Minghuang** said: The **Superior Sage** acts yet seems inactive, in advancement seems retreat, in simplicity seems common—thus the **Middle Sage** doubts and the **Lesser Sage** laughs.

○ **Hé** said: *Yí* means *plain*. Those who embody the Great *Dao/Way* do not distinguish themselves, like the many trembling leaves.

○ **Bì** said: *Lèi* means *to assimilate*. The Great *Dao/Way* follows the nature of things, not forcing them—its plainness is unseen, yet it returns again, as if assimilating.

○ **Páng** said: *Zhuangzi* says: *"To level the uneven is to level."*

The *Dao/Way* in advancement seems retreat.

○ **Hé** said: Those who pursue the *Dao/Way* seem to lag behind.

○ **Bì** said: To place oneself behind yet lead, to set oneself aside yet preserve—

○ **Yú** said: *Mencius* says: *"The swift in advance are swift in retreat."* Thus, in pursuing the *Dao/Way*, one must follow the times and align with principle, nurturing it straightly—one must not rush for results, hence it seems retreat.

This is unlike the man who uproots seedlings.

**Superior virtue** is like a valley.

The **Emperor Minghuang** said: *To be empty and receptive, containing all things.*

○ **Hé** said: The person of **superior virtue** is like a deep valley—
they do not shame themselves for filth and turbidity.

○ **Bì** said: Not claiming one’s own virtue,
there is nothing to hoard.

○ **Yú** said: **Superior virtue** does not claim virtue,
yet its efficacy is inexhaustible—
like a valley, empty yet responsive.

**Great whiteness is like humiliation.**
The **Emperor Minghuang** said: *Pure yet containing impurity.*

○ **Hé** said: The truly white, if stained with humiliation,
does not flaunt itself.

○ **Bì** said: Knowing its whiteness,
yet guarding its blackness—
only then does great whiteness emerge.

○ **Yú** said: Knowing its whiteness,
yet guarding its blackness—
*Confucius* said: *"Dyed yet not stained,
dyed yet seeming humiliated."*

**Broad virtue seems insufficient.**
The **Emperor Minghuang** said: *Greatly accomplished yet practicing humility.*

○ **Hé** said: The person of vast virtue,
seems dull and lacking.

○ **Bì** said: Broad virtue is never full—
vast and formless, never satisfied.

○ **Yú** said: The clod-like, the overabundant—
how could they be broad in virtue?

**Establishing virtue seems lazy.**
The **Emperor Minghuang** said: *Fulfilling deeds yet not boasting.*

○ **Hé** said: Those who establish virtue,
seem to slacken, making themselves empty.

○ **Bì** said: *Lazy*—matching.
Establishing virtue follows things naturally,
neither forcing nor applying—
thus it seems lazy, unhurried.

○ **Yú** said: *Lazy*—temporizing.
Hasty desires for speed,
striving to achieve something—
how could this be virtue?
Only by following the times and aligning with principle,
as if lazy and sluggish,
can one achieve the great.

*Zhuangzi* says: *"Only when unavoidable does one act."*

**True substance seems lazy.**
The **Emperor Minghuang** said: *Simple and harmonious, blending light.*

○ **Hé** said: The unadorned person,
seems like colors faded, unclear.

○ **Bì** said: The truly simple
does not boast its simplicity—
thus it fades.

○ **Yú** said: Embracing nature, nurturing spirit,
moving among the world’s concerns—
in a thousand transformations, conforming to customs,
yet its truth remains constant.
Thus, none perceive its truth.
That old man of Han Yin,
fastidious in his truth,
separating himself from the world,
startled even Confucius’ disciples,
while the Sage deemed him a false modifier of Hun Dun—
was this not what is meant by *true substance*?

**The great square has no corners.**
The **Emperor Minghuang** said: *No petty angles established.*

○ **Hé** said: The person of great rectitude,
has no arbitrary curves or edges.

○ **Bì** said: Square yet not partitioned—
thus no corners.

○ **Yú** said: The great square is the body of the *Dao*.
If it has four corners,
its form is exhausted by what is seen—
its smallness is long past.

**Great vessels take long to mature.**
The **Emperor Minghuang** said: *Do not seek swift results.*

○ **Hé** said: The person of great capacity,
like the nine tripods or coral reefs,
cannot be hastily completed.

○ **Bì** said: Great vessels complete the world,
not clinging to partial achievements—
thus they mature late.

○ **Yú** said: The achievements of emperors
are not as swift as those of kings.

**Great sound is rare in voice.**
The **Emperor Minghuang** said: *No adornment in trifles.*

○ **Hé** said: Great sound is like thunder—
waiting for the moment to act,
illustrating the love of *qi* and rare speech.

○ **Bì** said: *Hearing yet not hearing*—rare sound,
a sound that cannot be heard.

If there is sound, there is division;
if there is division, it is no longer *official* and *commercial*—
it cannot govern the masses.
Thus, those with sound are not great sound.

○ **Yú** said: The *Dao* is full yet without boundary,
its essence ever still—
things cannot disturb it,
its sound is always sound, yet the world cannot hear it.

*Zhuangzi* says: *"In the silence, only harmony is heard."*
This is what is meant by *rare sound*.

**Great form is shapeless.**
The **Emperor Minghuang** said: *Thus it can respond to all kinds.*

○ **Hé** said: The person of great law and form,
unadorned, without appearance.

○ **Bì** said: If there is form, there is division—
those with division are neither too hot nor too cold,
yet if they have form, they are not great form.

○ **Yú** said: It can bestow form upon all things,
yet its essence is ever vast, unable to be possessed—
this is the complete body of the *Dao*.
Because it has things, it is called *great form*.

**The *Dao* is hidden, nameless.**
The **Emperor Minghuang** said: *Its functions are not displayed—
an unnamed master.*

○ **Hé** said: The *Dao* is concealed,
making people unable to name it.

○ **Páng** said: From *rare sound* downward,
all are the complete *Dao*,
yet their paths differ—
thus it is called *great sound*, *great form*.

If one embodies *great sound* and *great form* as the *Dao*,
then one’s *Dao* is complete.

Only when the *Dao* is complete
does thought fail to reach it.

In being, it is substance;
in non-being, it is emptiness—
everywhere thus,
nowhere absent—
yet to name it is impossible to describe.
Within the nameless, this thing is always present,
desired yet unseen—
thus it is called *hidden*.

Only the *Dao* is good at lending and completing.
The **Emperor Minghuang** said: *Though hidden and nameless,
it is truly good—
with boundless harmony and wondrous efficacy,
lending *qi* to all things and bringing them to fulfillment.*

○ **Hé** said: *Complete*—to fulfill.

Speaking of the *Dao*’s goodness in bestowing *qi*
and completing things.

○ **Bì** said: All these virtues
are the *Dao*’s achievements—
in form, it is *great form* yet shapeless;
in sound, it is *great sound* yet rare in voice.
Things are completed by it yet see no form—
thus it is hidden and nameless.

Its lending is not merely to supply deficiencies—
a single lending suffices to perpetuate virtue forever—
thus it is *good at lending*.

Its completing does not rely on craftsmanship’s cutting—
no thing it does not aid in forming—
thus it is *good at completing*.

○ **Páng** said: The *Dao* can supply all things’ needs and complete them,
yet what things receive returns to its source—
though the *Dao* abundantly sustains all things,
I have never been depleted.

「**Lending**—this is the act of responding to another’s deficiency and yet ultimately seeing it returned.

I have privately reflected on this passage: The *Dao* is not distant from people, yet the world cannot perceive it—this is because it is not illuminated. Hence, I begin with *Illuminating the Dao*. The *Great Dao* is most plain, yet only the enlightened can follow it to progress. Therefore, I next speak of *Following the Plain Dao* and *Advancing along the Dao*.

To advance along the *Dao* and return to the constant of *nature and life*, this is the *virtue without virtue*—thus, I next speak of *Establishing Virtue as the Humble*.

The *virtue of the Humble* is self-sustaining yet does not align with the common masses—thus, it is unstained and pure, for its whiteness inherently distinguishes it from things. How then could it be as tranquil as a valley? Hence, I next speak of *Establishing Virtue as the Unadorned*.

To be unadorned and yet pure is the narrowness of *Boyi*—how then could it be broad? Thus, I speak of *Establishing Virtue as the Humble* only after *Establishing Virtue as the Unadorned*.

The *virtue of the Humble* that can be broadened is the virtue of one who walks with the people yet can serve as a ruler and teacher—thus, I say *Establishing Virtue as the Unassuming*.
