The *Unassuming* does not eagerly seek to establish anything—*establishing virtue* means setting up a foundation, yet this risks straying from the true and approaching falsity. Therefore, it is essential to preserve my genuine nature, and thus I next speak of *Maintaining True Nature*.

With this progression, the *Dao* within me is now fully embodied—thus, I say *The Great Square has no corners*.

Having the *Great Square* as its foundation, one can then respond to the transmission of the *Dao* without bounds and thus weave the remaining threads for humanity—hence, I say *The Great Vessel*.

The *Great Vessel* is one’s *achievement*—the *Perfect Person* uses even the husks of grain, straw, and soil as vessels, yet the vessel is never small. Confucius called *Guan Zhong* a *small vessel*—then the *achievements of an emperor and king* are indeed the *Great Vessel*.

Though the *Dao* accomplishes these things, its ultimate purpose is not to dwell on them—it is never doing, never possessing—thus, I say *The Great Sound* and *The Great Form*. Hence, it is fully exhausted, yet it can align with the *Dao* as one and supply all things’ needs, completing their *nature*.

Ah! This *Dao*, one cannot recognize it with mere recognition—how much less can one describe it with words! Yet my discourse on it is but crude.

**Chapter 42: The Dao Gives Rise to Oneness**
The *Dao* gives rise to *Oneness*—**He** said: The *Dao*’s initial generation is *Oneness*.
*Oneness* gives rise to *Twoness*—**He** said: *Oneness* generates *Yin* and *Yang*.
*Twoness* gives rise to *Threeness*—**Emperor Minghuang** said: *Oneness* is the *Pungent Qi*—speaking of how the *Dao* moves to emit the *Pungent, Harmonious, and Marvelous Qi* into the principle of generating things, yet this alone is insufficient, so it further generates *Yang Qi*. *Yang Qi* cannot generate alone, so it further generates *Yin Qi*. The accumulation of *Pungent Qi* as *Oneness* is why it is said *Oneness* gives rise to *Twoness*. The accumulation of *Yang Qi* as *Twoness* is why it is said *Twoness* gives rise to *Threeness*.
**He** said: *Yin* and *Yang* generate the *Harmonious Qi*, and the three *Qi*—*Harmonious, Clear, and Turbid*—distinguish into *Heaven, Earth,* and *Human*.
*Threeness* gives rise to *all things*—**Emperor Minghuang** said: *Yin* and *Yang* contain and nurture, the *Pungent Qi* harmonizes and adjusts, and thus all things flourish and complete—hence, it is said *Threeness* gives rise to *all things*.
**He** said: *Heaven* and *Earth* together generate all things—*Heaven* bestows, *Earth* transforms, and *Human* nurtures them.
**Pang** said: The *Dao* encompasses *Yin* and *Yang*—it has *Yin*, it has *Yang*, and it has the *balance between Yin and Yang*. These three things are the beginning of the numbers *One, Two,* and *Three*, and all things’ kinds emerge and enter through them. Hence, their essence and form are most minute and exhaustive, and examining their patterns and symbols all conform to this. Knowing this is to know the origin of all things—knowing their origin, what remains of their end?

The *Perfect Person*’s use rests in the present yet knows the ten thousand ages thereafter—perhaps it lies here.

All things bear *Yin* yet embrace *Yang*—**He** said: All things without exception bear *Yin* yet turn toward *Yang*, revolving inward to return to the *Sun* and the *Pungent Qi* as harmony.
**Emperor Minghuang** said: All things obtain *Yin, Yang,* and *Pungent Qi* to generate and complete—hence, they bear and embrace *Yin* and *Yang*, containing and nourishing the *Pungent Qi* as gentle and harmonious.
**He** said: Within all things lies the *Original Qi*—obtaining it brings harmony and gentleness, like the *breasts having their store* and *bones their marrow*, or how *plants and trees have hollow spaces* and *paths for Qi* to flow, thus they can endure long life.
**Pang** said: Through this, they generate—thus, there is nothing that does not bear and embrace. Observing my form reveals its principle.

When *Yin* and *Yang* are balanced in moderation, harmony arises. All this illustrates how all things are governed by *Yin* and *Yang*—bound by numbers, they must correspond to their waxing and waning.

What people detest is *loneliness, widowhood,* and *not being filled*—yet rulers and nobles take these as their titles.
**Emperor Minghuang** said: All things take the *Pungent, Harmonious Qi* as their foundation, and the *Pungent Qi* that is harmonious, gentle, and guards the foundation must be humble and weak. Hence, though rulers and nobles are most exalted, they take *loneliness, widowhood,* and *not being filled* as their titles—this is because humility and gentleness are their foundation.
**He** said: *Loneliness, widowhood,* and *not being filled* are names of *non-divinity*, yet rulers and nobles take them as titles—this is to dwell in humility, following the law of *emptiness, spaciousness,* and gentle harmony.
**Pang** said: When *Yin* reaches its extreme, it generates *Yang*; when *Yang* reaches its extreme, it generates *Yin*—this is the nature of *Yin* and *Yang*.
Thus, the high becomes low, and the low becomes full. Though *lords and kings* occupy the most exalted positions, their power is most perilous—hence, they take what people detest as their own names, to adapt to the harmony of *Yin* and *Yang*—thus, the following passage states:

Hence, things may be diminished yet benefit, or benefited yet diminished.
**Emperor Minghuang** said: When one diminishes oneself, others benefit. When one benefits oneself, others are diminished. Thus, those who *return to the root* are good at descending, the humble and weak hold the power of life, and the titles of *loneliness, widowhood,* and *not being filled* are not inappropriate.

**He** said: To increase one’s height leads to collapse; to covet wealth invites calamity.
**Bi** said: All things take myriad forms, yet their return is to *Oneness*—how does one achieve *Oneness*? Through *non-being*.

From *non-being* comes *Oneness*—*Oneness* can be called *non-being*, yet *non-being* is already called *Oneness*—how can one avoid speaking of it?
If there is speech, there is *Oneness*—how is this not *Twoness*?
If there is *Oneness* and *Twoness*, then *Threeness* arises—following the *Oneness* of *non-being*, the numbers are exhausted here.

Beyond this point lies not the flow of the Dao.
Thus, though myriad things arise, I know their master:
Though forms may differ, their essence is one—*qi* undivided.

The common folk possess hearts,
Diverse lands, distinct customs,
Yet those who grasp *Oneness* rule as kings and lords.

To take *Oneness* as master—how can one command it?
The more one seeks, the farther one strays;
To diminish is to draw near.
When diminished to exhaustion, one attains its utmost.

Though called *Oneness*, it still reaches *Threeness*—
How much more so when its origin is not *Oneness*?
Yet to diminish and gain—is this not true speech?

**Yun** said:
Moisture nourishes the lowlands, mountains grow barren.
At noon, the sun’s arc reverses;
Deficit yields to fullness—
All things follow *yin-yang* cycles.
Numbers arise from *Oneness* and *Twoness*;
At their limit, they invert—
Thus all things conform.
The perfected one adapts to fullness and emptiness,
Follows the rhythms of ebb and flow,
Ever maintaining humility and openness in self-cultivation—
How could one forcefully resist and invite ruin?

What people are taught—
**He** said:
Common teaching urges the weak to become strong,
The yielding to become rigid.

Yet this is my teaching’s intent.
**Emperor Minghuang** said:
Laozi taught:
When rulers establish teachings to instruct others,
They should use the principle of *brimming* as *gently weak*—
Not empty words.

**He** said:
My teaching to the masses is to discard strength for weakness,
Rigidity for yielding.

**Bi** said:
My non-strength makes others follow it.
Using natural spontaneity, upholding ultimate principle—
To conform brings fortune, to defy brings calamity.
Thus, when people teach, defiance invites self-inflicted ruin.
Yet my teaching to others—do not defy it.

The overbearing perish without proper death—
**Emperor Minghuang** said:
The overbearing clash with all things;
Seeking gain, they lose—
Things strike them down, so they die untimely.

**He** said:
The overbearing are those who reject the Mysterious and Virtuous,
Betray the Dao, disregard teachings, and rely on power.
To die untimely means to be cut off by Heaven,
Slain by weapons, executed by law—
Not dying by natural lifespan.

I shall make this my foundational teaching.
**Emperor Minghuang** said:
I have seen the overbearing perish, the yielding survive—
Thus, I establish this teaching of gentle weakness as the parent of all teachings.

**He** said:
*Parent* means origin.
Laozi begins with the overbearing as a warning.

**Bi** said:
The overbearing will not die properly—
If one teaches others to be overbearing, they should not be.
To cite their untimely deaths as teaching is fortunate—
Those who follow my teaching will prosper.
Thus, those who defy it are best suited to serve as its warning.

**Yu** said:
To discern *yin-yang*’s nature reveals that all things have principle—
All can be models.
The overbearing meet ruin—
The utmost evil—
Yet those who grasp principle see it as a mirror for *ebbing and flowing*,
Making it the parent of teaching—is this not fitting?

**Chapter 43: The Ultimate Softness of the World**
The world’s ultimate softness
Overcomes the world’s ultimate hardness.

**Emperor Minghuang** said:
The world’s ultimate softness is true nature.
If one gallops through worldly affairs,
Stained by dusty realms,
Desires overwhelming—
Then one becomes the world’s ultimate hardness.

**He** said:
The ultimate softness is water;
The ultimate hardness is metal and stone.
Water penetrates hardness, enters rigidity—
Nothing it cannot pass through.

**Bi** said:
*Qi* enters everywhere;
Water flows everywhere.

**Yu** said:
The Sage governs all rigidity through softness—
Observed in water.

Without gaps—
**He** said:
*Without gaps* refers to the Dao—
Formless, boundless,
Entering and exiting without obstruction,
Communing with all life.

**Xiu** said:
If even the finest things obstruct,
How could one enter the gapless?

Observed in phenomena, it is the utmost emptiness—
Thus the Sage embodies the formless yet responds to all forms.

Hence, one knows the benefits of *wuwei*.

**Emperor Minghuang** said:
*Without gaps* means the heart is unstained by worldly dust,
Empty of all attachments.
*Gapless* refers to the Dao’s pure stillness,
Its wondrous, unified body—
No fissures exist.
If one is not disturbed by desires,
Heart and realm both still,
Empty of all attachments—
Then heart and Dao merge, entering the gapless.

Thus the Sage says:
*I see that a tranquil mind and body can unite with the Dao—*
Knowing that active teachings are not as beneficial as *wuwei*.

**He** said:
I see the Dao’s *wuwei* yet all things spontaneously transform—
Thus I know *wuwei* benefits humanity.

**Bi** said:
Empty, soft, weak—
Nothing it cannot penetrate,
Nothing it cannot exhaust.
The utmost softness cannot be bent—
Extending this, I know *wuwei*’s benefits.

**Chapter 44: Names and the Self**
Which is closer—name or self?
**Emperor Minghuang** said:
Names are guests of reality.
Those who sacrifice themselves for names perish—
Asking who is closer urges one to abandon fame and function,
Preserving the true self.

**He** said:
Names advance, the self retreats.

**Bi** said:
To prize fame and seek loftiness—
His body grows frail and sparse.
Which is more abundant—body or wealth?
**Emperor Minghuang** said:
Those who sacrifice themselves for fame seek wealth,
Yet gain wealth only to lose their bodies.
Asking which is more abundant urges one to discard jade and shatter pearls,
Preserving their harmony.

**He** said:
Wealth in excess harms the body.

**Bi** said:
Greed for wealth is insatiable—
Yet the body grows ever fewer.

**Mou** said:
*Zhuangzi* says:
*"Distinguish the boundaries of inner and outer,
Discern the realms of honor and disgrace—*
I have spoken before:
Though true and false may not intermingle,
Though noble and base stand far apart,
Yet they constantly shift in weight—
One must be ever vigilant.

Those who lose their nature
Forget their boundless possession,
Yet chase external things,
Hoarding endlessly, proud of their gains—
They think themselves fortunate,
Yet what they take is dust and filth, rot and stench,
Things not meant to be kept.
What they consume and lose is what defines *them*—
Their reckoning of closeness and distance, abundance and scarcity,
Is already foolish beyond measure—
How can one not pity them?

Only those who depend on nothing external
And attend solely to the inner
Can speak of the Dao.
Thus, Yanzi’s virtue earned Confucius’ praise
For his joy in the humble fare of the narrow lane—
Then the cultivation of the gentleman is clear.

Today’s scholars lack not for cleverness,
Yet their wills serve the external.
The external grows heavy, the inner light—
Their innate nature, once bright in liver and diaphragm,
Now lies beneath the surface of face and eyes,
Far from its root.
Yet they still seek to discuss the deepest truths of antiquity—
Their labor yields no fruit.

Observe Laozi’s words:
They seem meaningless at first glance,
Yet they are the scholar’s ultimate warning,
The essential task of self-cultivation.
Thus I expand their meaning and elaborate.

Which is the greater affliction—gain or loss?
**Emperor Minghuang** said:
Asking whether gaining fame and wealth
Or losing fame and wealth
Is the greater affliction to the body.

**He** said:
Love of profit brings corruption in conduct.

**Bi** said:
To gain much wealth yet lose one’s body—
Which is the greater affliction?

**Mou** said:
Gain brings possession,
Yet possession always lacks sufficiency.
Possession wearies the spirit and drains the essence in guarding it—
Its affliction is great indeed.

Thus, excessive love brings great expenditure.
**He** said:
Excessive love of beauty exhausts the spirit,
Excessive love of wealth invites calamity.
The loved is little, the lost is much—
Hence, it is called great expenditure.

Hoarding much brings heavy loss.
**Emperor Minghuang** said:
Excessive love of fame exhausts the spirit—
Is this not great expenditure?
Excessive wealth burdens the body—
Is this not heavy loss?

**Bi** said:
Excessive love cuts itself off from others,
Hoarding much refuses to disperse.
Many seek it, many attack it—
It is afflicted by external forces,
Thus great expenditure and heavy loss.

**Mou** said:
This is the most profound truth,
Yet the world remains unaware.

Knowing sufficiency brings no disgrace.
**He** said:
Those who know sufficiency
Are content with what they have, free of desire—
Their bodies suffer no disgrace.

**Mou** said:
What is noble lies within—
How could there be disgrace?

Knowing when to stop brings no peril.
**He** said:
Knowing when to halt,
Wealth and profit do not burden the body,
Sounds and colors do not confuse the senses—
Thus the body faces no peril.

**Mou** said:
Seeking nothing from without,
One remains ever at ease.

This can endure long.
**Emperor Minghuang** said:
Those who know sufficiency do not love excessively.
Those who know when to stop do not hoard excessively.
Since there is no disgrace or peril,
It can endure long.

**He** said:
If people know when to stop and suffice,
Fortune and blessings lie within themselves.
Those who cultivate the self exhaust not their spirits,
Those who govern the state disturb not their people—
Thus it can endure long.

**Chapter 45: The Great Fullness as if Deficient**

The great fullness as if deficient—
**He** said:
This refers to a sovereign of supreme **Dao** and **virtue**,
As if deficient—
He extinguishes fame and reputation,
Like the destruction of a broken vessel.
His use does not wear out.
**He** said:
If his governance is as such,
Then there will never be a time when it is exhausted.

**Mou** said:
When learning and practice reach great fullness,
They are always as if stained and incomplete—
Humility brings benefit,
So his governance never faces exhaustion or failure.

**Wang Rú** said:
This is true great fullness—
A lesser fullness stands alone as a complete entity,
And thus fails to attain the **Dao**.
*Zhuangzi* says:
*"Fame achieved brings loss."*

The great overflow as if emptied—
**He** said:
This refers to a sovereign of supreme **Dao** and **virtue**,
Overflowing with blessings.
He who is noble does not dare to be arrogant,
He who is rich does not dare to be extravagant.
His use does not run dry.
**Emperor Minghuang** said:
When blessings and rank are full,
They are always as if emptied and void—
Frugality does not harm wealth,
So his use never lacks.

**He** said:
If his governance is as such,
Then there will never be a time when it is exhausted.

**Wang Rú** said:
Great fullness is complete and abundant,
Yet it follows things and gives without favor,
So it seems as if emptied.

**Wang Yú** said:
Fullness leads to depletion—
How can there be endless use without exhaustion?

The great upright as if bent—
**Emperor Minghuang** said:
Upright yet not rigid,
So it seems as if bent.

**He** said:
Great uprightness refers to the rectitude of **Dao** and **virtue**,
Unchanging as one.
As if bent—
He does not contend with the vulgar,
Like something that can be bent and folded.

**Wang Rú** said:
Following things to be upright,
Uprightness does not lie in rigidity alone,
So it seems as if bent.

**Wang Xiù** said:
For those upright in principle,
Flexibility meets the changes,
Not clinging to their own rigidity,
So none see their uprightness.

The great skill as if clumsy—
**Emperor Minghuang** said:
Skill does not stray beyond its proper bounds,
So it seems as if clumsy.

**He** said:
Great skill refers to manifold talents,
Yet he does not dare to flaunt his abilities.

**Wang Rú** said:
Great skill arises from nature’s completion,
Not contrived as an eccentric deviation,
So it seems as if clumsy.

**Wang Yú** said:
Transmitting things to their natural fulfillment,
Then externally there is no artificial skill,
Yet true skill is attained—
Those who carve and shape all forms
Do not verify this.

The great eloquence as if tongue-tied—
**Emperor Minghuang** said:
Not embellishing trivial tales,
So it seems as if tongue-tied.

**He** said:
Great eloquence is wisdom without doubt.
As if tongue-tied—
His words are without artifice.

**Wang Rú** said:
Great eloquence follows things in speech,
Creating nothing of his own,
So it seems as if tongue-tied.

**Wang Yú** said:
Skillfully explaining all things,
This is the source of eloquence’s name—
But the highest truth is not verbose,
So it seems as if tongue-tied.

**Agitation overcomes cold**—
**He** said:
*Overcomes*—the extreme.

「Spring and summer, the **Yang qi** is agitated and swift above, all things flourish and reach their peak—
At the extreme, there is cold, and cold brings decay and death.
Thus, one should not be overly rigid and agitated.

**Staticness overcomes heat**—
**He** said:
In autumn and winter, all things rest beneath the Yellow Springs,
At the extreme, there is heat, and heat is the source of life.

**Clarity and stillness** are the **Dao/Way** of the world’s rectitude.
**Emperor Minghuang** said:
When agitation overcomes, there is cold—cold, thinness.
When staticness overcomes, there is heat—heat, harmony.
Thus, if one bends, it is the greatest straightness;
The clear and still is the rectitude of the world.

**He** said:
If one can maintain clarity and stillness,
They will endure as the world’s **long** (eternal).
To uphold rectitude is to have no end of days.

**Wang Rú** said:
Agitation, then, can overcome cold,
Stillness without action overcomes heat—
By this reasoning, clarity and stillness are the world’s rectitude.

Stillness preserves the **true nature** of all things,
Agitation offends the **nature** of all things—
Thus, only clarity and stillness can attain the above greatness.

**Wang Yú** said:
Agitation overcomes with superficiality,
Stillness overcomes with essence—
To overcome with essence is to attain the above greatness,
To overcome with superficiality is to make all movement small.
Thus, it is known that clarity and stillness are the utmost rectitude.

**Chapter Forty-Six: "The World in Harmony"**
When the **Dao/Way** prevails in the world...」

**Chapter Forty-Six: "The World in Harmony"**
*(continued)*

**Chapter Forty-Seven: "Not Leaving the Door"**

Not leaving the door,
One knows the world.

**He** said:
The **Sage** knows the world without leaving the door by understanding one’s own body to know others’ bodies, one’s own household to know others’ households—thus perceiving the world.

Not peering through a window,
One sees the **Dao/Way** of Heaven.

**Ming Huang** said:
With **non-action** and **spontaneous transformation** prevailing, no decrees are issued beyond the door. This is knowing the **principle** of governing the world—when human affairs are in harmony, the celestial patterns follow suit, so one need not laboriously peer through windows to know the **Dao/Way** of Heaven.

**He** said:
The **Dao/Way** of Heaven and the **Dao/Way** of humanity are the same; Heaven and humanity are interconnected, their **vital essence and qi** flowing through each other. If the ruler is **clear and still**, the Heavenly **qi** will naturally align. If the ruler is **excessively desirous**, the Heavenly **qi** will grow troubled and chaotic, and all matters of **auspiciousness, inauspiciousness, benefit, and harm** arise from within oneself.

**Wang Rú** said:
All things have their **root**, and all affairs have their **master**—though their paths differ, their destination is the same; though their concerns vary, their essence converges. The **Dao/Way** has its **great constancy**, and **principle** has its **great governance**—if one holds to the ancient **Dao/Way**, one can guide the present. Even when dwelling in the present, one can discern the origins of antiquity, hence the saying: "Not leaving the door nor peering through windows, yet one can know."

**Wang Yú** said:
The myriad affairs of the world and the subtle **Dao/Way** of Heaven all share the same **root in human nature**. The ultimate refinement of this **root** is to **fulfill one’s true nature**—closing the eyes and ears yet perceiving all things. If one merely sees and then recognizes, then recognizes and then knows, this is grasping the myriad distinct forms while obscuring the singular **principle** of **governance**. Thus, what they call "recognition and knowledge" is merely the superficial use of ears and eyes, not the profound essence of the heart’s **expansion**. They claim to be **comprehensive**, yet they know nothing of their utter **paucity**; they claim to be **wise**, yet they know nothing of their extreme **foolishness**. The farther they go, the less they know.

**Ming Huang** said:
If one cannot **abide in non-action**, even if one issues decrees far and wide, one’s understanding of the **principle** of governing the world will only grow shallower.

**He** said:
It means departing from one’s own household to observe others’ households, abandoning one’s own body to observe others’ bodies—the farther one looks, the less one truly sees.

**Wang Rú** said:
**Non-action** lies in **unity**, yet one seeks it among the myriad—**Dao/Way** is invisible when gazed upon, inaudible when listened to, unattainable when grasped. If one truly knows it, there is no need to leave the door. If one does not know it, the farther one goes, the more lost one becomes.

**Wang Yú** said:
The **principle** of the **Ultimate** is fully realized in a single speck of dust—no trifling thoughts arise, and all myriad **affiliations** manifest. The **key** to learning the **Dao/Way** lies here. Yet they seek it far afield—this is already to have lost the way. The farther they go, the less they know—is this not so?

Hence, the **Sage** knows without action,
**Ming Huang** said:
Not leaving the door, hence the saying "without action"—**spontaneous transformation** in **purity and simplicity**, and knowing is the **principle** of **governance**.

**He** said:
The **Sage** neither ascends to Heaven nor descends to the depths, yet knows Heaven and Earth through the heart’s **perception**.

Not seeing yet naming,
**Ming Huang** said:
Not peering through windows, hence the saying "not seeing"—when humanity is in harmony and Heaven follows, one can rightly name the era as **"Great Peace"**.

**He** said:
When the ruler **upholds virtue/power**, the people **love virtue/power**; when the ruler **loves martial ways**, the people **love strength**. The **Sage** discerns the **small** to grasp the **great**, examines the **inner** to know the **outer**.

**Wang Rú** said:
Having attained the **essence** of all things, even if the **Sage** does not act, the **principle** can still be discerned. Having grasped the **root** of all things, even if the **Sage** does not see, the **logic** of right and wrong can be understood and named.

**○ Pan said:**
Those who exhaust principle and grasp the root suffice for this. But for those who embody the boundless and exhaust all extremes—when they gaze upon the four horizons, their insight pierces without obstruction, and the myriad transformations, though diverse, never stray from before their eyes—this is truly wondrous. What need is there to speak of it?

**Non-action yet completion.**
**Ming Huang said:**
Without spoken teaching, the world transforms itself.

**○ He said:**
When the ruler does nothing, the people have no burdens; when households are ample and people are content, all things naturally transform and align.

**○ Bi said:**
Clarifying the nature of things and following it—thus, even without action, completion is achieved.

**○ Pan said:**
What does Heaven speak? The four seasons proceed therein, and all things are born therein. Those who embody the Dao are but Heaven itself.

---
**Chapter 48: Daily Increase in Learning**

**Daily increase in learning.**
**He said:**
Learning refers to the study of governance, rites, music, and education. Daily increase means that desires and adornments grow ever more refined.

**○ Bi said:**
Strive to advance what one is capable of and refine what one practices.

**○ Xiu said:**
When one exhausts principle, one examines all things and unites them, seeking only one’s true essence—not for mere breadth. Thus, daily increase brings no harm, but when principle is exhausted, further increase becomes a detriment, and one must turn to subtraction as benefit.

**Daily subtraction in the Dao.**
**Ming Huang said:**
The scholar daily increases knowledge.
The practitioner of the Dao daily subtracts deeds.

Increasing knowledge is the gradual path of study; subtracting deeds is the gateway to enlightenment. Thus, one accumulates deeds through increase, yet forgets deeds to embody the Dao.

**○ He said:**
The Dao refers to the natural Way; daily subtraction means that desires and adornments are daily diminished.

**○ Bi said:**
Strive to return to emptiness and nothingness.

**○ Pan said:**
After perceiving principle, one recognizes the faults in all things—subtraction is not willed, yet all accretions gradually fade. Observing Heaven’s Way, increase and subtraction generate each other, a constant truth of all things.

**Subtract again and again.**
**He said:**
Subtract desires again and again, thus gradually removing them.

**○ Pan said:**
When one reaches utter emptiness, even emptiness is not a thing—then the extent of subtraction is known.

**Until non-action.**
**He said:**
Be serene and simple as an infant, doing nothing contrived.

**Non-action yet nothing remains undone.**
**Ming Huang said:**
The scholar accumulates deeds; the practitioner of the Dao forgets subtraction. Though deeds are subtracted, the desire to subtract remains—when even this desire is forgotten, one attains tranquil non-action. Like a mirror reflecting all things without fail, thus nothing remains undone.

**○ He said:**
When desires are severed, virtue aligns with the Dao, and nothing is beyond its application or beyond its doing.

**○ Bi said:**
Action entails loss; thus, non-action is the way of nothing remaining undone.

**○ Pan said:**
Only those who exhaust emptiness and voidness can nurture and unleash the myriad transformations endlessly. How could they compare to those of one-sided pursuit, stagnant in seclusion, like withered trees?

Thus, those who take the world must always act with non-interference.
**Ming Huang said:**
Non-action and non-interference—then the world turns with devotion. Governing the world must always be with non-interference, not with toil.

**○ Bi said:**
Action must always follow its natural course.

**○ Pan said:**
Non-interference is the true essence of the Sage. When responding to the times, the Dao is diminished.

Thus, though the Sage may seem close to action, what makes them sage and able to subdue the world lies always in non-interference. When they act,
**Bi said:**
It is self-created.

**It is insufficient to take the world.**
**Ming Huang said:**
Action brings toil; toil brings decay—thus it is insufficient to take the world.

**○ He said:**
When one delights in action, governance becomes burdensome, and the people are unsettled—thus it is insufficient to rule the world.

**○ Bi said:**
This is losing the fundamental root.

**○ Xiu said:**
Action entails intention; intention means the people also have intentions—though one seeks to govern, they will be distant indeed.

This passage’s essence: non-interference is the ultimate moral governance for the world; action is the utmost achievement of worldly affairs. Learning while daily subtracting, until reaching non-action—thus one attains this.

---
**Chapter 49: The Sage Has No Fixed Mind**

**The Sage has no fixed mind.**
**He said:**
The Sage values transformation and adaptation, following true spontaneity as if without mind.

**With the people’s mind as mind.**
**Ming Huang said:**
The Sage’s mind responds to things and follows sensation—response lies in sensation, hence no fixed mind. Though the mind is unfixed, it is always to transform goodness, thus constantly taking the people’s mind as mind.

**○ He said:**
Follow what benefits the people’s mind.

**○ Bi said:**
Action must always follow its natural course.

**○ Pan said:**
The Sage is still and exhausts nature, embodying true emptiness. Whatever is thought or done arises in response to things—like fire’s nature, which spreads without bound, yet follows the sun’s warmth to manifest its use. Thus, without clinging to any single thing, it suffices for all things endlessly.

The *Book of Documents* says:
*"Let my people be enlightened and wise.
The good, I too shall make good."*

**He said:**
When the people act in goodness, the Sage responds with goodness. When they act in evil, I too respond with goodness.
**He said:** Though the people may have those who act in evil, the Sage transforms them to goodness.
**○ Bi said:** Each follows its own function, thus goodness is never lost.
**○ Pan said:** Goodness and evil arise from deluded perception, deluded perception arises from selfishness. If one is open to the Great Dao, even though the eyes may discern good and evil, the heart holds no distinguishing thoughts.
Thus, the Sage follows the world’s emotions, firmly establishes praise and blame, and knows good and evil in the heart—yet originally, these are not fixed forms. Hence, when responding to evil as goodness, it is not out of pity or anger, but because one does not perceive them as different.
Thus, goodness is attained.

**He said:** When the people’s virtue is transformed, the Sage acts with goodness.
**○ Bi said:** Never abandon the people.
**○ Pan said:** To forget goodness and evil in responding with goodness is true goodness.

When the people are faithful, I too am faithful.
**He said:** When the people act with faith, the Sage responds with faith.
When they are unfaithful, I too respond with faith.
**He said:** When the people act with unfaith, the Sage transforms them to faith.
**○ Pan said:** Though all dharmas differ, they are equal in their true reality. Faith and unfaith arise from selfishness.
Thus, faith is attained.

**Ming Huang said:**
When I wish to cultivate goodness and faith among the people, I respond with goodness and faith. When they lack goodness and faith, I also teach them with goodness and faith, so that the people may be moved by my virtue and act in goodness and faith.
**○ He said:** When the people’s virtue is transformed, the Sage acts with faith.
**○ Pan said:** Knowing that all phenomena are not inherently false, the Sage responds to evil as if it were good. Knowing that all phenomena are not inherently real, the Sage responds to unfaith as if it were faith. When deluded, one knows reality; when real, one knows delusion—this is how the Sage’s wisdom differs from that of the common people.

The Sage’s presence in the world is **incautious**—
**Ming Huang** originally wrote *incautious*,
**Bi** originally wrote *restrained*.
**○ He said:** The Sage’s presence in the world is cautious, always fearing awe. Though rich and noble, he does not dare to be arrogant or extravagant.
He acts with a unified heart for the world.
**Ming Huang said:** The Sage, in governing the world, transforms and guides the people, always acting with an **incautious** heart, so that virtue, goodness, and faith may flourish while the Sage’s heart remains still and empty. Virtue shines with perfect clarity, and his unified, unrestrained heart serves the world entirely—thus, he acts with a unified heart for the world.
**○ He said:** This means the Sage makes the people’s hearts pure, as if they were simple and ignorant, not discerning.
**○ Pan said:** *Incautious* means fear and reverence. Though the Sage embodies emptiness and does not cling to any single thing, when he responds to the world, he never fails to observe proper conduct and restraint. For if he acts, even as a Sage, he does not dare to be careless.

The Sage takes the world as his heart, thus establishing all dharmas. The world takes the Sage as its heart, thus returning to the Great Dao.
The heart can perceive and distinguish, yet the Sage strives to return people to a state of undiscriminating awareness—thus, he acts with a unified heart.

(Alternative readings: *Incautious* originally written as *restrained*, meaning *restrained* in the sense of gathering and not dispersing, also acceptable. Another reading: *unified his heart*.)

The people all focus their ears and eyes,
**Ming Huang said:** The people, transformed by the Sage’s virtue, act in goodness, thus inclining their ears and eyes to observe and listen to the Sage.
**○ He said:** *Focus* means *utilize*.
The people all utilize their ears and eyes as the Sage’s means of perception.
**○ Bi said:** Each uses their wisdom and insight.
**○ Xiu said:** They look up to him and follow his ways.
The Sage treats them all as infants.
**Ming Huang said:** The Sage pities the common folk, as if they were tender infants, thus in all his dealings with the people, he acts as a loving mother would.
**○ He said:** The Sage loves and cherishes the people as infants and young children, nurturing them without expecting repayment.
**○ Bi said:** He makes them all harmonious and desireless, like infants.

Now, heaven and earth establish their positions, the Sage fulfills their capacities, and people scheme with both spirits and men. The people’s capacities are in harmony with what they can do, and those who can do it are given it. The greater their capacity, the greater their reward; the more noble their resources, the more noble their status. All things have their master, all affairs have their ruler. Thus, one may wear the crown and hold the scepter without fear of deceit, or block the ears with noise without suffering from arrogance. Why then would the Sage exert his own wisdom to discern the people’s emotions?

If one uses clarity to perceive things, things will also vie to respond with their own clarity. If one uses unfaith to perceive things, things will also vie to respond with unfaith.
The hearts of the world need not be the same, yet their responses dare not differ—then none will dare to utilize their true emotions.
Great is the harm of clarity! None is greater than using one’s own clarity.

If one relies on wisdom, people will dispute with him. If one relies on strength, people will contend with him. Wisdom that does not surpass others yet stands in the realm of dispute will be exhausted. Strength that does not surpass others yet stands in the realm of contention will be in peril.
There has never been one who can make people abandon their wisdom, nor one who can make people not use their wisdom and strength against him. Thus, he alone with one wisdom faces the people, while the people with myriad wisdoms face him alone.

If he multiplies his laws and nets, complicates his punishments, blocks their paths, and raids their hidden dwellings, then all things lose their natural order, the people lose their means of livelihood, chaos reigns above and below. Thus, the Sage’s heart toward the world is **restrained**, with no fixed attachment.
He acts with a unified heart for the world, his intentions wandering nowhere, his discernment nowhere fixed. The people have nothing to fear, nothing to respond to, nothing to seek. With nothing to fear and nothing to respond to, none will fail to utilize their true emotions.
No one should discard their own capacities to do what they cannot, nor abandon their strengths to do what they lack. Thus, those who speak do so from their knowledge, those who act do so from their abilities. The people all focus their ears and eyes, and I treat them all as infants.

**○ Pan said:** The ignorant people act without reason in all things. The Sage pities them and leads them gently, like parents guiding a child—bending and yielding to their inclinations, yet without resentment or anger. None but he who embodies the Dao and forgets things, whose compassion accompanies heaven and earth, could act thus.

**Chapter Fifty: Birth and Death**

**Birth and Death.**
The Sage said: Enlightenment brings birth; delusion and attachment bring death—this is the standard.

**○ He said:** Birth means the emotions and desires emerge from the five viscera, the spirit steadies, and the soul and vital essence remain tranquil—thus life. Death means the emotions and desires sink into the chest and mind, the spirit grows weary and confused—thus death.

**○ Bi said:** The realm of birth, the realm of death.

**○ Xiu said:** Those who are moved by the interplay of yin and yang cannot escape this—all because of their false attachment to life. There are thirteen paths to life, thirteen paths to death.

The Sage said: Broadly speaking, among all beings, those who live in accordance with life’s principles attain true life, and those who die in accordance with death’s principles attain true death. Such cases, in the grand scheme, are three out of ten.

**○ He said:** The categories of birth and death each have thirteen aspects—namely, the nine orifices and four passages. In life, the eyes do not gaze wantonly, the ears do not listen indiscriminately, the nose does not indulge in odors, the mouth does not speak idly, the tongue does not crave tastes, the hands do not grasp recklessly, the feet do not walk aimlessly, the vital essence does not stir in vain. In death, the opposite holds true.

**○ Pan said:** Those who claim to uphold the Dao often either cling to the vigor of yang and rely on its strength, or fixate on the stillness of yin and stagnate in decay—both are deviations from the true virtue and power, and thus become entangled in birth and death.

The paths that lead to death from life are also thirteen.

The Sage said: If one indulges life excessively, nourishing it at the cost of vitality, and clings too tightly to this life, then any movement leads to the realm of death. Such beings have already lost the principles of life and death—these are three out of every ten.

**○ He said:** The actions taken in pursuit of life lead one into thirteen realms of death.

**○ Pan said:** Excessive attachment to life harms one’s nature, so every movement brings injury.

Beyond the ignorant common folk, some uphold the Dao as a shield, while others indulge desire and court misfortune—all because they cling to life and thus cannot escape birth and death. Why is this?

**○ He said:** Why do they enter the realms of death? Because their attachment to life is too deep.

The Sage said: Why do they enter the realms of death? Because their pursuit of this life is excessively heavy—like the green grass, why does it not wither? Simply because their fixation on life is too intense.

**○ He said:** They enter the realms of death because their obsession with living is excessive, defying the natural order and acting without measure.

**○ Pan said:** The Perfect One knows neither death nor life, and thus neither dies nor lives. Therefore, it is said that they have never died, never lived. Those who fixate on yin or yang and become entangled in birth and death, or who indulge desire and court ruin, moving into the realms of death—these three categories differ in their appearances. Yet tracing their confusion from the true Dao’s rectitude, they still cling to life too heavily.

Some say: Those who pursue the Dao to the point of emaciation—are they not excessively attached to life?

It is said: My way is to transcend life and death, serene and ever one. Only by stilling falsehood can one attain this. But they cling to their perceptions, even to the point of emaciation. Though their intent may lean toward emptiness, their very fixation on this intent makes them the most attached to the self.

Now, those who excel in nurturing life—

**○ He said:** *Shè* (摄) means to nourish.

—walk on land without encountering rhinoceroses or tigers,

**○ He said:** They naturally avoid harm; danger does not reach them.

—enter battlefields without being struck by weapons,

**○ He said:** They do not delight in killing others.

—rhinoceroses have no place to thrust their horns, tigers no place to strike with their claws, weapons no place to lodge their blades.

The Sage said: Those who master the art of preserving life maintain a clear and tranquil mind, free from reckless intentions. Thus, all external harm cannot touch them. They do not seek to encounter rhinoceroses or tigers on land, nor to be struck by armored soldiers in battle—this is not inviting harm. Therefore, harm cannot reach their mind, and there is no place for horns to thrust, claws to strike, or blades to lodge.

**○ He said:** For those who nurture life, tigers and rhinoceroses cannot harm them, and weapons cannot reach them.

Why is this?

**○ He said:** Why do tigers, rhinoceroses, and weapons not harm them? Because they have no realm of death.

The Sage said: Why do tigers, rhinoceroses, and weapons have no place to strike? Because they follow the natural transformation without partiality, not treating death as death. Thus, harm cannot touch their life, and it is said they have no realm of death.

**○ He said:** Because they do not violate the thirteen paths of death, their spirit and clarity protect them—thus, harm cannot dare to approach.

**○ Bi said:** Thirteen refers to three out of ten—taking the extreme of the life-path where life is fully preserved, three out of ten. Taking the extreme of the death-path where death is fully realized, three out of ten as well. Yet common folk, with their excessive attachment to life, enter the realm of non-life.

Those who excel in nurturing life do not treat life as life, and thus have no realm of death.

The most harmful tools are spears and blades; the most harmful beasts are rhinoceroses and tigers. Yet those who do not burden themselves with desire are such that spears and blades find no place to strike, and rhinoceroses and tigers find no place to claw or gore. Truly, they do not invite harm upon themselves.

As for entering the realm of death—

The *yǎn* (蚖) and *tuó* (蟺) mistake deep waters for shallow and dig holes within them, while eagles and sea birds mistake mountains for lowlands and build nests upon them. Snares and nets cannot reach them, and traps cannot ensnare them—this is to dwell in a realm where death cannot touch. Yet they ultimately fall into the realm of non-life because of sweet bait. Is this not due to their excessive attachment to life?

Thus, if one does not abandon their root or sever their true nature, even entering battlefields will not harm them, nor will land travel invite assault.

The infant is worthy of emulation for its trustworthiness and sincerity.

○ **Hé** said: Those who dwell in the realm of non-death do so because they are without attachment to life. Those without attachment to life are perpetually alive yet do not cling to life themselves, so they never die nor ever truly live. When the Dao reaches this state, even if their form has *chán* (禅), their spirit remains unaltered—how could death ever touch them?

This is the sacred essence of the Central Kingdom, and the Buddha of the Western Realm.

If one is thus, their transformations are without end; water and fire cannot scorch or dampen them, and hewing or flogging cannot wound them. Moving through emptiness yet touching solidity, they go without obstruction—what desire could they possess, much less attain? How much more could they be harmed?

Someone asked: Then why is it called *shè shēng* (摄生)?
It was replied: Only when one descends to this lesser state does death and life become their domain. Thus, *shè shēng* must reach this point before life can be perpetually preserved.

This is akin to those with profound *dé* (德), the ultimate indeed.

**Chapter Fifty-One: The Dao Nourishes Life (《道生之》)**
The Dao nourishes life,
The *Míng Huáng* (明皇) said: The wondrous origin moves and harmonizes the *hé qì* (和气).
○ **Hé** (河) said: The Dao gives birth to all things.

The *virtue/power* (德) nurtures them,
The *Míng Huáng* said: Things thus receive the means to flourish and sustain all classes.
○ **Hé** said: The *virtue/power* is singular—one *virtue/power* spreads the *qi* (气) to give birth and to sustain.

Things take form from it,
The *Míng Huáng* said: The masculine principle knows, the feminine principle acts, and signs and portents establish their form and position.
○ **Hé** said: One endows all things with their shapes and likenesses.

Their *shì* (势) completes them.
The *Míng Huáng* said: The *shì* (势) of cold and heat each arises and fulfills its purpose.
○ **Hé** said: One endows all things with the *shì* (势) of cold and heat to complete them.
○ **Bì** (弼) said: Things first arise, then are nurtured; nurtured, then take form; formed, then are completed. What gives them birth? The Dao. What grants them nurture? *Virtue/power*. What endows them with form? Things themselves. What brings them to completion? Their *shì* (势). Yet because of their origin, they can exist without form; because of their *shì*, they can be completed without form.

All things thus arise, and all functions are fulfilled—all have their source, and none can escape the Dao.
Thus, when one traces it to its utmost, it is still the pursuit of the Dao.
Following their origins, each thing has its own Dao.

○ **Xiù** (秀) said: These four are all the Dao; yet because each attains its own Dao, they are merely *virtue/power*. *Virtue/power* is what is nurtured. When nurturing reaches its natural fulfillment and form is established, then it is merely things. Things having their form, then near and far interact, and the firm and yielding intertwine—each follows its *shì* (势) to manifest its state. Thus, *virtue/power* is the Dao’s division, things are the *virtue/power*’s instruments, and *shì* (势) is the principle of things. Understanding the Dao and *virtue/power*, then form and *shì* are not worth knowing, and one can effortlessly respond to all things.

Therefore, all things revere the Dao and hold *virtue/power* in high esteem.
The *Míng Huáng* said: All things arise from the Dao and *virtue/power*, hence their reverence and esteem.
○ **Hé** said: The Dao and *virtue/power* act without fail, stirring all things and teaching them.
○ **Bì** said: The Dao is the source from which things arise, and *virtue/power* is what things obtain. Arising from it, they cannot but gain; revering it, they cannot but be harmed—yet they must esteem it.

○ **Xiù** said: The Dao is exalted while *virtue/power* is humble; *virtue/power* is honored while things are cheapened. The exalted, like sovereigns and fathers, the honored, like gold and jade—this is the distinction between exaltation and honor.

The exaltation of the Dao and the honor of *virtue/power*—none confer titles upon them, yet they are perpetually natural.
The *Míng Huáng* and *Wáng Bì* (王弼) both interpreted this as conferring titles.
○ The *Míng Huáng* said: It speaks of the exaltation and honor of the Dao and *virtue/power*—not by artificial titles, but by the perpetual function of giving birth and completing, bestowed upon things naturally, hence their inherent honor.
○ **Hé** said: The Dao is singular, uncommanded; it calls forth all things and remains perpetually natural, responding like the shadow to the form.
○ **Páng** (雱) said: To receive a mandate from Heaven is to be the Son of Heaven; to receive a mandate from the Son of Heaven is to be a feudal lord. Having received a mandate, one can be degraded by those who gave it—yet the Dao precedes all things and governs their mandates. Who could artificially confer upon it? Thus, it remains perpetually natural.

Hence, the Dao nourishes life—it gives birth, nurtures, completes, matures, sustains, and protects it.
The *Míng Huáng* said: Therefore, no one fails to revere the Dao and honor *virtue/power*.
○ **Hé** said: The Dao’s work for all things is not merely giving birth, but also prolonging, nurturing, completing, maturing, and protecting their nature and life. A sovereign ruling a state and cultivating oneself should act likewise.

○ **Bì** said: It means completing their substance, each attaining its shelter and shade, without harming their essence.
○ **Páng** said: The *Míng Huáng*’s original text spoke of *virtue/power* nurturing them.

Giving birth yet not possessing,
○ **Hé** said: The Dao gives birth to all things, yet does not take possession—this is not for selfish gain.

Doing yet not relying,
○ **Hé** said: The Dao’s actions are not done with expectation of reward.
○ **Bì** said: Doing yet not possessing.

Nurturing yet not slaughtering,
○ **Hé** said: The Dao nurtures all things, yet does not slaughter them for use.
This is called *Xuán Dé* (玄德, Mysterious Virtue).
The *Míng Huáng* said: It is as explained in the chapter on *Zǎi Yíng Pò* (载营魄), where it speaks of human cultivation mirroring the Dao, and this chapter clarifies that the Dao’s application is akin to human use.

○ **Hé** said: The Dao’s actions are profound and dark, its *virtue/power* unseen.
○ **Bì** said: *Virtue/power* exists yet its master is unknown—it emerges from the depths of obscurity, hence *Xuán Dé* (玄德).
○ **Páng** said: The Dao, by not clinging to life, can thus perpetually nourish life. When it nourishes life, it is merely *virtue/power*.

**Chapter Fifty-Two: The Beginning of All Under Heaven (《天下有始》)**
All Under Heaven has a beginning,
And this beginning is the mother of All Under Heaven.
The *Míng Huáng* said: The beginning is the *chōng qì* (冲气, pristine *qi*), stating that this wondrous *qi* gives birth to and completes all things, possessing the nurturing *virtue/power* of flourishing, hence it can be the mother of All Under Heaven.

○ **Hé** said: The beginning has the Dao.
The Dao is the mother of all Under Heaven and all things.
○ **Bì** said: If one begins well, one will also nurture and sustain well. Thus, All Under Heaven having a beginning, it can be the mother of All Under Heaven.

○ **Hé** said: The mother of all named things is the Dao/Way. The beginning and the mother are fundamentally one—when the Dao/Way manifests all things, it is thus called the mother. Once one knows the mother, one can understand its offspring.

○ **Míng Huáng** said: When all things receive the pristine *chōng qì* (冲气) and its nurturing *virtue/power*, they recognize themselves as the offspring of this pristine *qi*.

○ **Hé** said: The offspring is the One. Having known the Dao/Way, one must then know the One.

○ **Hé** said: All things arise from the Dao/Way, making it their mother. Thus, they are called offspring—when one attains the Dao/Way, the principles of all things are known without need of recognition. Once one knows the offspring, one must then guard the mother.

○ **Hé** said: Having known the One, one must then guard the Dao/Way and return to non-action.

○ **Hé** said: The perfected sage, though meditating upon the principles of all things, knows that these principles have no fixed form. Thus, though he knows them, he does not follow them and depart from the Dao/Way. Hence, it is said: *"Return and guard its mother"* (复守其母). Thus, one’s body does not perish.

○ **Míng Huáng** said: Once one knows that the body is the offspring of pristine *chōng qì* (冲气), one must guard this harmonious and wondrous *qi*, preventing it from dispersing. Then, one’s body will never face danger or harm.

○ **Hé** said: No danger or harm.

○ **Bì** said: The mother is the root; the offspring is the end. By attaining the root, one knows the end. One must not abandon the root to chase the end.

○ **Hé** said: The root of *yuè* (兑, joy/delight) is where desires arise, and the root of *mén* (门, gate) is where desires follow.

○ **Hé** said: *Yuè* (兑) means delight—when a person delights, their form opens, hence it is called *yuè*. Once delighted, things enter. What is the cause of delight leading to the opening of one’s form? It is from not guarding the Dao/Way and recklessly pursuing the beauty of things, thus delighting and following them to manifest.

○ **Hé** said: The gate is where the spirit (神) emerges. Externally perceiving principles, one’s form opens to receive them, and then the spirit again emerges, mingling with them. This creates endless turmoil—all entanglements begin from here. Thus, one must block *yuè* (兑) and close the gate, always guarding the mother.

○ **Míng Huáng** said: *Yuè* (兑) is love and delight. The eyes delight in colors, the ears in sounds—each of the six senses (六根) has its own delight. If indulged, it leads to suffering. Therefore, one must block them.

○ By not indulging the six senses in love and delight, the door to calamity is closed. Thus, one’s life will never be weary or labored.

○ **Hé** said: If one blocks the eyes from reckless gazing and closes the mouth from reckless speech, then one’s life will never be weary.

○ **Bì** said: Without action, one remains forever at ease. Thus, one’s life will never be weary.

○ **Hé** said: Beyond blocking *yuè* (兑) and closing the gate, one responds to all things externally. Thus, one’s efforts are ample and ever sufficient.

○ **Hé** said: Opening *yuè* (兑) means gazing at desires. Indulging in desires leads to endless calamity.

○ **Míng Huáng** said: Opening the six senses, indulging in sight and sound to fulfill desires of love and delight, will always invite disaster. Thus, one’s life will never be saved.

○ **Hé** said: Disaster will flourish.

○ **Bì** said: If one does not close the source, yet indulges in its affairs, then one’s life will never be saved.

○ **Hé** said: Once *yuè* (兑) is opened, things enter. Then, one expends their spirit to indulge in these affairs, submitting internally to externally. Chasing things without return, who could save them from this current?

○ **Míng Huáng** said: When one can perceive even the smallest matters and adjust their conduct accordingly, this is called clarity.

○ **Hé** said: The seeds of disaster have not yet sprouted, nor have the signs of calamity appeared. The small is evident—only those who see it are clear.

○ **Hé** said: Guarding the Dao/Way means one’s perception is subtle; following principles means one’s knowledge is broad.

○ **Míng Huáng** said: Guarding softness is strength. If one guards softness and weakness, no one can overpower them—this is called strength.

○ **Hé** said: Guarding softness and weakness leads to ever-increasing strength.

○ **Bì** said: The efficacy of governance does not lie in grandiosity. Seeing what is grand is not clarity; seeing what is small is clarity. Guarding strength is not strength; guarding softness is strength.

○ **Hé** said: The sage’s light is the nature of fire. Fire’s nature is to envelop emptiness and shine, relying on fuel for its form. Once its illumination ceases, it returns to emptiness, initially not self-luminous, but only revealing light through fuel.

○ **Bì** said: Manifesting the Dao/Way to remove the people.

○ **Hé** said: The sage’s light shines through things. Things being without constancy, the light follows them. Though one’s responses may be endless, one does not weary from it. Who else in All Under Heaven could be as divine as this?

○ **Míng Huáng** said: Thus, one does not abandon their body to suffering.

○ **Hé** said: Internally observing to preserve the spirit, one does not fall into leakage.

This is called **Clinging to the Constant**.

**Emperor Ming Huang** said: *"遗 (yí)"* means *"with"*—that is, if one returns to guard the inner light, there will be none to harm or blame the body. Thus, this is called **secretly employing the Dao/Way of True Constancy**.

○ **Hé** said: If a person can practice this, it is called **cultivating the Constant Dao**.

○ **Bì** said: This is the **Constant of the Dao**.

○ **Páng** said: If there is such clarity, then there is a *self-view* (我相). Once the *self-view* is established, all things become calamities. Thus, one can only understand the earlier statement: *"and thus, there is ultimately no calamity."* Those outside this Dao are all subject to birth-and-death. Only by embodying this principle does one begin to **abide in constancy**.

*"Clinging"* means to **embody it while remaining unmanifest**.

---
**Chapter 53: "Make Me Firmly Aware"**

*"Make me firmly aware, walking the Great Dao..."*

○ **Hé** said: *"介 (jiè)"* means *"great."* Laozi laments that the rulers of his time do not walk the Great Dao, hence this statement: *"If I were firmly aware of governance, I would walk the Great Dao, embodying the transformation of non-action."*

*"Only what is applied is to be feared."*

**Emperor Ming Huang** said: Laozi speaks: *"If I were to stand firm in my awareness, desiring to walk the Great Dao, since it does not align with the Dao, then whatever is applied will be fearful."*

○ **Hé** said: *"唯 (wéi)"* means *"solely."* One fears only that what is applied deviates from the Dao’s intent—desiring to reward the virtuous risks fostering false virtue, desiring to trust the loyal risks giving rise to deceitful loyalty.

○ **Bì** said: *"If I were to stand firmly aware, walking the Great Dao in the world, then only what is applied is to be feared."*

○ **Páng** said: Even a slight awareness of the Dao, yet walking the Great Dao, already fears the act of governance. How much more so for those with great awareness? For this reason, the Dao of the Perfect Person does not harm the root with superficial actions.

When governance flourishes externally, the root within becomes hollowed out. Hence, the chapter concludes:

*"The Great Dao is most simple,"*

○ **Hé** said: *"夷 (yí)"* means *"plain and easy."*

*"yet the people prefer shortcuts."*

**Emperor Ming Huang** said: The Great Dao is plain and easy, yet those with awareness fear the multitude of affairs, seeking swiftness like travelers taking shortcuts. The harms of shortcuts are detailed below.

○ **Hé** said: *"径 (jìng)"* means *"crooked and not straight."* The Great Dao is utterly plain and easy, yet the people prefer crooked shortcuts. How much more so when governance obstructs the Great Dao? Hence, *"The Great Dao is most simple, yet the people prefer shortcuts."*

○ **Bì** said: The Great Dao is vast and upright, yet the people still abandon it and do not follow it, preferring shortcuts. How much more so when governance clogs the Great Dao? Thus, *"The Great Dao is most simple, yet the people prefer shortcuts."*

○ **Páng** said: Shortcuts may offer fleeting clarity, but those who stray from the Great Dao and indulge in governance are blinded by near-term wisdom and lack long-term vision.

*"The court is excessively adorned,"*

**Emperor Ming Huang** said: *"尚贤 (shàng xián)"* and *"矜智 (jīn zhì)"* give rise to cunning deceit. *"除 (chú)"* means *"to govern."* The court is adorned with high towers and palaces.

○ **Hé** said: The court is adorned with false elegance, valuing external splendor.

*"Fields lie fallow,"*

**Emperor Ming Huang** said: Gluttonous feasting and idleness neglect farming.

○ **Hé** said: Farming is abandoned; the land is left untended.

*"Granaries are empty,"*

**Emperor Ming Huang** said: The southern fields yield no harvest, and there are no reserves.

○ **Hé** said: The five grains are harmed; the state has no stores.

○ **Bì** said: When the court is excessively adorned, the fields lie fallow, and the granaries become empty. One action leads to countless harms.

○ **Páng** said: Tending the fields and filling the granaries are the foundations of the state. Now, the court pursues superficial glory while neglecting the exhausted roots—how can this be a lasting path? All who harm the root with superficial actions are the same.

*"Wearing ornate robes,"*

**Emperor Ming Huang** said: Carving and embroidery drain labor and resources.

○ **Hé** said: Indulging in adornment, valuing external display.

*"Bearing sharp swords,"*

**Emperor Ming Huang** said: When moral cultivation is neglected, there is reliance on martial preparedness.

○ **Hé** said: Favoring strength, indulging in martial excess.

*"Gluttonous in food and drink,"*

**Emperor Ming Huang** said: Feasting on rich meats and fresh delicacies, indulging in taste.

*"厌 (yàn)"* means *"sated."*

*"Wealth overflows."*

**Emperor Ming Huang** said: Hoarding treasures and accumulating rare riches.

○ **Hé** said: Excessive desires, without restraint.

○ **Páng** said: Extravagance externally to seek fleeting comfort, while neglecting the roots—this is clearly harming the root through superficial governance.

*(One variant reads: "货财有余" – "commodities and wealth in excess.")*

*"This is called 'robber’s vanity.'"*

○ **Hé** said: When the people lack, yet the ruler has excess, it is because they plunder like thieves for adornment, boasting to others, unaware that death and ruin will follow, along with their kin.

*"This is not the Dao."*

**Emperor Ming Huang** said: Standing firm in awareness, yet actions only bring ruin—governing like a thief while boasting, vanity and theft are not the Dao. This only invites sighs.

○ **Hé** said: If a ruler governs thus, this is not the Dao. The repeated *"also"* (哉) is a lament.

○ **Bì** said: If one does not obtain things by their proper means, they are all crooked. Crookedness is theft; boasting without proper means is vanity and theft. Holding high rank without proper means is usurpation. Thus, all that is not the Dao is theft and vanity.

○ **Páng** said: Thieves seek fleeting glory; the vain seek external adornment. Those who walk the Dao cultivate deep roots and unexhausted virtue—how could they indulge in governance for superficial gain?

---
**Chapter 54: "The Well-Built Cannot Be Uprooted"**

*"The well-built cannot be uprooted,"*

**Emperor Ming Huang** said: Those who skillfully establish the state and its foundations with the Dao cannot be overturned.

○ **Hé** said: *"建 (jiàn)"* means *"to establish."* Those who skillfully establish themselves and their state with the Dao cannot be pulled up and uprooted.

○ **Bì** said: First, fortify the root, then cultivate the branches—thus, it cannot be uprooted.

「○ **Hé** said: *"Jiàn"* means *"central harmony."* Those who skillfully embrace the core cannot be severed.

○ **Mínghuáng** said: Those who skillfully embrace the people with the Dao cannot be abandoned.

○ **Hé** said: Those who embrace the spirit with the Dao cannot be pulled up, extracted, or liberated.

○ **Bì** said: It is not a matter of abundance, but of equalizing one’s capacity—thus, it cannot be severed.

○ **Hé** said: *"Bào yī"* means *"embracing unity."* When descendants fail to repay sacrifices with proper rites,

○ **Mínghuáng** said: This refers to a ruler who embraces virtue and the Dao, whose benevolence extends to posterity—how much more so to his descendants? The Zhou Dynasty’s rise began with **Hòujì** (后稷), perfected by **Wénwǔ** (文武), and though divinations predicted its replacement after thirty generations and a lifespan of seven hundred years, the sacrifices to **Hòujì** and **Wénwǔ** were never ceased.

○ **Hé** said: If descendants uphold the Dao in this manner, they attain eternal life and longevity, their sacrifices to ancestors and the ancestral temple enduring without interruption through generations.

○ **Bì** said: If descendants transmit this Dao through sacrifices, then the rites will never cease.

○ **Hé** said: The sage governs self and others, centering on *embracing unity and central harmony*—this is the ultimate principle of governance, immutable as heaven and earth. Thus, the law they leave behind is endless, their merit encompassing the four seas, and all under heaven and the spirits unite in reverence, their blessings extending to the remotest descendants.

*"Yīběn wúyǐ zì"* (一本无以字): Cultivate it within oneself, and its virtue becomes authentic.

○ **Mínghuáng** said: Cultivate the Dao within oneself, and its virtue becomes pure and true.

○ **Hé** said: Cultivate the Dao within oneself, cherish *qi* and nourish the spirit, thereby prolonging life and extending years—such is the virtue of the **Zhēnrén** (真人, True Person).

*"Xiū zhī jiā, qí dé nài yú"* (修之家，其德乃余): Cultivate it within the family, and its virtue becomes abundant.

○ **Mínghuáng** said: If the entire family cultivates it, its virtue overflows with blessings.

○ **Hé** said: Cultivate the Dao within the family, where fathers are benevolent, sons filial, brothers harmonious, younger siblings obedient, husbands trustworthy, and wives chaste—such virtue yields enduring blessings, reaching future generations of descendants.

○ **Bì** said: This extends from self to others—cultivate it within oneself, and its virtue is authentic; cultivate it within the family, and its virtue overflows abundantly. If cultivation is never neglected, its application is vast and profound.

*"Xiū zhī xiāng, qí dé nài zhǎng"* (修之乡，其德乃长): Cultivate it within the village, and its virtue becomes enduring.

○ **Mínghuáng** said: If the entire village cultivates it, its virtue becomes lasting.

○ **Hé** said: Cultivate the Dao within the village, honoring elders and loving the young, teaching the foolish and instructing the ignorant—such virtue ensures none are left uncovered.

*"Xiū zhī guó, qí dé nài fēng"* (修之国，其德乃丰): Cultivate it within the state, and its virtue becomes abundant.

○ **Mínghuáng** said: If the entire state cultivates it, its virtue becomes full and rich.

○ **Hé** said: Cultivate the Dao within the state, and the ruler is trustworthy while ministers are loyal; benevolence and righteousness arise naturally, rituals and music flourish spontaneously, governance is fair and impartial—such virtue is profound and thick for the people.

*"Xiū zhī tiānxià, qí dé nài pǔ"* (修之天下，其德乃普): Cultivate it across the world, and its virtue becomes universal.

○ **Mínghuáng** said: If all under heaven cultivate it, its virtue spreads universally.

○ **Hé** said: When the sovereign cultivates the Dao across the world, transformation occurs without words, governance without instruction—those below respond as shadows follow their forms, with unwavering trust. Such virtue is universal and broad.

○ **Hé** said: By extending the method of self-cultivation outward, virtue becomes abundant, then enduring, then rich, and finally universal. Thus, observing oneself with the law of self-cultivation,

○ **Mínghuáng** said: Observing oneself with the method of self-cultivation, one who attains clarity and tranquility is the True Person.

○ **Hé** said: Observing the self that cultivates the Dao with the self that does not, one discerns which perishes and which survives.

Observing the family with the method of family cultivation,

○ **Mínghuáng** said: Observing the family with the method of family cultivation, one that achieves harmony and accord has abundant virtue.

○ **Hé** said: Observing the family that cultivates the Dao with the family that does not, one discerns their differences.

Observing the village with the method of village cultivation,

○ **Mínghuáng** said: Observing the village with the method of village cultivation, one that follows order and sequence has enduring virtue.

○ **Hé** said: Observing the village that cultivates the Dao with the village that does not, one discerns their distinctions.

Observing the state with the method of state cultivation,

○ **Mínghuáng** said: Observing the state with the method of state cultivation, one that practices diligence and frugality has rich virtue.

○ **Hé** said: Observing the state that cultivates the Dao with the state that does not, one discerns their disparities.

○ **Bì** said: All these distinctions are evident.

Observing the world with the method of world cultivation,

○ **Mínghuáng** said: Observing the world with the method of world cultivation, one that practices *wuwei* (無為, non-action) attains universal virtue.

○ **Hé** said: Observing the sovereign who cultivates the Dao with the sovereign who does not, one discerns their differences.

○ **Bì** said: Observing the world’s hundred families with the heart of the world’s people, the Dao of the world mirrors the Dao of individuals—its reversals and conformities, its fortune and misfortune, are all as with the Dao of a single person.

○ **Hé** said: The sage’s approach to all things is to exhaust the principles of reason without indulging private wisdom—thus, there is nothing they cannot master.

Thus, cultivating oneself aligns with the principles of one’s body, exhausting one’s body’s principles brings self-governance. From this point onward, governing a family means taking the family as one’s heart, governing a village means taking the village as one’s law. Such is the way—encountering ever broader applications, ever more abundant blessings.

The *Shū* (书, *Classic of Documents*) says: *"Heaven is wise and clear, and so are my people."* The sage is but heaven itself. How do I know the world is as such? By this.

○ **Mínghuáng** said: By observing this method of self, family, and world, one can understand it thus.

○ **Hé** said: The **Lǎozǐ** (老子) states how I know that those who cultivate the Dao flourish while those who abandon it perish—by observing these five matters.

○ **Bì** said: This is what was previously said: *"How do I know the world?"* By examining oneself, seeking no further. This is the meaning of *"not stepping outside the door to know the world."*»»»»○ **Hé** said: The sage upholds a single Dao, admitting no divergent teachings—thus, their means of action and means of knowing all arise from this Dao.

**Chapter 55: "The Thickness of Embracing Virtue"**

**Chapter 55: "Embracing the Depth of Virtue"**

**Embracing the Depth of Virtue**
**Hé** said: It refers to those who embrace the profound virtue of Dao and morality, comparing them to an infant.

**Mínghuáng** said: The perfected sage, embracing the depth of virtue, acts like an infant.

○ **Hé** said: The divine protection of virtue’s endurance is like a parent’s care for an infant.

○ **Péng** said: Virtue is never inherently shallow, yet it becomes so through deliberate concern and calculation. Thus, preserving one’s true nature without external depletion or internal dissipation allows pure *qi* to accumulate within, filling the human path to its fullest—worthy of being called great. Therefore, it is no wonder that one may tame tigers and leopards, subdue spirits and gods.

Someone asked: *Why then cannot an infant do these things?*
It was replied: The preservation of pure *qi* is not something a single body can fully contain. In the corrupt ages, though one may have the form of an infant, their lost true nature has long since faded. How could the world truly understand this?

Poisonous creatures do not sting,
**Hé** said: Bees, fireflies, snakes, and venomous serpents do not sting.

Fierce beasts do not attack,
Grasping crows do not snatch.

**Mínghuáng** said: The perfected sage is divine—harm cannot touch them. Since they harbor no intent to harm, there is no ground for retaliation. This is the depth of virtue in the perfected sage.

○ **Hé** said: The infant harms no thing, and no thing harms it. Thus, in the age of great peace, all people—regardless of rank—possess benevolence. Even creatures with venom return to their natural state, and poisonous beasts do not harm humans.

○ **Bì** said: The infant has no desires or cravings, does not offend against things, so venomous creatures do not strike them. Those who embrace deep virtue do not offend against things, and thus nothing can diminish their wholeness.

○ **Péng** said: The preservation of pure *qi* suffices for this.

I have often reasoned thus: All things harm one another because their *qi* is received unevenly. Humans, as the noblest of creatures, inherit the utmost harmony, yet sometimes suffer harm from things due to losing their natural state. Thus, yin and yang achieve harmony through the balance of *qi*. Only by guarding the true, harmonious *qi* can one prevent harm from entering like a door.

Yet even this is but the virtue of the great person—still not the sage.

Their bones are weak, their sinews supple, yet their grip is firm,
**Hé** said: Though an infant’s bones and sinews are weak, their grasp is unyielding because their will and heart do not waver.

○ **Bì** said: Because of their very weakness, their grasp is firm.

They do not know the union of male and female yet [their vitality] thrives,
**Bì** said: *Thrive* means *grow*.

Nothing can harm their body, so they remain whole and grow. Those who embrace deep virtue suffer no harm to their virtue, no corruption of their true nature. Gentle and yielding, they are never broken—all such as this. This is the pinnacle of essence.

**Hé** said: Though the infant does not know the union of yin and yang, yet their *qi* surges—this is due to the abundance of their vital essence.

○ **Péng** said: It is not from external desire that their *qi* surges, but from following their natural flow without the interference of the heart. If one’s eyes wander outward while the mind strays inward, essence is lost, and death follows. How could such a one endure?

The greatest affliction of the age is this, and the most solemn warning for the scholar.

Crying all day yet their voice does not grow hoarse,
**Bì** originally wrote *hoarse* as *ya* (噫).

○ **Bì** said: Without the burden of desire, they cry all day yet their voice does not grow hoarse.

This is the ultimate harmony.

**Mínghuáng** said: The infant’s bones are weak, their sinews supple, yet their clenched fist is firm. They do not know the pairing of yin and yang, yet their source of *qi* thrives. Their movements are like the essence at its purest. Crying all day yet their voice does not grow hoarse—this is the ultimate harmony of the infant.

○ **Hé** said: From morning to evening, the infant’s cries do not change—this is due to the abundance of harmonious *qi*.

○ **Péng** said: Mencius said: *"Nurturing rectitude without harm, one fills the space between heaven and earth—this is harmonious *qi*."* In the other, it is called vast; here, it is called harmony. Though the names differ, the *qi* is one. Thus, when heart and *qi* move together, straying from principle and losing constancy leads to ruin. Is it that their inherent nature was different? It is merely that they did not hold to the proper way.

Those who preserve their virtue whole may interact with things physically, yet their heart remains tranquil. Thus, though they use *qi*, it moves of its own accord. Therefore, though they act, there is no burden of action. Yet relying on the preservation of pure *qi*, who could disturb them?

Thus, even in old age, they do not lose the constancy of the infant. Guāngchéngzǐ cultivated his body for twelve hundred years without decay—this is how it is.

*Hoarse* (*sè*) is the summer season, when heaven’s harmony disperses. The hoarseness of the throat (*sè*) comes from the lack of accumulated harmonious *qi*. Another version reads *ya* (噫), meaning dispersed breath.

*Ya* also means *truth*, and is acceptable.

**Knowing the constancy of harmony,**
**Mínghuáng** said: To remain like an infant, steadfast in gentle harmony—this is the practice of knowing constancy.

○ **Hé** said: If one knows the benefit of harmonious *qi*’s gentleness to humanity, then they understand the constancy of the Dao.

○ **Bì** said: Things take harmony as their constancy; thus, knowing harmony is to grasp constancy.

○ **Péng** said: The constancy of returning to one’s true nature is the embodiment of spirit. Knowing the constancy of harmony is guarding *qi*. To know how to guard *qi* is to speak of returning to one’s true nature—though not yet having attained it. These two are what make the sage the sage and the worthy the worthy; there is no other path.

Ancient scholars all began here. With the Great Dao hidden, scholars pursued trivialities while forgetting the root. Learning began to stray beyond this.

Constancy is the fixed portion of nature. If one fully realizes their nature, they naturally distinguish themselves from things, and nothing can alter them. Thus, it is called constancy.

「For what lies beyond the fixed portion of nature, all else is artifice and deceit, with no constancy.

Knowing constancy is called *ming* (明).
The enlightened sovereign said: Guarding harmony and knowing constancy is called *mingliào* (明了).

○ **Hé** (河) said: If one can act in accordance with the constancy of the Dao/Way, then it is called attaining *ming* (明) to penetrate the profound and mysterious.

○ **Bì** (弼) said: Neither hunger nor oblivion, neither warmth nor cold—this is constancy.
Without form, it cannot be perceived with the eyes; this is called *ming* (明).

○ **Péng** (雱) said: Those who do not know constancy, though they possess slight wisdom in discerning things, yet fail to align with the Great Root—still, they may be said to have *ming* (明).

*Yìshēng* (益生) is auspicious signs.
The enlightened sovereign said: Auspicious signs are omens of fortune or misfortune; if one does not know how to guard constancy but instead seeks to augment life beyond its proper measure, they stir themselves toward death’s domain—this is called inauspicious signs.

○ **Hé** (河) said: Auspicious (*xiáng* 祥) means longevity.
It speaks of augmenting life to grow and expand oneself.

○ **Bì** (弼) said: Life cannot be augmented; to augment it is to invite monstrosity.
