○ **Péng** (雱) said: The principle of life is already complete, with neither deficiency nor excess. If one nourishes it uprightly, it will reach its fullness. To add further is but to encumber it. Auspicious signs are matters beyond the ordinary.

When the mind compels *qi*, it is called *qiáng* (强).

The enlightened sovereign said: The mind has judgments of right and wrong, while *qi* has no discrimination. If one forces the mind to compel *qi*, then they are the kind of strong-willed, domineering people.

○ **Hé** (河) said: The mind should be singularly harmonious and soft, while *qi* naturally manifests as internal duality, hence the body appears soft.
Yet if one reverses this, allowing *qi* to act recklessly, harmony departs from the center, and thus the form becomes rigid and strong.

○ **Bì** (弼) said: The mind ought not to compel *qi*—that is *qiáng* (强).

○ **Péng** (雱) said: If the mind compels *qi*, then *qi* in turn compels the mind. When mind and *qi* mutually compel each other, the natural harmony is ruined, and one’s true essence is diminished. What people receive is not something that can be augmented or diminished; thus, to increase life or deplete *qi* is both a loss of principle.

Mencius warned against cultivating the *shùmiáo* (枢苗) without weeding, while Laozi spoke of augmenting life and compelling *qi*—all these aim to preserve their pure *qi* (淳气).

This is not the strength of self-mastery, but the strength of domineering force.

When things reach their peak, they age.
○ **Hé** (河) said: All things, when they reach their full vigor, become withered and aged.

This is called *bùdào* (不道).

○ **Hé** (河) said: The aged do not attain the Dao/Way.

○ **Péng** (雱) said: The vigor and aging of people are matters of form.
Yet for those who possess a fixed portion of nature, they remain constant and unchanging. Those who have lost their true nature undergo transformations in form and mind—thus, when they are vigorous, their blood and *qi* overflow, and when aged, their spirit and vitality wane. Only those with profound *dé* (德) are otherwise: my mind has never strayed, so *qi* also remains constant and unchanging. Though the body may outwardly display vigor or aging, the mind does not differ from that of an infant—these are the ones who have attained the Dao/Way.

All things that change with the seasons and follow the cycles of vigor and aging are mere things; how can they be the Dao/Way?

Those who do not follow the Dao/Way age prematurely.
The enlightened sovereign said: All things, when they reach their peak, decline into aging—thus, the warning against pride in strength and arrogance in vigor means that such behavior does not accord with the Dao/Way, and one must cease early.

○ **Hé** (河) said: Those who do not attain the Dao/Way age prematurely—this is death.

○ **Péng** (雱) said: Those who do not follow the Dao/Way, even in their youth, have already lost the principle of life—thus, it is called premature aging.

**Chapter Fifty-Six: The Silent Wisdom of the Knowing**
The knowing do not speak.」

**Chapter Fifty-Seven: The Silent Wisdom of the Knowing (Continued)**

**Hé** said: The knowing value action, not speech.
○ **Bì** said: Follow spontaneity.
○ **Xiāo** said: Principle reaches its peak in silence.
Those who speak do not know.
The Enlightened Sovereign said: *Knowing* means awakening. *Speech* means argumentation.
○ **Hé** said: A four-horse chariot cannot outpace the tongue—many words bring many calamities.
○ **Bì** said: They create discord.
○ **Péng** said: Once one speaks, they have already strayed from true understanding.

**塞其兑** *(Block its mouth)*
The Enlightened Sovereign said: Those who awaken are unattached to teachings and do not cling to words—thus, it is said, *block its mouth*.
○ **Péng** said: Their spirit is undivided.

**闭其门** *(Close its gate)*
The Enlightened Sovereign said: When there is no attachment, the gate of desires is closed.
○ **Hé** said: To block and close is to cut off its source.

**挫其锐** *(Dull its sharpness)*
**Hé** said: When desires sharpen, one must recall the Dao’s non-action to blunt them.
○ **Bì** said: Nurture simplicity.

**解其纷** *(Untangle its chaos)*
**Hé** said: *Halting* refers to unresolved grudges—one must recall the Dao’s non-action to release them.
○ **Bì** said: Remove the roots of contention.

**和其光** *(Harmonize its brilliance)*
**Hé** said: Though one may possess singular insight, it must be harmonized to merge with obscurity, lest it disrupt with glare.
○ **Bì** said: Without singular display, all things remain free of partiality.

**同其尘** *(Unify its dust)*
**Hé** said: One must not distinguish oneself.
○ **Bì** said: Without partiality, all things remain free of shame.
○ **Péng** said: True knowing dwells thus within oneself.
This is called *Xuántóng* *(Mysterious Unity)*.

The Enlightened Sovereign said: Its resolution is as in *Chapter on the Dao’s Effortless Flow*—there, it speaks of the Dao; here, it speaks of the person. To say that a person embodies the Dao is to share in *Mysterious Unity’s virtue*.
○ **Hé** said: *Xuán* is Heaven—when a person acts in accordance with these highest matters, it is to align with Heaven’s Dao.
○ **Péng** said: When the Dao reaches *Xuán*, subject and object become one—without establishing self to oppose others, others cannot possess it.
Thus, it cannot be *intimately known*—
The Enlightened Sovereign said: Mysterious Unity is without bias, hence it cannot be *intimately known*.
○ **Hé** said: It does not take honor as joy, nor solitude as sorrow.
It cannot be *distanced*—
The Enlightened Sovereign said: It blends universally with all, hence it cannot be *distanced*.
○ **Hé** said: With a tranquil mind and no desires, it harbors no grievance toward others.
○ **Bì** said: If it can be *intimately known*, it can also be *distanced*.

It cannot be *exploited*—
The Enlightened Sovereign said: Without desires, it cannot be *exploited*.
○ **Hé** said: The body does not crave wealth and rank; the mouth does not crave the five flavors.

It cannot be *harmed*—
The Enlightened Sovereign said: Without contention, it cannot be *harmed*.
○ **Hé** said: It does not contend with the powerful for gain, nor with the bold for vigor.
○ **Bì** said: If it can be *exploited*, it can also be *harmed*.

It cannot be *elevated*—
The Enlightened Sovereign said: To embody the Dao naturally is to transcend elevation.
○ **Hé** said: It does not serve as a ruler in chaotic times, nor occupy a position under a tyrannical lord.

Nor can it be *degraded*—
The Enlightened Sovereign said: Pure and untainted, it transcends degradation.
○ **Hé** said: It does not rely on power to become proud, nor does it bend its will to avoid humiliation.
○ **Bì** said: If it can be *elevated*, it can also be *degraded*.

Thus, it is *noble beyond the world*.
The Enlightened Sovereign said: With awakening unobstructed, speech flows effortlessly; sharpness and chaos are resolved, brilliance and dust unified—neither intimate nor distant, neither elevated nor degraded, it is the world’s supreme responsibility.
○ **Hé** said: Its virtue is such that even the Son of Heaven cannot subdue it, nor lords bend it. It floats with the world, preserves itself, and avoids harm—thus, it is *noble beyond the world*.
○ **Bì** said: Nothing can surpass it.
○ **Péng** said: The silent do not speak in secrecy, but truly have nothing to say—why? Because they perceive no single principle.
To perceive no single principle means they are not ensnared by things, yet are not ensnared by things—how much more so can they be *intimately known, distanced, elevated, or degraded*? They are merely things.
Those who are not ensnared by things yet do not ensnare things—thus, though unhonored, they are always noble.

---
**Chapter Fifty-Seven: Governing with Rectitude**

**以正治国** *(Govern with rectitude)*
**Hé** said: *Yǐ* means *reach*. Heaven appoints those who rectify themselves to govern with daily rectitude.
○ **Péng** said: Governing the nation lies in exhausting the Dao’s rectitude alone—there is no room for private wisdom.

**以奇用兵** *(Employ the unconventional in warfare)*
**Hé** said: *Qí* means *deception*. Heaven appoints those skilled in deceit and falsehood to wield arms.
○ **Péng** said: War is not an instrument of the Dao, yet the Sage cannot do without it—only it must not be relied upon habitually, hence *employ the unconventional*.

**以无事取天下** *(Conquer the world with non-action)*
The Enlightened Sovereign said: To govern the world lies in *non-action*—if one governs through policies and teachings, or employs unconventional warfare, these are all contrary to the Dao. Only through *non-action and non-doing* can one conquer the world—these three sentences mark the standard.
○ **Hé** said: Heaven appoints those of *non-action and non-doing* to take the world as their ruler.
○ **Bì** said: To govern with the Dao brings peace; to govern with rectitude sparks unconventional warfare.

To conquer with *non-action* is to truly take the world.
The previous chapter states: Those who take the world always act with *non-action*—when they act, they are unworthy of taking the world.
Thus, to govern with rectitude is unworthy of taking the world, and so one employs unconventional warfare.
When one governs with rectitude but neglects the root, the branches grow shallow—people have nothing to grasp, hence it descends to unconventional warfare.
○ **Péng** said: To govern with rectitude means the world submits naturally—why resort to action?

「Indeed, the divine treasures of the world can only be possessed by those who practice *non-action*.
Hence, it is said below:

How do I know this to be so? By this.
**Mínghuáng** said: By this, one understands the lower realm.
○ **Hé** said: This refers to the present. *Lǎozi* spoke: How do I know Heaven’s intent to be thus? By what I see today.

The world abounds with prohibitions, yet the people grow ever poorer.
**Mínghuáng** said: When governing with strict decrees, one multiplies prohibitions, causing the people to abandon their livelihoods, thus making them ever poorer.
○ **Hé** said: The world refers to the sovereign. Prohibitions are restrictions. Too many decrees breed deceit, while excessive prohibitions risk the people’s survival, hence poverty.
○ **Péng** said: When affairs are restricted by prohibitions, the people are disturbed and lose their livelihoods—this is where chaos begins.

When the people wield many clever tools, the state grows more corrupt.
**Mínghuáng** said: Clever tools refer to stratagems. If the sovereign relies too heavily on stratagems, unable to return to simplicity, the people respond with deceit—thus the state grows ever more corrupt and chaotic.
○ **Hé** said: The people are the sovereigns and feudal lords. Many who favor cleverness—engraving palaces, embroidering robes, and adorning strange objects—lead the people to imitate them, lavishing gold, iron, jade, and vibrant dyes, worsening the trend daily.
○ **Bì** said: When the people are too clever, cunning arises. When cunning arises, evil deeds multiply.
○ **Péng** said: In ancient times, the people’s tools were crude and simple. As customs grew refined, their craftsmanship became ever more intricate, cleverness flourished, and morals declined—thus, extraordinary things increased.

One root benefits; the branches grow many.
When laws and decrees proliferate, thieves and bandits abound.
**Mínghuáng** and **Wáng Bì** both refer to *objects* as *decrees*.
○ **Mínghuáng** said: When *non-action* is lost and laws and decrees multiply, those who steal laws become criminals, and all become thieves and bandits—is this not their abundance?
○ **Hé** said: Laws and decrees refer to treasured objects. When rare and luxurious items proliferate, farming is neglected, and hunger and cold ensue—thus thieves and bandits abound.
○ **Bì** said: Establishing rectitude seeks to suppress evil, yet unconventional warfare is employed. Many prohibitions seek to shame poverty, yet the people grow ever poorer.
Those who desire clever tools to strengthen the state only make it more corrupt and chaotic—all stem from prioritizing the branches over the root, thus leading to this.

Therefore, the Sage says:」

「Hé said: This refers to *governing the people*.

When I practice *non-action*, the people transform themselves.
Hé said: The Sage speaks of my cultivation of the Dao/Way and alignment with Heaven, making no alterations, yet the people transform themselves naturally.

○ **Páng** said: *Non-action* means maintaining the constant Way. When the ruler maintains the constant Way, the people also maintain their constant Way, hence they transform themselves.
*Expanding upon the previous point about clever tools*: When I love tranquility, the people naturally become upright.
Hé said: The Sage speaks of my love of tranquility, not saying "do not teach," yet the people all become naturally loyal and upright.
○ **Páng** said: Loving tranquility means returning to one's true nature. When the ruler returns to their true nature, the people also return to their true nature, hence they become upright.

*Expanding upon the previous point about thieves and bandits*: When I have no interference, the people naturally become wealthy.
Hé said: When I have no burdens of forced labor or conscription, the people are secure in their livelihoods, hence they all naturally become wealthy.
○ **Páng** said: *Non-action* and loving tranquility mean I have no need for interference, and thus the people fulfill their lives. How could there be so many prohibitions?

When I have no desires, the people naturally become simple.
Mínghuáng said: *Non-action* leads to purity and tranquility, hence the people transform themselves.
Having no interference means no disturbance, hence the people naturally become wealthy.
Loving tranquility means attaining one's true nature, hence the people naturally become upright.
Having no desires means preserving harmony, hence the people naturally become simple.
This is the way to acquire the world without interference.

○ Hé said: I always have no desires. I abandon ornate speech and elaborate dress, and the people follow me in simplicity and plainness.
○ **Bì** said: The ruler’s desires, the people follow them swiftly.
My only desire is to have no desires, and thus the people also have no desires and naturally become simple.
These four principles honor the root to suppress the branches.

○ **Páng** said: *Expanding upon the previous point about strange objects*: I have often reasoned that the Sage’s governance transforms through *non-action*, upholds uprightness through loving tranquility, allows all to fulfill their potential in wealth and abundance, and ultimately returns them to simplicity. Thus, there are no clever tools or strange objects, nor are laws and decrees used as means of control.

The people’s tendency to abandon simplicity and pursue artifice all stems from excessive desires. If the ruler truly has no desires, how could the people obtain desires? This is the ultimate attainment of the *Emperor-Sage*.」

**Translation:**

**The Way of Governance (闷闷者第五十八)**

**He** said: Its governance and teachings are broad and expansive, dim and indistinct, as though unclear. Yet its people are pure and sincere.

**The Emperor-Sage** said: When governance is broad and expansive, without contrivance, the people respond with sincerity and natural simplicity.

**He** said: Because governance is broad and expansive, the people become pure, wealthy, and harmonious with one another.

**Bi** said: The art of good governance lies in having no fixed form or name, no rigid structures or strictures. Yet it achieves great order through this very dimness—thus it is called *dim governance*. The people, unburdened by competition, thrive in broad sincerity—thus it is called *pure people*.

**The Way of Governance (察察者)**

**He** said: Its governance is sharp and hasty, with decrees spoken and heard in haste.

**Páng** said: *察察* is excessive governance.

**The people are *缺缺*.**

**The Emperor-Sage** said: When governance is sharp and hasty, the people respond with depletion and decay.

**He** said: When governance is urgent, the people suffer hardship, and thus their vitality dwindles day by day.

**Bi** said: By establishing laws and punishments, distinguishing right from wrong, and suppressing deceit, governance becomes *察察*. When distinctions are rigidly drawn, the people grow contentious—thus it is called *缺缺*.

**Páng** said: *缺缺* refers to the broken and incomplete, as with shattered vessels.

**Disaster is the reverse of fortune.**

**He** said: *倚* means *to arise from*.

When disaster arises from fortune, those who suffer it may repent, reflect, and cultivate virtue—thus disaster departs and fortune returns.

**Fortune is the hidden source of disaster.**

**He** said: Disaster lies concealed within fortune. When fortune breeds arrogance, fortune departs and disaster comes.

**Who knows its ultimate measure?**

**The Emperor-Sage** said: *倚* means *to arise from*; *伏* means *to be concealed within*.

When governance is *dim*, the people assume there is no principle at work, yet they return to sincerity and simplicity—this is how disaster arises from fortune. When governance is *sharp*, the people assume rigid order prevails, yet they wither and decay—this is how fortune conceals disaster.

**He** said: Disaster and fortune alternate; who can fathom their limits?

**Páng** said: All things flow through the unity of *qi*, and the movement of *qi* cycles endlessly—returning to its beginning, shifting like water, with no ultimate end. Thus disaster and fortune succeed one another like the four seasons. The Sage knows this, so governance values moderation, avoiding excess. If governance is *察察*, seeking to uphold the proper and suppress the strange, to curb the anomalous and promote the good—all to exhaust fortune in the world—it fails to recognize that proper and strange, anomalous and good, transform into one another. Seeking fortune, it does not know that fortune’s excess becomes disaster. Thus Zhuangzi spoke in parable of the *dim* between talent and lack of talent. Then, in governance, is it not also the *dim* between discernment and obscurity?

Thus it is said: *dim governance*—for if one does not distinguish between good and evil, right and wrong, the lesser wise may find it unsettling. This aligns with the saying *"wild and boundless."* For in both cases, the logic is the same.

**Is there no proper way?**

*(The Emperor-Sage and Bi’s texts both originally include "邪" beneath "正")*

**He** said: *无* means *not*—that the ruler does not rectify himself, and thus there is no state.

**Páng** said: Who knows the ultimate of good governance? Only the *dim* can be held up—no fixed principle, no named law—yet the world transforms naturally. This is its ultimate.

**Páng** said: The great flow does not halt. What is timely is right; to fix the proper and strange, yet not know that the proper cannot be constant—can this be called wisdom?

Though it is said that *dim* is proper, it is proper by being *not-proper*.

*邪* is an uncertain term, also not fixed in *dim*.

**The proper becomes strange.**

**He** said: *奇* means *deceit*—when the ruler is not proper, even if the people are, they in turn deceive above.

**Bi** said: If governance upholds the proper, it will soon resort to the strange in warfare—thus the proper becomes strange.

**The good becomes anomalous.**

**The Emperor-Sage** said: At the extremes of disaster and fortune, is there no proper or strange? Yet beings cling to delusion, calling the proper *deceit* and the good *anomalous*—thus disaster and fortune alternate, as though there were no proper way.

**He** said: Even the virtuous transform above into anomalous omens.

**Bi** said: When goodness is established to harmonize the people, anomalous flattery soon arises—this is the peril of the anomalous.

**The age of delusion is long.**

**The Emperor-Sage** said: To call the proper *strange* and the good *anomalous*—such delusion has endured for long.

**He** said: The ruler’s delusion and loss of the proper have lasted long.

**Bi** said: The people’s delusion and abandonment of the Way have endured for long—do not hastily demand proper governance.

**Páng** said: The people have lost their nature yet remain unaware. Since the Way’s emergence, it has been thus—not the work of a single day. Yet rulers now pursue *察察* governance, yet seek to force the world into their one-sided properness. This is impossible, and they do not even know what they call *proper*—for they have never truly rectified.

The Sage acts differently: though square and straight, his virtue shines like the sun, yet his form remains naturally blended, not forcing a path upon the people. Thus the people rest, return to simplicity, and customs grow pure—how could this be mere minor correction?

Thus the Sage is square yet does not cut—

**He** said: The Sage practices rectitude to guide the people, not to sever them.

**Pì** said: Guide things with rectitude, yet abandon their perversity—do not sever things with rectitude. This is what is meant by *"the great square has no corners."*

**Páng** said: *"The great square has no corners"*—this speaks of blending naturally. If there is a path of sharp division, then boundaries are set between things.

**Lián** yet not **jù**—[The *Míng Huáng* and *Wáng Bì* manuscripts corrupt this as *jù* (harsh).]

**Hé** said: The Sage’s virtue is serene and clear, seeking to transform the people, not to harm them. Now it is otherwise: one rectifies oneself to harm others.

**Pì** said: *Kāng* (serene)—this is *qīng kāng* (clear and serene). *Chuàng* (injury)—this is harm. To use clear serenity to purify the people, removing their filth, yet not to inflict injury upon things with this clear serenity.

**Páng** said: Though there may be corners of serenity, they do not reach the point of harshness. [One manuscript corrupts *jù* (harsh) as *huì* (filthy)—this is incorrect.]

**Zhí** yet not **sì**—

**Hé** said: *Sì* (excess)—this is *shēn* (to press). The Sage, though straight, bends himself to follow others, not pressing his own views.

**Pì** said: Guide things with straightness, ordering them to abandon their eccentricities, yet do not provoke turmoil in things with this straightness—this is what is meant by *"the greatest straightness seems to bend."*

**Páng** said: Straightness that presses is merely straightness; the greatest straightness, in accordance with principle, is always yielding and conforming to principle.

**Guāng** yet not **yào**—

**Míng Huáng** said: The Sage excels in transformation, not carving boundaries to make them square, not inflicting injury to make them sharp, not pressing others to make them straight, not dazzling others to make them bright. Cultivate within oneself, and the world transforms naturally.

*Sì* (excess)—this is *shēn* (to press).

**Hé** said: Though the Sage possesses solitary wisdom, he remains as if in darkness, not dazzling others to disrupt them.

**Pì** said: Use light to reveal their confusion, yet do not shine light to seek out their hidden faults—this is what is meant by *"the bright Way seems dim."*

All these are to exalt the root and thus still the branches, not attacking yet causing return.

**Páng** said: Light that dazzles things is called *yào* (dazzling). To harmonize the light and return to its clarity—how could this be dazzling to blind things?

---
**Chapter Fifty-Nine: Governing People and Serving Heaven**

**Governing people**—

**Hé** said: This refers to the sovereign governing the people.

**Serving Heaven**—

**Hé** said: *Shì* (serve)—this means *yòng* (to apply). One should apply the Way of Heaven, following the four seasons.

**No better than *shè* (frugality).**

**Míng Huáng** said: Pronounced *ài* (love). When the sovereign seeks to govern people and serve Heaven, nothing surpasses frugality. Fill the granaries, let the people know propriety, and let the three seasons not be harmed—then Heaven will bestow auspicious omens. Harmony among the people suffices to govern them; Heaven’s preservation suffices to serve Heaven.

**Hé** said: *Shè*—this is *shí* (food). Those who govern the state should love the people’s resources, not indulging in extravagance. Those who cultivate themselves should cherish their vital essence, not dissipating it.

**Pì** said: *Mò ruò*—this means "nothing exceeds." *Shè*—the farmer’s method of tending the fields, striving to eliminate distinctions and return to uniformity. Preserve their natural state, not hastening their decay or disease, removing what causes decay and disease. Above, it aligns with Heaven’s mandate; below, it soothes the common people—nothing exceeds this.

**Páng** said: Governing people lies in rectifying oneself; serving Heaven lies in fulfilling one’s nature. These two are unified in *shè* (frugality) alone. Preserve one’s vital spirit, not wasting it externally or internally—this is frugality. Humanity’s original nature fills the six extremes, yet ultimately becomes insufficient—this is due to excess and reckless self-indulgence. Only frugality can preserve what Heaven has bestowed, not squandering it on vain pursuits. Then, is there any failing to fulfill one’s nature? Any failing to rectify oneself?

Mencius said: *"Exhaust your mind, know your nature—this is how one serves Heaven."* Indeed, preserving what was originally commanded ensures Heaven’s will is fulfilled. People share with me what they have received—if I truly preserve it, those who share it will respond accordingly. Then, what difficulty is there in governance?

Only frugality thus brings early return.

**Míng Huáng** said: How does one gather the people? It is through wealth—thus, if one is frugal and loving, the people from all directions will come bearing offerings, serving their sovereign early.

*Fú*—this means *shì* (to serve).

**Hé** said: *Zǎo*—this is *xiān* (early). *Fú*—this is *dé* (virtue). If one alone loves the people’s resources and cherishes vital essence, then one can first attain the Way of Heaven.

**Pì** said: *Fù*—this is *cháng* (constant).

**Páng** said: When movement reaches its extreme and becomes still, then its return is late. Only frugality, not indulging in self-expenditure, never strays far from its root—thus its return to stillness is early. [One manuscript corrupts *fù* (return) as *fú* (serve)—this is incorrect.] Early return is called *heavy accumulation of virtue.*

**Míng Huáng** said: Those who practice frugality thereby possess virtue, and the people, drawn to virtue, serve their sovereign early—hence, *"heavy accumulation of virtue."*

**Hé** said: Light attaining the Great Way is called *"heavy accumulation of virtue within oneself."*

**Pì** said: Only by heavy accumulation of virtue, not desiring swiftness, can one then cause early return to constancy—thus, *"early return is called heavy accumulation of virtue."*

**Páng** said: Virtue not wasted externally accumulates within. Accumulated within, its resources are inexhaustible—this accumulation is accumulation upon accumulation. Heavy accumulation of virtue thus conquers all.

**Míng Huáng** said: The Sage accumulates virtue, and the four seas return to ren—then there is nothing he cannot subdue.

*Kè*—this means *néng* (ability).

**Hé** said: *Kè*—this is *shèng* (to overcome). Heavy accumulation of virtue within oneself means overcoming all.

**Páng** said: Those who exhaust their nature will give birth to Heaven and Earth, govern creation and transformation—what cannot they accomplish? If there is nothing they cannot overcome, then none know their limits.

**Míng Huáng** said: The sovereign’s virtue knows no limits in governance—thus, none from afar know its extent.

**Hé** said: If there is nothing one cannot overcome, then none know the limits of one’s virtue.

**Bì** said: The Dao is boundless.

**Páng** said: To exhaust one’s nature is great indeed. Greatness in transformation makes one a Sage. Transformation is boundless—thus, none know its limits.

None knowing its limits is the way of having a realm.

**Míng Huáng** said: Only when none know its limits can one truly have a realm.

**Hé** said: If none know the limits of one’s virtue, then one can establish a state and bring blessings to the people.

**Bì** said: To claim a realm with finite limits is not to truly have a realm.

**Páng** said: The Sage refines coarse and pure alike—enough to mold Yao and Shun. What is there to having a realm?

The mother of having a realm is what makes it enduring.

**Míng Huáng** said: If one governs a realm and nourishes the people well, then its blessings and lineage shall endure.

**Hé** said: The realm and the body are one. The mother is the Dao. If one preserves the Dao within, letting qi not labor and the five spirits not suffer, then endurance is possible.

**Bì** said: The reason a realm is secure is called its mother. To accumulate virtue heavily is to focus on its roots—only then does one tend to the branches and achieve its end.

**Páng** said: The mother of having a realm is what makes one have a realm—none know its limits. This is called deep roots and firm foundations.

**Hé** said: If one takes qi as root and essence as the calyx, then as a tree’s roots are not deep, its calyx is not firm and it falls. One must deeply conceal the qi and firmly guard the essence, ensuring no leakage.

**Páng** said: Nature and life are the root; beyond this are branches and leaves. Those who lose their nature flourish in branches and leaves to harm the root. When the root is injured, essence and qi decline, and the calyx is not firm—this is why they die young.

Only when one does not harm the root from without does the root deepen, the calyx firm, and the form bind to life, delaying separation. *Zhì* (calyx), in one text *tí* (calyx)—sound and meaning are the same.

This is the Dao of longevity and enduring vision.

**Míng Huáng** said: Accumulating virtue to have a realm makes roots deep and calyx firm. The deeply firm is the Dao of longevity and enduring vision.

**Hé** said: Those with deep roots and firm calyx are on the path of longevity and enduring vision.

**Páng** said: Spirit manifests in the eyes, so when a person dies, the eyes grow dim and lose their light. If one conserves deeply, the calyx is firm, and the roots deep—thus, vision endures long.

**Chapter Sixty: Governing a Great Realm as Cooking Fresh Delicacies**

**Míng Huáng** said: When cooking fresh delicacies, one must not disturb them. When governing a great realm, one must not weary the people. To weary them is to exhaust the people; to disturb them is to spoil the fish.

**Hé** said: *Xian* (fresh) refers to fish. When cooking small fish, one does not remove the intestines or scales, nor dare one disturb it, for fear it will disintegrate. To weary the people in governing the realm brings chaos below; to weary oneself in cultivating the body scatters the spirit.

**Bì** said: It means not to agitate. Agitation brings many harms; stillness preserves the true. Thus, the greater the realm, the more still its ruler must be, only then can one broadly win the hearts of the people.

**Páng** said: In cooking fresh delicacies, one only waits for them to cook naturally, applying no artificial skill—if stirred and disturbed, they will spoil. The Dao of governing a realm is the same: though the realm be vast as the world, if one follows the Way, it is very easy to govern. Those who wield the Way over the world make their spirits unmanifest. **Míng Huáng** said: When one governs the world with the Way, seeking no false blessings, thus the spirits have no means to display their marvels and harm people.

**Hé** said: When one governs the world with virtue and the Dao, the spirits dare not manifest their spirits to harm people.

**Bì** said: When governing a great realm, it is like cooking fresh delicacies; when applying the Way to the world, its spirits are unmanifest.

**Páng** said: If the people are undisturbed, they may fully manifest their nature. When the people manifest their nature, the harmony of Heaven and Earth responds, and all things flourish without obstruction. Thus, the spirits also rest in their place and cannot manifest as marvels. Though human and spirit follow different paths, when their interactions become entangled, the *qi* of yin and yang becomes disordered, and their proper places are lost—thus, all things gain opportunity to exploit the chaos. It is not that the spirits lack marvels, nor that their marvels harm people. **Míng Huáng** said: Earlier, it was said that the spirits are unmanifest—not that their marvels are extinguished, but that their marvels do not appear to harm people.

**Hé** said: The spirits are not without spirit—evil cannot enter the upright, and thus they cannot harm the natural people.

**Bì** said: The marvels do not harm the natural. Things abide in their nature, and the marvels have nothing to add; when the marvels have nothing to add, one does not know the marvels as marvels. It is not that the marvels do not harm people, nor that the Sage does not harm people. **Míng Huáng** said: When spirits manifest marvels, they harm people; when the Sage acts, they harm people. Now, the reason the spirits do not manifest marvels to harm people is that the Sage acts with non-action and stillness.

**Hé** said: It is not that the spirits and marvels cannot harm people—when the Sage is in power and does not harm people, the spirits dare not interfere.

**Bì** said: When the Way is pervasive, the marvels do not harm people; when the marvels do not harm people, one does not know the marvels as marvels. When the Way is pervasive, the Sage also does not harm people; when the Sage does not harm people, one also does not know the Sage as Sage. It is as if one does not know the marvels as marvels, nor the Sage as Sage. Those who rely on coercive laws to control things are signs of declining governance. When one does not know the marvels and the Sage as such, this is the ultimate of the Way.

**Páng** said: Only the Sage does not harm people, thus the harmony of Heaven and Earth responds, and people and spirits each flourish without mutual harm. The two do not harm each other—**Hé** said: The spirits and the Sage, both do not harm each other—thus virtue returns and is reciprocated. **Míng Huáng** said: When spirits and marvels harm people, the realm suffers loss and its foundations weaken; when the Sage harms people, the spirit is depleted and sacrifices fail. Now, since neither harms the other, virtue returns and is reciprocated.

**Hé** said: When the two do not harm each other, the people are governed in the yang, the spirits in the yin; the people preserve their life and nature, the spirits preserve their spirit—thus virtue returns and is reciprocated.

**Bì** said: The marvels do not harm people, nor does the Sage harm people; the Sage does not harm people, nor do the marvels harm people—thus it is said the two do not harm each other. The marvels and the Sage unite in the Way and return virtue.

**Páng** said: The people return virtue to the spirits; the spirits return virtue to the people. *(End of Volume Eight of the* Dao De Jing *with Annotations by Míng Huáng, Hé, Wáng Bì, and Wáng Páng.)*

**Chapter Sixty-One: The Great State as the Downstream**

**The Great State as the Downstream**
The great state is the downstream.
**Hé** said: Governing a great state should be like residing in the downstream—never opposing the subtle currents.
**Bì** said: The rivers and seas dwell great yet low, and thus all streams flow to them. The great state dwells great yet low, and thus all under Heaven flows to it. Hence it is said, *"The great state is the downstream."*

**Páng** said: Like the rivers and seas to all the valleys. The confluence of all under Heaven.
**Míng Huáng** said: The downstream is the virtue of humility. The great state, if it opens itself in humility, will attract all under Heaven.

**Hé** said: The great state is where the scholars and commoners of all under Heaven gather.
**Bì** said: The place where all under Heaven converges.
**The Feminine of All Under Heaven**
**Hé** said: The feminine is of the yin category—soft, humble, harmonious, and unaggrieved.
**Bì** said: Quiet yet unseeking, and things return to it of their own accord.
**Páng** said: One should receive things with the stillness of the feminine. *(Original text: "One root: 'the confluence of all under Heaven'")*

**The Feminine Always Overcomes the Masculine Through Stillness**
**Hé** said: The female submits to the male, yet yin overcomes yang through tranquility, seeking first to be low.
**The Stillness as the Low**
**Míng Huáng** said: When the people of all under Heaven converge through humility, it is like the feminine’s stillness—always sought by the masculine’s agitation, hence stillness as the low.

**Hé** said: The Dao of yin takes tranquility as humility.
**Bì** said: Because of its stillness, it can be low.
The feminine is the receptive; the masculine is impulsive and desiring. The receptive always remains still, thus overcoming the impulsive. Because of its stillness, it can be low, and thus things return to it.
**Páng** said: *(Original text: "One root: 'because of its stillness, it is low'")*

**Thus the Great State Subdues the Small State**
**Bì** said: *"The great state subdues"* means the great state, in being low, takes the small state.
**Hé** said: If it can humble itself, it will always possess it.
**Bì** said: The small state attaches itself to it.
The small state subdues the great state, then takes the great state.
**Míng Huáng** said: The great takes the small as subjects; the small takes the great as allies.

**Hé** said: This means that states, whether great or small, if they practice humility and nurture others, will have no faults.
**Bì** said: The great state receives them.
Thus, sometimes it is low to take, sometimes it is low while taking.
**Míng Huáng** said: *"To take"* refers to the great taking the small. *"While taking"* refers to the small taking the great.
**Hé** said: *"The low"* means the great state is low to the small state, the small state is low to the great state, and they take each other through righteousness.
**Bì** said: It speaks only of cultivating lowliness, then each obtains what it should.
**Páng** said: By nature, one does not seek to subdue others; if one acts kindly, it is not to take others, but by nature, one is low to others, and others naturally return to it.

**The Great State Does Not Seek to Overwhelm Others**
The great state does not seek to overwhelm others; the small state does not seek to serve others.
**Míng Huáng** said: The great state, in humbling itself to the small state, does not seek to overwhelm them as subjects. The small state, in offering tribute and submission to the great state, does not seek to serve it as an ally.

**Hé** said: To make them subjects and servants.
Both thus obtain what they desire, hence the great should be low.

**Míng Huáng** said: One seeks subjects, the other seeks allies—both obtain what they desire. Yet the great state must always guard against fullness, hence it is said the great should be low.
**Hé** said: The great and small states each seek what they desire; the great state should especially practice humility.
**Bì** said: The small state cultivates lowliness only to preserve itself and cannot make all under Heaven return to it. The great state cultivates lowliness, and all under Heaven returns to it. Hence it is said, *"each obtains what it desires, thus the great should be low."*

**Páng** said: *(Original text: "Balanced in taking: the great state is low to the small state, which is the Dao of joying in Heaven. The small state is low to the great state, which is the Dao of fearing Heaven. Joy and fear of Heaven are matters of power. The Dao is the mystery of all things.")*

---

**Chapter Sixty-Two: The Dao as the Mystery of All Things**

**The Dao as the Mystery of All Things**
The Dao is the mystery of all things.
**Míng Huáng** said: All things draw upon the wondrous origin to take form—this is where all things derive their sustenance, hence it is said to be the mystery. *(Mystery: inner essence.)*
**Hé** said: Mystery means "to conceal." The Dao conceals all things, containing all without exception.
**Bì** said: Mystery is like "love"—a term for what can shelter and shade.
**Páng** said: The Great Dao is profound and secret, able to shelter all things and where all things are hidden.

**The Treasure of the Virtuous**
**Míng Huáng** said: The virtuous know that those who uphold the Dao flourish, while those who lose it perish, hence they always treasure it without neglect.
**Hé** said: The virtuous take the Dao as their personal treasure, daring not to defy it.
**Bì** said: Treasure is for use.
**Páng** said: The treasure of the virtuous—what the Sage embodies. *(Original text: "One version: 'the treasure of the virtuous'")*

**The Preservation of the Unvirtuous**
**Míng Huáng** said: Preservation means "to rely upon." The unvirtuous, unable to treasure the Dao, when facing ruin, seek to rely upon it for self-preservation.
**Hé** said: The Dao is what the unvirtuous rely upon in times of crisis. Even then, they know to repent and humble themselves.
**Bì** said: Preservation is for survival.

**Fine Words Can Be Bartered**
**Hé** said: Fine words are only fit for the marketplace—once the exchange is done, they depart, and no further pleasantries are exchanged. Buyers seek swift purchase, sellers swift sale.

**Noble Conduct Can Be Bestowed Upon Others**
**Míng Huáng** said: Sweetening one’s speech can win barter; elevating one’s conduct can influence others. This illustrates the Sage: with sweet and lawful speech, and noble, tranquil conduct, transforming the unvirtuous—just as merchants sell goods, leading others to follow virtue.

Thus it follows that the lower text states:

**Hé** said: *Jiā* (加), means to distinguish.
When a person possesses noble and distinguished conduct, they can be set apart from the common folk, yet this alone is not enough to honor the Dao.

**Bì** said: The Dao precedes all speech, and nothing is more precious than this.
Even with treasures of jade and fine horses, they cannot uphold the Dao.
Sweet words can sway the market’s value, thus it is said: *Fine words can be bartered*.
Noble conduct can draw response even from afar, thus it is said: *Noble conduct can be bestowed upon others*.

**Pān** said: *Fine words and noble conduct* are the lesser currents of the Dao, yet they still suffice to barter and bestow upon others—how much more so for those who embody the Dao itself!
(One text omits the character *yú* (於).)

Why should the unvirtuous be abandoned?
**Míng Huáng** said: The unvirtuous are not to be abandoned; they too must be transformed. Why should they be cast aside?

**Hé** said: Though people may be unvirtuous, they should be transformed by the Dao. Before the Three Sovereigns (三皇), was there ever abandonment of the people? The virtue of their transformation was pure and simple.

**Bì** said: The unvirtuous must rely upon the Dao to avoid imitation.

**Pān** said: The marketplace unites through mutual benefit, but human nature resents those above them and cannot be bestowed upon.
If one has sweet words and noble conduct, even those driven by profit may engage with them, and noble conduct can be bestowed without resentment.
Thus, those who embody the Dao—when transforming others, what can they not subdue?
Hence, the unvirtuous are not abandoned.

Therefore, the Sage establishes the Son of Heaven and appoints the Three Ministers:
**Míng Huáng** said: To teach the unvirtuous.

**Hé** said: To make the teaching of the unvirtuous possible.

**Bì** said: To speak of honoring noble conduct and the Dao.
Even with a jade tablet held high and four horses leading the way, it is better to sit and present this Dao.
**Míng Huáng** said: Though the Three Ministers assist with jade tablets and four horses, even if they come in silence, it is still not as good as sitting and presenting this Dao of *Wuwei* (無為) to me, so that I may transform others.

**Hé** said: Though jade tablets and four horses come first, it is still not as good as sitting and presenting this Dao.

**Bì** said: This is what was spoken of earlier—establishing the Son of Heaven and appointing the Three Ministers, honoring their rank, valuing their persons, all for the sake of the Dao. Nothing is more precious than this, so even if jade tablets are held high and four horses lead the way, it is still not as good as sitting and presenting this Dao.

**Pān** said: The Son of Heaven and the Three Ministers take transforming the people as their duty. If the Dao is practiced, the people will naturally come to reverence. Jade tablets and horses are used to attract the worthy, and attracting the worthy is the greatest of governance. Though the worthy are obtained, if the Dao cannot be presented, the people will still not submit. Thus, it is better not to seek externally but to present the essence of the Dao.

Why is this Dao held in such esteem in antiquity?
**Míng Huáng** said: *Why?* is an interrogative phrase.
Does it mean *seeking to obtain*?
**Hé** said: In antiquity, this Dao was held in esteem because one did not need to travel far to seek it—it could be found within oneself.

Does it mean *using guilt to be pardoned*?
**Hé** said: *Guilt* refers to those in turbulent times under tyrannical rulers, who suffer unjust punishment. Cultivating the Dao can dissolve death, freeing one from all calamities.

**Pān** said: *Seeking to obtain* is why the virtuous cherish it; *using guilt to be pardoned* is why the unvirtuous rely upon it.
Thus, it is held in esteem throughout the world.

**Míng Huáng** said: The Dao lies in awakening, not in seeking. It is not like wealth, which can be sought daily and obtained. Thus it is said: *Not daily seeking to obtain*.
Once awakened, there is no guilt to burden oneself—why wait for guilt to arise before seeking pardon?
It is thus held in esteem throughout the world.

**Hé** said: The virtue of the Dao is vast and profound, encompassing all without exception. It preserves the body and governs the state, tranquil and effortless, thus it can be held in esteem throughout the world.

**Bì** said: Seeking brings obtaining; seeking pardon brings freedom. It applies to all without fail, thus it is held in esteem throughout the world.

**Chapter Sixty-Three: On *Wuwei* (無為)**
On *Wuwei* (無為)...

**Chapter Sixty-Three: On *Wuwei* (無為) — Continued**

**Hé** said: Follow completion to cultivate—thus there is no artificial contrivance.
**Páng** said: This is the Dao in action.

*Wuwei* in affairs—doing nothing in action.
**Hé** said: Prepare beforehand to eliminate troubles and simplify matters.
**Páng** said: This is the Dao in practice.

*Wuwei* in taste—flavorless flavor.
**Hé** said: Deep contemplation and foresight—this is savoring the Dao’s intent.
**Bì** said: To dwell in *wuwei*, to teach through silence, to find serenity in simplicity—this is the pinnacle of governance.
**Páng** said: This is savoring the Dao. These three matters—*wuwei*, *wuwei* in action, and *wuwei* in taste—are the constants of the Dao. Though one practices *wuwei* as a constant, one does not dare neglect it, thus light as it is toward all things, yet when encountering things with the Dao, and reaching toward things, one does not dare be negligent. Hence it is said below:

Great and small, much and little—
**Hé** said: This is setting forth warnings and prohibitions.
Desiring greatness, one returns to smallness; desiring much, one returns to little—this is the natural Way.
**Páng** said: To fear small matters as great, to regard few things as many—*Zhuangzi* says: *"Do not overlook others."*

Repaying enmity with virtue.
**Míng Huáng** said: In *wuwei*, in doing nothing in action, in savoring nothing in taste—if greatness and smallness, much and little, do not exceed their proper measure, then there will be none to harbor enmity. When desires arise from circumstances and transgressions harm nature, then neither great nor small shall escape the retribution of enmity. Now, having preserved one’s measure and maintained harmony, thus one repays enmity with virtue.
**Hé** said: Cultivating the Dao and doing good—this cuts off misfortune before it arises.
**Bì** said: A small enmity is insufficient to repay a great one; thus what the world desires to punish, what aligns with universal accord, is virtue.
**Yú** said: To repay enmity with justice is mere action. To repay enmity with virtue is virtue itself. Action shares in fortune and calamity with the people, hence all the above methods—none may be omitted. But virtue transcends all things; how could enmity arise? These three matters above are the embodiment of the Dao. When one embodies the Dao, thus it must be so.

Raise enmity and repay with virtue—then one knows virtue is never unused.

Plan for difficulty when it is easy—
**Hé** said: To harmonize with great difficulties, one must act when they are still easy, before they take form.
To achieve greatness when it is yet small—
**Míng Huáng** said: Those who indulge their passions and desires find nothing easy, nothing small—now, to plan for their great difficulties and undertake their vast enterprises, one must act when nature is undispersed and measure is untransgressed—that is, when things are still easy and small.
**Hé** said: To accomplish great deeds, one must begin with small ones—for calamity arises from the small.
**Yú** said: Though one who relies on virtue practices *wuwei* as a constant, yet when encountering affairs and things, one must always be vigilant and prepared, as the *Classic of Documents* says: *"Diligently, diligently—day and night, myriad affairs."* *Zhuangzi* says: *"To have and yet make it easy—Heaven does not approve. The world’s calamities often arise from what is overlooked. Be wary of what is easy and what is small, then there will never be blame."*

The world’s great difficulties must begin in what is easy—
**Hé** said: Difficulty arises from ease.
The world’s great enterprises must begin in what is small—
**Míng Huáng** said: This clarifies the earlier point about preemptive planning.
**Hé** said: From smallness to manifestation.
**Páng** said: What is easy emerges from what is overlooked; what is small grows from the subtle to the manifest.

Thus the Sage never pursues what is great—
**Hé** said: This is dwelling in humility.
And so is able to achieve what is great.
**Míng Huáng** said: Having said that great enterprises must begin in smallness, this explains how the Sage can achieve greatness—by not pursuing what is difficult or vast, thus attaining true grandeur.
**Hé** said: This is why the world reverts to them.
**Páng** said: The Sage constantly attends to small matters to accomplish great deeds. Once the work is done, this is what is called the Great Person. The *Odes* say: *"If small difficulties are neglected, the Central States will decline."* This also illustrates how great governance lies in the accumulation of small things.

Heedless promises bring few truths—
**Hé** said: This is not taking words lightly.
Too many easy actions bring many difficulties—
**Míng Huáng** said: Light promises to others will lead to few truths; frequent ease in action will inevitably bring many difficulties.
**Hé** said: This is not being cautious in one’s breath.
Thus even the Sage finds it difficult—
**Hé** said: The Sage, in all actions and undertakings, still weighs progress and retreat with difficulty, seeking to stem their source.
**Bì** said: Even with the Sage’s talent, they still find difficulty in what is small and easy—how much more so for those without such virtue? Thus it is said: *"Even the Sage finds it difficult."*
Thus there will never be difficulty.
**Míng Huáng** said: Difficulty arises from light promises and ease in action, hence there will never be great difficulty.
**Hé** said: The Sage, throughout their life, avoids what is difficult, yet still shuns harm deeply.
**Páng** said: The Sage does not merely guard against the subtle to avoid creating trouble—they always regard action with caution and never act lightly. Thus there will never be difficulty.

---
**Chapter Sixty-Four: On *Its An-Ease-Holding* (其安易持)**

Its ease in holding—
**Hé** said: Governing the body and the state—what is tranquil is easy to uphold.

Its premonitory signs are easy to plan for—
**Míng Huáng** said: When one’s nature is upright and tranquil, and one wishes to grasp and hold it, lest it scatter—even if desires have not yet taken form, one plans to cut them off, making them not arise. This is very easy.

**Hé** said: Emotions, desires, and calamities—when their signs have not yet manifested, they are easy to forestall.
**Páng** said: Vigilance must come before matters arise—*"To act before there is anything to act upon."*

Its fragility is easy to dispel—
**Hé** said: Calamities have not yet stirred in the court; desires have not yet shown in expression—like something fragile, easy to break apart.
**Páng** said: *"Fragile"* is here used in place of *"breakable."*

Its subtlety is easy to disperse—
**Míng Huáng** said: When desire first takes root, it is still perilous and fragile—those who cut it off find it easy to break.

「When calamities first arise, their signs are still subtle—if one wishes to prevent them, their subtlety makes them easy to disperse.

**Hé** said: When their signs have not yet manifested, they are still small and easy to disperse.

**Bì** said: Though one may fail to act, there is no harm; because of their subtlety and fragility, they are not yet sufficient to provoke great consequences—this is why they are easy to address.

These four teachings all speak of *cautious completion* (慎终). One must not abandon them because there is no harm yet, nor neglect to disperse them because they are subtle. If there is no harm yet but one does not hold to caution, harm will arise. If they are subtle but not dispersed, great harm will follow. Therefore, considering the potential harm at its end, as one would the beginning of a calamity, ensures no failure in affairs.

**Páng** said: *"To act before there is anything to act upon"* (为之於未有) and *"to treat before chaos arises"* (治於未乱).

**Míng Huáng** said: *"Covering above"* (覆上) is easy to grasp and easy to plan—this is why it is easy, for one acts before the signs and forms have manifested.

**Hé** said: If one wishes to take action, it must be done when the signs have not yet sprouted—one should block the source.

**Bì** said: This means to secure what is still tranquil and unmanifested.

*"To treat before chaos arises"* (治之於未乱).

**Míng Huáng** said: *"Covering above"* (覆上) is easy to break and easy to disperse—this is why it is easy, for one removes the disorder before it has fully formed into calamity.

**Hé** said: In governing oneself and the state, when chaos has not yet arisen, one must seal and close its gates.

**Bì** said: This means to block what is subtle and fragile.

A tree thick enough to embrace grows from a tiny sprout.
**Hé** said: From small to great.
**Páng** said: Growth.

A nine-tiered tower begins with piled soil.
**Hé** said: From low to high.
**Páng** said: Accumulation.

A thousand-li journey starts beneath one’s feet.
**Míng Huáng** said: These two teachings illustrate how one must not delay in planning well, lest later harm arise.

**Hé** said: From near to far.

**Páng** said: Progress.

These three teachings all describe the principle of *transformation* (物化), from near to far, from subtle to manifest. Therefore, the Sage does not dare to provoke the beginnings of things, lest they grow and spread, for fear that they will later become great and distant, leaving ruin for posterity.

Hence it is said: *"If you act upon what has already transformed, I shall suppress it with the nameless simplicity."* Then the Sage’s foresight is indeed early.

Those who act upon things bring about their ruin.
**Hé** said: To act upon affairs is to ruin spontaneity; to act upon righteousness is to ruin benevolence; to act upon form is to ruin vitality and spirit.

Those who cling to things lose them.
**Míng Huáng** said: Ordinary emotions cannot follow their natural course; artificial schemes are external. Those who act seek fulfillment, but their very schemes bring ruin. In affairs, one cannot abandon and forget them—every movement creates attachment. Attachment seeks possession, but its very nature brings loss.

**Hé** said: Clinging to advantage invites ruin; clinging to the Dao preserves life. Insisting on what one cannot hold, while yielding and letting go returns to its source.

**Bì** said: One should use *cautious completion* to remove what is subtle, and vigilance over what is subtle to prevent chaos. Yet if one acts upon it and clings to it, the origins of affairs will only multiply, and clever scheming will breed further complications—this is why ruin and loss occur.

**Páng** said: The True Constant (真常) has no form. If one imposes form upon it, one violates its nature. To act and to cling are both acts of the will—if I have this will, then the people’s desires will grow daily. How can this be called *"securing tranquility and planning before signs manifest"*? Therefore, the Sage acts without acting, and thus there is no ruin.

**Hé** said: The Sage does not value ostentatious words, nor does he pursue superficial gains or violent conquests—thus there is no ruin or loss.

Without clinging, there is no loss.
**Hé** said: The Sage possesses virtue to teach the ignorant and wealth to aid the poor, holding nothing back for himself—thus he loses nothing to others.

**Páng** said: If I do not ruin the True Constant nor lose my inherent nature, then the world will also be complete. This is its inherent nature.

Therefore, when the people engage in affairs, they often bring ruin upon themselves when success is nearly achieved.
**Míng Huáng** said: Those who begin with good intentions often ruin their own progress when success is near.

**Hé** said: *"From"* (从) means *"to act"* (为).

When the people act upon affairs, they often, when their merits are nearly complete, become greedy for position and reputation, indulgent in luxury, and filled with arrogance—thus they ruin themselves.

**Bì** said: They lack *cautious completion*.

**Páng** said: Affairs have their natural course; when the time comes, they will be complete. *Zhuangzi* says: *"The beauty of completion lies in its duration."* Yet the people are foolish and unaware, ignorant of this principle. They are hasty and seek speed, aiding human efforts against the natural order—thus, when affairs are nearly complete, they often meet ruin. This is the fault of letting humans overcome nature, of letting affairs overcome the Dao.

If one is *cautious in completion as in the beginning*, then there will be no ruined affairs.

**Míng Huáng** said: If one is vigilant in its final stages, always maintaining the original intention of doing good, then there will certainly be no overstepping or ruin.

**Hé** said: The end should be like the beginning—one must not slacken.

**Páng** said: Affairs naturally unfold on their own; they do not rely on external force. Like planting seeds, one only needs to deepen their roots and remove their harm—then, when autumn comes, they will flourish. Their completion can be awaited.

Therefore, the Sage desires what others do not desire.
**Hé** said: The Sage desires what others do not desire—when people’s desires are manifest, the Sage desires to conceal their brilliance; when people seek adornment, the Sage desires simplicity; when people crave form, the Sage desires virtue.

**Páng** said: *"Desiring what is not desired"* is not the absence of desire—desire lies in not desiring it. Therefore, one does not value treasures that are difficult to obtain.

Not valuing treasures that are difficult to obtain—
**Míng Huáng** said: Treasures that are difficult to obtain are those beyond one’s inherent nature. Now, the Sage, in desiring what is not desired, does not scheme for what lies outside his nature—thus he always preserves his natural state. This is not valuing treasures that are difficult to obtain.

**Hé** said: The Sage is not dazzled by artificial adornment, nor does he devalue stones while esteeming jade.

**Bì** said: Though desires may be subtle, competition and striving will arise from them; though treasures that are difficult to obtain may be minor, their value will provoke theft.

**Páng** said: The Sage’s principle of *"no action, no clinging"* means he does not yet reach the stage of complete abandonment and forgetfulness—only that he does not scheme beyond his inherent nature, generating further desires. Moreover, when the people have food to eat and warm clothing, these are necessities of their nature, which they cannot avoid. They desire no more than this, and thus they do not have further desires.»

「Yet after abandoning inherent nature, further valuing treasures that are difficult to obtain—this is the folly of the ignorant, straying far from their original foundation.

Thus, the Sage *wishes yet does not wish*, using this to awaken their confusion and return them to their inherent nature. He *learns yet does not learn*—

**Hé** said: The Sage learns what ordinary people cannot learn. Ordinary people learn cleverness and deceit; the Sage learns natural spontaneity. Ordinary people learn to govern the world; the Sage learns to govern himself, safeguarding the true Dao.

**Páng** said: *Learning yet not learning* is not the absence of learning—rather, what is learned lies in *not learning*, to rectify the excesses of the multitude. To rectify the excesses of the multitude—

**Hé** said: The multitude’s pursuit of learning is inverted; their errors treat the root as a branch and reality as mere ornamentation. To rectify this is to return them to their root.

**Bì** said: Those who are skilled without learning are guided by natural spontaneity. This is a caution against the errors of those who do not learn. Thus, *learning yet not learning* is to rectify the excesses of the multitude.

**Páng** said: The multitude chases superficiality and multiplies affairs, while the Sage, through *learning yet not learning*, seizes their errors and returns them to the Dao. To aid the natural spontaneity of all things—

**Hé** said: Teaching people to return to true roots is to assist all things in fulfilling their natural spontaneity, yet he does not dare to act.

**Míng Huáng** said: The Sage does not seek learning beyond his inherent nature—this is *learning yet not learning*, intending to restore the multitude’s excessive learning and assist their natural spontaneity. Thus, he does not dare to engage in vulgar learning or excessive desires.

**Páng** said: Aiding natural spontaneity is what *Zhuāngzi* calls *"returning to align with Heaven"*—to act otherwise would extinguish human nature in favor of Heaven’s. Thus, he does not dare to act.

Then, all things rest in the constancy of their **性命** (*xìngmìng*), and affairs reveal no traces of deviation. How could there exist anything fragile enough to float away or subtle enough to disperse?」

「Hé said: "Discussing the ancient **Dao**-adept, one who governs himself with the **Dao** and extends this to governing the realm. He does not do so to enlighten the people, but rather—

Hé said: "He does not teach the people with his **Dao**, for that would merely reveal their cunning and cleverness. Instead, he seeks to make them simple.

**Míng Huáng** said: "A ruler skilled in the **Dao** does not openly display his **Dao** to the people, but rather guides them with harmony, leading them back to their **pǔ** (primordial simplicity), making them like the unknowing.

○ Hé said: "He teaches the people with **virtue** and **power** (*déyuè*), so that their **pǔ** (primordial simplicity) remains uncorrupted by deceit and falsehood.

○ Bì said: "‘Enlighten’ refers to their many knowledges obscuring their **pǔ** (primordial simplicity), while ‘make them simple’ means they lack knowledge yet uphold truth, relying on natural spontaneity.

○ Páng said: "What he hates in the ‘simple’ is not ignorance—those who do not understand yet defy laws and prohibitions—but rather, he opposes those who deviate from the **pǔ** (primordial simplicity), indulging in cleverness and deceit. The *Shījīng* (*Book of Poetry*) says: ‘Unknowing yet unknowing, following the norms of constancy.’ The difficulty in governing the people lies in their excessive cleverness.

**Míng Huáng** said: "When a ruler governs by enlightenment, the people must exert their cleverness to respond to him. If cleverness is excessive, deceit arises, making governance difficult.

○ Hé said: "Because of their excessive cleverness, they resort to deceit and falsehood.

○ Bì said: "Excessive cleverness and deceit make them difficult to govern.

○ Páng said: "Cleverness makes them hard to know. Thus, governing with cleverness is the ruin of the state.

**Míng Huáng** said: "‘With’ is ‘use’ (*yòng*). If a ruler employs clever ministers to govern, they will inevitably create laws. When laws are made, wickedness thrives, hence they become the ruin of the state.

○ Hé said: "To let clever and wise people govern state affairs is to distance oneself from **virtue** and **power** (*déyuè*), to arbitrarily wield authority and rewards, and thus become the ruin of the state.

○ Bì said: "Cleverness is akin to governance (*zhì*). To govern with cleverness is why it is called ‘the ruin of the state.’ The people are difficult to govern because of their excessive cleverness. Thus, one must block their cunning and close the gates, making them ignorant and unmusical, while using cleverness to guard against stirring the people’s evil hearts. Once stirred, they will again resort to cleverness to guard against deceit, and their scheming will grow ever more refined, while wickedness and falsehood flourish further. Hence, it is said that governing with cleverness is the ruin of the state.

○ Páng said: "If one governs by discernment, the people will vie to emerge with their cleverness and deceit. *Zhuāngzi* says: ‘Those who open the way for others are the ones who breed thieves,’ referring to this very point. Not governing with cleverness is the blessing of the state.

**Míng Huáng** said: "If one does not employ clever and cunning ministers, but instead selects those of pure **virtue** (*chún dé*), who rest quietly and flourish naturally, who play with the **Way** (*dào*) and resolve difficulties, then cleverness and deceit will wane daily, while simplicity and **pǔ** (primordial simplicity) will arise. The people will be harmonious, and the years will bring abundance, hence this is the blessing of the state.

○ Hé said: "If one does not let clever and wise people govern state affairs, the people will uphold rectitude, not indulging in evil adornments. The ruler and subjects will grow close, and the sovereign and ministers will work together in harmony, hence this is the blessing of the state.

○ Páng said: "A ruler must model himself after Heaven, and modeling Heaven means embodying the **Dao**. To abandon the **Dao** and rely on cleverness is not his proper role. As for the **至人** (*Zhìrén*—perfected/true person), who does nothing yet the world returns to **pǔ** (primordial simplicity), the blessings are beyond words. *Zhuāngzi* says: ‘Those who close the gates of Heaven are where **virtue** (*dé*) arises,’ referring to this very point.

Knowing these two principles is also a standard (*kǎishì*).

**Míng Huáng** said: "Employing cleverness and deceit harms the people, while entrusting pure **virtue** (*chún dé*) brings blessings to the state. If a ruler knows these two principles, he will entrust himself to ministers of pure **virtue**, making them the standard and model for governance.

○ Hé said: "The two principles are cleverness and lack of cleverness. The clever can become thieves, while the unknowing can bring blessings, hence they are the standards for governing oneself and the realm.

○ Páng said: "‘Standard’ (*kǎi*) is sometimes written as ‘**稽**’ (*jì*). Knowing the standard is called **玄德** (*Xuándé*—mysterious virtue).

**Míng Huáng** said: "If a ruler always knows whom to entrust, this is called profound, mysterious, and supreme **virtue** (*dé*).

○ Hé said: "**玄** (*Xuán*) is Heaven. To know the standards of governing oneself and the realm is to align with Heaven’s **virtue** (*dé*).

**Míng Huáng** said: "**玄德** (*Xuándé*—mysterious virtue) is profound and distant.

○ Hé said: "The person of **玄德** (*Xuándé*) is profound beyond measure, distant beyond reach.

○ Bì said: "**稽** (*kǎi*) means ‘align.’ The principles that are consistent between ancient and modern must not be abandoned. To know the standard is to possess **玄德** (*Xuándé*), which is profound and distant.

**Míng Huáng** said: "The person of **玄德** (*Xuándé*) is contrary to all things. All things seek to benefit themselves, while the person of **玄德** benefits others.

○ Bì said: "It is contrary to their true nature.

○ Páng said: "**物** (*wù*) here means ‘affairs.’ Those who entrust themselves to **virtue** (*dé*) act contrary to affairs, yet affairs are ultimately governed through them—this is like the analogy of root and branch. The common people see only the shallow and near, following the branches while losing sight of the roots, hence they see only affairs and not **virtue**.

Then, only does one attain **大顺** (*Dàshùn*—great harmony).

**Míng Huáng** said: "**玄德** (*Xuándé*) is profound and distant, contrary to all things, returning to its root, thus all things attain **Dàshùn** (*great harmony*) in their natural spontaneity.

○ Hé said: "**玄德** (*Xuándé*) is contrary to all things, hence it can attain **Dàshùn** (*great harmony*), which follows Heaven’s principles.

○ Páng said: "At the time when **virtue** (*dé*) is entrusted, it seems contrary to affairs, yet affairs are ultimately governed through it. This is contrary to immediate appearances yet in harmony with the greater flow, hence the saying.

(Alternative reading: ‘Then only does one return to **Dàshùn** (*great harmony*).’)

**Jiāng Hǎi as the King of All Streams** (Chapter 66)

**Jiāng Hǎi** (*the Yangtze and Seas*) is able to be the King of All Streams because it is good at being lowly.

Hé said: "The **Jiāng Hǎi** (*Yangtze and Seas*) are humble, hence all the streams flow to them, just as the people flow to their king. Thus, they are able to be the King of All Streams.

**Míng Huáng** said: "**Jiāng Hǎi** (*the Yangtze and Seas*) are able to make all the rivers and streams converge and return to their source because they excel at being humble and lowly. Hence, all the rivers and streams pay homage to their **宗** (*Zōng*—ancestral root/sacred origin).

○ Hé said: "Because they are humble and lowly, they are able to be the King of All Streams.

○ Páng said: "The meaning of ‘king’ is to receive the flowing. It can accept all things without resistance, hence they flow to it. Therefore, the **至人** (*Zhìrén*—perfected/true person) desires to be above others,」

The **Jiāng Hǎi** (*Yangtze and Seas*) said: "To be above the people. By his words being humble; the **Jiāng Hǎi** said: "To act like the **Jiāng Hǎi** in being humble and lowly. To be before the people. By his body being behind them; the **Jiāng Hǎi** said: "To be before others while placing oneself behind. Thus, the **perfected/true person** (聖人) stands above others while the people do not feel burdened; the **Jiāng Hǎi** said: "The **perfected/true person** is above the people as their sovereign, not using his noble status to oppress them like **Long** (龍) below, hence the people revere him without feeling burdened. He stands before them while the people do not feel harmed.

**Míng Huáng** said: "Humility is the **virtue/power** (德) of governance, while nobility brings added radiance. By humbling his words, the people joyfully revere him, hence he stands above them while none consider him burdensome. By retreating and placing himself behind, the people flock to him like children to a mother, hence he stands before them while none consider him harmful. The **Jiāng Hǎi** said: "The **perfected/true person** stands before the people, not using his radiance to obscure those behind him, so the people love him as a long-lost mother, with no desire to harm him."

○ The **Jiāng Hǎi** said: "The **perfected/true person**’s virtue is vast and benevolent, hence the people of the world gladly acclaim him as sovereign without growing weary. **Míng Huáng** said: "Because of his being neither burdensome nor harmful, the people of the world gladly acclaim and honor him as their sovereign, without ever tiring."

○ The **Jiāng Hǎi** said: "The **perfected/true person**’s kindness is profound and love is heavy; he views the people as his own children, hence the people of the world gladly acclaim and honor him as their sovereign without growing weary. The **Jiāng Hǎi** said: "The **perfected/true person** does not seek personal gain for this; it is merely **virtue/power** (德). When **virtue/power** is humble, its form is exalted; when **virtue/power** is behind, its form is before. Thus, he is always held in high esteem by the world.

Because he does not contend, the **Jiāng Hǎi** said: "The world does not weary of the **perfected/true person**’s time, for he does not contend with others for precedence. Hence, none in the world can contend with him."

**Míng Huáng** said: "The **perfected/true person**’s humility and retreat are such that he does not contend with anything. The world collectively acclaims him—who would dare to contend?"

○ The **Jiāng Hǎi** said: "Others all contend for their own interests, while I do not contend for **non-action** (無為). The world all say: The **Jiāng Hǎi**’s **Dao/Way** (道) is vast, like **not resembling** (不肖).

**Míng Huáng** said: "Resembling, that is to say, resembling. **Lǎo Jūn** (老君) said: 'The people of the world all say my **Dao/Way** (道) is vast, and there is nothing to which it resembles. I then reply:'

○ The **Jiāng Hǎi** said: "**Lǎo Jūn** speaks of the world saying my **virtue/power** (德) is vast, yet I feign ignorance, appearing not resembling. Only because it is truly vast does it seem not resembling. The **Jiāng Hǎi** said: "Only those whose **virtue/power** (德) is truly great are harmed by their own name, hence they feign ignorance, appearing not resembling, making no distinctions or divisions, not demeaning others while elevating themselves. If they were resembling for a long time, the **Jiāng Hǎi** said: "Resembling, that is goodness. It refers to **discernment and kindness** (辨惠). If one is a great practitioner of discernment and kindness, his noble stature and virtuous conduct have long been established. But this is trivial.

**Míng Huáng** said: "Only my **Dao/Way** (道) is supremely vast, hence there is nothing to which it resembles. If, like the **Dài** (代) asking about all **laws** (法), there were something to which it resembled, it could not be called vast, and its greatness would have long been diminished and trivialized."

○ The **Jiāng Hǎi** said: "Those who practice **discernment and kindness** (辨惠) are merely like petty men, not true **long-standing/wise ones** (長者)." ○ **Bì** (弼) said: "For a long time, it has been trivial. Still, it says, 'For a long time, it has been trivial.' If resembling, then it loses its claim to being great, hence the saying, 'If resembling for a long time, it has been trivial.'"

○ The **Jiāng Hǎi** said: "Resembling means taking on a likeness, while the **Dao/Way** (道) is the ancestral root of all things. Thus, those who embody the **Dao/Way** should not resemble things—rather, all things should resemble them. If there is resemblance, then it is like imitating others, and thus others must be great while I am small. I have three treasures; the **Jiāng Hǎi** said: "All of these three treasures are what the common emotions call small, yet they are what the **perfected/true person** uses to be truly great."

Treasure and hold them: **Míng Huáng** said: "Though my **Dao/Way** (道) is vast and resembles nothing, I possess these three practices, which are truly precious. If one can always preserve and rely on them, they can govern oneself and the nation."

○ The **Jiāng Hǎi** said: "**Lǎo Zǐ** (老子) says I have three treasures, which I embrace and preserve. The first is **compassion** (慈). The **Jiāng Hǎi** said: "Love the people as one’s own children."

○ The **Jiāng Hǎi** said: "**Compassion** (慈) is rooted in love, and love is the **virtue/power** (德) of kindness toward all. Yet it is called **compassion** alone, for kindness extends broad **virtue/power** to cover those below, and its fullest manifestation is at the end. **Lǎo Zǐ** speaks of its origin, hence he does not say **kindness** (仁) but **compassion** (慈). **Compassion** is the **Dao/Way** of the father, the root of kindness, yet it does not depend on outward acts.

The second is **frugality** (俭). The **Jiāng Hǎi** said: "Taxation and levies should be like taking from oneself."

○ The **Jiāng Hǎi** said: "The **virtue/power** of **frugality** (俭) is to have few desires, to honor the essentials, and to love all things. In one word, it encompasses three virtues. The **perfected/true person**’s restraint is such that if **Confucius** said, 'Let **rites** (禮) be frugal rather than extravagant,' it is because the **perfected/true person** establishes **rites** to promote **frugality**, yet when he does so, he cannot be solely restrained. Without **rites**, it is trivial; without **Lǎo Zǐ**, one does not know the essentials. Essential! Essential! The **perfected/true person**’s **Dao/Way** is thus supremely refined."

The third is: "I dare not be first for the world." **Míng Huáng** said: "**Compassion** (慈) leads to broad salvation, **frugality** (俭) ensures sufficiency, and not daring to be first for the world means the people gladly acclaim him without growing weary."

○ The **Jiāng Hǎi** said: "Embracing humility and retreat, he does not initiate contention."

○ The **Jiāng Hǎi** said: "In the *I Ching*, it is **humility** (謙) that is spoken of. Heaven, earth, humans, and spirits all hold **humility** as noble, hence the **perfected/true person** treasures it. **Compassion** (慈) thus leads to **courage** (勇); **Míng Huáng** said: "**Compassion** (慈) is kind and gentle, hence his **courage** (勇) lies in salvation and aid."

○ The **Jiāng Hǎi** said: "For **kindness** (仁), hence his **courage** (勇) lies in loyalty and filial piety."

○ **Bì** (弼) said: "**Compassion** (慈) is such that when used in action, it prevails; when held in defense, it is steadfast, hence it leads to **courage** (勇)."

○ The **Jiāng Hǎi** said: "**Compassion** (慈) is victory without contention, and there is no greater **courage** (勇) than this."

**Frugality** (俭) thus leads to **broadness** (廣); **Míng Huáng** said: "**Frugality** (俭) is to cherish expenditure with restraint, ensuring abundance in wealth and resources, hence his deeds and benefits are ever more broad."

○ The **Jiāng Hǎi** said: "The sovereign’s body can practice **frugality**, hence the people’s daily use is broad."

○ **Bì** (弼) said: "**Frugality** (俭) cherishes expenditure with restraint, and the world is not impoverished, hence it leads to **broadness** (廣)."
