○ **Pān** said: "Those who seek **broadness** (廣) through **excessive indulgence** (奢侈) are in truth the most **narrow-minded** (狭). Only those who do not let trivial matters harm the fundamental, nor external things extinguish the internal, attain true **broadness** (廣)."

One does not dare to act first for all under Heaven,
**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "Not to act first for all under Heaven."
Hence one can become the **vessel’s leader** (成器長).

**Míng Huáng** said: "The **virtue/power** (德) of **compassion** (慈) and **frugality** (俭) shines through **humility** (謙), and by giving precedence to others, people are never sated, hence one can become the **vessel’s leader** (神器之長)."

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "To become the **vessel’s leader** (成器長) means to be the **Dao/Way-person** (得道人), and I can be the leader of such **Dao/Way-persons**."

**Bì** said: "Only by placing oneself last externally, becoming what all things return to, can one then establish the **vessel’s leadership** (成器), benefiting all under Heaven, and becoming the leader of all things."

**Pān** said: "This is the conduct of the **Sage** (聖人), hence it is merely to be the **vessel’s leader** (器長). If it were to be the leader of the **Dao/Way**, it would never lag behind things."

Now they abandon **compassion** (慈) and **courage** (勇),
**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "Now people abandon **compassion** (慈仁) and only pursue **martial valor** (勇武)."
**Bì** said: "Yet they still take [what they want]."
They abandon **frugality** (俭) and **broadness** (廣),
**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "They abandon **frugality** (俭約) and only pursue **luxury** (奢華)."
They abandon **placing oneself last** (後) and **acting first** (先),
**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "They abandon placing themselves last and only act first for others."
This is death.
**Míng Huáng** said: "Now they abandon **compassion** (慈) and **courage** (勇); **courage** harms things. They combine **audacity** (检) and **broadness** (廣); **broadness** depletes wealth. They hold **placing oneself last** and **acting first**; acting first invites resentment. Depleting wealth and harming things, gathering resentment from people—this is the path to certain death, hence it is said, 'This is death.'"

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "Such conduct, in every action, leads to death."

**Pān** said: "To destroy humanity for Heaven, to let affairs overcome the **Dao/Way**—even if by chance they escape, they have lost what sustains life. The Three Treasures are all Heaven’s **virtue/power** (德) and fundamentals.

**Compassion** (慈), when deployed, is upright.
**Bì** said: "Confronting difficulties without retreat, hence upright."
**Pān** said: "Like the righteousness of proper arms—proper arms mean the army is disciplined yet unmoving. Loving the people as one’s children, the people love them as their father; they are orderly without command. Those who turn their weapons against their own rear do so because their superiors lack **compassion** (慈)." [One version reads: "When used in battle, it brings victory."]

When used in defense, it is steadfast.
**Míng Huáng** said: "Using **compassion** (慈) in battle benefits in preserving the multitude; using **compassion** in defense benefits in securing the people. Each preserves safety, hence one can achieve victory and steadfastness."

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "Those who practice **compassion** (慈) have the people naturally attached, their hearts and intentions unified, hence they can achieve victory in battle and firmness in defense."

**Pān** said: "In war and defense, the focus is on slaughter, yet **compassion** (慈) is still upheld as **virtue/power** (德)—the rest follows accordingly. Heaven will aid and protect them with **compassion**."

**Míng Huáng** said: "Using **compassion** (慈) in defense is not merely about human harmony; the **Dao/Way** of Heaven is profoundly clear and will also aid and protect. Victory in battle is Heaven’s aid. Steadfastness in defense is Heaven’s protection. All this is because of **compassion** (慈), hence it is said, 'Heaven will aid and protect them with **compassion**.'"

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "Heaven aids those who are benevolent, certainly aligning with the nature of the **compassionate** (慈人), enabling them to self-nurture and assist."

**Pān** said: "The Three Treasures all take **compassion** (慈) as their core. Speaking of Heaven’s aid and protection is because of their **compassion**."

**Chapter Sixty-Eight: The Virtuous Scholar**
The virtuous scholar does not rely on **martial valor** (武).
**Míng Huáng** said: "Scholar (士) means 'affairs' (事). The virtuous one who governs with the **Dao/Way** values **virtue/power** (德), hence it is said they do not rely on **martial valor**."

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "It speaks of valuing **moral cultivation** (道德) over **martial force** (武力)."

**Bì** said: "Scholar (士) is the leader of soldiers (卒). **Martial valor** (武) is to value trampling others first."

**Pān** said: "Scholar (士) is not a title for achieving **virtue/power** (德), hence *scholar* (士) means 'to discern' (察) and 'martial' (武). The **judge** (狱师) is called *scholar* for discernment. The leader of soldiers is called *scholar* for martial prowess. Those who take **martial valor** (武) as their affair must root their **virtue/power** (德) in not relying on **martial valor**, for this is their foundation. Hence, those who rely on **martial valor** may still be called *scholars*, but they are not **virtuous scholars** (德士). The virtue of being a scholar lies in **virtue/power**."

The virtuous warrior does not grow angry.
**Míng Huáng** said: "When affairs cannot be avoided and one must confront the enemy, **compassion** (慈) makes it virtuous, hence one does not rely on anger."

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "The virtuous warrior who fights with the **Dao/Way** restrains evil in their heart, cutting off disaster before it sprouts, and does not indulge in slaughterous rage."

**Bì** said: "They act last yet do not lag, respond yet do not provoke, hence they are not driven by anger."

**Pān** said: "When **King Wǔ** of **Zhōu** grew angry and pacified all under Heaven, it was due to his affairs; in terms of **virtue/power** (德), he never grew angry."

The virtuous victor does not contend.
**Míng Huáng** said: "Military triumph lies in harmony; harmony brings virtuous victory, the ultimate in complete victory, hence there is no contention."

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "The virtuous warrior who conquers with the **Dao/Way** attracts the near with **ren** (仁) and the distant with **virtue/power** (德); they do not contend with the enemy, yet the enemy submits on its own."

**Bì** said: "They do not contend."

**Pān** said: "The **virtue/power** (德) of not contending sometimes involves contention, but contention is a matter of affairs; in **virtue/power**, there is never contention."

The virtuous user of people places themselves beneath them.
**Míng Huáng** said: "[Left blank in original]."

Persuading and ordering people to exhaust their efforts requires first placing oneself beneath them—this is the virtue of virtuous use.

This is called the **virtue/power** (德) of not contending,
**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "It means the superior places themselves beneath the inferior."
This is the **moral cultivation** (道德) of not contending with people.

**Pān** said: "This is **virtue/power** (德), not affairs. This is the **Dao/Way** of **virtue/power** (德), hence it is always spoken of as **virtue/power**—the rest is similar."

This is called using their strength,
**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "To personally place oneself beneath others is to use the strength of ministers."

**Bì** said: "If one uses people yet does not place themselves beneath them, their strength is not utilized."

**Pān** said: "All under Heaven aids them, hence they triumph over the strong without exerting force."

This is called aligning with Heaven.
**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "Those who practice this have **virtue/power** (德) that aligns with Heaven."

**Pān** said: "Not conforming to affairs yet attaining **virtue/power** (德), hence one can fulfill their nature. Fulfilling nature completes humanity, hence one can align with Heaven."

「This is the ultimate ancient principle.
**Emperor Minghuang** said: "To excel without contention is the **virtue/power** (德) of non-striving. To place oneself beneath others is the strength of using people. Those who can do this may align with Heaven and be called an emperor—this is the most essential **Dao/Way** of the ancients."

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "This is indeed the most essential **Dao/Way** of the ancients."
**Pān** said: "The phrase begins with *yǒu* (有, 'this is'), for the ultimate ancient principle is the **Dao/Way** of great centrality."

---
**On the Use of Arms (用兵有吉闷章第六十九)**

**The Use of Arms (用兵) has a saying:**
**Emperor Minghuang** said: "Laozi laments the age's kings and people harming one another through warfare, hence he expounds upon the ancient ways to issue a warning. The saying refers to the following lines."

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "He sets forth the **Dao/Way** of using arms, for Laozi despises the age's use of warfare, hence he employs this text to establish its meaning."

*"I dare not be the host,*
**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "Host (主) means to act first; I dare not first raise arms."

*but I will be the guest.*
**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "Guest (客) means to respond without initiating, to use arms only after Heaven's will is followed."

**Pān** said: "To stir up attack is to be the host of arms. I bear the burden as a guest."

*"I dare not advance an inch, but retreat a foot.*
**Emperor Minghuang** said: "If the host takes action, it generates strife and greed. Without scheming or doing, one guards with benevolence. Guarding preserves total victory, while strife leads to defeat. Though advancing is slight, one cannot avoid conflict; though retreating is much, it does not lose humility, hence I dare not advance an inch but retreat a foot."

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "To encroach upon another’s territory or seize their wealth is advancing; to close the gates and defend the city is retreating."

**Pān** said: "To not be bold in slaughter."

*"This is called acting without acting,*
**Emperor Minghuang** said: "To retreat as a guest and not contend with others—though one acts to repel the enemy, it is akin to acting without acting."

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "If they persist unchecked, they become bandits to all under Heaven; though one acts to punish them, it is not done with force."

**Bì** said: "If they persist unchecked..."
**Pān** said: "The benevolent use of arms has conquest without battle, akin to acting without acting."

*"To take without arms,*
**Emperor Minghuang** said: "To bare an arm signifies anger; the skilled warrior does not anger. Hence, it is as if there are no arms to bare."

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "Though one desires great anger, it is as if there are no arms to bare."

*"To seize without weapons,*
**Emperor Minghuang** said: "To 'seize' (仍) means to draw out. Drawing out the enemy is to desire contention without contending, hence it is as if there are no weapons to seize."

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "Though one desires to draw out the enemy, it is as if there are no weapons to seize."

**Bì** said: "To act (行) means to deploy troops. The saying is: with humility, benevolence, and pity, one does not dare act first; using war is akin to acting without acting, to bare without arms, to seize without weapons, to draw without enemies. This means there is none to oppose them."

*"To seize without weapons,"*
**Emperor Minghuang** said: "To seize weapons signifies killing; now benevolence is the principle, hence though one seizes weapons, it is akin to seizing none."

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "Though one desires to seize and use them, it is as if there are no weapons or blades to hold."

*"Why so?*
Because harming their people brings retribution from Heaven, encountering unrighteous rulers evokes pity for their suffering."

**Pān** said: "Though there is the name of battle, the enemy is nonexistent before it, hence it is akin to no battle."

*"No disaster is greater than lightly regarding the enemy,*
**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "The harm of disorder and chaos is nothing greater than deceiving and lightly regarding the enemy, encroaching without rest, and greedily waging war for wealth. Lightly regarding the enemy nearly causes the loss of my precious self."

**Emperor Minghuang** said: "The greatest disaster is lightly regarding the enemy, for those who lightly regard the enemy nearly lose their precious benevolence."

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "*Jī* (几) means near. *Bǎo* (宝) means the body. To deceive and lightly regard the enemy is to nearly lose one’s life."

**Bì** said: "The saying is: my benevolence and humility are not for seizing strength; I have no enemy under Heaven. If I am forced to the point of having no enemy, this is the reason why I bring about great disaster. *Bǎo* (宝) refers to the Three Treasures (三宝), hence it is said that I nearly lose my Treasures."

**Pān** said: "Arms are cruel instruments; yet the benevolent warrior, though unstoppable, does not dare lightly regard the enemy. To lightly regard the enemy is to lose one’s benevolence, and to lose benevolence is to risk defeat."

*"Hence, when armies clash,*
**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "Two enemies battle."

*the compassionate shall prevail.*
**Emperor Minghuang** said: "To clash (抗) means to raise arms. When two nations raise arms against each other, the one who guards with benevolence and compassion shall prevail."

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "The compassionate are benevolent and kind; their soldiers do not shun death."

**Bì** said: "To clash (抗) means to raise arms. *Ruò* (若) means to meet. The compassionate must necessarily hold each other in regard, and not pursue gain or avoid harm, hence they must prevail."

**Pān** said: "Compassion and pity are benevolence; benevolence gives courage, hence they prevail."

One text reads *xiāng ruò* (相若), which is also acceptable. *Ruò* (若) means that the armies’ forces are equal.

**Emperor Minghuang** said: "I have often pondered this: the *Shū* (书) says, 'Let your威克厥爱允济,' and again, 'Exhort, O prince, and be resolute!' Yet Laozi’s words on arms are always thus—this is the **Dao/Way** of using arms. The other is merely the practice of arms; the Sage does not differ in intent, but their words differ due to the times."

---
**My Sayings Are Very Easy to Know (吾－禹甚易知章第七十)**

*"My sayings are very easy to know,*
**Emperor Minghuang** said: "Laozi says, 'My words accord with the Way, hence easy to know; my affairs are simple, hence easy to practice.'"

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "Laozi’s words are concise and easy to know, brief and easy to practice."

*"Yet none under Heaven can know or practice them.*
**Emperor Minghuang** said: "People under Heaven are entangled in words and do not awaken, burdened by affairs and not simplified, hence none can know or practice them."

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "People detest softness and weakness, yet favor strength and rigidity."

*"The Master said:*
**Bi** said: "One may know it without stepping outside—glimpsing through a window suffices, hence 'very easy to know.' Without action, it is accomplished, hence 'very easy to practice.' Confused by restless desires, hence none can know it. Bewildered by honor and profit, hence none can practice it."

**Páng** said: "The Sage follows the utmost of nature and life as his teaching. He does not weary with simplicity, nor does he contrive the unusual—merely returning to oneself. Thus, one knows that aligning with nature is natural. If one seeks to know and practice it, how could it not be very easy?

All difficult matters under Heaven arise from abandoning the root for the branches, from reckless contrivance. Thus, inner and outer chaos ensues, strange things proliferate, and wisdom cannot prevail. Only when one departs from this does a single word suffice. Though it is very easy to know and practice, none can do so because their minds are fixed on trifles and they pursue the strange and difficult.

There is a root for speech, a master for affairs.
**Emperor Minghuang** said: "Speech rests in principle; when principle is attained, speech is forgotten, hence speech takes 'no-speech' as its root. Affairs rest in function; when function is fulfilled, there is no governance, hence affairs take 'no-affairs' as their master."

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "My words have a fundamental origin; affairs have their hierarchy. Those who do not understand are not without virtue—it is their hearts that oppose mine."

**Bi** said: "Root is the root of all things; master is the sovereign of all affairs."

**Páng** said: "Speech returns to principle; affairs rest in the Dao. Knowing principle and Dao, even if speech and affairs are many, there is nothing difficult to know. It is only the lack of knowing that makes them unknown to others."

**Emperor Minghuang** said: "Only the vulgar lack enlightened knowing, hence they do not know my teaching of no-speech and no-affairs."

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "The world is my virtue’s barrier—hidden from view, probing the subtle and reaching the marvelous, hence none know it."

**Bi** said: "Because my words have a root and my affairs a master, those who know cannot fail to know."

**Páng** said: "What is called knowing is not ultimate principle—such knowing is not true knowing. To presume knowing while not knowing the Sage, can this be called knowing? Few know me, hence I am noble."

**Emperor Minghuang** said: "Few comprehend the intent of forgetting knowing, hence my unsaid teaching is most noble."

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "Few—meaning rare. Only those who grasp the Dao can know me, hence my nobility."

**Bi** said: "Only the profound understand, hence few know me. The fewer who know me, the more I stand alone—thus, 'few know me, hence I am noble.'"

**Páng** said: "The Dao is vast, so few know it. Across ten thousand generations, one may know it—like encountering it at dawn or dusk. Some texts read: 'then I am noble.' Thus, the Sage wears coarse robes while treasuring jade within."

**Emperor Minghuang** said: "Wearing coarse robes—obscuring the exterior. Treasuring jade—illuminating the interior. Hence few know me."

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "Wearing coarse robes—thin without. Treasuring jade—rich within. Concealing treasure, hoarding virtue, not revealing it to others."

**Bi** said: "Wearing coarse robes blends with dust; treasuring jade cherishes the true. The Sage’s difficulty in being known lies in blending with dust yet not differing, treasuring jade yet not waning—hence difficult to know and thus noble."

**Páng** said: "Is it to distinguish oneself by display? Chapter Seventy: Knowing the Unknown

Knowing the unknown—how profound!
**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "Claiming to know while unknown is the highest virtue."

**Páng** said: "Not recognizing the unknown is the ultimate knowing. To know while unknown is an ailment."

**Emperor Minghuang** said: "Realizing the emptiness of dharma-nature, originally not knowing dharma, in knowing, forgetting knowing—this is the highest virtue. To know while unknown, dharma-nature being empty, yet in knowing, forcing knowledge—this is the disease of practice."

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "Claiming to know while unknown is the disease of virtue."

**Bi** said: "The inability to know while unknown is the ailment."

**Páng** said: "The Dao cannot be known, nor is it worth knowing, yet they presume to know clearly—this is not having grasped the Dao at all. Not having grasped the Dao yet presuming to know is mere delusion, and the ailment is grave.

Only by recognizing the ailment does one avoid it.
**Emperor Minghuang** said: "Only by recognizing the ailment of forced knowing does one not fall prey to it."

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "Only by recognizing the ailment—if the masses have the ailment of forced knowing—does one not fall prey to it. The Sage’s freedom from ailment lies in recognizing the ailment in others. Hence, not falling prey to it."

**Emperor Minghuang** said: "Only the Sage does not fall prey to this ailment because they recognize the masses’ forced knowing. Hence, not falling prey to it."

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "This is not human—hence not falling prey to it. The Sage harbors comprehensive knowing, cloaked in unknownness, desiring the world to be sincere and loyal, each guarding their pure nature. The petty do not understand this intent yet recklessly pursue forced knowing, seeking display, internally harming their spirit, diminishing life and years."

**Bi** said: "Recognizing the ailment means knowing its cause."

**Páng** said: "If one is ailing yet unaware, the ailing will never awaken.

Chapter Seventy-Two: The People Do Not Fear威 for Long
The people do not fear威, then great威 arrives.
**Emperor Minghuang** said: "威 is what can be feared when present. If people are careless in minor matters, then great and fearful威 emerges."

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "威 is harm."

「People do not fear minor harm, then major harm arrives—this is death.

Those who fear it must cherish their *qi* (spirit), conforming to Heaven and aligning with Earth.

**Páng** said: "When the people are pure and thick in virtue, they fear *威*. When the ruler loses the Dao/Way, indulges in excessive action, and diminishes the Dao/Way, obstructing their natural flow, the people become cunning and deceitful, and *威* cannot subdue them. Thus, when Heaven’s retribution is invoked, calamity and disorder arise, so it is said that great *威* arrives. Do not constrict their dwelling—

**Emperor Minghuang** said: "The place where the spirit resides is the heart."

To not constrict means to remove emotions and desires, leaving the heart empty and pure, generating *white* (purity).

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "It means the spirit resides in the heart, which should be expansive and gentle, not narrow and constricted."

Do not exhaust their natural inclinations—

**Emperor Minghuang** said: "What the body produces is the spirit.

To not exhaust means to think little and desire little, so as not to overburden oneself.

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "The reason people are born is to possess *qi* (spirit), relying on emptiness, delighting in tranquility and simplicity. If they do not regulate their diet, neglect the Dao/Way while fixating on color, fill their hearts with perversity, they are indeed exhausting their foundation and offending the spirit.

**Bi** said: "Tranquility and non-action are called *residence* (居), humility and modesty are called *natural inclinations* (生). Though one practices tranquility, if they indulge in hasty desires, abandon humility to pursue arrogance, and submit to *威*, then chaos disturbs and the people grow perverse. *威* can no longer control them. The virtuous cannot endure such *威*, so the ruler and people collapse in great disorder, and Heaven’s retribution is near. Hence it is said: ‘If the people do not fear *威*, then great *威* arrives.’

Do not constrict their dwelling, do not exhaust their natural inclinations—this means *威* cannot be relied upon.

**Páng** said: "The people’s nature is originally broad and flowing, but as the teachings of the world decline, they cannot return to their original purity. Instead, their dwelling is narrowed and their natural inclinations are obstructed. It is because their dwelling is constricted that the people are not sincere but deceitful; it is because their natural inclinations are exhausted that the people are not virtuous but thin and narrow.

The Sage does not do this. Instead, they let the people roam freely in the vast dwelling of the world, each fulfilling their boundless natural inclinations, so that none would dare offend *威*.

*The Zhuangzi* says: ‘The sparse borders of the State of Wéi, the hunting grounds of the Yellow Emperor, the territories of Yao and Shun, the halls of Tang and Wu—these are the signs of the world’s gradual decline, narrowing their dwellings.’

The Sage is not unequal, yet their dwelling is narrowed by the times they face. Only when one does not exhaust—

**Bi** said: "It means not exhausting oneself."

Thus it is said: "Only when people do not exhaust their spirit does the spirit not exhaust them."

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "Only when one does not exhaust their spirit does the spirit reside without exhaustion. They cleanse the heart of impurities, maintaining calm and desirelessness, so the spirit dwells without exhaustion.

**Bi** said: "Not exhausting oneself means the world does not exhaust you. The people do not exhaust you—

**Páng** said: "The ruler does not exhaust their own natural inclinations while fully expressing their nature, so the people also gain to express their nature."

*The Zhuangzi* says: ‘This is what is meant by not exhausting one’s Heaven.’ Exhaustion is Heaven’s exhaustion of them. Hence the Sage knows themselves—

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "They know their own gains and losses."

They do not display themselves—

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "They do not flaunt their virtue and beauty outwardly, but keep it inwardly."

They cherish themselves—

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "They cherish their body to preserve their *qi* (spirit).

They do not exalt themselves—

**Emperor Minghuang** said: "They know their own body, guarding against fearful matters. They cherish their body, avoiding Heaven’s retribution for exhausting the spirit. They do not display their abilities to provoke Heaven’s wrath, nor do they exalt themselves to gather resentment."

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "They do not seek high honor or fame in the world."

**Bi** said: "If one exalts themselves, others will grow narrow and resentful, exhausting their dwelling and natural inclinations.

**Páng** said: "If one displays themselves, they become proud and self-centered; if one exalts themselves, they belittle others. This is how one constricts their own dwelling and exhausts their own natural inclinations, just as they constrict the people’s dwelling and exhaust the people’s natural inclinations.

Self-knowledge clarifies one’s nature, preventing reckless action. Self-cherishing preserves one’s body, preventing wrongdoing. Thus, how could they ever offend Heaven’s *威*?

Hence, abandon that and embrace this.

**Emperor Minghuang** said: "Abandon that which leads to exaltation, embrace this which leads to self-knowledge and cherishing."

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "Abandon that which leads to self-display and self-exaltation, embrace this which leads to self-knowledge and self-cherishing."

---
**Chapter Seventy-Three: On Courage (勇於敢)**
Courage in daring leads to death—

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "Courage and daring with excessive action lead to self-destruction."

**Bi** said: "They will certainly not achieve their natural end."

Courage in not daring leads to life—

**Emperor Minghuang** said: "Daring means decisive action. If one is courageous in decisive action yet lacks humility and yielding, they will surely harm themselves—hence it is said to lead to death. Not daring means they can preserve their life.

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "Courage in not daring with excessive action preserves their life."

**Bi** said: "They will certainly achieve longevity."

**Páng** said: "The daring are those who act recklessly, following their *qi* to create matters, lightly regarding death for the sake of their actions, treating actions as the Dao/Way. The courageous yet not daring move only when pressed, not preemptively competing with others for glory, treating the Dao/Way as their actions.

These two types—

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "They refer to daring and not daring.

One is beneficial, the other harmful—

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "Preserving life is beneficial; self-destruction is harmful."

**Bi** said: "Both are courageous, yet their applications differ, so benefit and harm diverge—hence it is said one is beneficial, the other harmful.

**Páng** said: "What people deem beneficial, Heaven truly harms—hence it is said: ‘Heaven’s noble ones are the world’s petty ones.’ If people consider them petty, they are indeed the ones the world follows, yet Heaven truly precedes them. What Heaven abhors—

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: "It abhors excessive action.

Who knows its reason?

**Emperor Minghuang** said: "These two types—daring and not daring—are the ones. Some are excessive in action.»

Those who know when *not* to dare have benefit, while those who dare have harm—one must be courageous in *not* daring. This is the courageous person.

Action brings disaster and harm, which is what Heaven abhors. Who can truly know its reason?

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: *"Who can know Heaven’s intent and not transgress?"*

**Zhòng** said: *"The lowly and the small-witted see only what benefits the crowd and do not know what Heaven abhors. Thus, the following passage clarifies the *Dao* of Heaven—one must observe it as law."*

Therefore, even the Sage finds it difficult.

**Emperor Minghuang** said: *"The Sage still finds daring deeds difficult."*

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: *"This speaks of the Sage’s enlightened *virtue*—even daring is difficult for them, let alone those without the Sage’s *virtue* who would attempt it."*

**Páng** said: *"The Sage harmonizes with Heaven’s *virtue*, yet still does not lightly promise or act frivolously."*

The *Dao* of Heaven does not contend yet is good at victory.

**Emperor Minghuang** said: *"Here, it speaks of Heaven’s humility and modesty, warning against human daring."*

Heaven does not contend with things. The four seasons fill and empty, and nothing resists—thus, it is good at victory.

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: *"Heaven does not contend with people over nobility or lowliness, yet people naturally fear it."*

**Bi** said: *"Only because it does not contend, the world cannot contend with it."*

**Páng** said: *"Heaven is the parent of all things—how could it be an enemy to its children? This is how it is good at victory."*

It does not speak yet responds well.

**Emperor Minghuang** said: *"How does Heaven speak? It rewards the virtuous and punishes the licentious without distinction—thus, it is said to respond well."*

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: *"Heaven does not speak, yet all things move in response to the seasons."*

**Bi** said: *"Compliance brings fortune; defiance brings calamity—it responds without speaking."*

**Páng** said: *"How does Heaven speak? The four seasons proceed, all things flourish, rewarding virtue and punishing licentiousness—its retribution encompasses myriad transformations without exception."*

It does not summon yet they come of their own accord.

**Emperor Minghuang** said: *"The *Dao* of Heaven does not summon things to follow it; things cannot defy it and come naturally in accordance with Heaven."*

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: *"Heaven does not summon—things follow it naturally."*

All things bear *yin* and turn toward *yang*.

When summoned, all things bear *yin* and turn toward *yang*.

**Bi** said: *"If one is humble, things will naturally gather."*

**Páng** said: *"To be summoned by others is the way of the subject and child."*

Heaven is the sovereign and parent—who can control it? When its course arrives, things come; it is not summoned.

It is open yet skillfully plans.

**Emperor Minghuang** said: *"The *Dao* of Heaven is profound and vast, broad and expansive, manifesting symbols and transformations—people can take it as a model, hence it is said to plan skillfully."*

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: *"‘Open’ means broad. Though Heaven’s *Dao* is vast and expansive, it skillfully plans for human affairs, rewarding virtue and punishing vice, each receiving its due."*

**Bi** said: *"It manifests symbols to show fortune and calamity, preempts events with warnings, remains secure yet does not forget danger, and plans before omens appear—thus, it is said to be open yet skillfully plan."*

**Páng** said: *"It is always easy, hence open; it is always perilous, hence skillful in planning."*

One version reads: *"It is broad yet skillfully plans."*

The Heavenly Net is vast and wide, sparse yet never misses.

**Emperor Minghuang** said: *"Though Heaven’s net is vast and distant, it punishes licentiousness and rewards virtue without the slightest error."*

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: *"The net Heaven weaves is vast and great—though distant, it oversees and judges human virtue and vice without fail."*

**Páng** said: *"Heaven follows principle, not whim. Its rewards and punishments are entrusted to natural consequences—like a tree bearing flowers, it must bear fruit. How could it open flowers without bearing fruit, yet fail to conform to principle?"*

Now, people of this age doubt the correspondence of fortune and calamity, shackled by petty wisdom, selfish and willful, yet unaware of principle. Thus, they see only one side and fail to grasp the grand governance. Knowing this *Dao*, one understands that in the world, virtue and vice, calamity and fortune, follow the measure of one’s capacity—each has its own timing of response, yet none escape retribution.

Moreover, when the Sage governs, they value *wu-wu* [effortless action]. If one scrutinizes Heaven’s great *Dao* with a probing mind, its retribution could never be exhaustive.

The people do not commonly fear death.

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: *"When rulers impose cruel punishments, the people have no means to live, hence they do not fear death. For those governing themselves, excessive desires harm the spirit, and reckless spending kills the body—the people do not know to fear this. How can one frighten them with death?"*

**Emperor Minghuang** said: *"If one indulges in desires and acts recklessly, leading to death, and this becomes habitual, those who do not fear death—how can rulers further establish laws and execute killings to instill fear?"*

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: *"Rulers should lighten punishments and teach the people to abandon desires. How can they establish laws to frighten with death?"*

**Páng** said: *"When the ruler loses the *Dao*, customs become corrupt and the people are impoverished. With no means to sustain life, they defy laws, yet the ruler still frightens them with death—though many are killed, rebellion will not cease. This is the case since Qin."*

If the people were to commonly fear death,

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: *"One must eliminate one’s own cruelty and teach the people to abandon greed."*

Yet those who act strangely—if I seize and kill them, who would dare?

**Emperor Minghuang** said: *"If the people all follow the transformation of *wu-wei* [non-action], daring not to indulge in desires, constantly fearing death, and only the deceitful few act strangely—even if I, in my power, seize and kill these deceitful ones, who would dare to do so?"*

Thus, the following passage states:

**Jiāng Hǎi** said: *"If one governs with the *Dao*, yet the people do not comply and instead act deceitfully, they should be arrested and executed according to law—who would dare to transgress?"*

Laozi lamented that the ruling kings did not first transform with *Dao* and *virtue*, but instead relied on punishments.

**Bi** said: *"Deceitful and disorderly behavior is called ‘strange.’"*

**Páng** said: *"The *virtue* of cherishing life permeates the people’s hearts, and they hold death in reverence—only then can punishments be enforced and things brought into submission."*

There is always someone appointed to kill.

Emperor Ming said: *"For such deceitful ones, the Heavenly Net does not fail—it is the Heavenly Appointee who kills them."*

**He** said: *"The Heavenly Appointee stands high above, overseeing all, and examines human transgressions. The Heavenly Net is vast and open, yet it does not fail. But if one substitutes for the Heavenly Appointee, it is as though a clumsy artisan were to take the place of the Master Carpenter in felling timber."*

Emperor Ming said: *"The Way of Heaven is most clear, and the Appointee to kill has its fixed order—like the seasons of spring growth, summer lengthening, autumn harvest, and winter storage, or the movement of the Dipper and Handle (星辰) to regulate and govern. If a ruler attempts to substitute for the Heavenly Appointee in killing, it is like a clumsy artisan taking the place of the Master Carpenter in cutting wood, laboring without success."*

**Pang** said: *"The ruler is exalted, the minister is lowly; each has its fixed role. The ruler acts with non-action (無為) and entrusts to the Dao, while the minister acts with purpose (有為) and governs affairs. The Dao and affairs are vastly different. Thus, when it comes to judicial executions, there is the Heavenly Appointee; when it comes to woodworking, there is the Master Carpenter. The ruler does not interfere—only to complete what is above him. The world knows that substituting in judgment is wrong, yet does not realize the loss inherent in substituting in killing. The *Zhuangzi* says: 'The ruler has action, the minister has action—this is where the ruler and minister share the same *virtue*.' The Classic says: 'What did Shun do? Only to be respectful and correct his posture facing south.' The Dao encompasses all affairs, so the ruler can encompass the minister. Yet if the ruler meddles in all affairs, he loses what makes him a ruler. If the minister meddles in all affairs and resorts to killing and punishing, it is even more contrary to the Dao. Hence, it is deeply elaborated upon. The *Classic* truly states that one does not use military might to coerce the world, yet it still says, 'Use the Dao to assist the ruler'—for if the Dao is the ruler, who would bother discussing that which is secondary? One reading has 'as though' (夫) instead of 'the ruler' (君). If one substitutes for the Master Carpenter in cutting, it is rare that one does not harm one’s hands. Emperor Ming said: 'If a clumsy artisan substitutes in cutting, not only does he harm the wood, but he also harms his own hands. If a ruler takes charge of punishments, substituting for the Heavenly Appointee, not only does he cruelly harm the people, but he also forfeits the Heavenly Harmony.' **He** said: 'If a ruler enforces punishments, it is like a clumsy artisan substituting for the Master Carpenter in cutting wood—then the square and round cannot be properly achieved, and the one who substitutes for Heaven in killing will himself suffer harm. If the principles of governance are not upheld, the ruler will bear the consequences.' **Bi** said: 'This is what those who reverse order and justice despise and resent, and what the unmerciful harm among the people—hence, it is said that there is always the Heavenly Appointee to kill.' **Pang** said: 'Substituting in cutting only harms one’s hands, but substituting in killing is to lose the Dao entirely.'" One reading lacks the character 有 (*has*). The people’s hunger is the seventy-fifth chapter.

The people’s hunger is due to the excessive taxes imposed by their superiors.

**He** said: *"The people suffer hunger and cold because their lords and rulers levy excessive taxes upon them. Hence, they hunger."*

**Ming Huang** said: *"The reason the people of the world suffer from hunger and insufficiency is that their lords and rulers consume and tax them excessively."*

**He** said: *"The people all emulate their superiors in greed, abandoning the Dao and violating virtue—thus, they hunger."*

**Pang** said: *"Establishing official ranks and stipends, instituting rites and expenditures—these are meant to enrich the people. Yet with increased costs and taxes, the roots wither while the branches flourish, and this only serves to make them hunger."*

One reading lacks the character 有 (*has*).

The people’s difficulty in governance arises from their superiors’ excessive actions.

**He** said: *"The people are ungovernable because their lords and rulers are overambitious and fond of action. Hence, they are difficult to govern."*

**Ming Huang** said: *"The reason the people of the world are so difficult to guide and transform is that their lords and rulers engage in excessive actions. Excessive actions bring many difficulties, and difficulties breed deceit—thus, they are difficult to govern."*

**He** said: *"Therefore, the people emulate their superiors’ actions, and their falsehoods make them hard to govern."*

**Pang** said: *"If one relies on scrutiny to create strife, it is meant to govern the people. Yet the people become impoverished and their customs refined, while cunning and deceit only grow—making them even harder to govern. This is evident when comparing the ancient past with later times."*

The people’s disregard for death stems from their excessive pursuit of life.

**He** said: *"The people lightly risk death because their means of seeking life are too extravagant, and their greed for gain endangers themselves. Hence, they lightly court death."*

**Ming Huang** said: *"The reason the people of the world so lightly regard death is that they violate natural measures in their pursuit of life—hence, they lightly court death."*

**He** said: *"Because their pursuit of life is too extravagant, they lightly enter perilous situations."*

**Pang** said: *"If one does not truly possess life, then life remains whole. But if they exceed natural measures, their desires know no end, leading them to offend laws and court danger—thus, they regard death lightly. These three cases all arise from seeking what harms their very means of seeking. This clearly illustrates the principle of *wuwei* (non-action). Only those who do not excessively pursue life are truly wiser than those who prize life."*

**Ming Huang** said: *"Natural measure suffices for life’s preservation, but if nourishment exceeds this measure, life perishes. Thus, those who do not excessively pursue life are wiser than those who overly cherish it."*

**He** said: *"Only those who do not make life their concern—whose titles and wealth hold no sway over them, whose riches and profits do not enter their bodies—are wiser than those who prize life. Even the Son of Heaven cannot command them, nor lords employ them."*

**Bi** said: *"This speaks of how the people’s deviations and the chaos in governance all stem from the superiors, not the inferiors. The people follow their superiors. This passage may not be the work of Laozi."*

**Pang** said: *"Excessive pursuit of life inevitably leads to regarding death lightly. Only those who do not make life their concern can remain whole. Those who prize life are merely wiser than those who regard death lightly, but they do not reach the ultimate wisdom of *wuwei*. This chapter clarifies one of these three matters; the other two may be inferred."*

**Chapter Seventy-Six: On the Nature of Life**

Human life is soft and weak.

**He** said: *"At birth, humans are filled with harmonious *qi* and embrace spirit—hence, they are soft and weak."*

At death, they become rigid and strong.

**He** said: *"At death, harmonious *qi* is exhausted and spirit departs—hence, they become rigid and strong."*

Grass and trees, in life, are soft and brittle.

**He** said: *"This is because harmonious *qi* remains."*

At death, they wither and decay.

**He** said: *"This is because harmonious *qi* departs."*

Thus, rigidity and strength are the companions of death, while softness and weakness are the companions of life.

**Ming Huang** said: *"The softness and weakness of life are the completeness of harmonious *qi*. The rigidity and strength of death are the dispersal of harmonious *qi*. Those who uphold softness and weakness preserve life and extend years. Those who rely on rigidity and strength lose their bodies and forfeit their nature."*

**He** said: *"From the two cases above, one knows that rigidity and strength lead to death, while softness and weakness lead to life. Hence, strong armies do not prevail."*

**Ming Huang** said: *"Those who show compassion win—thus, those who rely on strength must surely fail."*

**He** said: *"Strong armies, eager for battle and delighting in slaughter, spread poison and breed resentment, while the weak unite against them—thus, the strong do not prevail."*

**Bi** said: *"Armies that rely on violence across the land are what all things despise—thus, they cannot prevail."*

**Pang** said: *"The skilled conqueror accumulates small defeats to achieve great victory. If one takes strength as virtue, their cause will surely weaken."*

Wood that is strong is felled.

**Ming Huang** said: *"The strong trunk lies low, while the supple branches grow upward. This is because the supple and weak occupy the high ground, while the rigid and strong occupy the low."*

**He** said: *"Strong wood bears branches and leaves above it."*

**Bi** said: *"This is where things are applied."*

**Pang** said: *"It is cut down and felled."*

Thus, rigidity and strength reside below.

**Bi** said: *"This is the root of greatness."*

Softness and weakness occupy the high ground.

**He** said: *"When nurturing things and achieving deeds, great trees are rooted below, while small things rise above. The Great Dao suppresses the strong and upholds the weak—this is the natural order."*

**Bi** said: *"This refers to branches and twigs."*

**Pang** said: *"I have often reflected: The Dao of yin and yang is such that yin precedes and yang follows. Heaven and Earth are such that Heaven is exalted and Earth is humble. Thus, in matters of *de* (virtue/power), softness and weakness suffice to overcome rigidity and strength, while in calculating affairs, rigidity and strength suffice to govern softness and weakness. Yet people of the world observe affairs without comprehending *de*—thus, those called 'strong' often become companions of death."*

One reading has *"strong and great"* instead of *"strong"* in *"strong and great reside below."*

**Chapter Seventy-Seven: The Dao of Heaven**

**Chapter Seventy-Eight: The Dao of Heaven and the Gentle and Weak**

The Dao of Heaven—is it not like drawing a bow?

**Emperor Minghuang** said: *"The Dao of Heaven is profound and distant; if it cannot be explained directly, then the analogy of drawing a bow is used to illustrate its function."*

**He** said: *"The Dao of Heaven is obscure; thus, comparisons with things are made to explain it."*

The high are suppressed, the low are elevated; the excessive are diminished, the deficient are supplemented.

**Emperor Minghuang** said: *"When drawing a bow in this way, only then can one hit the mark—just as the Dao of Heaven diminishes excess and supplements deficiency, desiring that the sovereign emulate Heaven’s Dao in governing the people. Hence, it demonstrates the way of suppressing the high and elevating the low."*

**He** said: *"Speaking of the bow’s balanced harmony—only then can it be used."*

To suppress the high and elevate the low, to diminish the strong and supplement the weak—this is the Dao of Heaven.

The Dao of Heaven diminishes excess and supplements deficiency.

**He** said: *"The Dao of Heaven diminishes excess and supplements deficiency, always valuing moderation and harmony as supreme."*

**Pang** said: *"The Dao of Heaven follows its natural course, thus achieving balance."*

One reading substitutes *with* for *supplement*.

But the Dao of humanity is not so.

**He** said: *"The Dao of humanity is contrary to the Dao of Heaven."*

**Bi** said: *"Only by aligning with Heaven and Earth’s virtue can one encompass all things. Like the Dao of Heaven, like the measure of humanity—each has its own nature, and they cannot be equalized. Only when there is no self, no private interest in natural spontaneity, can one then align with Heaven and Earth’s virtue."*

To diminish deficiency and serve excess.

**Emperor Minghuang** said: *"The Dao of Heaven distributes evenly, taking from the abundant to aid the scarce, while humanity defies Heaven, instead diminishing the deficient."*

**He** said: *"Common people diminish the poor to serve the rich, plunder the weak to strengthen the powerful."*

**Pang** said: *"The Dao of humanity follows desire, thus it is unbalanced."*

Who, then, can diminish excess and serve deficiency throughout the world? Only the Dao can do so.

**Emperor Minghuang** said: *"Who can use their own excess to serve the deficiency of the world? Only the Dao can do so."*

**He** said: *"Who can occupy a position of excess, self-reflecting to forgo rank and wealth to serve the world’s deficiency? Only the sovereign of the Dao can act thus."*

**Pang** said: *"The Dao aligns with Heaven’s Dao."*

One reading states: *"Who can use excess to serve the world? Only the Dao can do so."*

Thus, the Sage acts without relying [on results],

**Emperor Minghuang** said: *"The Sage follows Heaven, measures things appropriately, and distributes evenly, yet does not rely on his deeds."*

**He** said: *"The Sage acts in virtue and distribution, not expecting recompense."*

[The Sage] completes deeds without claiming them.

**Emperor Minghuang** said: *"He attributes success to others, not dwelling on achievement."*

**He** said: *"When deeds are accomplished, he does not dwell in that position."*

**Pang** said: *"One reading substitutes *dwell* for *claim*."*

Is it not because he does not wish to be seen as virtuous?

**He** said: *"He does not wish others to know of his virtue, concealing his deeds to avoid fame, fearing Heaven’s punishment for excess."*

**Pang** said: *"If one relies on deeds to claim achievement, then virtue is seen, and excess follows—this is the path to ruin."*

One reading omits the character *evil*.

**Chapter Seventy-Eight: The Gentle and Weak of the World**

---

**Chapter Seventy-Eight: The Gentle and Weak of the World (continued)**

**Chapter Seventy-Nine: Harmonizing Great Grievances**

The world has none more gentle and weak than water.
**He** said: *"It is round when round, square when square, clings when pressed, flows when released. Yet in overcoming the firm and strong, none can surpass it."*
**He** said: *"Water can embrace mountains and level hills, erode iron and dissolve copper—none can conquer water and achieve success."*

Is it not because nothing can replace it?
**He** said: *"To overcome the firm and strong, nothing is more unreplaceable than water."*

**Pang** said: *"Water adapts to all shapes—round, square, curved, straight—yet never alters its own nature. This is why it ultimately conquers all."*

Jade and stone are firm and strong, yet when used to attack others, they often shatter—because they can be altered.

The gentle and weak overcome the strong,
**He** said: *"Water can extinguish fire, and yin can consume yang. Softness can conquer rigidity."*
**He** said: *"The tongue is soft, the teeth are rigid, yet the teeth perish before the tongue."*

None in the world does not know,
**He** said: *"All know that the gentle and weak endure long, while the rigid and strong are broken and injured."*

Yet none can practice it.

**Emperor Minghuang** said: *"The Dao/Way of the gentle and weak surpasses that of the rigid and strong. None in the world does not know this. Though all know the Dao/Way, none can practice it."*

**He** said: *"They are ashamed of humility, yet love rigidity and force."*

**Pang** said: *"Few possess this one virtue."*

One reading states: *"Originally, it was written: 'Softness conquers rigidity, weakness overcomes strength.'"*

Thus the Sage speaks:
**He** said: *"Referring to governance."*

To bear the filth of the state is called the sovereign of the land.
**He** said: *"A ruler who bears the state’s filth, like the sea that does not reject small streams, can long preserve the land and become its sovereign."*

**Pang** said: *"Filth is not as grave as ill omens, so it is only called the sovereign of the land."*

To bear the ill omens of the state is called the king of the world.
**Emperor Minghuang** said: *"To assume the guilt of all is to bear the state’s filth. To claim 'loneliness, poverty, and lack of grain' is to bear the ill omens of the state. If his virtue is thus, then the land will be offered to him, and all under Heaven will turn toward him."*

**He** said: *"A ruler who takes blame upon himself and bears the ill omens of the people can thus rule all under Heaven."*

**Pang** said: *"The Sage can be gentle and weak because he embodies the nature of water. To bear filth and ill omens is to accept impurities and humiliation—this is the Dao/Way of the wise."*

Straight words seem reversed.
**Emperor Minghuang** said: *"To bear the filth of the state is to be the sovereign of the land; to bear the ill omens of the state is to be the king of the world. This must be straight speech at first, seeming contrary to common sense, hence 'straight words seem reversed.'"*
**He** said: *"This is the speech of the upright. The world does not know it as such."*

**Pang** said: *"It is contrary to shallow understanding’s near-truth, yet aligns with the ultimate rectitude of the Dao/Way."*

---

Harmonizing great grievances,
**Emperor Minghuang** said: *"Great grievances are those opposing the ruler’s own body—passions and desires. Harmonize means to reconcile."*

This refers to the ruler’s desire to reconcile the people through instruction, so that none harbor passions and desires.
**He** said: *"Those who cook for others die, those who harm others are punished—yet they still retaliate in kind. There will always be lingering grievances."*

**Emperor Minghuang** said: *"When establishing instruction for the people, one cannot avoid traces. These traces lead to grievances, hence 'there will always be lingering grievances.'"*

**He** said: *"Those who enforce punishments—human nature will inevitably cause grievances to extend even to the innocent."*

**Bi** said: *"If the ruler does not first clarify the principles of justice, great grievances will arise. Then, if he relies on virtue to reconcile them, the harm cannot be undone, hence 'there will always be lingering grievances.'"*

**Pang** said: *"If the ruler offers rites and ceremonies, yet the people do not respond, he will force them—then grievances begin. If he cannot restore order to its original state, yet still attempts to unite them in joy, the grievances will only grow worse. How can this be considered good?"*

**Emperor Minghuang** said: *"If lingering grievances exist, then it cannot be considered good."*

**He** said: *"If one man sighs in complaint, the ruler loses the hearts of the people. How can reconciling grievances be considered good?"*

**Pang** said: *"Though the intention may be good, the method itself is not."*

Thus the Sage holds the left token.
**He** said: *"In ancient times, the Sage held the left token, which was used to verify agreements and trust. Without written laws or documents, tokens were carved and matched to establish credibility."*

**Bi** said: *"The left token is what prevents grievances from arising."*

Yet he does not blame the people.
**Emperor Minghuang** said: *"The left token is the heart. The heart is the yin repository, matching with the external realm—hence it is called the left token."*

The Sage knows that establishing instruction will inevitably lead to grievances. If he explains them, lingering grievances will arise. Thus, he holds this heart, making it pure and still, and transforms the people through it. Then there will be no passions or desires, no need for punishments or blame, and naturally, the Dao/Way of non-action will be fulfilled.

**He** said: *"Only the token of trust is held below. The people are blamed for other matters."*

**Pang** said: *"The left token is taken from the people, the right token is given to the people. The left is without affairs, the right holds authority—thus the ancient division of tokens was like this. The Sage holds the left token, not engaging in affairs, yet all things come and align. I respond to their alignment alone. This is what is meant by 'responding and thus communicating with all under Heaven.'"*

Thus, the Sage is always burdened with the world’s blame yet never blames others. This is why he ultimately has no grievances.

*Zhuangzi* says: *"To gain is seen as belonging to others, to lose is seen as belonging to oneself."* Tang said: *"All under Heaven have guilt; the guilt lies upon my own body."* This is what is meant.

*Records* say: *"Those who offer oxen and horses hold the right token, as they present the tokens silently together."* Thus, it is known that the left token is held by those who bear blame.

*The Historical Records* say: *"The right token is used to hold people accountable."*

Thus, the virtuous ruler oversees tokens and trust.
**He** said: *"The virtuous ruler oversees only the tokens and trust."*

The **Bì** said: *"The virtuous person reflects upon the tokens of virtue, preventing resentment from arising before blaming others."*

The **Páng** said: *"The common people engage in affairs, responding to what harmonizes with them. The unvirtuous oversee only the enforcement of laws."*

The **Míng Huáng** said: *"'Oversee' means to govern. 'Enforcement' means to impose upon people. It is said that the virtuous ruler governs through the harmony of virtue, and thus people transform naturally. The unvirtuous ruler, however, establishes laws to impose upon people, and such laws bring harm—this is not yet good governance."*

The **Hé** said: *"The unvirtuous ruler betrays the tokens of trust and holds people accountable for their failures."*

The **Bì** said: *"'Enforcement' means to hold people accountable for their faults."*

The **Páng** said: *"'Enforcement' means to impose upon people, and when one seeks to impose upon all things, the obstacles become many. The Dao of Heaven has no favorites; it always aligns with the virtuous."*

The **Míng Huáng** said: *"To govern through the tokens of virtue is to maintain clarity and stillness; to govern through laws is to bring exhaustion and ruin. Heaven has no favorites—it aids only virtue. Thus, the sovereign must always strive for spontaneous transformation through non-action, never establishing laws to create affairs."*

The **Hé** said: *"The Dao of Heaven has no distinctions of near or far; it aligns only with the virtuous, meaning it aligns with those who govern through the tokens of virtue."*

The **Páng** said: *"Only Heaven has no favorites—it aids only the good, making clear that Heaven and the Sage share the same Way."*

---
**Chapter Eighty: Small States, Few People**

*"Small states, few people."*

The **Hé** said: *"Though the Sage governs a great state, he still regards it as small, practicing frugality without extravagance. Though the people are many, he treats them as if they were few, never burdening them."*

The **Bì** said: *"If a state is already small and its people few, it is still possible to return to antiquity. How much more so for a great state with many people? Thus, extolling the small state is auspicious."*

The **Páng** said: *"Small governance and few people gather the populace in sincerity and thickness. When a state is large and its people many, conflicts of interest arise, cunning and deceit flourish daily. Observe the people of great cities and crowded settlements—their honesty and guile differ greatly, and this is the proof."*

*"Let the people have implements of ten or a hundred, yet not use them."*

The **Míng Huáng** said: *"'Ten' means a group of ten; 'a hundred' means a group of a hundred. This chapter reveals that people are naturally harmonious and unassuming, possessing talents and implements fit to serve as leaders of tens or hundreds—but they are not used."*

The **Hé** said: *"Let the people each lead their households and groups of tens or hundreds, so that high and low do not encroach upon one another. 'Implements' refers to the tools of farmers, which are not used for conscription, nor to seize the people’s prime agricultural time."*

The **Bì** said: *"If the people, though possessing implements for tens or hundreds, are not used—what need is there to fear scarcity?"*

The **Páng** said: *"The implements of ten are those shared by ten people; the implements of a hundred are those shared by a hundred. Under governance of clarity and stillness, the aim is to let the people fulfill their natural inclinations without unnecessary labor, thus avoiding gatherings and assemblies. Hence, though implements of tens or hundreds exist, they are not used."*

*"Let the people hold life as precious,"*

The **Hé** said: *"If the ruler can promote the people’s well-being and remove their harms, letting each find their place, then the people will value life and avoid death."*

*"and not move far."*

The **Míng Huáng** said: *"With few thoughts and desires, they do not lightly squander their lives, rooted in their essence without seeking, hence they do not migrate far."*

The **Hé** said: *"When governance is not burdensome, the people remain settled in their occupations, thus they do not move far from their customary dwellings."*

The **Bì** said: *"If the people are not burdened, they treasure their own lives, not coveting wealth or paths, hence they remain in their homes, valuing life and not migrating far."*

The **Páng** said: *"Delighting in life and fulfilling nature, they value death and cherish their homeland, seeking nothing, hence they do not migrate far. In an age of chaos, the people are thin and affairs are many, while greed and desire overcome their love of life. The roots wither and the branches flourish, while covetousness extends beyond their due—thus they stumble into punishments and dangers as if walking on level ground, and their cart tracks and footprints crisscross the four directions. This is the extreme of disorder."*

*"Though they have boats and carriages, there is no need to ride them."*

The **Hé** said: *"In governance of clarity and non-action, there is no indulgence in extravagance, nor fondness for travel and amusement."*

*"Though they have armor and weapons, there is no need to display them."*

The **Hé** said: *"There is no resentment toward the world."*

The **Páng** said: *"When the people suffice within their natural allotments, there are no calamities of distant journeys or warfare."*

*"Let the people return to using knotted cords."*

The **Míng Huáng** said: *"Boats and carriages are for facilitating migration; armor and weapons are for waging war. Since there is no desire for these, there is no need to use or fear them. Thus, they return to the simplicity of the Three Sovereigns, using only knotted cords."*

The **Hé** said: *"Abandoning artifice and returning to simplicity, they act without deceit."*

The **Páng** said: *"With simple governance and sincere people, there is no need for written contracts."*

*"Let them find their food sweet,"*

The **Míng Huáng** said: *"They do not crave flavors, so their food is always sweet."*

The **Hé** said: *"They find sweetness in their plain fare, not craving the hundred beasts."*

*"and their clothing beautiful."*

The **Míng Huáng** said: *"They do not indulge in embroidered fabrics, so their garments are all beautiful."*

The **Hé** said: *"They find beauty in their coarse clothing, not valuing the five colors."*

The **Páng** said: *"Sweet food and beautiful clothing—without effort, they are rich."*

*"Let their customs be settled,"*

The **Míng Huáng** said: *"They do not adorn their dwellings, so their homes are settled."*

The **Hé** said: *"They find contentment in their thatched huts, not favoring ornate houses."*

*"and their occupations joyful."*

The **Míng Huáng** said: *"They do not corrupt their natural simplicity, so their customs are joyful."*

The **Hé** said: *"They delight in their simple and unadorned customs, not altering them."*

*"Neighboring states are in sight, and the sounds of chickens and dogs are heard between them."*

The **Míng Huáng** said: *"This speaks of their proximity."*

The **Hé** said: *"They are near one another."*

The **Páng** said: *"People fulfill their natural lives, thus they multiply and rejoice."*

*"Let the people reach old age and death without traveling far."*

The **Míng Huáng** said: *"This is the extreme of having no desires."*

The **Hé** said: *"They lack emotional attachments."*

The **Bì** said: *"They have no desires or cravings."*

The **Páng** said: *"Though near, they do not interact—having no external desires."*

*"This is governance that fulfills nature, hence the people also fulfill their nature."*

*"I have often examined the final chapters of the* Analects *and* Mencius*, where they all speak of the deeds of the Sage-kings Yáo, Shùn, Yǔ, and Tāng. It seems that if one applies this entire text to governance, its effects can be compared to theirs."*

The **Lǎo Zǐ**, the great Sage, encountered changes that aligned precisely with the time of returning to the origin and fulfilling nature. Therefore, he alone illuminated the meaning of **Dao** and **virtue**, using it to gather and restrain the scattered phenomena of the world—unifying them in the **Pú** (primordial simplicity).

If one applies his entire text to governance, then transforming the people and establishing customs would be its effect, as seen in this passage. Thus, the meaning of the *Classics* concludes here.

Yáng Zǐ composed *The Law of Speech*, which also ends with the words of the **Sage-kings** of **Táng** and **Yǔ**. Indeed, there is a method in the teachings of **Kǒng** and **Mèng**, and this text. Yet, Yáng Zǐ’s words, though truly applicable to the world, are hardly sufficient to compare with the deeds of the **Sages**—one may say he grasped their intent, but his speech exceeded it.

(Original: *信言不美章第八十一*)
**Faithful speech is not ornate.**

The **Míng Huáng** said: *"Faithful speech is the **Sage’s teaching**."*

Speech that is true and real does not conform to the tastes of the common people, hence it is not ornate.

The **Hé** said: *"Faithful speech is like its substance. Ornate speech is simple and unadorned."*

The **Bì** said: *"Its substance lies in its simplicity."*

The **Páng** said: *"Faithful speech is essential to the **Dao**. The **Dao’s** words, when spoken, are plain and without relish—what ornamentation could there be? Ornate speech is not faithful."*

The **Míng Huáng** said: *"Ornate speech is a substitute for teaching."* Speech that is sweet and pleasing appeals to the tastes of the common people, hence it is not faithful.

The **Hé** said: *"Excessively ornate speech is elaborate and flowery. Faithlessness is embellishment and deception, full of emptiness."*

The **Bì** said: *"Its origin lies in simplicity."*

The **Páng** said: *"When one speaks of phenomena apart from the **Dao**, even if it pleases the shallow ears of the unlearned, it is not profound discourse. Speech is like wind and waves—why seek ornamentation? The good do not argue. The **Míng Huáng** said: *"The good manifest in action, not in argument."* The **Hé** said: *"The good cultivate themselves with the **Dao**, not with rhetorical flourishes."* The **Páng** said: *"Speech may be close, but its meaning is distant—no need for elaborate words."*

The **Míng Huáng** said: *"The skilled are not good."* Clever and convoluted argumentation renders one unworthy.

The **Hé** said: *"The skilled are those who use clever words."* The unworthy suffer from their tongues.

The **Páng** said: *"The earth yields jade, but digging exhausts its mountain; water yields pearls, but turbidity befogs its depths. A mouth that speaks much loses its life."*

The **Páng** said: *"Speech serves only to elucidate the **Dao**. The essential is not to be verbose—why indulge in much talk?"*
**Kǒng Zǐ** said: *"Words suffice when meaning is conveyed."*

**The wise are not indiscriminately learned.**

The **Hé** said: *"The wise refer to those who grasp the **Dao**—indiscriminate learning is clinging to a single principle."*

The **Bì** said: *"The extreme lies in unity."*

The **Páng** said: *"The extreme should lie in the most subtle essence—what need is there for indiscriminate learning? One who is indiscriminately learned does not truly know."*

The **Míng Huáng** said: *"The wise are those who have awakened understanding; the indiscriminately learned are those who have heard much."*

The **Hé** said: *"The indiscriminately learned see and hear much, but the unwise lose the true essence."*

The **Páng** said: *"Drowned in the multitude of phenomena, they cannot return to simplicity. The most foolish among them—where could true wisdom be found?"*

**The Sage has no accumulation.**

The **Míng Huáng** said: *"Accumulation means clinging to words and obstructing teachings, having fixed attachments."* The **Sage** awakens to speech and forgets it, understands teachings and discards them—unbound by any fixation, hence no accumulation.

The **Bì** said: *"Without private possession, only goodness is given—trusting in all things."*

The **Páng** said: *"The **Sage’s Dao** flows without direction, yet the **Sage** remains ever without obstruction. Thus, with utter simplicity, they fulfill the demands of all things. If one accumulates, the gains are few—how could they transform and govern all?"* This is the clear understanding that the wise are not indiscriminately learned.

**The more one gives to others, the more one possesses.**

The **Hé** said: *"The more one gives to others in bestowing **virtue** and transforming customs, the more one’s own **virtue** increases."*

The **Bì** said: *"This is what things revere."*

The **Páng** said: *"To give to others means to apply oneself to governance and the world. Following the natural course of things and benefiting them, then one’s **Dao** is not diminished, and one’s deeds are ever more expansive."*

---

**Emperor Xuānzōng said:** "This is the **Dao/Way** of the **Sage**—its nature is inexhaustible. The **Sage**, though not hoarding or obstructing words of instruction, yet entices the common and vulgar with the **law/flavor** of the **Dao/Way**, exhaustively bestowing it upon others. To the **Sage’s** serene and tranquil nature, this brings no depletion; rather, it only grows clearer and more luminous. Hence the saying: *the more one gives to others, the more one possesses.* **‘Have’** refers to the **Sage’s** own clarity of nature; **‘many’** refers to the outer brilliance that increases.

**The Hé said:** "If one gives wealth and bribes in alms to others, the wealth becomes ever more abundant—like the light of the sun and moon, which knows no end."

**The Bì said:** "This is what things follow."

**The Páng said:** "To give to others is to impart the **Dao/Way**. To impart the **Dao/Way** to others is like Heaven nurturing all things: I have never expended myself, yet things daily grow in number. Since it is said that there is no accumulation, it is further made clear that one can thus satisfy all things. Only by having no accumulation can one satisfy them all. The **Dao/Way** of Heaven is to benefit without harming.

**The Hé said:** "Heaven generates all things, cherishes and nourishes them, allowing them to grow and flourish without harm."

**The Bì said:** "It is ever in motion, creating and sustaining all."

**The Páng said:** "Heaven is the progenitor of all things, ever fostering them with benevolent care. How could it harbor intent to harm? This is the **Dao/Way** of trustworthy speech—if it is beautiful here, it will harm there. The **Dao/Way** of the **Sage** is to act without contention. The **Emperor Xuānzōng** said: ‘The **Dao/Way** of Heaven benefits all things without harming—this is to clarify the **Dao/Way** of the **Sage** in action and expansive benefit, ever bestowing it upon others, hence no contention.’

**The Hé said:** "The **Sage** follows Heaven’s **Dao/Way**, and all actions and transformations are complete. They do not contend with the lower ranks for fame or renown, thus preserving their **Sage’s** true merit."

**The Bì said:** "They align with Heaven’s benefit, never causing injury."

**The Páng said:** "With one breath of **qi**, all things are fulfilled. The **Sage** embodies the great **Dao/Way** in action, moving at times swift, at times slow—how could they harbor thoughts of contention? Indeed, only thus do they establish words and transmit **laws**. They adapt to the times and follow **principles**, doing just what is fitting, not for mere argument’s sake. Yet they ultimately rely on this sentence: truly desiring to embody the **Dao/Way** of **virtue/power** and **moral integrity**, nothing surpasses *acting without contention.* The *Lǎozǐ*’s **sutra/scripture** concludes at the **eighty-first chapter**, this chapter summarizing the intent of his composition. It is also because *Nán Huá* [Southern Flower, i.e., *Zhuāngzǐ*] ends with the **Sage**’s death, and yet there is further *Tianxià* [Heaven and Earth, i.e., *Tianxia*], one chapter, clarifying the **dharma/principles** of **Dao/Way** and **technique/art**—right and wrong—so as to leave behind the traces of their compilation.

---

**Postscript:**
The writings of *Lǎozǐ* have long been transmitted in the world, their words subtle, their meaning profound, yet none could fully grasp them. Scholars who do not clearly comprehend and penetrate to their depths cannot fathom the **Dao/Way** of **virtue/power**, life, and nature—its boundaries are difficult to measure.

*Lǎozǐ* did not intend to construct lofty theories to bewilder the world. The **Dao/Way**’s ultimate essence is mysterious and obscure, even the **Sage** struggles to name its bounds. When the **Dao/Way** and its techniques were scattered, if one did not reveal their origins and outline their broad principles, then the world and future generations would have those who are foolish yet unenlightened—lost and adrift, without anchor or guard. They would not know the **Dao/Way**’s fundamental source and become inverted citizens.

Thus, compelled, I forcefully articulate these words to clarify. The **Dao/Way** is vast, filling all of Heaven and Earth without limit; it is small, entering the minutest crevices without gap. Its profundity opens as the gateway to all marvels; its coarseness governs the home, the state, and the world—it is nowhere absent.

There have been three who previously commented: **Hé Shànggōng**, **Emperor Xuānzōng**, and **Wáng Bì**. Though their interpretations differ in what they adopt or reject, each has its strengths. Ultimately, they all seek to explore the **Dao/Way**’s fundamental essence.

In recent times, **Wáng Páng** delved deeply into the study of the **Dao/Way** of **virtue/power** and life and nature, further elucidating *Lǎozǐ*’s intent. He penetrated the subtle and illuminated the hidden, forming a unique interpretation, making the **eighty-first chapter** yet more apparent in the world.

Yet scholars of the world regard *Lǎozǐ*’s writings as mere empty, useless words, seldom paying attention. They cling to shallow, vulgar opinions, thinking they have attained understanding, yet they truly do not grasp the **Dao/Way**’s fundamental essence, which was clarified only by *Lǎozǐ*. His **sutra/scripture** was fully known only through the interpretations of these scholars. This is not easily heard by the common learner.

**Magistrate Zhāng** [public official’s title], profoundly understood the **Dao/Way** of **virtue/power**, life, and nature. He cultivated himself through literature and art, and as a guardian and teacher, he lamented that those who held the **scripture** did not know the **Dao/Way**. Thus, he commanded the students in the **school/academy** to study these four interpretations, neither adding nor detracting, and compiled them for circulation in his time, broadening their teachings. So that those who inherit learning might know that *Lǎozǐ*’s writings are not merely empty, fanciful words—his research is profound and refined, all capable of reaching the ultimate truth. Its core is for self-cultivation; its residue extends to governance of the world and the state. Thus, learning is far more than mere supplementary aid.

I humbly follow the teachings of my predecessors, fortunate to receive their guidance directly. Therefore, I merely request to record the year of this work, lest its transmission be forgotten.

In the year **Yuánfú** the First, on the first day of the tenth month, **Liáng Jiào** (my humble name), formerly the **judge of military affairs** in **Qián Yīng Prefecture**, respectfully composed this preface.
